17  The Picture of Dorian Gray
18  
19  by
20  
21  Oscar Wilde
22  
23  
24  
25  
26  THE PREFACE
27  
28  The artist is the creator of beautiful things.  To reveal art and
29  conceal the artist is art's aim.  The critic is he who can translate
30  into another manner or a new material his impression of beautiful
31  things.
32  
33  The highest as the lowest form of criticism is a mode of autobiography.
34  Those who find ugly meanings in beautiful things are corrupt without
35  being charming.  This is a fault.
36  
37  Those who find beautiful meanings in beautiful things are the
38  cultivated.  For these there is hope.  They are the elect to whom
39  beautiful things mean only beauty.
40  
41  There is no such thing as a moral or an immoral book.  Books are well
42  written, or badly written.  That is all.
43  
44  The nineteenth century dislike of realism is the rage of Caliban seeing
45  his own face in a glass.
46  
47  The nineteenth century dislike of romanticism is the rage of Caliban
48  not seeing his own face in a glass.  The moral life of man forms part
49  of the subject-matter of the artist, but the morality of art consists
50  in the perfect use of an imperfect medium.  No artist desires to prove
51  anything.  Even things that are true can be proved.  No artist has
52  ethical sympathies.  An ethical sympathy in an artist is an
53  unpardonable mannerism of style.  No artist is ever morbid.  The artist
54  can express everything.  Thought and language are to the artist
55  instruments of an art.  Vice and virtue are to the artist materials for
56  an art.  From the point of view of form, the type of all the arts is
57  the art of the musician.  From the point of view of feeling, the
58  actor's craft is the type.  All art is at once surface and symbol.
59  Those who go beneath the surface do so at their peril.  Those who read
60  the symbol do so at their peril.  It is the spectator, and not life,
61  that art really mirrors.  Diversity of opinion about a work of art
62  shows that the work is new, complex, and vital.  When critics disagree,
63  the artist is in accord with himself.  We can forgive a man for making
64  a useful thing as long as he does not admire it.  The only excuse for
65  making a useless thing is that one admires it intensely.
66  
67                 All art is quite useless.
68  
69                              OSCAR WILDE
70  
71  
72  
73  
74  CHAPTER 1
75  
76  The studio was filled with the rich odour of roses, and when the light
77  summer wind stirred amidst the trees of the garden, there came through
78  the open door the heavy scent of the lilac, or the more delicate
79  perfume of the pink-flowering thorn.
80  
81  From the corner of the divan of Persian saddle-bags on which he was
82  lying, smoking, as was his custom, innumerable cigarettes, Lord Henry
83  Wotton could just catch the gleam of the honey-sweet and honey-coloured
84  blossoms of a laburnum, whose tremulous branches seemed hardly able to
85  bear the burden of a beauty so flamelike as theirs; and now and then
86  the fantastic shadows of birds in flight flitted across the long
87  tussore-silk curtains that were stretched in front of the huge window,
88  producing a kind of momentary Japanese effect, and making him think of
89  those pallid, jade-faced painters of Tokyo who, through the medium of
90  an art that is necessarily immobile, seek to convey the sense of
91  swiftness and motion.  The sullen murmur of the bees shouldering their
92  way through the long unmown grass, or circling with monotonous
93  insistence round the dusty gilt horns of the straggling woodbine,
94  seemed to make the stillness more oppressive.  The dim roar of London
95  was like the bourdon note of a distant organ.
96  
97  In the centre of the room, clamped to an upright easel, stood the
98  full-length portrait of a young man of extraordinary personal beauty,
99  and in front of it, some little distance away, was sitting the artist
100  himself, Basil Hallward, whose sudden disappearance some years ago
101  caused, at the time, such public excitement and gave rise to so many
102  strange conjectures.
103  
104  As the painter looked at the gracious and comely form he had so
105  skilfully mirrored in his art, a smile of pleasure passed across his
106  face, and seemed about to linger there.  But he suddenly started up,
107  and closing his eyes, placed his fingers upon the lids, as though he
108  sought to imprison within his brain some curious dream from which he
109  feared he might awake.
110  
111  "It is your best work, Basil, the best thing you have ever done," said
112  Lord Henry languidly.  "You must certainly send it next year to the
113  Grosvenor.  The Academy is too large and too vulgar.  Whenever I have
114  gone there, there have been either so many people that I have not been
115  able to see the pictures, which was dreadful, or so many pictures that
116  I have not been able to see the people, which was worse.  The Grosvenor
117  is really the only place."
118  
119  "I don't think I shall send it anywhere," he answered, tossing his head
120  back in that odd way that used to make his friends laugh at him at
121  Oxford.  "No, I won't send it anywhere."
122  
123  Lord Henry elevated his eyebrows and looked at him in amazement through
124  the thin blue wreaths of smoke that curled up in such fanciful whorls
125  from his heavy, opium-tainted cigarette.  "Not send it anywhere?  My
126  dear fellow, why?  Have you any reason?  What odd chaps you painters
127  are!  You do anything in the world to gain a reputation.  As soon as
128  you have one, you seem to want to throw it away.  It is silly of you,
129  for there is only one thing in the world worse than being talked about,
130  and that is not being talked about.  A portrait like this would set you
131  far above all the young men in England, and make the old men quite
132  jealous, if old men are ever capable of any emotion."
133  
134  "I know you will laugh at me," he replied, "but I really can't exhibit
135  it.  I have put too much of myself into it."
136  
137  Lord Henry stretched himself out on the divan and laughed.
138  
139  "Yes, I knew you would; but it is quite true, all the same."
140  
141  "Too much of yourself in it! Upon my word, Basil, I didn't know you
142  were so vain; and I really can't see any resemblance between you, with
143  your rugged strong face and your coal-black hair, and this young
144  Adonis, who looks as if he was made out of ivory and rose-leaves. Why,
145  my dear Basil, he is a Narcissus, and you--well, of course you have an
146  intellectual expression and all that.  But beauty, real beauty, ends
147  where an intellectual expression begins.  Intellect is in itself a mode
148  of exaggeration, and destroys the harmony of any face.  The moment one
149  sits down to think, one becomes all nose, or all forehead, or something
150  horrid.  Look at the successful men in any of the learned professions.
151  How perfectly hideous they are!  Except, of course, in the Church.  But
152  then in the Church they don't think.  A bishop keeps on saying at the
153  age of eighty what he was told to say when he was a boy of eighteen,
154  and as a natural consequence he always looks absolutely delightful.
155  Your mysterious young friend, whose name you have never told me, but
156  whose picture really fascinates me, never thinks.  I feel quite sure of
157  that.  He is some brainless beautiful creature who should be always
158  here in winter when we have no flowers to look at, and always here in
159  summer when we want something to chill our intelligence.  Don't flatter
160  yourself, Basil:  you are not in the least like him."
161  
162  "You don't understand me, Harry," answered the artist.  "Of course I am
163  not like him.  I know that perfectly well.  Indeed, I should be sorry
164  to look like him.  You shrug your shoulders?  I am telling you the
165  truth.  There is a fatality about all physical and intellectual
166  distinction, the sort of fatality that seems to dog through history the
167  faltering steps of kings.  It is better not to be different from one's
168  fellows.  The ugly and the stupid have the best of it in this world.
169  They can sit at their ease and gape at the play.  If they know nothing
170  of victory, they are at least spared the knowledge of defeat.  They
171  live as we all should live--undisturbed, indifferent, and without
172  disquiet.  They neither bring ruin upon others, nor ever receive it
173  from alien hands.  Your rank and wealth, Harry; my brains, such as they
174  are--my art, whatever it may be worth; Dorian Gray's good looks--we
175  shall all suffer for what the gods have given us, suffer terribly."
176  
177  "Dorian Gray?  Is that his name?" asked Lord Henry, walking across the
178  studio towards Basil Hallward.
179  
180  "Yes, that is his name.  I didn't intend to tell it to you."
181  
182  "But why not?"
183  
184  "Oh, I can't explain.  When I like people immensely, I never tell their
185  names to any one.  It is like surrendering a part of them.  I have
186  grown to love secrecy.  It seems to be the one thing that can make
187  modern life mysterious or marvellous to us.  The commonest thing is
188  delightful if one only hides it.  When I leave town now I never tell my
189  people where I am going.  If I did, I would lose all my pleasure.  It
190  is a silly habit, I dare say, but somehow it seems to bring a great
191  deal of romance into one's life.  I suppose you think me awfully
192  foolish about it?"
193  
194  "Not at all," answered Lord Henry, "not at all, my dear Basil.  You
195  seem to forget that I am married, and the one charm of marriage is that
196  it makes a life of deception absolutely necessary for both parties.  I
197  never know where my wife is, and my wife never knows what I am doing.
198  When we meet--we do meet occasionally, when we dine out together, or go
199  down to the Duke's--we tell each other the most absurd stories with the
200  most serious faces.  My wife is very good at it--much better, in fact,
201  than I am.  She never gets confused over her dates, and I always do.
202  But when she does find me out, she makes no row at all.  I sometimes
203  wish she would; but she merely laughs at me."
204  
205  "I hate the way you talk about your married life, Harry," said Basil
206  Hallward, strolling towards the door that led into the garden.  "I
207  believe that you are really a very good husband, but that you are
208  thoroughly ashamed of your own virtues.  You are an extraordinary
209  fellow.  You never say a moral thing, and you never do a wrong thing.
210  Your cynicism is simply a pose."
211  
212  "Being natural is simply a pose, and the most irritating pose I know,"
213  cried Lord Henry, laughing; and the two young men went out into the
214  garden together and ensconced themselves on a long bamboo seat that
215  stood in the shade of a tall laurel bush.  The sunlight slipped over
216  the polished leaves.  In the grass, white daisies were tremulous.
217  
218  After a pause, Lord Henry pulled out his watch.  "I am afraid I must be
219  going, Basil," he murmured, "and before I go, I insist on your
220  answering a question I put to you some time ago."
221  
222  "What is that?" said the painter, keeping his eyes fixed on the ground.
223  
224  "You know quite well."
225  
226  "I do not, Harry."
227  
228  "Well, I will tell you what it is.  I want you to explain to me why you
229  won't exhibit Dorian Gray's picture.  I want the real reason."
230  
231  "I told you the real reason."
232  
233  "No, you did not.  You said it was because there was too much of
234  yourself in it.  Now, that is childish."
235  
236  "Harry," said Basil Hallward, looking him straight in the face, "every
237  portrait that is painted with feeling is a portrait of the artist, not
238  of the sitter.  The sitter is merely the accident, the occasion.  It is
239  not he who is revealed by the painter; it is rather the painter who, on
240  the coloured canvas, reveals himself.  The reason I will not exhibit
241  this picture is that I am afraid that I have shown in it the secret of
242  my own soul."
243  
244  Lord Henry laughed.  "And what is that?" he asked.
245  
246  "I will tell you," said Hallward; but an expression of perplexity came
247  over his face.
248  
249  "I am all expectation, Basil," continued his companion, glancing at him.
250  
251  "Oh, there is really very little to tell, Harry," answered the painter;
252  "and I am afraid you will hardly understand it.  Perhaps you will
253  hardly believe it."
254  
255  Lord Henry smiled, and leaning down, plucked a pink-petalled daisy from
256  the grass and examined it.  "I am quite sure I shall understand it," he
257  replied, gazing intently at the little golden, white-feathered disk,
258  "and as for believing things, I can believe anything, provided that it
259  is quite incredible."
260  
261  The wind shook some blossoms from the trees, and the heavy
262  lilac-blooms, with their clustering stars, moved to and fro in the
263  languid air.  A grasshopper began to chirrup by the wall, and like a
264  blue thread a long thin dragon-fly floated past on its brown gauze
265  wings.  Lord Henry felt as if he could hear Basil Hallward's heart
266  beating, and wondered what was coming.
267  
268  "The story is simply this," said the painter after some time.  "Two
269  months ago I went to a crush at Lady Brandon's. You know we poor
270  artists have to show ourselves in society from time to time, just to
271  remind the public that we are not savages.  With an evening coat and a
272  white tie, as you told me once, anybody, even a stock-broker, can gain
273  a reputation for being civilized.  Well, after I had been in the room
274  about ten minutes, talking to huge overdressed dowagers and tedious
275  academicians, I suddenly became conscious that some one was looking at
276  me.  I turned half-way round and saw Dorian Gray for the first time.
277  When our eyes met, I felt that I was growing pale.  A curious sensation
278  of terror came over me.  I knew that I had come face to face with some
279  one whose mere personality was so fascinating that, if I allowed it to
280  do so, it would absorb my whole nature, my whole soul, my very art
281  itself.  I did not want any external influence in my life.  You know
282  yourself, Harry, how independent I am by nature.  I have always been my
283  own master; had at least always been so, till I met Dorian Gray.
284  Then--but I don't know how to explain it to you.  Something seemed to
285  tell me that I was on the verge of a terrible crisis in my life.  I had
286  a strange feeling that fate had in store for me exquisite joys and
287  exquisite sorrows.  I grew afraid and turned to quit the room.  It was
288  not conscience that made me do so:  it was a sort of cowardice.  I take
289  no credit to myself for trying to escape."
290  
291  "Conscience and cowardice are really the same things, Basil.
292  Conscience is the trade-name of the firm.  That is all."
293  
294  "I don't believe that, Harry, and I don't believe you do either.
295  However, whatever was my motive--and it may have been pride, for I used
296  to be very proud--I certainly struggled to the door.  There, of course,
297  I stumbled against Lady Brandon.  'You are not going to run away so
298  soon, Mr. Hallward?' she screamed out.  You know her curiously shrill
299  voice?"
300  
301  "Yes; she is a peacock in everything but beauty," said Lord Henry,
302  pulling the daisy to bits with his long nervous fingers.
303  
304  "I could not get rid of her.  She brought me up to royalties, and
305  people with stars and garters, and elderly ladies with gigantic tiaras
306  and parrot noses.  She spoke of me as her dearest friend.  I had only
307  met her once before, but she took it into her head to lionize me.  I
308  believe some picture of mine had made a great success at the time, at
309  least had been chattered about in the penny newspapers, which is the
310  nineteenth-century standard of immortality.  Suddenly I found myself
311  face to face with the young man whose personality had so strangely
312  stirred me.  We were quite close, almost touching.  Our eyes met again.
313  It was reckless of me, but I asked Lady Brandon to introduce me to him.
314  Perhaps it was not so reckless, after all.  It was simply inevitable.
315  We would have spoken to each other without any introduction.  I am sure
316  of that.  Dorian told me so afterwards.  He, too, felt that we were
317  destined to know each other."
318  
319  "And how did Lady Brandon describe this wonderful young man?" asked his
320  companion.  "I know she goes in for giving a rapid _precis_ of all her
321  guests.  I remember her bringing me up to a truculent and red-faced old
322  gentleman covered all over with orders and ribbons, and hissing into my
323  ear, in a tragic whisper which must have been perfectly audible to
324  everybody in the room, the most astounding details.  I simply fled.  I
325  like to find out people for myself.  But Lady Brandon treats her guests
326  exactly as an auctioneer treats his goods.  She either explains them
327  entirely away, or tells one everything about them except what one wants
328  to know."
329  
330  "Poor Lady Brandon!  You are hard on her, Harry!" said Hallward
331  listlessly.
332  
333  "My dear fellow, she tried to found a _salon_, and only succeeded in
334  opening a restaurant.  How could I admire her?  But tell me, what did
335  she say about Mr. Dorian Gray?"
336  
337  "Oh, something like, 'Charming boy--poor dear mother and I absolutely
338  inseparable.  Quite forget what he does--afraid he--doesn't do
339  anything--oh, yes, plays the piano--or is it the violin, dear Mr.
340  Gray?'  Neither of us could help laughing, and we became friends at
341  once."
342  
343  "Laughter is not at all a bad beginning for a friendship, and it is far
344  the best ending for one," said the young lord, plucking another daisy.
345  
346  Hallward shook his head.  "You don't understand what friendship is,
347  Harry," he murmured--"or what enmity is, for that matter.  You like
348  every one; that is to say, you are indifferent to every one."
349  
350  "How horribly unjust of you!" cried Lord Henry, tilting his hat back
351  and looking up at the little clouds that, like ravelled skeins of
352  glossy white silk, were drifting across the hollowed turquoise of the
353  summer sky.  "Yes; horribly unjust of you.  I make a great difference
354  between people.  I choose my friends for their good looks, my
355  acquaintances for their good characters, and my enemies for their good
356  intellects.  A man cannot be too careful in the choice of his enemies.
357  I have not got one who is a fool.  They are all men of some
358  intellectual power, and consequently they all appreciate me.  Is that
359  very vain of me?  I think it is rather vain."
360  
361  "I should think it was, Harry.  But according to your category I must
362  be merely an acquaintance."
363  
364  "My dear old Basil, you are much more than an acquaintance."
365  
366  "And much less than a friend.  A sort of brother, I suppose?"
367  
368  "Oh, brothers!  I don't care for brothers.  My elder brother won't die,
369  and my younger brothers seem never to do anything else."
370  
371  "Harry!" exclaimed Hallward, frowning.
372  
373  "My dear fellow, I am not quite serious.  But I can't help detesting my
374  relations.  I suppose it comes from the fact that none of us can stand
375  other people having the same faults as ourselves.  I quite sympathize
376  with the rage of the English democracy against what they call the vices
377  of the upper orders.  The masses feel that drunkenness, stupidity, and
378  immorality should be their own special property, and that if any one of
379  us makes an ass of himself, he is poaching on their preserves.  When
380  poor Southwark got into the divorce court, their indignation was quite
381  magnificent.  And yet I don't suppose that ten per cent of the
382  proletariat live correctly."
383  
384  "I don't agree with a single word that you have said, and, what is
385  more, Harry, I feel sure you don't either."
386  
387  Lord Henry stroked his pointed brown beard and tapped the toe of his
388  patent-leather boot with a tasselled ebony cane.  "How English you are
389  Basil!  That is the second time you have made that observation.  If one
390  puts forward an idea to a true Englishman--always a rash thing to
391  do--he never dreams of considering whether the idea is right or wrong.
392  The only thing he considers of any importance is whether one believes
393  it oneself.  Now, the value of an idea has nothing whatsoever to do
394  with the sincerity of the man who expresses it.  Indeed, the
395  probabilities are that the more insincere the man is, the more purely
396  intellectual will the idea be, as in that case it will not be coloured
397  by either his wants, his desires, or his prejudices.  However, I don't
398  propose to discuss politics, sociology, or metaphysics with you.  I
399  like persons better than principles, and I like persons with no
400  principles better than anything else in the world.  Tell me more about
401  Mr. Dorian Gray.  How often do you see him?"
402  
403  "Every day.  I couldn't be happy if I didn't see him every day.  He is
404  absolutely necessary to me."
405  
406  "How extraordinary!  I thought you would never care for anything but
407  your art."
408  
409  "He is all my art to me now," said the painter gravely.  "I sometimes
410  think, Harry, that there are only two eras of any importance in the
411  world's history.  The first is the appearance of a new medium for art,
412  and the second is the appearance of a new personality for art also.
413  What the invention of oil-painting was to the Venetians, the face of
414  Antinous was to late Greek sculpture, and the face of Dorian Gray will
415  some day be to me.  It is not merely that I paint from him, draw from
416  him, sketch from him.  Of course, I have done all that.  But he is much
417  more to me than a model or a sitter.  I won't tell you that I am
418  dissatisfied with what I have done of him, or that his beauty is such
419  that art cannot express it.  There is nothing that art cannot express,
420  and I know that the work I have done, since I met Dorian Gray, is good
421  work, is the best work of my life.  But in some curious way--I wonder
422  will you understand me?--his personality has suggested to me an
423  entirely new manner in art, an entirely new mode of style.  I see
424  things differently, I think of them differently.  I can now recreate
425  life in a way that was hidden from me before.  'A dream of form in days
426  of thought'--who is it who says that?  I forget; but it is what Dorian
427  Gray has been to me.  The merely visible presence of this lad--for he
428  seems to me little more than a lad, though he is really over
429  twenty--his merely visible presence--ah!  I wonder can you realize all
430  that that means?  Unconsciously he defines for me the lines of a fresh
431  school, a school that is to have in it all the passion of the romantic
432  spirit, all the perfection of the spirit that is Greek.  The harmony of
433  soul and body--how much that is!  We in our madness have separated the
434  two, and have invented a realism that is vulgar, an ideality that is
435  void.  Harry! if you only knew what Dorian Gray is to me!  You remember
436  that landscape of mine, for which Agnew offered me such a huge price
437  but which I would not part with?  It is one of the best things I have
438  ever done.  And why is it so?  Because, while I was painting it, Dorian
439  Gray sat beside me.  Some subtle influence passed from him to me, and
440  for the first time in my life I saw in the plain woodland the wonder I
441  had always looked for and always missed."
442  
443  "Basil, this is extraordinary!  I must see Dorian Gray."
444  
445  Hallward got up from the seat and walked up and down the garden.  After
446  some time he came back.  "Harry," he said, "Dorian Gray is to me simply
447  a motive in art.  You might see nothing in him.  I see everything in
448  him.  He is never more present in my work than when no image of him is
449  there.  He is a suggestion, as I have said, of a new manner.  I find
450  him in the curves of certain lines, in the loveliness and subtleties of
451  certain colours.  That is all."
452  
453  "Then why won't you exhibit his portrait?" asked Lord Henry.
454  
455  "Because, without intending it, I have put into it some expression of
456  all this curious artistic idolatry, of which, of course, I have never
457  cared to speak to him.  He knows nothing about it.  He shall never know
458  anything about it.  But the world might guess it, and I will not bare
459  my soul to their shallow prying eyes.  My heart shall never be put
460  under their microscope.  There is too much of myself in the thing,
461  Harry--too much of myself!"
462  
463  "Poets are not so scrupulous as you are.  They know how useful passion
464  is for publication.  Nowadays a broken heart will run to many editions."
465  
466  "I hate them for it," cried Hallward.  "An artist should create
467  beautiful things, but should put nothing of his own life into them.  We
468  live in an age when men treat art as if it were meant to be a form of
469  autobiography.  We have lost the abstract sense of beauty.  Some day I
470  will show the world what it is; and for that reason the world shall
471  never see my portrait of Dorian Gray."
472  
473  "I think you are wrong, Basil, but I won't argue with you.  It is only
474  the intellectually lost who ever argue.  Tell me, is Dorian Gray very
475  fond of you?"
476  
477  The painter considered for a few moments.  "He likes me," he answered
478  after a pause; "I know he likes me.  Of course I flatter him
479  dreadfully.  I find a strange pleasure in saying things to him that I
480  know I shall be sorry for having said.  As a rule, he is charming to
481  me, and we sit in the studio and talk of a thousand things.  Now and
482  then, however, he is horribly thoughtless, and seems to take a real
483  delight in giving me pain.  Then I feel, Harry, that I have given away
484  my whole soul to some one who treats it as if it were a flower to put
485  in his coat, a bit of decoration to charm his vanity, an ornament for a
486  summer's day."
487  
488  "Days in summer, Basil, are apt to linger," murmured Lord Henry.
489  "Perhaps you will tire sooner than he will.  It is a sad thing to think
490  of, but there is no doubt that genius lasts longer than beauty.  That
491  accounts for the fact that we all take such pains to over-educate
492  ourselves.  In the wild struggle for existence, we want to have
493  something that endures, and so we fill our minds with rubbish and
494  facts, in the silly hope of keeping our place.  The thoroughly
495  well-informed man--that is the modern ideal.  And the mind of the
496  thoroughly well-informed man is a dreadful thing.  It is like a
497  _bric-a-brac_ shop, all monsters and dust, with everything priced above
498  its proper value.  I think you will tire first, all the same.  Some day
499  you will look at your friend, and he will seem to you to be a little
500  out of drawing, or you won't like his tone of colour, or something.
501  You will bitterly reproach him in your own heart, and seriously think
502  that he has behaved very badly to you.  The next time he calls, you
503  will be perfectly cold and indifferent.  It will be a great pity, for
504  it will alter you.  What you have told me is quite a romance, a romance
505  of art one might call it, and the worst of having a romance of any kind
506  is that it leaves one so unromantic."
507  
508  "Harry, don't talk like that.  As long as I live, the personality of
509  Dorian Gray will dominate me.  You can't feel what I feel.  You change
510  too often."
511  
512  "Ah, my dear Basil, that is exactly why I can feel it.  Those who are
513  faithful know only the trivial side of love: it is the faithless who
514  know love's tragedies."  And Lord Henry struck a light on a dainty
515  silver case and began to smoke a cigarette with a self-conscious and
516  satisfied air, as if he had summed up the world in a phrase.  There was
517  a rustle of chirruping sparrows in the green lacquer leaves of the ivy,
518  and the blue cloud-shadows chased themselves across the grass like
519  swallows.  How pleasant it was in the garden!  And how delightful other
520  people's emotions were!--much more delightful than their ideas, it
521  seemed to him.  One's own soul, and the passions of one's
522  friends--those were the fascinating things in life.  He pictured to
523  himself with silent amusement the tedious luncheon that he had missed
524  by staying so long with Basil Hallward.  Had he gone to his aunt's, he
525  would have been sure to have met Lord Goodbody there, and the whole
526  conversation would have been about the feeding of the poor and the
527  necessity for model lodging-houses. Each class would have preached the
528  importance of those virtues, for whose exercise there was no necessity
529  in their own lives.  The rich would have spoken on the value of thrift,
530  and the idle grown eloquent over the dignity of labour.  It was
531  charming to have escaped all that!  As he thought of his aunt, an idea
532  seemed to strike him.  He turned to Hallward and said, "My dear fellow,
533  I have just remembered."
534  
535  "Remembered what, Harry?"
536  
537  "Where I heard the name of Dorian Gray."
538  
539  "Where was it?" asked Hallward, with a slight frown.
540  
541  "Don't look so angry, Basil.  It was at my aunt, Lady Agatha's.  She
542  told me she had discovered a wonderful young man who was going to help
543  her in the East End, and that his name was Dorian Gray.  I am bound to
544  state that she never told me he was good-looking. Women have no
545  appreciation of good looks; at least, good women have not.  She said
546  that he was very earnest and had a beautiful nature.  I at once
547  pictured to myself a creature with spectacles and lank hair, horribly
548  freckled, and tramping about on huge feet.  I wish I had known it was
549  your friend."
550  
551  "I am very glad you didn't, Harry."
552  
553  "Why?"
554  
555  "I don't want you to meet him."
556  
557  "You don't want me to meet him?"
558  
559  "No."
560  
561  "Mr. Dorian Gray is in the studio, sir," said the butler, coming into
562  the garden.
563  
564  "You must introduce me now," cried Lord Henry, laughing.
565  
566  The painter turned to his servant, who stood blinking in the sunlight.
567  "Ask Mr. Gray to wait, Parker:  I shall be in in a few moments." The
568  man bowed and went up the walk.
569  
570  Then he looked at Lord Henry.  "Dorian Gray is my dearest friend," he
571  said.  "He has a simple and a beautiful nature.  Your aunt was quite
572  right in what she said of him.  Don't spoil him.  Don't try to
573  influence him.  Your influence would be bad.  The world is wide, and
574  has many marvellous people in it.  Don't take away from me the one
575  person who gives to my art whatever charm it possesses:  my life as an
576  artist depends on him.  Mind, Harry, I trust you."  He spoke very
577  slowly, and the words seemed wrung out of him almost against his will.
578  
579  "What nonsense you talk!" said Lord Henry, smiling, and taking Hallward
580  by the arm, he almost led him into the house.
581  
582  
583  
584  CHAPTER 2
585  
586  As they entered they saw Dorian Gray.  He was seated at the piano, with
587  his back to them, turning over the pages of a volume of Schumann's
588  "Forest Scenes."  "You must lend me these, Basil," he cried.  "I want
589  to learn them.  They are perfectly charming."
590  
591  "That entirely depends on how you sit to-day, Dorian."
592  
593  "Oh, I am tired of sitting, and I don't want a life-sized portrait of
594  myself," answered the lad, swinging round on the music-stool in a
595  wilful, petulant manner.  When he caught sight of Lord Henry, a faint
596  blush coloured his cheeks for a moment, and he started up.  "I beg your
597  pardon, Basil, but I didn't know you had any one with you."
598  
599  "This is Lord Henry Wotton, Dorian, an old Oxford friend of mine.  I
600  have just been telling him what a capital sitter you were, and now you
601  have spoiled everything."
602  
603  "You have not spoiled my pleasure in meeting you, Mr. Gray," said Lord
604  Henry, stepping forward and extending his hand.  "My aunt has often
605  spoken to me about you.  You are one of her favourites, and, I am
606  afraid, one of her victims also."
607  
608  "I am in Lady Agatha's black books at present," answered Dorian with a
609  funny look of penitence.  "I promised to go to a club in Whitechapel
610  with her last Tuesday, and I really forgot all about it.  We were to
611  have played a duet together--three duets, I believe.  I don't know what
612  she will say to me.  I am far too frightened to call."
613  
614  "Oh, I will make your peace with my aunt.  She is quite devoted to you.
615  And I don't think it really matters about your not being there.  The
616  audience probably thought it was a duet.  When Aunt Agatha sits down to
617  the piano, she makes quite enough noise for two people."
618  
619  "That is very horrid to her, and not very nice to me," answered Dorian,
620  laughing.
621  
622  Lord Henry looked at him.  Yes, he was certainly wonderfully handsome,
623  with his finely curved scarlet lips, his frank blue eyes, his crisp
624  gold hair.  There was something in his face that made one trust him at
625  once.  All the candour of youth was there, as well as all youth's
626  passionate purity.  One felt that he had kept himself unspotted from
627  the world.  No wonder Basil Hallward worshipped him.
628  
629  "You are too charming to go in for philanthropy, Mr. Gray--far too
630  charming." And Lord Henry flung himself down on the divan and opened
631  his cigarette-case.
632  
633  The painter had been busy mixing his colours and getting his brushes
634  ready.  He was looking worried, and when he heard Lord Henry's last
635  remark, he glanced at him, hesitated for a moment, and then said,
636  "Harry, I want to finish this picture to-day. Would you think it
637  awfully rude of me if I asked you to go away?"
638  
639  Lord Henry smiled and looked at Dorian Gray.  "Am I to go, Mr. Gray?"
640  he asked.
641  
642  "Oh, please don't, Lord Henry.  I see that Basil is in one of his sulky
643  moods, and I can't bear him when he sulks.  Besides, I want you to tell
644  me why I should not go in for philanthropy."
645  
646  "I don't know that I shall tell you that, Mr. Gray.  It is so tedious a
647  subject that one would have to talk seriously about it.  But I
648  certainly shall not run away, now that you have asked me to stop.  You
649  don't really mind, Basil, do you?  You have often told me that you
650  liked your sitters to have some one to chat to."
651  
652  Hallward bit his lip.  "If Dorian wishes it, of course you must stay.
653  Dorian's whims are laws to everybody, except himself."
654  
655  Lord Henry took up his hat and gloves.  "You are very pressing, Basil,
656  but I am afraid I must go.  I have promised to meet a man at the
657  Orleans.  Good-bye, Mr. Gray.  Come and see me some afternoon in Curzon
658  Street.  I am nearly always at home at five o'clock. Write to me when
659  you are coming.  I should be sorry to miss you."
660  
661  "Basil," cried Dorian Gray, "if Lord Henry Wotton goes, I shall go,
662  too.  You never open your lips while you are painting, and it is
663  horribly dull standing on a platform and trying to look pleasant.  Ask
664  him to stay.  I insist upon it."
665  
666  "Stay, Harry, to oblige Dorian, and to oblige me," said Hallward,
667  gazing intently at his picture.  "It is quite true, I never talk when I
668  am working, and never listen either, and it must be dreadfully tedious
669  for my unfortunate sitters.  I beg you to stay."
670  
671  "But what about my man at the Orleans?"
672  
673  The painter laughed.  "I don't think there will be any difficulty about
674  that.  Sit down again, Harry.  And now, Dorian, get up on the platform,
675  and don't move about too much, or pay any attention to what Lord Henry
676  says.  He has a very bad influence over all his friends, with the
677  single exception of myself."
678  
679  Dorian Gray stepped up on the dais with the air of a young Greek
680  martyr, and made a little _moue_ of discontent to Lord Henry, to whom he
681  had rather taken a fancy.  He was so unlike Basil.  They made a
682  delightful contrast.  And he had such a beautiful voice.  After a few
683  moments he said to him, "Have you really a very bad influence, Lord
684  Henry?  As bad as Basil says?"
685  
686  "There is no such thing as a good influence, Mr. Gray.  All influence
687  is immoral--immoral from the scientific point of view."
688  
689  "Why?"
690  
691  "Because to influence a person is to give him one's own soul.  He does
692  not think his natural thoughts, or burn with his natural passions.  His
693  virtues are not real to him.  His sins, if there are such things as
694  sins, are borrowed.  He becomes an echo of some one else's music, an
695  actor of a part that has not been written for him.  The aim of life is
696  self-development. To realize one's nature perfectly--that is what each
697  of us is here for.  People are afraid of themselves, nowadays.  They
698  have forgotten the highest of all duties, the duty that one owes to
699  one's self.  Of course, they are charitable.  They feed the hungry and
700  clothe the beggar.  But their own souls starve, and are naked.  Courage
701  has gone out of our race.  Perhaps we never really had it.  The terror
702  of society, which is the basis of morals, the terror of God, which is
703  the secret of religion--these are the two things that govern us.  And
704  yet--"
705  
706  "Just turn your head a little more to the right, Dorian, like a good
707  boy," said the painter, deep in his work and conscious only that a look
708  had come into the lad's face that he had never seen there before.
709  
710  "And yet," continued Lord Henry, in his low, musical voice, and with
711  that graceful wave of the hand that was always so characteristic of
712  him, and that he had even in his Eton days, "I believe that if one man
713  were to live out his life fully and completely, were to give form to
714  every feeling, expression to every thought, reality to every dream--I
715  believe that the world would gain such a fresh impulse of joy that we
716  would forget all the maladies of mediaevalism, and return to the
717  Hellenic ideal--to something finer, richer than the Hellenic ideal, it
718  may be.  But the bravest man amongst us is afraid of himself.  The
719  mutilation of the savage has its tragic survival in the self-denial
720  that mars our lives.  We are punished for our refusals.  Every impulse
721  that we strive to strangle broods in the mind and poisons us.  The body
722  sins once, and has done with its sin, for action is a mode of
723  purification.  Nothing remains then but the recollection of a pleasure,
724  or the luxury of a regret.  The only way to get rid of a temptation is
725  to yield to it.  Resist it, and your soul grows sick with longing for
726  the things it has forbidden to itself, with desire for what its
727  monstrous laws have made monstrous and unlawful.  It has been said that
728  the great events of the world take place in the brain.  It is in the
729  brain, and the brain only, that the great sins of the world take place
730  also.  You, Mr. Gray, you yourself, with your rose-red youth and your
731  rose-white boyhood, you have had passions that have made you afraid,
732  thoughts that have filled you with terror, day-dreams and sleeping
733  dreams whose mere memory might stain your cheek with shame--"
734  
735  "Stop!" faltered Dorian Gray, "stop! you bewilder me.  I don't know
736  what to say.  There is some answer to you, but I cannot find it.  Don't
737  speak.  Let me think.  Or, rather, let me try not to think."
738  
739  For nearly ten minutes he stood there, motionless, with parted lips and
740  eyes strangely bright.  He was dimly conscious that entirely fresh
741  influences were at work within him.  Yet they seemed to him to have
742  come really from himself.  The few words that Basil's friend had said
743  to him--words spoken by chance, no doubt, and with wilful paradox in
744  them--had touched some secret chord that had never been touched before,
745  but that he felt was now vibrating and throbbing to curious pulses.
746  
747  Music had stirred him like that.  Music had troubled him many times.
748  But music was not articulate.  It was not a new world, but rather
749  another chaos, that it created in us.  Words!  Mere words!  How
750  terrible they were!  How clear, and vivid, and cruel!  One could not
751  escape from them.  And yet what a subtle magic there was in them!  They
752  seemed to be able to give a plastic form to formless things, and to
753  have a music of their own as sweet as that of viol or of lute.  Mere
754  words!  Was there anything so real as words?
755  
756  Yes; there had been things in his boyhood that he had not understood.
757  He understood them now.  Life suddenly became fiery-coloured to him.
758  It seemed to him that he had been walking in fire.  Why had he not
759  known it?
760  
761  With his subtle smile, Lord Henry watched him.  He knew the precise
762  psychological moment when to say nothing.  He felt intensely
763  interested.  He was amazed at the sudden impression that his words had
764  produced, and, remembering a book that he had read when he was sixteen,
765  a book which had revealed to him much that he had not known before, he
766  wondered whether Dorian Gray was passing through a similar experience.
767  He had merely shot an arrow into the air.  Had it hit the mark?  How
768  fascinating the lad was!
769  
770  Hallward painted away with that marvellous bold touch of his, that had
771  the true refinement and perfect delicacy that in art, at any rate comes
772  only from strength.  He was unconscious of the silence.
773  
774  "Basil, I am tired of standing," cried Dorian Gray suddenly.  "I must
775  go out and sit in the garden.  The air is stifling here."
776  
777  "My dear fellow, I am so sorry.  When I am painting, I can't think of
778  anything else.  But you never sat better.  You were perfectly still.
779  And I have caught the effect I wanted--the half-parted lips and the
780  bright look in the eyes.  I don't know what Harry has been saying to
781  you, but he has certainly made you have the most wonderful expression.
782  I suppose he has been paying you compliments.  You mustn't believe a
783  word that he says."
784  
785  "He has certainly not been paying me compliments.  Perhaps that is the
786  reason that I don't believe anything he has told me."
787  
788  "You know you believe it all," said Lord Henry, looking at him with his
789  dreamy languorous eyes.  "I will go out to the garden with you.  It is
790  horribly hot in the studio.  Basil, let us have something iced to
791  drink, something with strawberries in it."
792  
793  "Certainly, Harry.  Just touch the bell, and when Parker comes I will
794  tell him what you want.  I have got to work up this background, so I
795  will join you later on.  Don't keep Dorian too long.  I have never been
796  in better form for painting than I am to-day. This is going to be my
797  masterpiece.  It is my masterpiece as it stands."
798  
799  Lord Henry went out to the garden and found Dorian Gray burying his
800  face in the great cool lilac-blossoms, feverishly drinking in their
801  perfume as if it had been wine.  He came close to him and put his hand
802  upon his shoulder.  "You are quite right to do that," he murmured.
803  "Nothing can cure the soul but the senses, just as nothing can cure the
804  senses but the soul."
805  
806  The lad started and drew back.  He was bareheaded, and the leaves had
807  tossed his rebellious curls and tangled all their gilded threads.
808  There was a look of fear in his eyes, such as people have when they are
809  suddenly awakened.  His finely chiselled nostrils quivered, and some
810  hidden nerve shook the scarlet of his lips and left them trembling.
811  
812  "Yes," continued Lord Henry, "that is one of the great secrets of
813  life--to cure the soul by means of the senses, and the senses by means
814  of the soul.  You are a wonderful creation.  You know more than you
815  think you know, just as you know less than you want to know."
816  
817  Dorian Gray frowned and turned his head away.  He could not help liking
818  the tall, graceful young man who was standing by him.  His romantic,
819  olive-coloured face and worn expression interested him.  There was
820  something in his low languid voice that was absolutely fascinating.
821  His cool, white, flowerlike hands, even, had a curious charm.  They
822  moved, as he spoke, like music, and seemed to have a language of their
823  own.  But he felt afraid of him, and ashamed of being afraid.  Why had
824  it been left for a stranger to reveal him to himself?  He had known
825  Basil Hallward for months, but the friendship between them had never
826  altered him.  Suddenly there had come some one across his life who
827  seemed to have disclosed to him life's mystery.  And, yet, what was
828  there to be afraid of?  He was not a schoolboy or a girl.  It was
829  absurd to be frightened.
830  
831  "Let us go and sit in the shade," said Lord Henry.  "Parker has brought
832  out the drinks, and if you stay any longer in this glare, you will be
833  quite spoiled, and Basil will never paint you again.  You really must
834  not allow yourself to become sunburnt.  It would be unbecoming."
835  
836  "What can it matter?" cried Dorian Gray, laughing, as he sat down on
837  the seat at the end of the garden.
838  
839  "It should matter everything to you, Mr. Gray."
840  
841  "Why?"
842  
843  "Because you have the most marvellous youth, and youth is the one thing
844  worth having."
845  
846  "I don't feel that, Lord Henry."
847  
848  "No, you don't feel it now.  Some day, when you are old and wrinkled
849  and ugly, when thought has seared your forehead with its lines, and
850  passion branded your lips with its hideous fires, you will feel it, you
851  will feel it terribly.  Now, wherever you go, you charm the world.
852  Will it always be so? ... You have a wonderfully beautiful face, Mr.
853  Gray.  Don't frown.  You have.  And beauty is a form of genius--is
854  higher, indeed, than genius, as it needs no explanation.  It is of the
855  great facts of the world, like sunlight, or spring-time, or the
856  reflection in dark waters of that silver shell we call the moon.  It
857  cannot be questioned.  It has its divine right of sovereignty.  It
858  makes princes of those who have it.  You smile?  Ah! when you have lost
859  it you won't smile.... People say sometimes that beauty is only
860  superficial.  That may be so, but at least it is not so superficial as
861  thought is.  To me, beauty is the wonder of wonders.  It is only
862  shallow people who do not judge by appearances.  The true mystery of
863  the world is the visible, not the invisible.... Yes, Mr. Gray, the
864  gods have been good to you.  But what the gods give they quickly take
865  away.  You have only a few years in which to live really, perfectly,
866  and fully.  When your youth goes, your beauty will go with it, and then
867  you will suddenly discover that there are no triumphs left for you, or
868  have to content yourself with those mean triumphs that the memory of
869  your past will make more bitter than defeats.  Every month as it wanes
870  brings you nearer to something dreadful.  Time is jealous of you, and
871  wars against your lilies and your roses.  You will become sallow, and
872  hollow-cheeked, and dull-eyed.  You will suffer horribly.... Ah!
873  realize your youth while you have it.  Don't squander the gold of your
874  days, listening to the tedious, trying to improve the hopeless failure,
875  or giving away your life to the ignorant, the common, and the vulgar.
876  These are the sickly aims, the false ideals, of our age.  Live!  Live
877  the wonderful life that is in you!  Let nothing be lost upon you.  Be
878  always searching for new sensations.  Be afraid of nothing.... A new
879  Hedonism--that is what our century wants.  You might be its visible
880  symbol.  With your personality there is nothing you could not do.  The
881  world belongs to you for a season.... The moment I met you I saw that
882  you were quite unconscious of what you really are, of what you really
883  might be.  There was so much in you that charmed me that I felt I must
884  tell you something about yourself.  I thought how tragic it would be if
885  you were wasted.  For there is such a little time that your youth will
886  last--such a little time.  The common hill-flowers wither, but they
887  blossom again.  The laburnum will be as yellow next June as it is now.
888  In a month there will be purple stars on the clematis, and year after
889  year the green night of its leaves will hold its purple stars.  But we
890  never get back our youth.  The pulse of joy that beats in us at twenty
891  becomes sluggish.  Our limbs fail, our senses rot.  We degenerate into
892  hideous puppets, haunted by the memory of the passions of which we were
893  too much afraid, and the exquisite temptations that we had not the
894  courage to yield to.  Youth!  Youth!  There is absolutely nothing in
895  the world but youth!"
896  
897  Dorian Gray listened, open-eyed and wondering.  The spray of lilac fell
898  from his hand upon the gravel.  A furry bee came and buzzed round it
899  for a moment.  Then it began to scramble all over the oval stellated
900  globe of the tiny blossoms.  He watched it with that strange interest
901  in trivial things that we try to develop when things of high import
902  make us afraid, or when we are stirred by some new emotion for which we
903  cannot find expression, or when some thought that terrifies us lays
904  sudden siege to the brain and calls on us to yield.  After a time the
905  bee flew away.  He saw it creeping into the stained trumpet of a Tyrian
906  convolvulus.  The flower seemed to quiver, and then swayed gently to
907  and fro.
908  
909  Suddenly the painter appeared at the door of the studio and made
910  staccato signs for them to come in.  They turned to each other and
911  smiled.
912  
913  "I am waiting," he cried.  "Do come in.  The light is quite perfect,
914  and you can bring your drinks."
915  
916  They rose up and sauntered down the walk together.  Two green-and-white
917  butterflies fluttered past them, and in the pear-tree at the corner of
918  the garden a thrush began to sing.
919  
920  "You are glad you have met me, Mr. Gray," said Lord Henry, looking at
921  him.
922  
923  "Yes, I am glad now.  I wonder shall I always be glad?"
924  
925  "Always!  That is a dreadful word.  It makes me shudder when I hear it.
926  Women are so fond of using it.  They spoil every romance by trying to
927  make it last for ever.  It is a meaningless word, too.  The only
928  difference between a caprice and a lifelong passion is that the caprice
929  lasts a little longer."
930  
931  As they entered the studio, Dorian Gray put his hand upon Lord Henry's
932  arm.  "In that case, let our friendship be a caprice," he murmured,
933  flushing at his own boldness, then stepped up on the platform and
934  resumed his pose.
935  
936  Lord Henry flung himself into a large wicker arm-chair and watched him.
937  The sweep and dash of the brush on the canvas made the only sound that
938  broke the stillness, except when, now and then, Hallward stepped back
939  to look at his work from a distance.  In the slanting beams that
940  streamed through the open doorway the dust danced and was golden.  The
941  heavy scent of the roses seemed to brood over everything.
942  
943  After about a quarter of an hour Hallward stopped painting, looked for
944  a long time at Dorian Gray, and then for a long time at the picture,
945  biting the end of one of his huge brushes and frowning.  "It is quite
946  finished," he cried at last, and stooping down he wrote his name in
947  long vermilion letters on the left-hand corner of the canvas.
948  
949  Lord Henry came over and examined the picture.  It was certainly a
950  wonderful work of art, and a wonderful likeness as well.
951  
952  "My dear fellow, I congratulate you most warmly," he said.  "It is the
953  finest portrait of modern times.  Mr. Gray, come over and look at
954  yourself."
955  
956  The lad started, as if awakened from some dream.
957  
958  "Is it really finished?" he murmured, stepping down from the platform.
959  
960  "Quite finished," said the painter.  "And you have sat splendidly
961  to-day. I am awfully obliged to you."
962  
963  "That is entirely due to me," broke in Lord Henry.  "Isn't it, Mr.
964  Gray?"
965  
966  Dorian made no answer, but passed listlessly in front of his picture
967  and turned towards it.  When he saw it he drew back, and his cheeks
968  flushed for a moment with pleasure.  A look of joy came into his eyes,
969  as if he had recognized himself for the first time.  He stood there
970  motionless and in wonder, dimly conscious that Hallward was speaking to
971  him, but not catching the meaning of his words.  The sense of his own
972  beauty came on him like a revelation.  He had never felt it before.
973  Basil Hallward's compliments had seemed to him to be merely the
974  charming exaggeration of friendship.  He had listened to them, laughed
975  at them, forgotten them.  They had not influenced his nature.  Then had
976  come Lord Henry Wotton with his strange panegyric on youth, his
977  terrible warning of its brevity.  That had stirred him at the time, and
978  now, as he stood gazing at the shadow of his own loveliness, the full
979  reality of the description flashed across him.  Yes, there would be a
980  day when his face would be wrinkled and wizen, his eyes dim and
981  colourless, the grace of his figure broken and deformed.  The scarlet
982  would pass away from his lips and the gold steal from his hair.  The
983  life that was to make his soul would mar his body.  He would become
984  dreadful, hideous, and uncouth.
985  
986  As he thought of it, a sharp pang of pain struck through him like a
987  knife and made each delicate fibre of his nature quiver.  His eyes
988  deepened into amethyst, and across them came a mist of tears.  He felt
989  as if a hand of ice had been laid upon his heart.
990  
991  "Don't you like it?" cried Hallward at last, stung a little by the
992  lad's silence, not understanding what it meant.
993  
994  "Of course he likes it," said Lord Henry.  "Who wouldn't like it?  It
995  is one of the greatest things in modern art.  I will give you anything
996  you like to ask for it.  I must have it."
997  
998  "It is not my property, Harry."
999  
1000  "Whose property is it?"
1001  
1002  "Dorian's, of course," answered the painter.
1003  
1004  "He is a very lucky fellow."
1005  
1006  "How sad it is!" murmured Dorian Gray with his eyes still fixed upon
1007  his own portrait.  "How sad it is!  I shall grow old, and horrible, and
1008  dreadful.  But this picture will remain always young.  It will never be
1009  older than this particular day of June.... If it were only the other
1010  way!  If it were I who was to be always young, and the picture that was
1011  to grow old!  For that--for that--I would give everything!  Yes, there
1012  is nothing in the whole world I would not give!  I would give my soul
1013  for that!"
1014  
1015  "You would hardly care for such an arrangement, Basil," cried Lord
1016  Henry, laughing.  "It would be rather hard lines on your work."
1017  
1018  "I should object very strongly, Harry," said Hallward.
1019  
1020  Dorian Gray turned and looked at him.  "I believe you would, Basil.
1021  You like your art better than your friends.  I am no more to you than a
1022  green bronze figure.  Hardly as much, I dare say."
1023  
1024  The painter stared in amazement.  It was so unlike Dorian to speak like
1025  that.  What had happened?  He seemed quite angry.  His face was flushed
1026  and his cheeks burning.
1027  
1028  "Yes," he continued, "I am less to you than your ivory Hermes or your
1029  silver Faun.  You will like them always.  How long will you like me?
1030  Till I have my first wrinkle, I suppose.  I know, now, that when one
1031  loses one's good looks, whatever they may be, one loses everything.
1032  Your picture has taught me that.  Lord Henry Wotton is perfectly right.
1033  Youth is the only thing worth having.  When I find that I am growing
1034  old, I shall kill myself."
1035  
1036  Hallward turned pale and caught his hand.  "Dorian!  Dorian!" he cried,
1037  "don't talk like that.  I have never had such a friend as you, and I
1038  shall never have such another.  You are not jealous of material things,
1039  are you?--you who are finer than any of them!"
1040  
1041  "I am jealous of everything whose beauty does not die.  I am jealous of
1042  the portrait you have painted of me.  Why should it keep what I must
1043  lose?  Every moment that passes takes something from me and gives
1044  something to it.  Oh, if it were only the other way!  If the picture
1045  could change, and I could be always what I am now!  Why did you paint
1046  it?  It will mock me some day--mock me horribly!"  The hot tears welled
1047  into his eyes; he tore his hand away and, flinging himself on the
1048  divan, he buried his face in the cushions, as though he was praying.
1049  
1050  "This is your doing, Harry," said the painter bitterly.
1051  
1052  Lord Henry shrugged his shoulders.  "It is the real Dorian Gray--that
1053  is all."
1054  
1055  "It is not."
1056  
1057  "If it is not, what have I to do with it?"
1058  
1059  "You should have gone away when I asked you," he muttered.
1060  
1061  "I stayed when you asked me," was Lord Henry's answer.
1062  
1063  "Harry, I can't quarrel with my two best friends at once, but between
1064  you both you have made me hate the finest piece of work I have ever
1065  done, and I will destroy it.  What is it but canvas and colour?  I will
1066  not let it come across our three lives and mar them."
1067  
1068  Dorian Gray lifted his golden head from the pillow, and with pallid
1069  face and tear-stained eyes, looked at him as he walked over to the deal
1070  painting-table that was set beneath the high curtained window.  What
1071  was he doing there?  His fingers were straying about among the litter
1072  of tin tubes and dry brushes, seeking for something.  Yes, it was for
1073  the long palette-knife, with its thin blade of lithe steel.  He had
1074  found it at last.  He was going to rip up the canvas.
1075  
1076  With a stifled sob the lad leaped from the couch, and, rushing over to
1077  Hallward, tore the knife out of his hand, and flung it to the end of
1078  the studio.  "Don't, Basil, don't!" he cried.  "It would be murder!"
1079  
1080  "I am glad you appreciate my work at last, Dorian," said the painter
1081  coldly when he had recovered from his surprise.  "I never thought you
1082  would."
1083  
1084  "Appreciate it?  I am in love with it, Basil.  It is part of myself.  I
1085  feel that."
1086  
1087  "Well, as soon as you are dry, you shall be varnished, and framed, and
1088  sent home.  Then you can do what you like with yourself." And he walked
1089  across the room and rang the bell for tea.  "You will have tea, of
1090  course, Dorian?  And so will you, Harry?  Or do you object to such
1091  simple pleasures?"
1092  
1093  "I adore simple pleasures," said Lord Henry.  "They are the last refuge
1094  of the complex.  But I don't like scenes, except on the stage.  What
1095  absurd fellows you are, both of you!  I wonder who it was defined man
1096  as a rational animal.  It was the most premature definition ever given.
1097  Man is many things, but he is not rational.  I am glad he is not, after
1098  all--though I wish you chaps would not squabble over the picture.  You
1099  had much better let me have it, Basil.  This silly boy doesn't really
1100  want it, and I really do."
1101  
1102  "If you let any one have it but me, Basil, I shall never forgive you!"
1103  cried Dorian Gray; "and I don't allow people to call me a silly boy."
1104  
1105  "You know the picture is yours, Dorian.  I gave it to you before it
1106  existed."
1107  
1108  "And you know you have been a little silly, Mr. Gray, and that you
1109  don't really object to being reminded that you are extremely young."
1110  
1111  "I should have objected very strongly this morning, Lord Henry."
1112  
1113  "Ah! this morning!  You have lived since then."
1114  
1115  There came a knock at the door, and the butler entered with a laden
1116  tea-tray and set it down upon a small Japanese table.  There was a
1117  rattle of cups and saucers and the hissing of a fluted Georgian urn.
1118  Two globe-shaped china dishes were brought in by a page.  Dorian Gray
1119  went over and poured out the tea.  The two men sauntered languidly to
1120  the table and examined what was under the covers.
1121  
1122  "Let us go to the theatre to-night," said Lord Henry.  "There is sure
1123  to be something on, somewhere.  I have promised to dine at White's, but
1124  it is only with an old friend, so I can send him a wire to say that I
1125  am ill, or that I am prevented from coming in consequence of a
1126  subsequent engagement.  I think that would be a rather nice excuse:  it
1127  would have all the surprise of candour."
1128  
1129  "It is such a bore putting on one's dress-clothes," muttered Hallward.
1130  "And, when one has them on, they are so horrid."
1131  
1132  "Yes," answered Lord Henry dreamily, "the costume of the nineteenth
1133  century is detestable.  It is so sombre, so depressing.  Sin is the
1134  only real colour-element left in modern life."
1135  
1136  "You really must not say things like that before Dorian, Harry."
1137  
1138  "Before which Dorian?  The one who is pouring out tea for us, or the
1139  one in the picture?"
1140  
1141  "Before either."
1142  
1143  "I should like to come to the theatre with you, Lord Henry," said the
1144  lad.
1145  
1146  "Then you shall come; and you will come, too, Basil, won't you?"
1147  
1148  "I can't, really.  I would sooner not.  I have a lot of work to do."
1149  
1150  "Well, then, you and I will go alone, Mr. Gray."
1151  
1152  "I should like that awfully."
1153  
1154  The painter bit his lip and walked over, cup in hand, to the picture.
1155  "I shall stay with the real Dorian," he said, sadly.
1156  
1157  "Is it the real Dorian?" cried the original of the portrait, strolling
1158  across to him.  "Am I really like that?"
1159  
1160  "Yes; you are just like that."
1161  
1162  "How wonderful, Basil!"
1163  
1164  "At least you are like it in appearance.  But it will never alter,"
1165  sighed Hallward.  "That is something."
1166  
1167  "What a fuss people make about fidelity!" exclaimed Lord Henry.  "Why,
1168  even in love it is purely a question for physiology.  It has nothing to
1169  do with our own will.  Young men want to be faithful, and are not; old
1170  men want to be faithless, and cannot: that is all one can say."
1171  
1172  "Don't go to the theatre to-night, Dorian," said Hallward.  "Stop and
1173  dine with me."
1174  
1175  "I can't, Basil."
1176  
1177  "Why?"
1178  
1179  "Because I have promised Lord Henry Wotton to go with him."
1180  
1181  "He won't like you the better for keeping your promises.  He always
1182  breaks his own.  I beg you not to go."
1183  
1184  Dorian Gray laughed and shook his head.
1185  
1186  "I entreat you."
1187  
1188  The lad hesitated, and looked over at Lord Henry, who was watching them
1189  from the tea-table with an amused smile.
1190  
1191  "I must go, Basil," he answered.
1192  
1193  "Very well," said Hallward, and he went over and laid down his cup on
1194  the tray.  "It is rather late, and, as you have to dress, you had
1195  better lose no time.  Good-bye, Harry.  Good-bye, Dorian.  Come and see
1196  me soon.  Come to-morrow."
1197  
1198  "Certainly."
1199  
1200  "You won't forget?"
1201  
1202  "No, of course not," cried Dorian.
1203  
1204  "And ... Harry!"
1205  
1206  "Yes, Basil?"
1207  
1208  "Remember what I asked you, when we were in the garden this morning."
1209  
1210  "I have forgotten it."
1211  
1212  "I trust you."
1213  
1214  "I wish I could trust myself," said Lord Henry, laughing.  "Come, Mr.
1215  Gray, my hansom is outside, and I can drop you at your own place.
1216  Good-bye, Basil.  It has been a most interesting afternoon."
1217  
1218  As the door closed behind them, the painter flung himself down on a
1219  sofa, and a look of pain came into his face.
1220  
1221  
1222  
1223  CHAPTER 3
1224  
1225  At half-past twelve next day Lord Henry Wotton strolled from Curzon
1226  Street over to the Albany to call on his uncle, Lord Fermor, a genial
1227  if somewhat rough-mannered old bachelor, whom the outside world called
1228  selfish because it derived no particular benefit from him, but who was
1229  considered generous by Society as he fed the people who amused him.
1230  His father had been our ambassador at Madrid when Isabella was young
1231  and Prim unthought of, but had retired from the diplomatic service in a
1232  capricious moment of annoyance on not being offered the Embassy at
1233  Paris, a post to which he considered that he was fully entitled by
1234  reason of his birth, his indolence, the good English of his dispatches,
1235  and his inordinate passion for pleasure.  The son, who had been his
1236  father's secretary, had resigned along with his chief, somewhat
1237  foolishly as was thought at the time, and on succeeding some months
1238  later to the title, had set himself to the serious study of the great
1239  aristocratic art of doing absolutely nothing.  He had two large town
1240  houses, but preferred to live in chambers as it was less trouble, and
1241  took most of his meals at his club.  He paid some attention to the
1242  management of his collieries in the Midland counties, excusing himself
1243  for this taint of industry on the ground that the one advantage of
1244  having coal was that it enabled a gentleman to afford the decency of
1245  burning wood on his own hearth.  In politics he was a Tory, except when
1246  the Tories were in office, during which period he roundly abused them
1247  for being a pack of Radicals.  He was a hero to his valet, who bullied
1248  him, and a terror to most of his relations, whom he bullied in turn.
1249  Only England could have produced him, and he always said that the
1250  country was going to the dogs.  His principles were out of date, but
1251  there was a good deal to be said for his prejudices.
1252  
1253  When Lord Henry entered the room, he found his uncle sitting in a rough
1254  shooting-coat, smoking a cheroot and grumbling over _The Times_.  "Well,
1255  Harry," said the old gentleman, "what brings you out so early?  I
1256  thought you dandies never got up till two, and were not visible till
1257  five."
1258  
1259  "Pure family affection, I assure you, Uncle George.  I want to get
1260  something out of you."
1261  
1262  "Money, I suppose," said Lord Fermor, making a wry face.  "Well, sit
1263  down and tell me all about it.  Young people, nowadays, imagine that
1264  money is everything."
1265  
1266  "Yes," murmured Lord Henry, settling his button-hole in his coat; "and
1267  when they grow older they know it.  But I don't want money.  It is only
1268  people who pay their bills who want that, Uncle George, and I never pay
1269  mine.  Credit is the capital of a younger son, and one lives charmingly
1270  upon it.  Besides, I always deal with Dartmoor's tradesmen, and
1271  consequently they never bother me.  What I want is information:  not
1272  useful information, of course; useless information."
1273  
1274  "Well, I can tell you anything that is in an English Blue Book, Harry,
1275  although those fellows nowadays write a lot of nonsense.  When I was in
1276  the Diplomatic, things were much better.  But I hear they let them in
1277  now by examination.  What can you expect?  Examinations, sir, are pure
1278  humbug from beginning to end.  If a man is a gentleman, he knows quite
1279  enough, and if he is not a gentleman, whatever he knows is bad for him."
1280  
1281  "Mr. Dorian Gray does not belong to Blue Books, Uncle George," said
1282  Lord Henry languidly.
1283  
1284  "Mr. Dorian Gray?  Who is he?" asked Lord Fermor, knitting his bushy
1285  white eyebrows.
1286  
1287  "That is what I have come to learn, Uncle George.  Or rather, I know
1288  who he is.  He is the last Lord Kelso's grandson.  His mother was a
1289  Devereux, Lady Margaret Devereux.  I want you to tell me about his
1290  mother.  What was she like?  Whom did she marry?  You have known nearly
1291  everybody in your time, so you might have known her.  I am very much
1292  interested in Mr. Gray at present.  I have only just met him."
1293  
1294  "Kelso's grandson!" echoed the old gentleman.  "Kelso's grandson! ...
1295  Of course.... I knew his mother intimately.  I believe I was at her
1296  christening.  She was an extraordinarily beautiful girl, Margaret
1297  Devereux, and made all the men frantic by running away with a penniless
1298  young fellow--a mere nobody, sir, a subaltern in a foot regiment, or
1299  something of that kind.  Certainly.  I remember the whole thing as if
1300  it happened yesterday.  The poor chap was killed in a duel at Spa a few
1301  months after the marriage.  There was an ugly story about it.  They
1302  said Kelso got some rascally adventurer, some Belgian brute, to insult
1303  his son-in-law in public--paid him, sir, to do it, paid him--and that
1304  the fellow spitted his man as if he had been a pigeon.  The thing was
1305  hushed up, but, egad, Kelso ate his chop alone at the club for some
1306  time afterwards.  He brought his daughter back with him, I was told,
1307  and she never spoke to him again.  Oh, yes; it was a bad business.  The
1308  girl died, too, died within a year.  So she left a son, did she?  I had
1309  forgotten that.  What sort of boy is he?  If he is like his mother, he
1310  must be a good-looking chap."
1311  
1312  "He is very good-looking," assented Lord Henry.
1313  
1314  "I hope he will fall into proper hands," continued the old man.  "He
1315  should have a pot of money waiting for him if Kelso did the right thing
1316  by him.  His mother had money, too.  All the Selby property came to
1317  her, through her grandfather.  Her grandfather hated Kelso, thought him
1318  a mean dog.  He was, too.  Came to Madrid once when I was there.  Egad,
1319  I was ashamed of him.  The Queen used to ask me about the English noble
1320  who was always quarrelling with the cabmen about their fares.  They
1321  made quite a story of it.  I didn't dare show my face at Court for a
1322  month.  I hope he treated his grandson better than he did the jarvies."
1323  
1324  "I don't know," answered Lord Henry.  "I fancy that the boy will be
1325  well off.  He is not of age yet.  He has Selby, I know.  He told me so.
1326  And ... his mother was very beautiful?"
1327  
1328  "Margaret Devereux was one of the loveliest creatures I ever saw,
1329  Harry.  What on earth induced her to behave as she did, I never could
1330  understand.  She could have married anybody she chose.  Carlington was
1331  mad after her.  She was romantic, though.  All the women of that family
1332  were.  The men were a poor lot, but, egad! the women were wonderful.
1333  Carlington went on his knees to her.  Told me so himself.  She laughed
1334  at him, and there wasn't a girl in London at the time who wasn't after
1335  him.  And by the way, Harry, talking about silly marriages, what is
1336  this humbug your father tells me about Dartmoor wanting to marry an
1337  American?  Ain't English girls good enough for him?"
1338  
1339  "It is rather fashionable to marry Americans just now, Uncle George."
1340  
1341  "I'll back English women against the world, Harry," said Lord Fermor,
1342  striking the table with his fist.
1343  
1344  "The betting is on the Americans."
1345  
1346  "They don't last, I am told," muttered his uncle.
1347  
1348  "A long engagement exhausts them, but they are capital at a
1349  steeplechase.  They take things flying.  I don't think Dartmoor has a
1350  chance."
1351  
1352  "Who are her people?" grumbled the old gentleman.  "Has she got any?"
1353  
1354  Lord Henry shook his head.  "American girls are as clever at concealing
1355  their parents, as English women are at concealing their past," he said,
1356  rising to go.
1357  
1358  "They are pork-packers, I suppose?"
1359  
1360  "I hope so, Uncle George, for Dartmoor's sake.  I am told that
1361  pork-packing is the most lucrative profession in America, after
1362  politics."
1363  
1364  "Is she pretty?"
1365  
1366  "She behaves as if she was beautiful.  Most American women do.  It is
1367  the secret of their charm."
1368  
1369  "Why can't these American women stay in their own country?  They are
1370  always telling us that it is the paradise for women."
1371  
1372  "It is.  That is the reason why, like Eve, they are so excessively
1373  anxious to get out of it," said Lord Henry.  "Good-bye, Uncle George.
1374  I shall be late for lunch, if I stop any longer.  Thanks for giving me
1375  the information I wanted.  I always like to know everything about my
1376  new friends, and nothing about my old ones."
1377  
1378  "Where are you lunching, Harry?"
1379  
1380  "At Aunt Agatha's. I have asked myself and Mr. Gray.  He is her latest
1381  _protege_."
1382  
1383  "Humph! tell your Aunt Agatha, Harry, not to bother me any more with
1384  her charity appeals.  I am sick of them.  Why, the good woman thinks
1385  that I have nothing to do but to write cheques for her silly fads."
1386  
1387  "All right, Uncle George, I'll tell her, but it won't have any effect.
1388  Philanthropic people lose all sense of humanity.  It is their
1389  distinguishing characteristic."
1390  
1391  The old gentleman growled approvingly and rang the bell for his
1392  servant.  Lord Henry passed up the low arcade into Burlington Street
1393  and turned his steps in the direction of Berkeley Square.
1394  
1395  So that was the story of Dorian Gray's parentage.  Crudely as it had
1396  been told to him, it had yet stirred him by its suggestion of a
1397  strange, almost modern romance.  A beautiful woman risking everything
1398  for a mad passion.  A few wild weeks of happiness cut short by a
1399  hideous, treacherous crime.  Months of voiceless agony, and then a
1400  child born in pain.  The mother snatched away by death, the boy left to
1401  solitude and the tyranny of an old and loveless man.  Yes; it was an
1402  interesting background.  It posed the lad, made him more perfect, as it
1403  were.  Behind every exquisite thing that existed, there was something
1404  tragic.  Worlds had to be in travail, that the meanest flower might
1405  blow.... And how charming he had been at dinner the night before, as
1406  with startled eyes and lips parted in frightened pleasure he had sat
1407  opposite to him at the club, the red candleshades staining to a richer
1408  rose the wakening wonder of his face.  Talking to him was like playing
1409  upon an exquisite violin.  He answered to every touch and thrill of the
1410  bow.... There was something terribly enthralling in the exercise of
1411  influence.  No other activity was like it.  To project one's soul into
1412  some gracious form, and let it tarry there for a moment; to hear one's
1413  own intellectual views echoed back to one with all the added music of
1414  passion and youth; to convey one's temperament into another as though
1415  it were a subtle fluid or a strange perfume: there was a real joy in
1416  that--perhaps the most satisfying joy left to us in an age so limited
1417  and vulgar as our own, an age grossly carnal in its pleasures, and
1418  grossly common in its aims.... He was a marvellous type, too, this lad,
1419  whom by so curious a chance he had met in Basil's studio, or could be
1420  fashioned into a marvellous type, at any rate.  Grace was his, and the
1421  white purity of boyhood, and beauty such as old Greek marbles kept for
1422  us.  There was nothing that one could not do with him.  He could be
1423  made a Titan or a toy.  What a pity it was that such beauty was
1424  destined to fade! ...  And Basil?  From a psychological point of view,
1425  how interesting he was!  The new manner in art, the fresh mode of
1426  looking at life, suggested so strangely by the merely visible presence
1427  of one who was unconscious of it all; the silent spirit that dwelt in
1428  dim woodland, and walked unseen in open field, suddenly showing
1429  herself, Dryadlike and not afraid, because in his soul who sought for
1430  her there had been wakened that wonderful vision to which alone are
1431  wonderful things revealed; the mere shapes and patterns of things
1432  becoming, as it were, refined, and gaining a kind of symbolical value,
1433  as though they were themselves patterns of some other and more perfect
1434  form whose shadow they made real:  how strange it all was!  He
1435  remembered something like it in history.  Was it not Plato, that artist
1436  in thought, who had first analyzed it?  Was it not Buonarotti who had
1437  carved it in the coloured marbles of a sonnet-sequence? But in our own
1438  century it was strange.... Yes; he would try to be to Dorian Gray
1439  what, without knowing it, the lad was to the painter who had fashioned
1440  the wonderful portrait.  He would seek to dominate him--had already,
1441  indeed, half done so.  He would make that wonderful spirit his own.
1442  There was something fascinating in this son of love and death.
1443  
1444  Suddenly he stopped and glanced up at the houses.  He found that he had
1445  passed his aunt's some distance, and, smiling to himself, turned back.
1446  When he entered the somewhat sombre hall, the butler told him that they
1447  had gone in to lunch.  He gave one of the footmen his hat and stick and
1448  passed into the dining-room.
1449  
1450  "Late as usual, Harry," cried his aunt, shaking her head at him.
1451  
1452  He invented a facile excuse, and having taken the vacant seat next to
1453  her, looked round to see who was there.  Dorian bowed to him shyly from
1454  the end of the table, a flush of pleasure stealing into his cheek.
1455  Opposite was the Duchess of Harley, a lady of admirable good-nature and
