1456  good temper, much liked by every one who knew her, and of those ample
1457  architectural proportions that in women who are not duchesses are
1458  described by contemporary historians as stoutness.  Next to her sat, on
1459  her right, Sir Thomas Burdon, a Radical member of Parliament, who
1460  followed his leader in public life and in private life followed the
1461  best cooks, dining with the Tories and thinking with the Liberals, in
1462  accordance with a wise and well-known rule.  The post on her left was
1463  occupied by Mr. Erskine of Treadley, an old gentleman of considerable
1464  charm and culture, who had fallen, however, into bad habits of silence,
1465  having, as he explained once to Lady Agatha, said everything that he
1466  had to say before he was thirty.  His own neighbour was Mrs. Vandeleur,
1467  one of his aunt's oldest friends, a perfect saint amongst women, but so
1468  dreadfully dowdy that she reminded one of a badly bound hymn-book.
1469  Fortunately for him she had on the other side Lord Faudel, a most
1470  intelligent middle-aged mediocrity, as bald as a ministerial statement
1471  in the House of Commons, with whom she was conversing in that intensely
1472  earnest manner which is the one unpardonable error, as he remarked once
1473  himself, that all really good people fall into, and from which none of
1474  them ever quite escape.
1475  
1476  "We are talking about poor Dartmoor, Lord Henry," cried the duchess,
1477  nodding pleasantly to him across the table.  "Do you think he will
1478  really marry this fascinating young person?"
1479  
1480  "I believe she has made up her mind to propose to him, Duchess."
1481  
1482  "How dreadful!" exclaimed Lady Agatha.  "Really, some one should
1483  interfere."
1484  
1485  "I am told, on excellent authority, that her father keeps an American
1486  dry-goods store," said Sir Thomas Burdon, looking supercilious.
1487  
1488  "My uncle has already suggested pork-packing, Sir Thomas."
1489  
1490  "Dry-goods! What are American dry-goods?" asked the duchess, raising
1491  her large hands in wonder and accentuating the verb.
1492  
1493  "American novels," answered Lord Henry, helping himself to some quail.
1494  
1495  The duchess looked puzzled.
1496  
1497  "Don't mind him, my dear," whispered Lady Agatha.  "He never means
1498  anything that he says."
1499  
1500  "When America was discovered," said the Radical member--and he began to
1501  give some wearisome facts.  Like all people who try to exhaust a
1502  subject, he exhausted his listeners.  The duchess sighed and exercised
1503  her privilege of interruption.  "I wish to goodness it never had been
1504  discovered at all!" she exclaimed.  "Really, our girls have no chance
1505  nowadays.  It is most unfair."
1506  
1507  "Perhaps, after all, America never has been discovered," said Mr.
1508  Erskine; "I myself would say that it had merely been detected."
1509  
1510  "Oh! but I have seen specimens of the inhabitants," answered the
1511  duchess vaguely.  "I must confess that most of them are extremely
1512  pretty.  And they dress well, too.  They get all their dresses in
1513  Paris.  I wish I could afford to do the same."
1514  
1515  "They say that when good Americans die they go to Paris," chuckled Sir
1516  Thomas, who had a large wardrobe of Humour's cast-off clothes.
1517  
1518  "Really!  And where do bad Americans go to when they die?" inquired the
1519  duchess.
1520  
1521  "They go to America," murmured Lord Henry.
1522  
1523  Sir Thomas frowned.  "I am afraid that your nephew is prejudiced
1524  against that great country," he said to Lady Agatha.  "I have travelled
1525  all over it in cars provided by the directors, who, in such matters,
1526  are extremely civil.  I assure you that it is an education to visit it."
1527  
1528  "But must we really see Chicago in order to be educated?" asked Mr.
1529  Erskine plaintively.  "I don't feel up to the journey."
1530  
1531  Sir Thomas waved his hand.  "Mr. Erskine of Treadley has the world on
1532  his shelves. We practical men like to see things, not to read about
1533  them. The Americans are an extremely interesting people. They are
1534  absolutely reasonable. I think that is their distinguishing
1535  characteristic. Yes, Mr. Erskine, an absolutely reasonable people. I
1536  assure you there is no nonsense about the Americans."
1537  
1538  "How dreadful!" cried Lord Henry.  "I can stand brute force, but brute
1539  reason is quite unbearable.  There is something unfair about its use.
1540  It is hitting below the intellect."
1541  
1542  "I do not understand you," said Sir Thomas, growing rather red.
1543  
1544  "I do, Lord Henry," murmured Mr. Erskine, with a smile.
1545  
1546  "Paradoxes are all very well in their way...." rejoined the baronet.
1547  
1548  "Was that a paradox?" asked Mr. Erskine.  "I did not think so.  Perhaps
1549  it was.  Well, the way of paradoxes is the way of truth.  To test
1550  reality we must see it on the tight rope.  When the verities become
1551  acrobats, we can judge them."
1552  
1553  "Dear me!" said Lady Agatha, "how you men argue!  I am sure I never can
1554  make out what you are talking about.  Oh!  Harry, I am quite vexed with
1555  you.  Why do you try to persuade our nice Mr. Dorian Gray to give up
1556  the East End?  I assure you he would be quite invaluable.  They would
1557  love his playing."
1558  
1559  "I want him to play to me," cried Lord Henry, smiling, and he looked
1560  down the table and caught a bright answering glance.
1561  
1562  "But they are so unhappy in Whitechapel," continued Lady Agatha.
1563  
1564  "I can sympathize with everything except suffering," said Lord Henry,
1565  shrugging his shoulders.  "I cannot sympathize with that.  It is too
1566  ugly, too horrible, too distressing.  There is something terribly
1567  morbid in the modern sympathy with pain.  One should sympathize with
1568  the colour, the beauty, the joy of life.  The less said about life's
1569  sores, the better."
1570  
1571  "Still, the East End is a very important problem," remarked Sir Thomas
1572  with a grave shake of the head.
1573  
1574  "Quite so," answered the young lord.  "It is the problem of slavery,
1575  and we try to solve it by amusing the slaves."
1576  
1577  The politician looked at him keenly.  "What change do you propose,
1578  then?" he asked.
1579  
1580  Lord Henry laughed.  "I don't desire to change anything in England
1581  except the weather," he answered.  "I am quite content with philosophic
1582  contemplation.  But, as the nineteenth century has gone bankrupt
1583  through an over-expenditure of sympathy, I would suggest that we should
1584  appeal to science to put us straight.  The advantage of the emotions is
1585  that they lead us astray, and the advantage of science is that it is
1586  not emotional."
1587  
1588  "But we have such grave responsibilities," ventured Mrs. Vandeleur
1589  timidly.
1590  
1591  "Terribly grave," echoed Lady Agatha.
1592  
1593  Lord Henry looked over at Mr. Erskine.  "Humanity takes itself too
1594  seriously.  It is the world's original sin.  If the caveman had known
1595  how to laugh, history would have been different."
1596  
1597  "You are really very comforting," warbled the duchess.  "I have always
1598  felt rather guilty when I came to see your dear aunt, for I take no
1599  interest at all in the East End.  For the future I shall be able to
1600  look her in the face without a blush."
1601  
1602  "A blush is very becoming, Duchess," remarked Lord Henry.
1603  
1604  "Only when one is young," she answered.  "When an old woman like myself
1605  blushes, it is a very bad sign.  Ah!  Lord Henry, I wish you would tell
1606  me how to become young again."
1607  
1608  He thought for a moment.  "Can you remember any great error that you
1609  committed in your early days, Duchess?" he asked, looking at her across
1610  the table.
1611  
1612  "A great many, I fear," she cried.
1613  
1614  "Then commit them over again," he said gravely.  "To get back one's
1615  youth, one has merely to repeat one's follies."
1616  
1617  "A delightful theory!" she exclaimed.  "I must put it into practice."
1618  
1619  "A dangerous theory!" came from Sir Thomas's tight lips.  Lady Agatha
1620  shook her head, but could not help being amused.  Mr. Erskine listened.
1621  
1622  "Yes," he continued, "that is one of the great secrets of life.
1623  Nowadays most people die of a sort of creeping common sense, and
1624  discover when it is too late that the only things one never regrets are
1625  one's mistakes."
1626  
1627  A laugh ran round the table.
1628  
1629  He played with the idea and grew wilful; tossed it into the air and
1630  transformed it; let it escape and recaptured it; made it iridescent
1631  with fancy and winged it with paradox.  The praise of folly, as he went
1632  on, soared into a philosophy, and philosophy herself became young, and
1633  catching the mad music of pleasure, wearing, one might fancy, her
1634  wine-stained robe and wreath of ivy, danced like a Bacchante over the
1635  hills of life, and mocked the slow Silenus for being sober.  Facts fled
1636  before her like frightened forest things.  Her white feet trod the huge
1637  press at which wise Omar sits, till the seething grape-juice rose round
1638  her bare limbs in waves of purple bubbles, or crawled in red foam over
1639  the vat's black, dripping, sloping sides.  It was an extraordinary
1640  improvisation.  He felt that the eyes of Dorian Gray were fixed on him,
1641  and the consciousness that amongst his audience there was one whose
1642  temperament he wished to fascinate seemed to give his wit keenness and
1643  to lend colour to his imagination.  He was brilliant, fantastic,
1644  irresponsible.  He charmed his listeners out of themselves, and they
1645  followed his pipe, laughing.  Dorian Gray never took his gaze off him,
1646  but sat like one under a spell, smiles chasing each other over his lips
1647  and wonder growing grave in his darkening eyes.
1648  
1649  At last, liveried in the costume of the age, reality entered the room
1650  in the shape of a servant to tell the duchess that her carriage was
1651  waiting.  She wrung her hands in mock despair.  "How annoying!" she
1652  cried.  "I must go.  I have to call for my husband at the club, to take
1653  him to some absurd meeting at Willis's Rooms, where he is going to be
1654  in the chair.  If I am late he is sure to be furious, and I couldn't
1655  have a scene in this bonnet.  It is far too fragile.  A harsh word
1656  would ruin it.  No, I must go, dear Agatha.  Good-bye, Lord Henry, you
1657  are quite delightful and dreadfully demoralizing.  I am sure I don't
1658  know what to say about your views.  You must come and dine with us some
1659  night.  Tuesday?  Are you disengaged Tuesday?"
1660  
1661  "For you I would throw over anybody, Duchess," said Lord Henry with a
1662  bow.
1663  
1664  "Ah! that is very nice, and very wrong of you," she cried; "so mind you
1665  come"; and she swept out of the room, followed by Lady Agatha and the
1666  other ladies.
1667  
1668  When Lord Henry had sat down again, Mr. Erskine moved round, and taking
1669  a chair close to him, placed his hand upon his arm.
1670  
1671  "You talk books away," he said; "why don't you write one?"
1672  
1673  "I am too fond of reading books to care to write them, Mr. Erskine.  I
1674  should like to write a novel certainly, a novel that would be as lovely
1675  as a Persian carpet and as unreal.  But there is no literary public in
1676  England for anything except newspapers, primers, and encyclopaedias.
1677  Of all people in the world the English have the least sense of the
1678  beauty of literature."
1679  
1680  "I fear you are right," answered Mr. Erskine.  "I myself used to have
1681  literary ambitions, but I gave them up long ago.  And now, my dear
1682  young friend, if you will allow me to call you so, may I ask if you
1683  really meant all that you said to us at lunch?"
1684  
1685  "I quite forget what I said," smiled Lord Henry.  "Was it all very bad?"
1686  
1687  "Very bad indeed.  In fact I consider you extremely dangerous, and if
1688  anything happens to our good duchess, we shall all look on you as being
1689  primarily responsible.  But I should like to talk to you about life.
1690  The generation into which I was born was tedious.  Some day, when you
1691  are tired of London, come down to Treadley and expound to me your
1692  philosophy of pleasure over some admirable Burgundy I am fortunate
1693  enough to possess."
1694  
1695  "I shall be charmed.  A visit to Treadley would be a great privilege.
1696  It has a perfect host, and a perfect library."
1697  
1698  "You will complete it," answered the old gentleman with a courteous
1699  bow.  "And now I must bid good-bye to your excellent aunt.  I am due at
1700  the Athenaeum.  It is the hour when we sleep there."
1701  
1702  "All of you, Mr. Erskine?"
1703  
1704  "Forty of us, in forty arm-chairs. We are practising for an English
1705  Academy of Letters."
1706  
1707  Lord Henry laughed and rose.  "I am going to the park," he cried.
1708  
1709  As he was passing out of the door, Dorian Gray touched him on the arm.
1710  "Let me come with you," he murmured.
1711  
1712  "But I thought you had promised Basil Hallward to go and see him,"
1713  answered Lord Henry.
1714  
1715  "I would sooner come with you; yes, I feel I must come with you.  Do
1716  let me.  And you will promise to talk to me all the time?  No one talks
1717  so wonderfully as you do."
1718  
1719  "Ah!  I have talked quite enough for to-day," said Lord Henry, smiling.
1720  "All I want now is to look at life.  You may come and look at it with
1721  me, if you care to."
1722  
1723  
1724  
1725  CHAPTER 4
1726  
1727  One afternoon, a month later, Dorian Gray was reclining in a luxurious
1728  arm-chair, in the little library of Lord Henry's house in Mayfair.  It
1729  was, in its way, a very charming room, with its high panelled
1730  wainscoting of olive-stained oak, its cream-coloured frieze and ceiling
1731  of raised plasterwork, and its brickdust felt carpet strewn with silk,
1732  long-fringed Persian rugs.  On a tiny satinwood table stood a statuette
1733  by Clodion, and beside it lay a copy of Les Cent Nouvelles, bound for
1734  Margaret of Valois by Clovis Eve and powdered with the gilt daisies
1735  that Queen had selected for her device.  Some large blue china jars and
1736  parrot-tulips were ranged on the mantelshelf, and through the small
1737  leaded panes of the window streamed the apricot-coloured light of a
1738  summer day in London.
1739  
1740  Lord Henry had not yet come in.  He was always late on principle, his
1741  principle being that punctuality is the thief of time.  So the lad was
1742  looking rather sulky, as with listless fingers he turned over the pages
1743  of an elaborately illustrated edition of Manon Lescaut that he had
1744  found in one of the book-cases. The formal monotonous ticking of the
1745  Louis Quatorze clock annoyed him.  Once or twice he thought of going
1746  away.
1747  
1748  At last he heard a step outside, and the door opened.  "How late you
1749  are, Harry!" he murmured.
1750  
1751  "I am afraid it is not Harry, Mr. Gray," answered a shrill voice.
1752  
1753  He glanced quickly round and rose to his feet.  "I beg your pardon.  I
1754  thought--"
1755  
1756  "You thought it was my husband.  It is only his wife.  You must let me
1757  introduce myself.  I know you quite well by your photographs.  I think
1758  my husband has got seventeen of them."
1759  
1760  "Not seventeen, Lady Henry?"
1761  
1762  "Well, eighteen, then.  And I saw you with him the other night at the
1763  opera."  She laughed nervously as she spoke, and watched him with her
1764  vague forget-me-not eyes.  She was a curious woman, whose dresses
1765  always looked as if they had been designed in a rage and put on in a
1766  tempest.  She was usually in love with somebody, and, as her passion
1767  was never returned, she had kept all her illusions.  She tried to look
1768  picturesque, but only succeeded in being untidy.  Her name was
1769  Victoria, and she had a perfect mania for going to church.
1770  
1771  "That was at Lohengrin, Lady Henry, I think?"
1772  
1773  "Yes; it was at dear Lohengrin.  I like Wagner's music better than
1774  anybody's. It is so loud that one can talk the whole time without other
1775  people hearing what one says.  That is a great advantage, don't you
1776  think so, Mr. Gray?"
1777  
1778  The same nervous staccato laugh broke from her thin lips, and her
1779  fingers began to play with a long tortoise-shell paper-knife.
1780  
1781  Dorian smiled and shook his head:  "I am afraid I don't think so, Lady
1782  Henry.  I never talk during music--at least, during good music.  If one
1783  hears bad music, it is one's duty to drown it in conversation."
1784  
1785  "Ah! that is one of Harry's views, isn't it, Mr. Gray?  I always hear
1786  Harry's views from his friends.  It is the only way I get to know of
1787  them.  But you must not think I don't like good music.  I adore it, but
1788  I am afraid of it.  It makes me too romantic.  I have simply worshipped
1789  pianists--two at a time, sometimes, Harry tells me.  I don't know what
1790  it is about them.  Perhaps it is that they are foreigners.  They all
1791  are, ain't they?  Even those that are born in England become foreigners
1792  after a time, don't they?  It is so clever of them, and such a
1793  compliment to art.  Makes it quite cosmopolitan, doesn't it?  You have
1794  never been to any of my parties, have you, Mr. Gray?  You must come.  I
1795  can't afford orchids, but I spare no expense in foreigners.  They make
1796  one's rooms look so picturesque.  But here is Harry!  Harry, I came in
1797  to look for you, to ask you something--I forget what it was--and I
1798  found Mr. Gray here.  We have had such a pleasant chat about music.  We
1799  have quite the same ideas.  No; I think our ideas are quite different.
1800  But he has been most pleasant.  I am so glad I've seen him."
1801  
1802  "I am charmed, my love, quite charmed," said Lord Henry, elevating his
1803  dark, crescent-shaped eyebrows and looking at them both with an amused
1804  smile.  "So sorry I am late, Dorian.  I went to look after a piece of
1805  old brocade in Wardour Street and had to bargain for hours for it.
1806  Nowadays people know the price of everything and the value of nothing."
1807  
1808  "I am afraid I must be going," exclaimed Lady Henry, breaking an
1809  awkward silence with her silly sudden laugh.  "I have promised to drive
1810  with the duchess.  Good-bye, Mr. Gray.  Good-bye, Harry.  You are
1811  dining out, I suppose?  So am I. Perhaps I shall see you at Lady
1812  Thornbury's."
1813  
1814  "I dare say, my dear," said Lord Henry, shutting the door behind her
1815  as, looking like a bird of paradise that had been out all night in the
1816  rain, she flitted out of the room, leaving a faint odour of
1817  frangipanni.  Then he lit a cigarette and flung himself down on the
1818  sofa.
1819  
1820  "Never marry a woman with straw-coloured hair, Dorian," he said after a
1821  few puffs.
1822  
1823  "Why, Harry?"
1824  
1825  "Because they are so sentimental."
1826  
1827  "But I like sentimental people."
1828  
1829  "Never marry at all, Dorian.  Men marry because they are tired; women,
1830  because they are curious:  both are disappointed."
1831  
1832  "I don't think I am likely to marry, Harry.  I am too much in love.
1833  That is one of your aphorisms.  I am putting it into practice, as I do
1834  everything that you say."
1835  
1836  "Who are you in love with?" asked Lord Henry after a pause.
1837  
1838  "With an actress," said Dorian Gray, blushing.
1839  
1840  Lord Henry shrugged his shoulders.  "That is a rather commonplace
1841  _debut_."
1842  
1843  "You would not say so if you saw her, Harry."
1844  
1845  "Who is she?"
1846  
1847  "Her name is Sibyl Vane."
1848  
1849  "Never heard of her."
1850  
1851  "No one has.  People will some day, however.  She is a genius."
1852  
1853  "My dear boy, no woman is a genius.  Women are a decorative sex.  They
1854  never have anything to say, but they say it charmingly.  Women
1855  represent the triumph of matter over mind, just as men represent the
1856  triumph of mind over morals."
1857  
1858  "Harry, how can you?"
1859  
1860  "My dear Dorian, it is quite true.  I am analysing women at present, so
1861  I ought to know.  The subject is not so abstruse as I thought it was.
1862  I find that, ultimately, there are only two kinds of women, the plain
1863  and the coloured.  The plain women are very useful.  If you want to
1864  gain a reputation for respectability, you have merely to take them down
1865  to supper.  The other women are very charming.  They commit one
1866  mistake, however.  They paint in order to try and look young.  Our
1867  grandmothers painted in order to try and talk brilliantly.  _Rouge_ and
1868  _esprit_ used to go together.  That is all over now.  As long as a woman
1869  can look ten years younger than her own daughter, she is perfectly
1870  satisfied.  As for conversation, there are only five women in London
1871  worth talking to, and two of these can't be admitted into decent
1872  society.  However, tell me about your genius.  How long have you known
1873  her?"
1874  
1875  "Ah!  Harry, your views terrify me."
1876  
1877  "Never mind that.  How long have you known her?"
1878  
1879  "About three weeks."
1880  
1881  "And where did you come across her?"
1882  
1883  "I will tell you, Harry, but you mustn't be unsympathetic about it.
1884  After all, it never would have happened if I had not met you.  You
1885  filled me with a wild desire to know everything about life.  For days
1886  after I met you, something seemed to throb in my veins.  As I lounged
1887  in the park, or strolled down Piccadilly, I used to look at every one
1888  who passed me and wonder, with a mad curiosity, what sort of lives they
1889  led.  Some of them fascinated me.  Others filled me with terror.  There
1890  was an exquisite poison in the air.  I had a passion for sensations....
1891  Well, one evening about seven o'clock, I determined to go out in search
1892  of some adventure.  I felt that this grey monstrous London of ours,
1893  with its myriads of people, its sordid sinners, and its splendid sins,
1894  as you once phrased it, must have something in store for me.  I fancied
1895  a thousand things.  The mere danger gave me a sense of delight.  I
1896  remembered what you had said to me on that wonderful evening when we
1897  first dined together, about the search for beauty being the real secret
1898  of life.  I don't know what I expected, but I went out and wandered
1899  eastward, soon losing my way in a labyrinth of grimy streets and black
1900  grassless squares.  About half-past eight I passed by an absurd little
1901  theatre, with great flaring gas-jets and gaudy play-bills.  A hideous
1902  Jew, in the most amazing waistcoat I ever beheld in my life, was
1903  standing at the entrance, smoking a vile cigar.  He had greasy
1904  ringlets, and an enormous diamond blazed in the centre of a soiled
1905  shirt. 'Have a box, my Lord?' he said, when he saw me, and he took off
1906  his hat with an air of gorgeous servility.  There was something about
1907  him, Harry, that amused me.  He was such a monster.  You will laugh at
1908  me, I know, but I really went in and paid a whole guinea for the
1909  stage-box. To the present day I can't make out why I did so; and yet if
1910  I hadn't--my dear Harry, if I hadn't--I should have missed the greatest
1911  romance of my life.  I see you are laughing.  It is horrid of you!"
1912  
1913  "I am not laughing, Dorian; at least I am not laughing at you.  But you
1914  should not say the greatest romance of your life.  You should say the
1915  first romance of your life.  You will always be loved, and you will
1916  always be in love with love.  A _grande passion_ is the privilege of
1917  people who have nothing to do.  That is the one use of the idle classes
1918  of a country.  Don't be afraid.  There are exquisite things in store
1919  for you.  This is merely the beginning."
1920  
1921  "Do you think my nature so shallow?" cried Dorian Gray angrily.
1922  
1923  "No; I think your nature so deep."
1924  
1925  "How do you mean?"
1926  
1927  "My dear boy, the people who love only once in their lives are really
1928  the shallow people.  What they call their loyalty, and their fidelity,
1929  I call either the lethargy of custom or their lack of imagination.
1930  Faithfulness is to the emotional life what consistency is to the life
1931  of the intellect--simply a confession of failure.  Faithfulness!  I
1932  must analyse it some day.  The passion for property is in it.  There
1933  are many things that we would throw away if we were not afraid that
1934  others might pick them up.  But I don't want to interrupt you.  Go on
1935  with your story."
1936  
1937  "Well, I found myself seated in a horrid little private box, with a
1938  vulgar drop-scene staring me in the face.  I looked out from behind the
1939  curtain and surveyed the house.  It was a tawdry affair, all Cupids and
1940  cornucopias, like a third-rate wedding-cake. The gallery and pit were
1941  fairly full, but the two rows of dingy stalls were quite empty, and
1942  there was hardly a person in what I suppose they called the
1943  dress-circle.  Women went about with oranges and ginger-beer, and there
1944  was a terrible consumption of nuts going on."
1945  
1946  "It must have been just like the palmy days of the British drama."
1947  
1948  "Just like, I should fancy, and very depressing.  I began to wonder
1949  what on earth I should do when I caught sight of the play-bill.  What
1950  do you think the play was, Harry?"
1951  
1952  "I should think 'The Idiot Boy', or 'Dumb but Innocent'.  Our fathers
1953  used to like that sort of piece, I believe.  The longer I live, Dorian,
1954  the more keenly I feel that whatever was good enough for our fathers is
1955  not good enough for us.  In art, as in politics, _les grandperes ont
1956  toujours tort_."
1957  
1958  "This play was good enough for us, Harry.  It was Romeo and Juliet.  I
1959  must admit that I was rather annoyed at the idea of seeing Shakespeare
1960  done in such a wretched hole of a place.  Still, I felt interested, in
1961  a sort of way.  At any rate, I determined to wait for the first act.
1962  There was a dreadful orchestra, presided over by a young Hebrew who sat
1963  at a cracked piano, that nearly drove me away, but at last the
1964  drop-scene was drawn up and the play began.  Romeo was a stout elderly
1965  gentleman, with corked eyebrows, a husky tragedy voice, and a figure
1966  like a beer-barrel. Mercutio was almost as bad.  He was played by the
1967  low-comedian, who had introduced gags of his own and was on most
1968  friendly terms with the pit.  They were both as grotesque as the
1969  scenery, and that looked as if it had come out of a country-booth. But
1970  Juliet!  Harry, imagine a girl, hardly seventeen years of age, with a
1971  little, flowerlike face, a small Greek head with plaited coils of
1972  dark-brown hair, eyes that were violet wells of passion, lips that were
1973  like the petals of a rose.  She was the loveliest thing I had ever seen
1974  in my life.  You said to me once that pathos left you unmoved, but that
1975  beauty, mere beauty, could fill your eyes with tears.  I tell you,
1976  Harry, I could hardly see this girl for the mist of tears that came
1977  across me.  And her voice--I never heard such a voice.  It was very low
1978  at first, with deep mellow notes that seemed to fall singly upon one's
1979  ear.  Then it became a little louder, and sounded like a flute or a
1980  distant hautboy.  In the garden-scene it had all the tremulous ecstasy
1981  that one hears just before dawn when nightingales are singing.  There
1982  were moments, later on, when it had the wild passion of violins.  You
1983  know how a voice can stir one.  Your voice and the voice of Sibyl Vane
1984  are two things that I shall never forget.  When I close my eyes, I hear
1985  them, and each of them says something different.  I don't know which to
1986  follow.  Why should I not love her?  Harry, I do love her.  She is
1987  everything to me in life.  Night after night I go to see her play.  One
1988  evening she is Rosalind, and the next evening she is Imogen.  I have
1989  seen her die in the gloom of an Italian tomb, sucking the poison from
1990  her lover's lips.  I have watched her wandering through the forest of
1991  Arden, disguised as a pretty boy in hose and doublet and dainty cap.
1992  She has been mad, and has come into the presence of a guilty king, and
1993  given him rue to wear and bitter herbs to taste of.  She has been
1994  innocent, and the black hands of jealousy have crushed her reedlike
1995  throat.  I have seen her in every age and in every costume.  Ordinary
1996  women never appeal to one's imagination.  They are limited to their
1997  century.  No glamour ever transfigures them.  One knows their minds as
1998  easily as one knows their bonnets.  One can always find them.  There is
1999  no mystery in any of them.  They ride in the park in the morning and
2000  chatter at tea-parties in the afternoon.  They have their stereotyped
2001  smile and their fashionable manner.  They are quite obvious.  But an
2002  actress!  How different an actress is!  Harry! why didn't you tell me
2003  that the only thing worth loving is an actress?"
2004  
2005  "Because I have loved so many of them, Dorian."
2006  
2007  "Oh, yes, horrid people with dyed hair and painted faces."
2008  
2009  "Don't run down dyed hair and painted faces.  There is an extraordinary
2010  charm in them, sometimes," said Lord Henry.
2011  
2012  "I wish now I had not told you about Sibyl Vane."
2013  
2014  "You could not have helped telling me, Dorian.  All through your life
2015  you will tell me everything you do."
2016  
2017  "Yes, Harry, I believe that is true.  I cannot help telling you things.
2018  You have a curious influence over me.  If I ever did a crime, I would
2019  come and confess it to you.  You would understand me."
2020  
2021  "People like you--the wilful sunbeams of life--don't commit crimes,
2022  Dorian.  But I am much obliged for the compliment, all the same.  And
2023  now tell me--reach me the matches, like a good boy--thanks--what are
2024  your actual relations with Sibyl Vane?"
2025  
2026  Dorian Gray leaped to his feet, with flushed cheeks and burning eyes.
2027  "Harry!  Sibyl Vane is sacred!"
2028  
2029  "It is only the sacred things that are worth touching, Dorian," said
2030  Lord Henry, with a strange touch of pathos in his voice.  "But why
2031  should you be annoyed?  I suppose she will belong to you some day.
2032  When one is in love, one always begins by deceiving one's self, and one
2033  always ends by deceiving others.  That is what the world calls a
2034  romance.  You know her, at any rate, I suppose?"
2035  
2036  "Of course I know her.  On the first night I was at the theatre, the
2037  horrid old Jew came round to the box after the performance was over and
2038  offered to take me behind the scenes and introduce me to her.  I was
2039  furious with him, and told him that Juliet had been dead for hundreds
2040  of years and that her body was lying in a marble tomb in Verona.  I
2041  think, from his blank look of amazement, that he was under the
2042  impression that I had taken too much champagne, or something."
2043  
2044  "I am not surprised."
2045  
2046  "Then he asked me if I wrote for any of the newspapers.  I told him I
2047  never even read them.  He seemed terribly disappointed at that, and
2048  confided to me that all the dramatic critics were in a conspiracy
2049  against him, and that they were every one of them to be bought."
2050  
2051  "I should not wonder if he was quite right there.  But, on the other
2052  hand, judging from their appearance, most of them cannot be at all
2053  expensive."
2054  
2055  "Well, he seemed to think they were beyond his means," laughed Dorian.
2056  "By this time, however, the lights were being put out in the theatre,
2057  and I had to go.  He wanted me to try some cigars that he strongly
2058  recommended.  I declined.  The next night, of course, I arrived at the
2059  place again.  When he saw me, he made me a low bow and assured me that
2060  I was a munificent patron of art.  He was a most offensive brute,
2061  though he had an extraordinary passion for Shakespeare.  He told me
2062  once, with an air of pride, that his five bankruptcies were entirely
2063  due to 'The Bard,' as he insisted on calling him.  He seemed to think
2064  it a distinction."
2065  
2066  "It was a distinction, my dear Dorian--a great distinction.  Most
2067  people become bankrupt through having invested too heavily in the prose
2068  of life.  To have ruined one's self over poetry is an honour.  But when
2069  did you first speak to Miss Sibyl Vane?"
2070  
2071  "The third night.  She had been playing Rosalind.  I could not help
2072  going round.  I had thrown her some flowers, and she had looked at
2073  me--at least I fancied that she had.  The old Jew was persistent.  He
2074  seemed determined to take me behind, so I consented.  It was curious my
2075  not wanting to know her, wasn't it?"
2076  
2077  "No; I don't think so."
2078  
2079  "My dear Harry, why?"
2080  
2081  "I will tell you some other time.  Now I want to know about the girl."
2082  
2083  "Sibyl?  Oh, she was so shy and so gentle.  There is something of a
2084  child about her.  Her eyes opened wide in exquisite wonder when I told
2085  her what I thought of her performance, and she seemed quite unconscious
2086  of her power.  I think we were both rather nervous.  The old Jew stood
2087  grinning at the doorway of the dusty greenroom, making elaborate
2088  speeches about us both, while we stood looking at each other like
2089  children.  He would insist on calling me 'My Lord,' so I had to assure
2090  Sibyl that I was not anything of the kind.  She said quite simply to
2091  me, 'You look more like a prince.  I must call you Prince Charming.'"
2092  
2093  "Upon my word, Dorian, Miss Sibyl knows how to pay compliments."
2094  
2095  "You don't understand her, Harry.  She regarded me merely as a person
2096  in a play.  She knows nothing of life.  She lives with her mother, a
2097  faded tired woman who played Lady Capulet in a sort of magenta
2098  dressing-wrapper on the first night, and looks as if she had seen
2099  better days."
2100  
2101  "I know that look.  It depresses me," murmured Lord Henry, examining
2102  his rings.
2103  
2104  "The Jew wanted to tell me her history, but I said it did not interest
2105  me."
2106  
2107  "You were quite right.  There is always something infinitely mean about
2108  other people's tragedies."
2109  
2110  "Sibyl is the only thing I care about.  What is it to me where she came
2111  from?  From her little head to her little feet, she is absolutely and
2112  entirely divine.  Every night of my life I go to see her act, and every
2113  night she is more marvellous."
2114  
2115  "That is the reason, I suppose, that you never dine with me now.  I
2116  thought you must have some curious romance on hand.  You have; but it
2117  is not quite what I expected."
2118  
2119  "My dear Harry, we either lunch or sup together every day, and I have
2120  been to the opera with you several times," said Dorian, opening his
2121  blue eyes in wonder.
2122  
2123  "You always come dreadfully late."
2124  
2125  "Well, I can't help going to see Sibyl play," he cried, "even if it is
2126  only for a single act.  I get hungry for her presence; and when I think
2127  of the wonderful soul that is hidden away in that little ivory body, I
2128  am filled with awe."
2129  
2130  "You can dine with me to-night, Dorian, can't you?"
2131  
2132  He shook his head.  "To-night she is Imogen," he answered, "and
2133  to-morrow night she will be Juliet."
2134  
2135  "When is she Sibyl Vane?"
2136  
2137  "Never."
2138  
2139  "I congratulate you."
2140  
2141  "How horrid you are!  She is all the great heroines of the world in
2142  one.  She is more than an individual.  You laugh, but I tell you she
2143  has genius.  I love her, and I must make her love me.  You, who know
2144  all the secrets of life, tell me how to charm Sibyl Vane to love me!  I
2145  want to make Romeo jealous.  I want the dead lovers of the world to
2146  hear our laughter and grow sad.  I want a breath of our passion to stir
2147  their dust into consciousness, to wake their ashes into pain.  My God,
2148  Harry, how I worship her!"  He was walking up and down the room as he
2149  spoke.  Hectic spots of red burned on his cheeks.  He was terribly
2150  excited.
2151  
2152  Lord Henry watched him with a subtle sense of pleasure.  How different
2153  he was now from the shy frightened boy he had met in Basil Hallward's
2154  studio!  His nature had developed like a flower, had borne blossoms of
2155  scarlet flame.  Out of its secret hiding-place had crept his soul, and
2156  desire had come to meet it on the way.
2157  
2158  "And what do you propose to do?" said Lord Henry at last.
2159  
2160  "I want you and Basil to come with me some night and see her act.  I
2161  have not the slightest fear of the result.  You are certain to
2162  acknowledge her genius.  Then we must get her out of the Jew's hands.
2163  She is bound to him for three years--at least for two years and eight
2164  months--from the present time.  I shall have to pay him something, of
2165  course.  When all that is settled, I shall take a West End theatre and
2166  bring her out properly.  She will make the world as mad as she has made
2167  me."
2168  
2169  "That would be impossible, my dear boy."
2170  
2171  "Yes, she will.  She has not merely art, consummate art-instinct, in
2172  her, but she has personality also; and you have often told me that it
2173  is personalities, not principles, that move the age."
2174  
2175  "Well, what night shall we go?"
2176  
2177  "Let me see.  To-day is Tuesday.  Let us fix to-morrow. She plays
2178  Juliet to-morrow."
2179  
2180  "All right.  The Bristol at eight o'clock; and I will get Basil."
2181  
2182  "Not eight, Harry, please.  Half-past six.  We must be there before the
2183  curtain rises.  You must see her in the first act, where she meets
2184  Romeo."
2185  
2186  "Half-past six!  What an hour!  It will be like having a meat-tea, or
2187  reading an English novel.  It must be seven.  No gentleman dines before
2188  seven.  Shall you see Basil between this and then?  Or shall I write to
2189  him?"
2190  
2191  "Dear Basil!  I have not laid eyes on him for a week.  It is rather
2192  horrid of me, as he has sent me my portrait in the most wonderful
2193  frame, specially designed by himself, and, though I am a little jealous
2194  of the picture for being a whole month younger than I am, I must admit
2195  that I delight in it.  Perhaps you had better write to him.  I don't
2196  want to see him alone.  He says things that annoy me.  He gives me good
2197  advice."
2198  
2199  Lord Henry smiled.  "People are very fond of giving away what they need
2200  most themselves.  It is what I call the depth of generosity."
2201  
2202  "Oh, Basil is the best of fellows, but he seems to me to be just a bit
2203  of a Philistine.  Since I have known you, Harry, I have discovered
2204  that."
2205  
2206  "Basil, my dear boy, puts everything that is charming in him into his
2207  work.  The consequence is that he has nothing left for life but his
2208  prejudices, his principles, and his common sense.  The only artists I
2209  have ever known who are personally delightful are bad artists.  Good
2210  artists exist simply in what they make, and consequently are perfectly
2211  uninteresting in what they are.  A great poet, a really great poet, is
2212  the most unpoetical of all creatures.  But inferior poets are
2213  absolutely fascinating.  The worse their rhymes are, the more
2214  picturesque they look.  The mere fact of having published a book of
2215  second-rate sonnets makes a man quite irresistible.  He lives the
2216  poetry that he cannot write.  The others write the poetry that they
2217  dare not realize."
2218  
2219  "I wonder is that really so, Harry?" said Dorian Gray, putting some
2220  perfume on his handkerchief out of a large, gold-topped bottle that
2221  stood on the table.  "It must be, if you say it.  And now I am off.
2222  Imogen is waiting for me.  Don't forget about to-morrow. Good-bye."
2223  
2224  As he left the room, Lord Henry's heavy eyelids drooped, and he began
2225  to think.  Certainly few people had ever interested him so much as
2226  Dorian Gray, and yet the lad's mad adoration of some one else caused
2227  him not the slightest pang of annoyance or jealousy.  He was pleased by
2228  it.  It made him a more interesting study.  He had been always
2229  enthralled by the methods of natural science, but the ordinary
2230  subject-matter of that science had seemed to him trivial and of no
2231  import.  And so he had begun by vivisecting himself, as he had ended by
2232  vivisecting others.  Human life--that appeared to him the one thing
2233  worth investigating.  Compared to it there was nothing else of any
2234  value.  It was true that as one watched life in its curious crucible of
2235  pain and pleasure, one could not wear over one's face a mask of glass,
2236  nor keep the sulphurous fumes from troubling the brain and making the
2237  imagination turbid with monstrous fancies and misshapen dreams.  There
2238  were poisons so subtle that to know their properties one had to sicken
2239  of them.  There were maladies so strange that one had to pass through
2240  them if one sought to understand their nature.  And, yet, what a great
2241  reward one received!  How wonderful the whole world became to one!  To
2242  note the curious hard logic of passion, and the emotional coloured life
2243  of the intellect--to observe where they met, and where they separated,
2244  at what point they were in unison, and at what point they were at
2245  discord--there was a delight in that!  What matter what the cost was?
2246  One could never pay too high a price for any sensation.
2247  
2248  He was conscious--and the thought brought a gleam of pleasure into his
2249  brown agate eyes--that it was through certain words of his, musical
2250  words said with musical utterance, that Dorian Gray's soul had turned
2251  to this white girl and bowed in worship before her.  To a large extent
2252  the lad was his own creation.  He had made him premature.  That was
2253  something.  Ordinary people waited till life disclosed to them its
2254  secrets, but to the few, to the elect, the mysteries of life were
2255  revealed before the veil was drawn away.  Sometimes this was the effect
2256  of art, and chiefly of the art of literature, which dealt immediately
2257  with the passions and the intellect.  But now and then a complex
2258  personality took the place and assumed the office of art, was indeed,
2259  in its way, a real work of art, life having its elaborate masterpieces,
2260  just as poetry has, or sculpture, or painting.
2261  
2262  Yes, the lad was premature.  He was gathering his harvest while it was
2263  yet spring.  The pulse and passion of youth were in him, but he was
2264  becoming self-conscious. It was delightful to watch him.  With his
2265  beautiful face, and his beautiful soul, he was a thing to wonder at.
2266  It was no matter how it all ended, or was destined to end.  He was like
2267  one of those gracious figures in a pageant or a play, whose joys seem
2268  to be remote from one, but whose sorrows stir one's sense of beauty,
2269  and whose wounds are like red roses.
2270  
2271  Soul and body, body and soul--how mysterious they were!  There was
2272  animalism in the soul, and the body had its moments of spirituality.
2273  The senses could refine, and the intellect could degrade.  Who could
2274  say where the fleshly impulse ceased, or the psychical impulse began?
2275  How shallow were the arbitrary definitions of ordinary psychologists!
2276  And yet how difficult to decide between the claims of the various
2277  schools!  Was the soul a shadow seated in the house of sin?  Or was the
2278  body really in the soul, as Giordano Bruno thought?  The separation of
2279  spirit from matter was a mystery, and the union of spirit with matter
2280  was a mystery also.
2281  
2282  He began to wonder whether we could ever make psychology so absolute a
2283  science that each little spring of life would be revealed to us.  As it
2284  was, we always misunderstood ourselves and rarely understood others.
2285  Experience was of no ethical value.  It was merely the name men gave to
2286  their mistakes.  Moralists had, as a rule, regarded it as a mode of
2287  warning, had claimed for it a certain ethical efficacy in the formation
2288  of character, had praised it as something that taught us what to follow
2289  and showed us what to avoid.  But there was no motive power in
2290  experience.  It was as little of an active cause as conscience itself.
2291  All that it really demonstrated was that our future would be the same
2292  as our past, and that the sin we had done once, and with loathing, we
2293  would do many times, and with joy.
2294  
2295  It was clear to him that the experimental method was the only method by
2296  which one could arrive at any scientific analysis of the passions; and
2297  certainly Dorian Gray was a subject made to his hand, and seemed to
2298  promise rich and fruitful results.  His sudden mad love for Sibyl Vane
2299  was a psychological phenomenon of no small interest.  There was no
2300  doubt that curiosity had much to do with it, curiosity and the desire
2301  for new experiences, yet it was not a simple, but rather a very complex
2302  passion.  What there was in it of the purely sensuous instinct of
2303  boyhood had been transformed by the workings of the imagination,
2304  changed into something that seemed to the lad himself to be remote from
2305  sense, and was for that very reason all the more dangerous.  It was the
2306  passions about whose origin we deceived ourselves that tyrannized most
2307  strongly over us.  Our weakest motives were those of whose nature we
2308  were conscious.  It often happened that when we thought we were
2309  experimenting on others we were really experimenting on ourselves.
2310  
2311  While Lord Henry sat dreaming on these things, a knock came to the
2312  door, and his valet entered and reminded him it was time to dress for
2313  dinner.  He got up and looked out into the street.  The sunset had
2314  smitten into scarlet gold the upper windows of the houses opposite.
2315  The panes glowed like plates of heated metal.  The sky above was like a
2316  faded rose.  He thought of his friend's young fiery-coloured life and
2317  wondered how it was all going to end.
2318  
2319  When he arrived home, about half-past twelve o'clock, he saw a telegram
2320  lying on the hall table.  He opened it and found it was from Dorian
2321  Gray.  It was to tell him that he was engaged to be married to Sibyl
2322  Vane.
2323  
2324  
2325  
2326  CHAPTER 5
2327  
2328  "Mother, Mother, I am so happy!" whispered the girl, burying her face
2329  in the lap of the faded, tired-looking woman who, with back turned to
2330  the shrill intrusive light, was sitting in the one arm-chair that their
2331  dingy sitting-room contained.  "I am so happy!" she repeated, "and you
2332  must be happy, too!"
2333  
2334  Mrs. Vane winced and put her thin, bismuth-whitened hands on her
2335  daughter's head.  "Happy!" she echoed, "I am only happy, Sibyl, when I
2336  see you act.  You must not think of anything but your acting.  Mr.
2337  Isaacs has been very good to us, and we owe him money."
2338  
2339  The girl looked up and pouted.  "Money, Mother?" she cried, "what does
2340  money matter?  Love is more than money."
2341  
2342  "Mr. Isaacs has advanced us fifty pounds to pay off our debts and to
2343  get a proper outfit for James.  You must not forget that, Sibyl.  Fifty
2344  pounds is a very large sum.  Mr. Isaacs has been most considerate."
2345  
2346  "He is not a gentleman, Mother, and I hate the way he talks to me,"
2347  said the girl, rising to her feet and going over to the window.
2348  
2349  "I don't know how we could manage without him," answered the elder
2350  woman querulously.
2351  
2352  Sibyl Vane tossed her head and laughed.  "We don't want him any more,
2353  Mother.  Prince Charming rules life for us now." Then she paused.  A
2354  rose shook in her blood and shadowed her cheeks.  Quick breath parted
2355  the petals of her lips.  They trembled.  Some southern wind of passion
2356  swept over her and stirred the dainty folds of her dress.  "I love
2357  him," she said simply.
2358  
2359  "Foolish child! foolish child!" was the parrot-phrase flung in answer.
2360  The waving of crooked, false-jewelled fingers gave grotesqueness to the
2361  words.
2362  
2363  The girl laughed again.  The joy of a caged bird was in her voice.  Her
2364  eyes caught the melody and echoed it in radiance, then closed for a
2365  moment, as though to hide their secret.  When they opened, the mist of
2366  a dream had passed across them.
2367  
2368  Thin-lipped wisdom spoke at her from the worn chair, hinted at
2369  prudence, quoted from that book of cowardice whose author apes the name
2370  of common sense.  She did not listen.  She was free in her prison of
2371  passion.  Her prince, Prince Charming, was with her.  She had called on
2372  memory to remake him.  She had sent her soul to search for him, and it
2373  had brought him back.  His kiss burned again upon her mouth.  Her
2374  eyelids were warm with his breath.
2375  
2376  Then wisdom altered its method and spoke of espial and discovery.  This
2377  young man might be rich.  If so, marriage should be thought of.
2378  Against the shell of her ear broke the waves of worldly cunning.  The
2379  arrows of craft shot by her.  She saw the thin lips moving, and smiled.
2380  
2381  Suddenly she felt the need to speak.  The wordy silence troubled her.
2382  "Mother, Mother," she cried, "why does he love me so much?  I know why
2383  I love him.  I love him because he is like what love himself should be.
2384  But what does he see in me?  I am not worthy of him.  And yet--why, I
2385  cannot tell--though I feel so much beneath him, I don't feel humble.  I
2386  feel proud, terribly proud.  Mother, did you love my father as I love
2387  Prince Charming?"
2388  
2389  The elder woman grew pale beneath the coarse powder that daubed her
2390  cheeks, and her dry lips twitched with a spasm of pain.  Sybil rushed
2391  to her, flung her arms round her neck, and kissed her.  "Forgive me,
2392  Mother.  I know it pains you to talk about our father.  But it only
2393  pains you because you loved him so much.  Don't look so sad.  I am as
2394  happy to-day as you were twenty years ago.  Ah! let me be happy for
2395  ever!"
2396  
2397  "My child, you are far too young to think of falling in love.  Besides,
2398  what do you know of this young man?  You don't even know his name.  The
2399  whole thing is most inconvenient, and really, when James is going away
2400  to Australia, and I have so much to think of, I must say that you
2401  should have shown more consideration.  However, as I said before, if he
2402  is rich ..."
2403  
2404  "Ah!  Mother, Mother, let me be happy!"
2405  
2406  Mrs. Vane glanced at her, and with one of those false theatrical
2407  gestures that so often become a mode of second nature to a
2408  stage-player, clasped her in her arms.  At this moment, the door opened
2409  and a young lad with rough brown hair came into the room.  He was
2410  thick-set of figure, and his hands and feet were large and somewhat
2411  clumsy in movement.  He was not so finely bred as his sister.  One
2412  would hardly have guessed the close relationship that existed between
2413  them.  Mrs. Vane fixed her eyes on him and intensified her smile.  She
2414  mentally elevated her son to the dignity of an audience.  She felt sure
2415  that the _tableau_ was interesting.
2416  
2417  "You might keep some of your kisses for me, Sibyl, I think," said the
2418  lad with a good-natured grumble.
2419  
2420  "Ah! but you don't like being kissed, Jim," she cried.  "You are a
2421  dreadful old bear."  And she ran across the room and hugged him.
2422  
2423  James Vane looked into his sister's face with tenderness.  "I want you
2424  to come out with me for a walk, Sibyl.  I don't suppose I shall ever
2425  see this horrid London again.  I am sure I don't want to."
2426  
2427  "My son, don't say such dreadful things," murmured Mrs. Vane, taking up
2428  a tawdry theatrical dress, with a sigh, and beginning to patch it.  She
2429  felt a little disappointed that he had not joined the group.  It would
2430  have increased the theatrical picturesqueness of the situation.
2431  
2432  "Why not, Mother?  I mean it."
2433  
2434  "You pain me, my son.  I trust you will return from Australia in a
2435  position of affluence.  I believe there is no society of any kind in
2436  the Colonies--nothing that I would call society--so when you have made
2437  your fortune, you must come back and assert yourself in London."
2438  
2439  "Society!" muttered the lad.  "I don't want to know anything about
2440  that.  I should like to make some money to take you and Sibyl off the
2441  stage.  I hate it."
2442  
2443  "Oh, Jim!" said Sibyl, laughing, "how unkind of you!  But are you
2444  really going for a walk with me?  That will be nice!  I was afraid you
2445  were going to say good-bye to some of your friends--to Tom Hardy, who
2446  gave you that hideous pipe, or Ned Langton, who makes fun of you for
2447  smoking it.  It is very sweet of you to let me have your last
2448  afternoon.  Where shall we go?  Let us go to the park."
2449  
2450  "I am too shabby," he answered, frowning.  "Only swell people go to the
2451  park."
2452  
2453  "Nonsense, Jim," she whispered, stroking the sleeve of his coat.
2454  
2455  He hesitated for a moment.  "Very well," he said at last, "but don't be
2456  too long dressing."  She danced out of the door.  One could hear her
2457  singing as she ran upstairs.  Her little feet pattered overhead.
2458  
2459  He walked up and down the room two or three times.  Then he turned to
2460  the still figure in the chair.  "Mother, are my things ready?" he asked.
2461  
2462  "Quite ready, James," she answered, keeping her eyes on her work.  For
2463  some months past she had felt ill at ease when she was alone with this
2464  rough stern son of hers.  Her shallow secret nature was troubled when
2465  their eyes met.  She used to wonder if he suspected anything.  The
2466  silence, for he made no other observation, became intolerable to her.
2467  She began to complain.  Women defend themselves by attacking, just as
2468  they attack by sudden and strange surrenders.  "I hope you will be
2469  contented, James, with your sea-faring life," she said.  "You must
2470  remember that it is your own choice.  You might have entered a
2471  solicitor's office.  Solicitors are a very respectable class, and in
2472  the country often dine with the best families."
2473  
2474  "I hate offices, and I hate clerks," he replied.  "But you are quite
2475  right.  I have chosen my own life.  All I say is, watch over Sibyl.
2476  Don't let her come to any harm.  Mother, you must watch over her."
2477  
2478  "James, you really talk very strangely.  Of course I watch over Sibyl."
2479  
2480  "I hear a gentleman comes every night to the theatre and goes behind to
2481  talk to her.  Is that right?  What about that?"
2482  
2483  "You are speaking about things you don't understand, James.  In the
2484  profession we are accustomed to receive a great deal of most gratifying
2485  attention.  I myself used to receive many bouquets at one time.  That
2486  was when acting was really understood.  As for Sibyl, I do not know at
2487  present whether her attachment is serious or not.  But there is no
2488  doubt that the young man in question is a perfect gentleman.  He is
2489  always most polite to me.  Besides, he has the appearance of being
2490  rich, and the flowers he sends are lovely."
2491  
2492  "You don't know his name, though," said the lad harshly.
2493  
2494  "No," answered his mother with a placid expression in her face.  "He
2495  has not yet revealed his real name.  I think it is quite romantic of
2496  him.  He is probably a member of the aristocracy."
2497  
2498  James Vane bit his lip.  "Watch over Sibyl, Mother," he cried, "watch
2499  over her."
2500  
2501  "My son, you distress me very much.  Sibyl is always under my special
2502  care.  Of course, if this gentleman is wealthy, there is no reason why
2503  she should not contract an alliance with him.  I trust he is one of the
2504  aristocracy.  He has all the appearance of it, I must say.  It might be
2505  a most brilliant marriage for Sibyl.  They would make a charming
2506  couple.  His good looks are really quite remarkable; everybody notices
2507  them."
2508  
2509  The lad muttered something to himself and drummed on the window-pane
2510  with his coarse fingers.  He had just turned round to say something
2511  when the door opened and Sibyl ran in.
2512  
2513  "How serious you both are!" she cried.  "What is the matter?"
2514  
2515  "Nothing," he answered.  "I suppose one must be serious sometimes.
2516  Good-bye, Mother; I will have my dinner at five o'clock. Everything is
2517  packed, except my shirts, so you need not trouble."
2518  
2519  "Good-bye, my son," she answered with a bow of strained stateliness.
2520  
2521  She was extremely annoyed at the tone he had adopted with her, and
2522  there was something in his look that had made her feel afraid.
2523  
2524  "Kiss me, Mother," said the girl.  Her flowerlike lips touched the
2525  withered cheek and warmed its frost.
2526  
2527  "My child! my child!" cried Mrs. Vane, looking up to the ceiling in
2528  search of an imaginary gallery.
2529  
2530  "Come, Sibyl," said her brother impatiently.  He hated his mother's
2531  affectations.
2532  
2533  They went out into the flickering, wind-blown sunlight and strolled
2534  down the dreary Euston Road.  The passersby glanced in wonder at the
2535  sullen heavy youth who, in coarse, ill-fitting clothes, was in the
2536  company of such a graceful, refined-looking girl.  He was like a common
2537  gardener walking with a rose.
2538  
2539  Jim frowned from time to time when he caught the inquisitive glance of
2540  some stranger.  He had that dislike of being stared at, which comes on
2541  geniuses late in life and never leaves the commonplace.  Sibyl,
2542  however, was quite unconscious of the effect she was producing.  Her
2543  love was trembling in laughter on her lips.  She was thinking of Prince
2544  Charming, and, that she might think of him all the more, she did not
2545  talk of him, but prattled on about the ship in which Jim was going to
2546  sail, about the gold he was certain to find, about the wonderful
2547  heiress whose life he was to save from the wicked, red-shirted
2548  bushrangers.  For he was not to remain a sailor, or a supercargo, or
2549  whatever he was going to be.  Oh, no!  A sailor's existence was
2550  dreadful.  Fancy being cooped up in a horrid ship, with the hoarse,
2551  hump-backed waves trying to get in, and a black wind blowing the masts
2552  down and tearing the sails into long screaming ribands!  He was to
2553  leave the vessel at Melbourne, bid a polite good-bye to the captain,
2554  and go off at once to the gold-fields. Before a week was over he was to
2555  come across a large nugget of pure gold, the largest nugget that had
2556  ever been discovered, and bring it down to the coast in a waggon
2557  guarded by six mounted policemen.  The bushrangers were to attack them
2558  three times, and be defeated with immense slaughter.  Or, no.  He was
2559  not to go to the gold-fields at all.  They were horrid places, where
2560  men got intoxicated, and shot each other in bar-rooms, and used bad
2561  language.  He was to be a nice sheep-farmer, and one evening, as he was
2562  riding home, he was to see the beautiful heiress being carried off by a
2563  robber on a black horse, and give chase, and rescue her.  Of course,
2564  she would fall in love with him, and he with her, and they would get
2565  married, and come home, and live in an immense house in London.  Yes,
2566  there were delightful things in store for him.  But he must be very
2567  good, and not lose his temper, or spend his money foolishly.  She was
2568  only a year older than he was, but she knew so much more of life.  He
2569  must be sure, also, to write to her by every mail, and to say his
2570  prayers each night before he went to sleep.  God was very good, and
2571  would watch over him.  She would pray for him, too, and in a few years
2572  he would come back quite rich and happy.
2573  
2574  The lad listened sulkily to her and made no answer.  He was heart-sick
2575  at leaving home.
2576  
2577  Yet it was not this alone that made him gloomy and morose.
2578  Inexperienced though he was, he had still a strong sense of the danger
2579  of Sibyl's position.  This young dandy who was making love to her could
2580  mean her no good.  He was a gentleman, and he hated him for that, hated
2581  him through some curious race-instinct for which he could not account,
2582  and which for that reason was all the more dominant within him.  He was
2583  conscious also of the shallowness and vanity of his mother's nature,
2584  and in that saw infinite peril for Sibyl and Sibyl's happiness.
2585  Children begin by loving their parents; as they grow older they judge
2586  them; sometimes they forgive them.
2587  
2588  His mother!  He had something on his mind to ask of her, something that
2589  he had brooded on for many months of silence.  A chance phrase that he
2590  had heard at the theatre, a whispered sneer that had reached his ears
2591  one night as he waited at the stage-door, had set loose a train of
2592  horrible thoughts.  He remembered it as if it had been the lash of a
2593  hunting-crop across his face.  His brows knit together into a wedge-like
2594  furrow, and with a twitch of pain he bit his underlip.
2595  
2596  "You are not listening to a word I am saying, Jim," cried Sibyl, "and I
2597  am making the most delightful plans for your future.  Do say something."
2598  
2599  "What do you want me to say?"
2600  
2601  "Oh! that you will be a good boy and not forget us," she answered,
2602  smiling at him.
2603  
2604  He shrugged his shoulders.  "You are more likely to forget me than I am
2605  to forget you, Sibyl."
2606  
2607  She flushed.  "What do you mean, Jim?" she asked.
2608  
2609  "You have a new friend, I hear.  Who is he?  Why have you not told me
2610  about him?  He means you no good."
2611  
2612  "Stop, Jim!" she exclaimed.  "You must not say anything against him.  I
2613  love him."
2614  
2615  "Why, you don't even know his name," answered the lad.  "Who is he?  I
2616  have a right to know."
2617  
2618  "He is called Prince Charming.  Don't you like the name.  Oh! you silly
2619  boy! you should never forget it.  If you only saw him, you would think
2620  him the most wonderful person in the world.  Some day you will meet
2621  him--when you come back from Australia.  You will like him so much.
2622  Everybody likes him, and I ... love him.  I wish you could come to the
2623  theatre to-night. He is going to be there, and I am to play Juliet.
2624  Oh! how I shall play it!  Fancy, Jim, to be in love and play Juliet!
2625  To have him sitting there!  To play for his delight!  I am afraid I may
2626  frighten the company, frighten or enthrall them.  To be in love is to
2627  surpass one's self.  Poor dreadful Mr. Isaacs will be shouting 'genius'
2628  to his loafers at the bar.  He has preached me as a dogma; to-night he
2629  will announce me as a revelation.  I feel it.  And it is all his, his
2630  only, Prince Charming, my wonderful lover, my god of graces.  But I am
2631  poor beside him.  Poor?  What does that matter?  When poverty creeps in
2632  at the door, love flies in through the window.  Our proverbs want
2633  rewriting.  They were made in winter, and it is summer now; spring-time
2634  for me, I think, a very dance of blossoms in blue skies."
2635  
2636  "He is a gentleman," said the lad sullenly.
2637  
2638  "A prince!" she cried musically.  "What more do you want?"
2639  
2640  "He wants to enslave you."
2641  
2642  "I shudder at the thought of being free."
2643  
2644  "I want you to beware of him."
2645  
2646  "To see him is to worship him; to know him is to trust him."
2647  
2648  "Sibyl, you are mad about him."
2649  
2650  She laughed and took his arm.  "You dear old Jim, you talk as if you
2651  were a hundred.  Some day you will be in love yourself.  Then you will
2652  know what it is.  Don't look so sulky.  Surely you should be glad to
2653  think that, though you are going away, you leave me happier than I have
2654  ever been before.  Life has been hard for us both, terribly hard and
2655  difficult.  But it will be different now.  You are going to a new
2656  world, and I have found one.  Here are two chairs; let us sit down and
2657  see the smart people go by."
2658  
2659  They took their seats amidst a crowd of watchers.  The tulip-beds
2660  across the road flamed like throbbing rings of fire.  A white
2661  dust--tremulous cloud of orris-root it seemed--hung in the panting air.
2662  The brightly coloured parasols danced and dipped like monstrous
2663  butterflies.
2664  
2665  She made her brother talk of himself, his hopes, his prospects.  He
2666  spoke slowly and with effort.  They passed words to each other as
2667  players at a game pass counters.  Sibyl felt oppressed.  She could not
2668  communicate her joy.  A faint smile curving that sullen mouth was all
2669  the echo she could win.  After some time she became silent.  Suddenly
2670  she caught a glimpse of golden hair and laughing lips, and in an open
2671  carriage with two ladies Dorian Gray drove past.
2672  
2673  She started to her feet.  "There he is!" she cried.
2674  
2675  "Who?" said Jim Vane.
2676  
2677  "Prince Charming," she answered, looking after the victoria.
2678  
2679  He jumped up and seized her roughly by the arm.  "Show him to me.
2680  Which is he?  Point him out.  I must see him!" he exclaimed; but at
2681  that moment the Duke of Berwick's four-in-hand came between, and when
2682  it had left the space clear, the carriage had swept out of the park.
2683  
2684  "He is gone," murmured Sibyl sadly.  "I wish you had seen him."
2685  
2686  "I wish I had, for as sure as there is a God in heaven, if he ever does
2687  you any wrong, I shall kill him."
2688  
2689  She looked at him in horror.  He repeated his words.  They cut the air
2690  like a dagger.  The people round began to gape.  A lady standing close
2691  to her tittered.
2692  
2693  "Come away, Jim; come away," she whispered.  He followed her doggedly
2694  as she passed through the crowd.  He felt glad at what he had said.
2695  
2696  When they reached the Achilles Statue, she turned round.  There was
2697  pity in her eyes that became laughter on her lips.  She shook her head
2698  at him.  "You are foolish, Jim, utterly foolish; a bad-tempered boy,
2699  that is all.  How can you say such horrible things?  You don't know
2700  what you are talking about.  You are simply jealous and unkind.  Ah!  I
2701  wish you would fall in love.  Love makes people good, and what you said
2702  was wicked."
2703  
2704  "I am sixteen," he answered, "and I know what I am about.  Mother is no
2705  help to you.  She doesn't understand how to look after you.  I wish now
2706  that I was not going to Australia at all.  I have a great mind to chuck
2707  the whole thing up.  I would, if my articles hadn't been signed."
2708  
2709  "Oh, don't be so serious, Jim.  You are like one of the heroes of those
2710  silly melodramas Mother used to be so fond of acting in.  I am not
2711  going to quarrel with you.  I have seen him, and oh! to see him is
2712  perfect happiness.  We won't quarrel.  I know you would never harm any
2713  one I love, would you?"
2714  
2715  "Not as long as you love him, I suppose," was the sullen answer.
2716  
2717  "I shall love him for ever!" she cried.
2718  
2719  "And he?"
2720  
2721  "For ever, too!"
2722  
2723  "He had better."
2724  
2725  She shrank from him.  Then she laughed and put her hand on his arm.  He
2726  was merely a boy.
2727  
2728  At the Marble Arch they hailed an omnibus, which left them close to
2729  their shabby home in the Euston Road.  It was after five o'clock, and
2730  Sibyl had to lie down for a couple of hours before acting.  Jim
2731  insisted that she should do so.  He said that he would sooner part with
2732  her when their mother was not present.  She would be sure to make a
2733  scene, and he detested scenes of every kind.
2734  
2735  In Sybil's own room they parted.  There was jealousy in the lad's
2736  heart, and a fierce murderous hatred of the stranger who, as it seemed
2737  to him, had come between them.  Yet, when her arms were flung round his
2738  neck, and her fingers strayed through his hair, he softened and kissed
2739  her with real affection.  There were tears in his eyes as he went
2740  downstairs.
2741  
2742  His mother was waiting for him below.  She grumbled at his
2743  unpunctuality, as he entered.  He made no answer, but sat down to his
2744  meagre meal.  The flies buzzed round the table and crawled over the
2745  stained cloth.  Through the rumble of omnibuses, and the clatter of
2746  street-cabs, he could hear the droning voice devouring each minute that
2747  was left to him.
2748  
2749  After some time, he thrust away his plate and put his head in his
2750  hands.  He felt that he had a right to know.  It should have been told
2751  to him before, if it was as he suspected.  Leaden with fear, his mother
2752  watched him.  Words dropped mechanically from her lips.  A tattered
2753  lace handkerchief twitched in her fingers.  When the clock struck six,
2754  he got up and went to the door.  Then he turned back and looked at her.
2755  Their eyes met.  In hers he saw a wild appeal for mercy.  It enraged
2756  him.
2757  
2758  "Mother, I have something to ask you," he said.  Her eyes wandered
2759  vaguely about the room.  She made no answer.  "Tell me the truth.  I
2760  have a right to know.  Were you married to my father?"
2761  
2762  She heaved a deep sigh.  It was a sigh of relief.  The terrible moment,
2763  the moment that night and day, for weeks and months, she had dreaded,
2764  had come at last, and yet she felt no terror.  Indeed, in some measure
2765  it was a disappointment to her.  The vulgar directness of the question
2766  called for a direct answer.  The situation had not been gradually led
2767  up to.  It was crude.  It reminded her of a bad rehearsal.
2768  
2769  "No," she answered, wondering at the harsh simplicity of life.
2770  
2771  "My father was a scoundrel then!" cried the lad, clenching his fists.
2772  
2773  She shook her head.  "I knew he was not free.  We loved each other very
2774  much.  If he had lived, he would have made provision for us.  Don't
2775  speak against him, my son.  He was your father, and a gentleman.
2776  Indeed, he was highly connected."
2777  
2778  An oath broke from his lips.  "I don't care for myself," he exclaimed,
2779  "but don't let Sibyl.... It is a gentleman, isn't it, who is in love
2780  with her, or says he is?  Highly connected, too, I suppose."
2781  
2782  For a moment a hideous sense of humiliation came over the woman.  Her
2783  head drooped.  She wiped her eyes with shaking hands.  "Sibyl has a
2784  mother," she murmured; "I had none."
2785  
2786  The lad was touched.  He went towards her, and stooping down, he kissed
2787  her.  "I am sorry if I have pained you by asking about my father," he
2788  said, "but I could not help it.  I must go now.  Good-bye. Don't forget
2789  that you will have only one child now to look after, and believe me
2790  that if this man wrongs my sister, I will find out who he is, track him
2791  down, and kill him like a dog.  I swear it."
2792  
2793  The exaggerated folly of the threat, the passionate gesture that
2794  accompanied it, the mad melodramatic words, made life seem more vivid
2795  to her.  She was familiar with the atmosphere.  She breathed more
2796  freely, and for the first time for many months she really admired her
2797  son.  She would have liked to have continued the scene on the same
2798  emotional scale, but he cut her short.  Trunks had to be carried down
2799  and mufflers looked for.  The lodging-house drudge bustled in and out.
2800  There was the bargaining with the cabman.  The moment was lost in
2801  vulgar details.  It was with a renewed feeling of disappointment that
2802  she waved the tattered lace handkerchief from the window, as her son
2803  drove away.  She was conscious that a great opportunity had been
2804  wasted.  She consoled herself by telling Sibyl how desolate she felt
2805  her life would be, now that she had only one child to look after.  She
2806  remembered the phrase.  It had pleased her.  Of the threat she said
2807  nothing.  It was vividly and dramatically expressed.  She felt that
2808  they would all laugh at it some day.
2809  
2810  
2811  
2812  CHAPTER 6
2813  
2814  "I suppose you have heard the news, Basil?" said Lord Henry that
2815  evening as Hallward was shown into a little private room at the Bristol
2816  where dinner had been laid for three.
2817  
2818  "No, Harry," answered the artist, giving his hat and coat to the bowing
2819  waiter.  "What is it?  Nothing about politics, I hope!  They don't
2820  interest me.  There is hardly a single person in the House of Commons
2821  worth painting, though many of them would be the better for a little
2822  whitewashing."
2823  
2824  "Dorian Gray is engaged to be married," said Lord Henry, watching him
2825  as he spoke.
2826  
2827  Hallward started and then frowned.  "Dorian engaged to be married!" he
2828  cried.  "Impossible!"
2829  
2830  "It is perfectly true."
2831  
2832  "To whom?"
2833  
2834  "To some little actress or other."
2835  
2836  "I can't believe it.  Dorian is far too sensible."
2837  
2838  "Dorian is far too wise not to do foolish things now and then, my dear
2839  Basil."
2840  
2841  "Marriage is hardly a thing that one can do now and then, Harry."
2842  
2843  "Except in America," rejoined Lord Henry languidly.  "But I didn't say
2844  he was married.  I said he was engaged to be married.  There is a great
2845  difference.  I have a distinct remembrance of being married, but I have
2846  no recollection at all of being engaged.  I am inclined to think that I
2847  never was engaged."
2848  
2849  "But think of Dorian's birth, and position, and wealth.  It would be
2850  absurd for him to marry so much beneath him."
2851  
2852  "If you want to make him marry this girl, tell him that, Basil.  He is
2853  sure to do it, then.  Whenever a man does a thoroughly stupid thing, it
2854  is always from the noblest motives."
2855  
2856  "I hope the girl is good, Harry.  I don't want to see Dorian tied to
2857  some vile creature, who might degrade his nature and ruin his
2858  intellect."
2859  
2860  "Oh, she is better than good--she is beautiful," murmured Lord Henry,
2861  sipping a glass of vermouth and orange-bitters. "Dorian says she is
2862  beautiful, and he is not often wrong about things of that kind.  Your
2863  portrait of him has quickened his appreciation of the personal
2864  appearance of other people.  It has had that excellent effect, amongst
2865  others.  We are to see her to-night, if that boy doesn't forget his
2866  appointment."
2867  
2868  "Are you serious?"
2869  
2870  "Quite serious, Basil.  I should be miserable if I thought I should
2871  ever be more serious than I am at the present moment."
2872  
2873  "But do you approve of it, Harry?" asked the painter, walking up and
2874  down the room and biting his lip.  "You can't approve of it, possibly.
2875  It is some silly infatuation."
2876  
2877  "I never approve, or disapprove, of anything now.  It is an absurd
2878  attitude to take towards life.  We are not sent into the world to air
2879  our moral prejudices.  I never take any notice of what common people
2880  say, and I never interfere with what charming people do.  If a
2881  personality fascinates me, whatever mode of expression that personality
2882  selects is absolutely delightful to me.  Dorian Gray falls in love with
2883  a beautiful girl who acts Juliet, and proposes to marry her.  Why not?
2884  If he wedded Messalina, he would be none the less interesting.  You
2885  know I am not a champion of marriage.  The real drawback to marriage is
2886  that it makes one unselfish.  And unselfish people are colourless.
2887  They lack individuality.  Still, there are certain temperaments that
2888  marriage makes more complex.  They retain their egotism, and add to it
2889  many other egos.  They are forced to have more than one life.  They
2890  become more highly organized, and to be highly organized is, I should
2891  fancy, the object of man's existence.  Besides, every experience is of
2892  value, and whatever one may say against marriage, it is certainly an
2893  experience.  I hope that Dorian Gray will make this girl his wife,
2894  passionately adore her for six months, and then suddenly become
2895  fascinated by some one else.  He would be a wonderful study."
2896  
2897  "You don't mean a single word of all that, Harry; you know you don't.
2898  If Dorian Gray's life were spoiled, no one would be sorrier than
2899  yourself.  You are much better than you pretend to be."
2900  
2901  Lord Henry laughed.  "The reason we all like to think so well of others
2902  is that we are all afraid for ourselves.  The basis of optimism is
2903  sheer terror.  We think that we are generous because we credit our
2904  neighbour with the possession of those virtues that are likely to be a
2905  benefit to us.  We praise the banker that we may overdraw our account,
2906  and find good qualities in the highwayman in the hope that he may spare
2907  our pockets.  I mean everything that I have said.  I have the greatest
2908  contempt for optimism.  As for a spoiled life, no life is spoiled but
2909  one whose growth is arrested.  If you want to mar a nature, you have
2910  merely to reform it.  As for marriage, of course that would be silly,
