2911  but there are other and more interesting bonds between men and women.
2912  I will certainly encourage them.  They have the charm of being
2913  fashionable.  But here is Dorian himself.  He will tell you more than I
2914  can."
2915  
2916  "My dear Harry, my dear Basil, you must both congratulate me!" said the
2917  lad, throwing off his evening cape with its satin-lined wings and
2918  shaking each of his friends by the hand in turn.  "I have never been so
2919  happy.  Of course, it is sudden--all really delightful things are.  And
2920  yet it seems to me to be the one thing I have been looking for all my
2921  life." He was flushed with excitement and pleasure, and looked
2922  extraordinarily handsome.
2923  
2924  "I hope you will always be very happy, Dorian," said Hallward, "but I
2925  don't quite forgive you for not having let me know of your engagement.
2926  You let Harry know."
2927  
2928  "And I don't forgive you for being late for dinner," broke in Lord
2929  Henry, putting his hand on the lad's shoulder and smiling as he spoke.
2930  "Come, let us sit down and try what the new _chef_ here is like, and then
2931  you will tell us how it all came about."
2932  
2933  "There is really not much to tell," cried Dorian as they took their
2934  seats at the small round table.  "What happened was simply this.  After
2935  I left you yesterday evening, Harry, I dressed, had some dinner at that
2936  little Italian restaurant in Rupert Street you introduced me to, and
2937  went down at eight o'clock to the theatre.  Sibyl was playing Rosalind.
2938  Of course, the scenery was dreadful and the Orlando absurd.  But Sibyl!
2939  You should have seen her!  When she came on in her boy's clothes, she
2940  was perfectly wonderful.  She wore a moss-coloured velvet jerkin with
2941  cinnamon sleeves, slim, brown, cross-gartered hose, a dainty little
2942  green cap with a hawk's feather caught in a jewel, and a hooded cloak
2943  lined with dull red.  She had never seemed to me more exquisite.  She
2944  had all the delicate grace of that Tanagra figurine that you have in
2945  your studio, Basil.  Her hair clustered round her face like dark leaves
2946  round a pale rose.  As for her acting--well, you shall see her
2947  to-night. She is simply a born artist.  I sat in the dingy box
2948  absolutely enthralled.  I forgot that I was in London and in the
2949  nineteenth century.  I was away with my love in a forest that no man
2950  had ever seen.  After the performance was over, I went behind and spoke
2951  to her.  As we were sitting together, suddenly there came into her eyes
2952  a look that I had never seen there before.  My lips moved towards hers.
2953  We kissed each other.  I can't describe to you what I felt at that
2954  moment.  It seemed to me that all my life had been narrowed to one
2955  perfect point of rose-coloured joy.  She trembled all over and shook
2956  like a white narcissus.  Then she flung herself on her knees and kissed
2957  my hands.  I feel that I should not tell you all this, but I can't help
2958  it.  Of course, our engagement is a dead secret.  She has not even told
2959  her own mother.  I don't know what my guardians will say.  Lord Radley
2960  is sure to be furious.  I don't care.  I shall be of age in less than a
2961  year, and then I can do what I like.  I have been right, Basil, haven't
2962  I, to take my love out of poetry and to find my wife in Shakespeare's
2963  plays?  Lips that Shakespeare taught to speak have whispered their
2964  secret in my ear.  I have had the arms of Rosalind around me, and
2965  kissed Juliet on the mouth."
2966  
2967  "Yes, Dorian, I suppose you were right," said Hallward slowly.
2968  
2969  "Have you seen her to-day?" asked Lord Henry.
2970  
2971  Dorian Gray shook his head.  "I left her in the forest of Arden; I
2972  shall find her in an orchard in Verona."
2973  
2974  Lord Henry sipped his champagne in a meditative manner.  "At what
2975  particular point did you mention the word marriage, Dorian?  And what
2976  did she say in answer?  Perhaps you forgot all about it."
2977  
2978  "My dear Harry, I did not treat it as a business transaction, and I did
2979  not make any formal proposal.  I told her that I loved her, and she
2980  said she was not worthy to be my wife.  Not worthy!  Why, the whole
2981  world is nothing to me compared with her."
2982  
2983  "Women are wonderfully practical," murmured Lord Henry, "much more
2984  practical than we are.  In situations of that kind we often forget to
2985  say anything about marriage, and they always remind us."
2986  
2987  Hallward laid his hand upon his arm.  "Don't, Harry.  You have annoyed
2988  Dorian.  He is not like other men.  He would never bring misery upon
2989  any one.  His nature is too fine for that."
2990  
2991  Lord Henry looked across the table.  "Dorian is never annoyed with me,"
2992  he answered.  "I asked the question for the best reason possible, for
2993  the only reason, indeed, that excuses one for asking any
2994  question--simple curiosity.  I have a theory that it is always the
2995  women who propose to us, and not we who propose to the women.  Except,
2996  of course, in middle-class life.  But then the middle classes are not
2997  modern."
2998  
2999  Dorian Gray laughed, and tossed his head.  "You are quite incorrigible,
3000  Harry; but I don't mind.  It is impossible to be angry with you.  When
3001  you see Sibyl Vane, you will feel that the man who could wrong her
3002  would be a beast, a beast without a heart.  I cannot understand how any
3003  one can wish to shame the thing he loves.  I love Sibyl Vane.  I want
3004  to place her on a pedestal of gold and to see the world worship the
3005  woman who is mine.  What is marriage?  An irrevocable vow.  You mock at
3006  it for that.  Ah! don't mock.  It is an irrevocable vow that I want to
3007  take.  Her trust makes me faithful, her belief makes me good.  When I
3008  am with her, I regret all that you have taught me.  I become different
3009  from what you have known me to be.  I am changed, and the mere touch of
3010  Sibyl Vane's hand makes me forget you and all your wrong, fascinating,
3011  poisonous, delightful theories."
3012  
3013  "And those are ...?" asked Lord Henry, helping himself to some salad.
3014  
3015  "Oh, your theories about life, your theories about love, your theories
3016  about pleasure.  All your theories, in fact, Harry."
3017  
3018  "Pleasure is the only thing worth having a theory about," he answered
3019  in his slow melodious voice.  "But I am afraid I cannot claim my theory
3020  as my own.  It belongs to Nature, not to me.  Pleasure is Nature's
3021  test, her sign of approval.  When we are happy, we are always good, but
3022  when we are good, we are not always happy."
3023  
3024  "Ah! but what do you mean by good?" cried Basil Hallward.
3025  
3026  "Yes," echoed Dorian, leaning back in his chair and looking at Lord
3027  Henry over the heavy clusters of purple-lipped irises that stood in the
3028  centre of the table, "what do you mean by good, Harry?"
3029  
3030  "To be good is to be in harmony with one's self," he replied, touching
3031  the thin stem of his glass with his pale, fine-pointed fingers.
3032  "Discord is to be forced to be in harmony with others.  One's own
3033  life--that is the important thing.  As for the lives of one's
3034  neighbours, if one wishes to be a prig or a Puritan, one can flaunt
3035  one's moral views about them, but they are not one's concern.  Besides,
3036  individualism has really the higher aim.  Modern morality consists in
3037  accepting the standard of one's age.  I consider that for any man of
3038  culture to accept the standard of his age is a form of the grossest
3039  immorality."
3040  
3041  "But, surely, if one lives merely for one's self, Harry, one pays a
3042  terrible price for doing so?" suggested the painter.
3043  
3044  "Yes, we are overcharged for everything nowadays.  I should fancy that
3045  the real tragedy of the poor is that they can afford nothing but
3046  self-denial. Beautiful sins, like beautiful things, are the privilege
3047  of the rich."
3048  
3049  "One has to pay in other ways but money."
3050  
3051  "What sort of ways, Basil?"
3052  
3053  "Oh!  I should fancy in remorse, in suffering, in ... well, in the
3054  consciousness of degradation."
3055  
3056  Lord Henry shrugged his shoulders.  "My dear fellow, mediaeval art is
3057  charming, but mediaeval emotions are out of date.  One can use them in
3058  fiction, of course.  But then the only things that one can use in
3059  fiction are the things that one has ceased to use in fact.  Believe me,
3060  no civilized man ever regrets a pleasure, and no uncivilized man ever
3061  knows what a pleasure is."
3062  
3063  "I know what pleasure is," cried Dorian Gray.  "It is to adore some
3064  one."
3065  
3066  "That is certainly better than being adored," he answered, toying with
3067  some fruits.  "Being adored is a nuisance.  Women treat us just as
3068  humanity treats its gods.  They worship us, and are always bothering us
3069  to do something for them."
3070  
3071  "I should have said that whatever they ask for they had first given to
3072  us," murmured the lad gravely.  "They create love in our natures.  They
3073  have a right to demand it back."
3074  
3075  "That is quite true, Dorian," cried Hallward.
3076  
3077  "Nothing is ever quite true," said Lord Henry.
3078  
3079  "This is," interrupted Dorian.  "You must admit, Harry, that women give
3080  to men the very gold of their lives."
3081  
3082  "Possibly," he sighed, "but they invariably want it back in such very
3083  small change.  That is the worry.  Women, as some witty Frenchman once
3084  put it, inspire us with the desire to do masterpieces and always
3085  prevent us from carrying them out."
3086  
3087  "Harry, you are dreadful!  I don't know why I like you so much."
3088  
3089  "You will always like me, Dorian," he replied.  "Will you have some
3090  coffee, you fellows?  Waiter, bring coffee, and _fine-champagne_, and
3091  some cigarettes.  No, don't mind the cigarettes--I have some.  Basil, I
3092  can't allow you to smoke cigars.  You must have a cigarette.  A
3093  cigarette is the perfect type of a perfect pleasure.  It is exquisite,
3094  and it leaves one unsatisfied.  What more can one want?  Yes, Dorian,
3095  you will always be fond of me.  I represent to you all the sins you
3096  have never had the courage to commit."
3097  
3098  "What nonsense you talk, Harry!" cried the lad, taking a light from a
3099  fire-breathing silver dragon that the waiter had placed on the table.
3100  "Let us go down to the theatre.  When Sibyl comes on the stage you will
3101  have a new ideal of life.  She will represent something to you that you
3102  have never known."
3103  
3104  "I have known everything," said Lord Henry, with a tired look in his
3105  eyes, "but I am always ready for a new emotion.  I am afraid, however,
3106  that, for me at any rate, there is no such thing.  Still, your
3107  wonderful girl may thrill me.  I love acting.  It is so much more real
3108  than life.  Let us go.  Dorian, you will come with me.  I am so sorry,
3109  Basil, but there is only room for two in the brougham.  You must follow
3110  us in a hansom."
3111  
3112  They got up and put on their coats, sipping their coffee standing.  The
3113  painter was silent and preoccupied.  There was a gloom over him.  He
3114  could not bear this marriage, and yet it seemed to him to be better
3115  than many other things that might have happened.  After a few minutes,
3116  they all passed downstairs.  He drove off by himself, as had been
3117  arranged, and watched the flashing lights of the little brougham in
3118  front of him.  A strange sense of loss came over him.  He felt that
3119  Dorian Gray would never again be to him all that he had been in the
3120  past.  Life had come between them.... His eyes darkened, and the
3121  crowded flaring streets became blurred to his eyes.  When the cab drew
3122  up at the theatre, it seemed to him that he had grown years older.
3123  
3124  
3125  
3126  CHAPTER 7
3127  
3128  For some reason or other, the house was crowded that night, and the fat
3129  Jew manager who met them at the door was beaming from ear to ear with
3130  an oily tremulous smile.  He escorted them to their box with a sort of
3131  pompous humility, waving his fat jewelled hands and talking at the top
3132  of his voice.  Dorian Gray loathed him more than ever.  He felt as if
3133  he had come to look for Miranda and had been met by Caliban.  Lord
3134  Henry, upon the other hand, rather liked him.  At least he declared he
3135  did, and insisted on shaking him by the hand and assuring him that he
3136  was proud to meet a man who had discovered a real genius and gone
3137  bankrupt over a poet.  Hallward amused himself with watching the faces
3138  in the pit.  The heat was terribly oppressive, and the huge sunlight
3139  flamed like a monstrous dahlia with petals of yellow fire.  The youths
3140  in the gallery had taken off their coats and waistcoats and hung them
3141  over the side.  They talked to each other across the theatre and shared
3142  their oranges with the tawdry girls who sat beside them.  Some women
3143  were laughing in the pit.  Their voices were horribly shrill and
3144  discordant.  The sound of the popping of corks came from the bar.
3145  
3146  "What a place to find one's divinity in!" said Lord Henry.
3147  
3148  "Yes!" answered Dorian Gray.  "It was here I found her, and she is
3149  divine beyond all living things.  When she acts, you will forget
3150  everything.  These common rough people, with their coarse faces and
3151  brutal gestures, become quite different when she is on the stage.  They
3152  sit silently and watch her.  They weep and laugh as she wills them to
3153  do.  She makes them as responsive as a violin.  She spiritualizes them,
3154  and one feels that they are of the same flesh and blood as one's self."
3155  
3156  "The same flesh and blood as one's self!  Oh, I hope not!" exclaimed
3157  Lord Henry, who was scanning the occupants of the gallery through his
3158  opera-glass.
3159  
3160  "Don't pay any attention to him, Dorian," said the painter.  "I
3161  understand what you mean, and I believe in this girl.  Any one you love
3162  must be marvellous, and any girl who has the effect you describe must
3163  be fine and noble.  To spiritualize one's age--that is something worth
3164  doing.  If this girl can give a soul to those who have lived without
3165  one, if she can create the sense of beauty in people whose lives have
3166  been sordid and ugly, if she can strip them of their selfishness and
3167  lend them tears for sorrows that are not their own, she is worthy of
3168  all your adoration, worthy of the adoration of the world.  This
3169  marriage is quite right.  I did not think so at first, but I admit it
3170  now.  The gods made Sibyl Vane for you.  Without her you would have
3171  been incomplete."
3172  
3173  "Thanks, Basil," answered Dorian Gray, pressing his hand.  "I knew that
3174  you would understand me.  Harry is so cynical, he terrifies me.  But
3175  here is the orchestra.  It is quite dreadful, but it only lasts for
3176  about five minutes.  Then the curtain rises, and you will see the girl
3177  to whom I am going to give all my life, to whom I have given everything
3178  that is good in me."
3179  
3180  A quarter of an hour afterwards, amidst an extraordinary turmoil of
3181  applause, Sibyl Vane stepped on to the stage.  Yes, she was certainly
3182  lovely to look at--one of the loveliest creatures, Lord Henry thought,
3183  that he had ever seen.  There was something of the fawn in her shy
3184  grace and startled eyes.  A faint blush, like the shadow of a rose in a
3185  mirror of silver, came to her cheeks as she glanced at the crowded
3186  enthusiastic house.  She stepped back a few paces and her lips seemed
3187  to tremble.  Basil Hallward leaped to his feet and began to applaud.
3188  Motionless, and as one in a dream, sat Dorian Gray, gazing at her.
3189  Lord Henry peered through his glasses, murmuring, "Charming! charming!"
3190  
3191  The scene was the hall of Capulet's house, and Romeo in his pilgrim's
3192  dress had entered with Mercutio and his other friends.  The band, such
3193  as it was, struck up a few bars of music, and the dance began.  Through
3194  the crowd of ungainly, shabbily dressed actors, Sibyl Vane moved like a
3195  creature from a finer world.  Her body swayed, while she danced, as a
3196  plant sways in the water.  The curves of her throat were the curves of
3197  a white lily.  Her hands seemed to be made of cool ivory.
3198  
3199  Yet she was curiously listless.  She showed no sign of joy when her
3200  eyes rested on Romeo.  The few words she had to speak--
3201  
3202      Good pilgrim, you do wrong your hand too much,
3203          Which mannerly devotion shows in this;
3204      For saints have hands that pilgrims' hands do touch,
3205          And palm to palm is holy palmers' kiss--
3206  
3207  with the brief dialogue that follows, were spoken in a thoroughly
3208  artificial manner.  The voice was exquisite, but from the point of view
3209  of tone it was absolutely false.  It was wrong in colour.  It took away
3210  all the life from the verse.  It made the passion unreal.
3211  
3212  Dorian Gray grew pale as he watched her.  He was puzzled and anxious.
3213  Neither of his friends dared to say anything to him.  She seemed to
3214  them to be absolutely incompetent.  They were horribly disappointed.
3215  
3216  Yet they felt that the true test of any Juliet is the balcony scene of
3217  the second act.  They waited for that.  If she failed there, there was
3218  nothing in her.
3219  
3220  She looked charming as she came out in the moonlight.  That could not
3221  be denied.  But the staginess of her acting was unbearable, and grew
3222  worse as she went on.  Her gestures became absurdly artificial.  She
3223  overemphasized everything that she had to say.  The beautiful passage--
3224  
3225      Thou knowest the mask of night is on my face,
3226      Else would a maiden blush bepaint my cheek
3227      For that which thou hast heard me speak to-night--
3228  
3229  was declaimed with the painful precision of a schoolgirl who has been
3230  taught to recite by some second-rate professor of elocution. When she
3231  leaned over the balcony and came to those wonderful lines--
3232  
3233          Although I joy in thee,
3234      I have no joy of this contract to-night:
3235      It is too rash, too unadvised, too sudden;
3236      Too like the lightning, which doth cease to be
3237      Ere one can say, "It lightens."  Sweet, good-night!
3238      This bud of love by summer's ripening breath
3239      May prove a beauteous flower when next we meet--
3240  
3241  she spoke the words as though they conveyed no meaning to her. It was
3242  not nervousness. Indeed, so far from being nervous, she was absolutely
3243  self-contained. It was simply bad art. She was a complete failure.
3244  
3245  Even the common uneducated audience of the pit and gallery lost their
3246  interest in the play. They got restless, and began to talk loudly and
3247  to whistle. The Jew manager, who was standing at the back of the
3248  dress-circle, stamped and swore with rage. The only person unmoved was
3249  the girl herself.
3250  
3251  When the second act was over, there came a storm of hisses, and Lord
3252  Henry got up from his chair and put on his coat.  "She is quite
3253  beautiful, Dorian," he said, "but she can't act.  Let us go."
3254  
3255  "I am going to see the play through," answered the lad, in a hard
3256  bitter voice.  "I am awfully sorry that I have made you waste an
3257  evening, Harry.  I apologize to you both."
3258  
3259  "My dear Dorian, I should think Miss Vane was ill," interrupted
3260  Hallward.  "We will come some other night."
3261  
3262  "I wish she were ill," he rejoined.  "But she seems to me to be simply
3263  callous and cold.  She has entirely altered.  Last night she was a
3264  great artist.  This evening she is merely a commonplace mediocre
3265  actress."
3266  
3267  "Don't talk like that about any one you love, Dorian.  Love is a more
3268  wonderful thing than art."
3269  
3270  "They are both simply forms of imitation," remarked Lord Henry.  "But
3271  do let us go.  Dorian, you must not stay here any longer.  It is not
3272  good for one's morals to see bad acting.  Besides, I don't suppose you
3273  will want your wife to act, so what does it matter if she plays Juliet
3274  like a wooden doll?  She is very lovely, and if she knows as little
3275  about life as she does about acting, she will be a delightful
3276  experience.  There are only two kinds of people who are really
3277  fascinating--people who know absolutely everything, and people who know
3278  absolutely nothing.  Good heavens, my dear boy, don't look so tragic!
3279  The secret of remaining young is never to have an emotion that is
3280  unbecoming.  Come to the club with Basil and myself.  We will smoke
3281  cigarettes and drink to the beauty of Sibyl Vane.  She is beautiful.
3282  What more can you want?"
3283  
3284  "Go away, Harry," cried the lad.  "I want to be alone.  Basil, you must
3285  go.  Ah! can't you see that my heart is breaking?"  The hot tears came
3286  to his eyes.  His lips trembled, and rushing to the back of the box, he
3287  leaned up against the wall, hiding his face in his hands.
3288  
3289  "Let us go, Basil," said Lord Henry with a strange tenderness in his
3290  voice, and the two young men passed out together.
3291  
3292  A few moments afterwards the footlights flared up and the curtain rose
3293  on the third act.  Dorian Gray went back to his seat.  He looked pale,
3294  and proud, and indifferent.  The play dragged on, and seemed
3295  interminable.  Half of the audience went out, tramping in heavy boots
3296  and laughing.  The whole thing was a _fiasco_.  The last act was played
3297  to almost empty benches.  The curtain went down on a titter and some
3298  groans.
3299  
3300  As soon as it was over, Dorian Gray rushed behind the scenes into the
3301  greenroom.  The girl was standing there alone, with a look of triumph
3302  on her face.  Her eyes were lit with an exquisite fire.  There was a
3303  radiance about her.  Her parted lips were smiling over some secret of
3304  their own.
3305  
3306  When he entered, she looked at him, and an expression of infinite joy
3307  came over her.  "How badly I acted to-night, Dorian!" she cried.
3308  
3309  "Horribly!" he answered, gazing at her in amazement.  "Horribly!  It
3310  was dreadful.  Are you ill?  You have no idea what it was.  You have no
3311  idea what I suffered."
3312  
3313  The girl smiled.  "Dorian," she answered, lingering over his name with
3314  long-drawn music in her voice, as though it were sweeter than honey to
3315  the red petals of her mouth.  "Dorian, you should have understood.  But
3316  you understand now, don't you?"
3317  
3318  "Understand what?" he asked, angrily.
3319  
3320  "Why I was so bad to-night. Why I shall always be bad.  Why I shall
3321  never act well again."
3322  
3323  He shrugged his shoulders.  "You are ill, I suppose.  When you are ill
3324  you shouldn't act.  You make yourself ridiculous.  My friends were
3325  bored.  I was bored."
3326  
3327  She seemed not to listen to him.  She was transfigured with joy.  An
3328  ecstasy of happiness dominated her.
3329  
3330  "Dorian, Dorian," she cried, "before I knew you, acting was the one
3331  reality of my life.  It was only in the theatre that I lived.  I
3332  thought that it was all true.  I was Rosalind one night and Portia the
3333  other.  The joy of Beatrice was my joy, and the sorrows of Cordelia
3334  were mine also.  I believed in everything.  The common people who acted
3335  with me seemed to me to be godlike.  The painted scenes were my world.
3336  I knew nothing but shadows, and I thought them real.  You came--oh, my
3337  beautiful love!--and you freed my soul from prison.  You taught me what
3338  reality really is.  To-night, for the first time in my life, I saw
3339  through the hollowness, the sham, the silliness of the empty pageant in
3340  which I had always played.  To-night, for the first time, I became
3341  conscious that the Romeo was hideous, and old, and painted, that the
3342  moonlight in the orchard was false, that the scenery was vulgar, and
3343  that the words I had to speak were unreal, were not my words, were not
3344  what I wanted to say.  You had brought me something higher, something
3345  of which all art is but a reflection.  You had made me understand what
3346  love really is.  My love!  My love!  Prince Charming!  Prince of life!
3347  I have grown sick of shadows.  You are more to me than all art can ever
3348  be.  What have I to do with the puppets of a play?  When I came on
3349  to-night, I could not understand how it was that everything had gone
3350  from me.  I thought that I was going to be wonderful.  I found that I
3351  could do nothing.  Suddenly it dawned on my soul what it all meant.
3352  The knowledge was exquisite to me.  I heard them hissing, and I smiled.
3353  What could they know of love such as ours?  Take me away, Dorian--take
3354  me away with you, where we can be quite alone.  I hate the stage.  I
3355  might mimic a passion that I do not feel, but I cannot mimic one that
3356  burns me like fire.  Oh, Dorian, Dorian, you understand now what it
3357  signifies?  Even if I could do it, it would be profanation for me to
3358  play at being in love.  You have made me see that."
3359  
3360  He flung himself down on the sofa and turned away his face.  "You have
3361  killed my love," he muttered.
3362  
3363  She looked at him in wonder and laughed.  He made no answer.  She came
3364  across to him, and with her little fingers stroked his hair.  She knelt
3365  down and pressed his hands to her lips.  He drew them away, and a
3366  shudder ran through him.
3367  
3368  Then he leaped up and went to the door.  "Yes," he cried, "you have
3369  killed my love.  You used to stir my imagination.  Now you don't even
3370  stir my curiosity.  You simply produce no effect.  I loved you because
3371  you were marvellous, because you had genius and intellect, because you
3372  realized the dreams of great poets and gave shape and substance to the
3373  shadows of art.  You have thrown it all away.  You are shallow and
3374  stupid.  My God! how mad I was to love you!  What a fool I have been!
3375  You are nothing to me now.  I will never see you again.  I will never
3376  think of you.  I will never mention your name.  You don't know what you
3377  were to me, once.  Why, once ... Oh, I can't bear to think of it!  I
3378  wish I had never laid eyes upon you!  You have spoiled the romance of
3379  my life.  How little you can know of love, if you say it mars your art!
3380  Without your art, you are nothing.  I would have made you famous,
3381  splendid, magnificent.  The world would have worshipped you, and you
3382  would have borne my name.  What are you now?  A third-rate actress with
3383  a pretty face."
3384  
3385  The girl grew white, and trembled.  She clenched her hands together,
3386  and her voice seemed to catch in her throat.  "You are not serious,
3387  Dorian?" she murmured.  "You are acting."
3388  
3389  "Acting!  I leave that to you.  You do it so well," he answered
3390  bitterly.
3391  
3392  She rose from her knees and, with a piteous expression of pain in her
3393  face, came across the room to him.  She put her hand upon his arm and
3394  looked into his eyes.  He thrust her back.  "Don't touch me!" he cried.
3395  
3396  A low moan broke from her, and she flung herself at his feet and lay
3397  there like a trampled flower.  "Dorian, Dorian, don't leave me!" she
3398  whispered.  "I am so sorry I didn't act well.  I was thinking of you
3399  all the time.  But I will try--indeed, I will try.  It came so suddenly
3400  across me, my love for you.  I think I should never have known it if
3401  you had not kissed me--if we had not kissed each other.  Kiss me again,
3402  my love.  Don't go away from me.  I couldn't bear it.  Oh! don't go
3403  away from me.  My brother ... No; never mind.  He didn't mean it.  He
3404  was in jest.... But you, oh! can't you forgive me for to-night? I will
3405  work so hard and try to improve.  Don't be cruel to me, because I love
3406  you better than anything in the world.  After all, it is only once that
3407  I have not pleased you.  But you are quite right, Dorian.  I should
3408  have shown myself more of an artist.  It was foolish of me, and yet I
3409  couldn't help it.  Oh, don't leave me, don't leave me." A fit of
3410  passionate sobbing choked her.  She crouched on the floor like a
3411  wounded thing, and Dorian Gray, with his beautiful eyes, looked down at
3412  her, and his chiselled lips curled in exquisite disdain.  There is
3413  always something ridiculous about the emotions of people whom one has
3414  ceased to love.  Sibyl Vane seemed to him to be absurdly melodramatic.
3415  Her tears and sobs annoyed him.
3416  
3417  "I am going," he said at last in his calm clear voice.  "I don't wish
3418  to be unkind, but I can't see you again.  You have disappointed me."
3419  
3420  She wept silently, and made no answer, but crept nearer.  Her little
3421  hands stretched blindly out, and appeared to be seeking for him.  He
3422  turned on his heel and left the room.  In a few moments he was out of
3423  the theatre.
3424  
3425  Where he went to he hardly knew.  He remembered wandering through dimly
3426  lit streets, past gaunt, black-shadowed archways and evil-looking
3427  houses.  Women with hoarse voices and harsh laughter had called after
3428  him.  Drunkards had reeled by, cursing and chattering to themselves
3429  like monstrous apes.  He had seen grotesque children huddled upon
3430  door-steps, and heard shrieks and oaths from gloomy courts.
3431  
3432  As the dawn was just breaking, he found himself close to Covent Garden.
3433  The darkness lifted, and, flushed with faint fires, the sky hollowed
3434  itself into a perfect pearl.  Huge carts filled with nodding lilies
3435  rumbled slowly down the polished empty street.  The air was heavy with
3436  the perfume of the flowers, and their beauty seemed to bring him an
3437  anodyne for his pain.  He followed into the market and watched the men
3438  unloading their waggons.  A white-smocked carter offered him some
3439  cherries.  He thanked him, wondered why he refused to accept any money
3440  for them, and began to eat them listlessly.  They had been plucked at
3441  midnight, and the coldness of the moon had entered into them.  A long
3442  line of boys carrying crates of striped tulips, and of yellow and red
3443  roses, defiled in front of him, threading their way through the huge,
3444  jade-green piles of vegetables.  Under the portico, with its grey,
3445  sun-bleached pillars, loitered a troop of draggled bareheaded girls,
3446  waiting for the auction to be over.  Others crowded round the swinging
3447  doors of the coffee-house in the piazza.  The heavy cart-horses slipped
3448  and stamped upon the rough stones, shaking their bells and trappings.
3449  Some of the drivers were lying asleep on a pile of sacks.  Iris-necked
3450  and pink-footed, the pigeons ran about picking up seeds.
3451  
3452  After a little while, he hailed a hansom and drove home.  For a few
3453  moments he loitered upon the doorstep, looking round at the silent
3454  square, with its blank, close-shuttered windows and its staring blinds.
3455  The sky was pure opal now, and the roofs of the houses glistened like
3456  silver against it.  From some chimney opposite a thin wreath of smoke
3457  was rising.  It curled, a violet riband, through the nacre-coloured air.
3458  
3459  In the huge gilt Venetian lantern, spoil of some Doge's barge, that
3460  hung from the ceiling of the great, oak-panelled hall of entrance,
3461  lights were still burning from three flickering jets: thin blue petals
3462  of flame they seemed, rimmed with white fire.  He turned them out and,
3463  having thrown his hat and cape on the table, passed through the library
3464  towards the door of his bedroom, a large octagonal chamber on the
3465  ground floor that, in his new-born feeling for luxury, he had just had
3466  decorated for himself and hung with some curious Renaissance tapestries
3467  that had been discovered stored in a disused attic at Selby Royal.  As
3468  he was turning the handle of the door, his eye fell upon the portrait
3469  Basil Hallward had painted of him.  He started back as if in surprise.
3470  Then he went on into his own room, looking somewhat puzzled.  After he
3471  had taken the button-hole out of his coat, he seemed to hesitate.
3472  Finally, he came back, went over to the picture, and examined it.  In
3473  the dim arrested light that struggled through the cream-coloured silk
3474  blinds, the face appeared to him to be a little changed.  The
3475  expression looked different.  One would have said that there was a
3476  touch of cruelty in the mouth.  It was certainly strange.
3477  
3478  He turned round and, walking to the window, drew up the blind.  The
3479  bright dawn flooded the room and swept the fantastic shadows into dusky
3480  corners, where they lay shuddering.  But the strange expression that he
3481  had noticed in the face of the portrait seemed to linger there, to be
3482  more intensified even.  The quivering ardent sunlight showed him the
3483  lines of cruelty round the mouth as clearly as if he had been looking
3484  into a mirror after he had done some dreadful thing.
3485  
3486  He winced and, taking up from the table an oval glass framed in ivory
3487  Cupids, one of Lord Henry's many presents to him, glanced hurriedly
3488  into its polished depths.  No line like that warped his red lips.  What
3489  did it mean?
3490  
3491  He rubbed his eyes, and came close to the picture, and examined it
3492  again.  There were no signs of any change when he looked into the
3493  actual painting, and yet there was no doubt that the whole expression
3494  had altered.  It was not a mere fancy of his own.  The thing was
3495  horribly apparent.
3496  
3497  He threw himself into a chair and began to think.  Suddenly there
3498  flashed across his mind what he had said in Basil Hallward's studio the
3499  day the picture had been finished.  Yes, he remembered it perfectly.
3500  He had uttered a mad wish that he himself might remain young, and the
3501  portrait grow old; that his own beauty might be untarnished, and the
3502  face on the canvas bear the burden of his passions and his sins; that
3503  the painted image might be seared with the lines of suffering and
3504  thought, and that he might keep all the delicate bloom and loveliness
3505  of his then just conscious boyhood.  Surely his wish had not been
3506  fulfilled?  Such things were impossible.  It seemed monstrous even to
3507  think of them.  And, yet, there was the picture before him, with the
3508  touch of cruelty in the mouth.
3509  
3510  Cruelty!  Had he been cruel?  It was the girl's fault, not his.  He had
3511  dreamed of her as a great artist, had given his love to her because he
3512  had thought her great.  Then she had disappointed him.  She had been
3513  shallow and unworthy.  And, yet, a feeling of infinite regret came over
3514  him, as he thought of her lying at his feet sobbing like a little
3515  child.  He remembered with what callousness he had watched her.  Why
3516  had he been made like that?  Why had such a soul been given to him?
3517  But he had suffered also.  During the three terrible hours that the
3518  play had lasted, he had lived centuries of pain, aeon upon aeon of
3519  torture.  His life was well worth hers.  She had marred him for a
3520  moment, if he had wounded her for an age.  Besides, women were better
3521  suited to bear sorrow than men.  They lived on their emotions.  They
3522  only thought of their emotions.  When they took lovers, it was merely
3523  to have some one with whom they could have scenes.  Lord Henry had told
3524  him that, and Lord Henry knew what women were.  Why should he trouble
3525  about Sibyl Vane?  She was nothing to him now.
3526  
3527  But the picture?  What was he to say of that?  It held the secret of
3528  his life, and told his story.  It had taught him to love his own
3529  beauty.  Would it teach him to loathe his own soul?  Would he ever look
3530  at it again?
3531  
3532  No; it was merely an illusion wrought on the troubled senses.  The
3533  horrible night that he had passed had left phantoms behind it.
3534  Suddenly there had fallen upon his brain that tiny scarlet speck that
3535  makes men mad.  The picture had not changed.  It was folly to think so.
3536  
3537  Yet it was watching him, with its beautiful marred face and its cruel
3538  smile.  Its bright hair gleamed in the early sunlight.  Its blue eyes
3539  met his own.  A sense of infinite pity, not for himself, but for the
3540  painted image of himself, came over him.  It had altered already, and
3541  would alter more.  Its gold would wither into grey.  Its red and white
3542  roses would die.  For every sin that he committed, a stain would fleck
3543  and wreck its fairness.  But he would not sin.  The picture, changed or
3544  unchanged, would be to him the visible emblem of conscience.  He would
3545  resist temptation.  He would not see Lord Henry any more--would not, at
3546  any rate, listen to those subtle poisonous theories that in Basil
3547  Hallward's garden had first stirred within him the passion for
3548  impossible things.  He would go back to Sibyl Vane, make her amends,
3549  marry her, try to love her again.  Yes, it was his duty to do so.  She
3550  must have suffered more than he had.  Poor child!  He had been selfish
3551  and cruel to her.  The fascination that she had exercised over him
3552  would return.  They would be happy together.  His life with her would
3553  be beautiful and pure.
3554  
3555  He got up from his chair and drew a large screen right in front of the
3556  portrait, shuddering as he glanced at it.  "How horrible!" he murmured
3557  to himself, and he walked across to the window and opened it.  When he
3558  stepped out on to the grass, he drew a deep breath.  The fresh morning
3559  air seemed to drive away all his sombre passions.  He thought only of
3560  Sibyl.  A faint echo of his love came back to him.  He repeated her
3561  name over and over again.  The birds that were singing in the
3562  dew-drenched garden seemed to be telling the flowers about her.
3563  
3564  
3565  
3566  CHAPTER 8
3567  
3568  It was long past noon when he awoke.  His valet had crept several times
3569  on tiptoe into the room to see if he was stirring, and had wondered
3570  what made his young master sleep so late.  Finally his bell sounded,
3571  and Victor came in softly with a cup of tea, and a pile of letters, on
3572  a small tray of old Sevres china, and drew back the olive-satin
3573  curtains, with their shimmering blue lining, that hung in front of the
3574  three tall windows.
3575  
3576  "Monsieur has well slept this morning," he said, smiling.
3577  
3578  "What o'clock is it, Victor?" asked Dorian Gray drowsily.
3579  
3580  "One hour and a quarter, Monsieur."
3581  
3582  How late it was!  He sat up, and having sipped some tea, turned over
3583  his letters.  One of them was from Lord Henry, and had been brought by
3584  hand that morning.  He hesitated for a moment, and then put it aside.
3585  The others he opened listlessly.  They contained the usual collection
3586  of cards, invitations to dinner, tickets for private views, programmes
3587  of charity concerts, and the like that are showered on fashionable
3588  young men every morning during the season.  There was a rather heavy
3589  bill for a chased silver Louis-Quinze toilet-set that he had not yet
3590  had the courage to send on to his guardians, who were extremely
3591  old-fashioned people and did not realize that we live in an age when
3592  unnecessary things are our only necessities; and there were several
3593  very courteously worded communications from Jermyn Street money-lenders
3594  offering to advance any sum of money at a moment's notice and at the
3595  most reasonable rates of interest.
3596  
3597  After about ten minutes he got up, and throwing on an elaborate
3598  dressing-gown of silk-embroidered cashmere wool, passed into the
3599  onyx-paved bathroom.  The cool water refreshed him after his long
3600  sleep.  He seemed to have forgotten all that he had gone through.  A
3601  dim sense of having taken part in some strange tragedy came to him once
3602  or twice, but there was the unreality of a dream about it.
3603  
3604  As soon as he was dressed, he went into the library and sat down to a
3605  light French breakfast that had been laid out for him on a small round
3606  table close to the open window.  It was an exquisite day.  The warm air
3607  seemed laden with spices.  A bee flew in and buzzed round the
3608  blue-dragon bowl that, filled with sulphur-yellow roses, stood before
3609  him.  He felt perfectly happy.
3610  
3611  Suddenly his eye fell on the screen that he had placed in front of the
3612  portrait, and he started.
3613  
3614  "Too cold for Monsieur?" asked his valet, putting an omelette on the
3615  table.  "I shut the window?"
3616  
3617  Dorian shook his head.  "I am not cold," he murmured.
3618  
3619  Was it all true?  Had the portrait really changed?  Or had it been
3620  simply his own imagination that had made him see a look of evil where
3621  there had been a look of joy?  Surely a painted canvas could not alter?
3622  The thing was absurd.  It would serve as a tale to tell Basil some day.
3623  It would make him smile.
3624  
3625  And, yet, how vivid was his recollection of the whole thing!  First in
3626  the dim twilight, and then in the bright dawn, he had seen the touch of
3627  cruelty round the warped lips.  He almost dreaded his valet leaving the
3628  room.  He knew that when he was alone he would have to examine the
3629  portrait.  He was afraid of certainty.  When the coffee and cigarettes
3630  had been brought and the man turned to go, he felt a wild desire to
3631  tell him to remain.  As the door was closing behind him, he called him
3632  back.  The man stood waiting for his orders.  Dorian looked at him for
3633  a moment.  "I am not at home to any one, Victor," he said with a sigh.
3634  The man bowed and retired.
3635  
3636  Then he rose from the table, lit a cigarette, and flung himself down on
3637  a luxuriously cushioned couch that stood facing the screen.  The screen
3638  was an old one, of gilt Spanish leather, stamped and wrought with a
3639  rather florid Louis-Quatorze pattern.  He scanned it curiously,
3640  wondering if ever before it had concealed the secret of a man's life.
3641  
3642  Should he move it aside, after all?  Why not let it stay there?  What
3643  was the use of knowing? If the thing was true, it was terrible.  If it
3644  was not true, why trouble about it?  But what if, by some fate or
3645  deadlier chance, eyes other than his spied behind and saw the horrible
3646  change?  What should he do if Basil Hallward came and asked to look at
3647  his own picture?  Basil would be sure to do that.  No; the thing had to
3648  be examined, and at once.  Anything would be better than this dreadful
3649  state of doubt.
3650  
3651  He got up and locked both doors.  At least he would be alone when he
3652  looked upon the mask of his shame.  Then he drew the screen aside and
3653  saw himself face to face.  It was perfectly true.  The portrait had
3654  altered.
3655  
3656  As he often remembered afterwards, and always with no small wonder, he
3657  found himself at first gazing at the portrait with a feeling of almost
3658  scientific interest.  That such a change should have taken place was
3659  incredible to him.  And yet it was a fact.  Was there some subtle
3660  affinity between the chemical atoms that shaped themselves into form
3661  and colour on the canvas and the soul that was within him?  Could it be
3662  that what that soul thought, they realized?--that what it dreamed, they
3663  made true?  Or was there some other, more terrible reason?  He
3664  shuddered, and felt afraid, and, going back to the couch, lay there,
3665  gazing at the picture in sickened horror.
3666  
3667  One thing, however, he felt that it had done for him.  It had made him
3668  conscious how unjust, how cruel, he had been to Sibyl Vane.  It was not
3669  too late to make reparation for that.  She could still be his wife.
3670  His unreal and selfish love would yield to some higher influence, would
3671  be transformed into some nobler passion, and the portrait that Basil
3672  Hallward had painted of him would be a guide to him through life, would
3673  be to him what holiness is to some, and conscience to others, and the
3674  fear of God to us all.  There were opiates for remorse, drugs that
3675  could lull the moral sense to sleep.  But here was a visible symbol of
3676  the degradation of sin.  Here was an ever-present sign of the ruin men
3677  brought upon their souls.
3678  
3679  Three o'clock struck, and four, and the half-hour rang its double
3680  chime, but Dorian Gray did not stir.  He was trying to gather up the
3681  scarlet threads of life and to weave them into a pattern; to find his
3682  way through the sanguine labyrinth of passion through which he was
3683  wandering.  He did not know what to do, or what to think.  Finally, he
3684  went over to the table and wrote a passionate letter to the girl he had
3685  loved, imploring her forgiveness and accusing himself of madness.  He
3686  covered page after page with wild words of sorrow and wilder words of
3687  pain.  There is a luxury in self-reproach. When we blame ourselves, we
3688  feel that no one else has a right to blame us.  It is the confession,
3689  not the priest, that gives us absolution.  When Dorian had finished the
3690  letter, he felt that he had been forgiven.
3691  
3692  Suddenly there came a knock to the door, and he heard Lord Henry's
3693  voice outside.  "My dear boy, I must see you.  Let me in at once.  I
3694  can't bear your shutting yourself up like this."
3695  
3696  He made no answer at first, but remained quite still.  The knocking
3697  still continued and grew louder.  Yes, it was better to let Lord Henry
3698  in, and to explain to him the new life he was going to lead, to quarrel
3699  with him if it became necessary to quarrel, to part if parting was
3700  inevitable.  He jumped up, drew the screen hastily across the picture,
3701  and unlocked the door.
3702  
3703  "I am so sorry for it all, Dorian," said Lord Henry as he entered.
3704  "But you must not think too much about it."
3705  
3706  "Do you mean about Sibyl Vane?" asked the lad.
3707  
3708  "Yes, of course," answered Lord Henry, sinking into a chair and slowly
3709  pulling off his yellow gloves.  "It is dreadful, from one point of
3710  view, but it was not your fault.  Tell me, did you go behind and see
3711  her, after the play was over?"
3712  
3713  "Yes."
3714  
3715  "I felt sure you had.  Did you make a scene with her?"
3716  
3717  "I was brutal, Harry--perfectly brutal.  But it is all right now.  I am
3718  not sorry for anything that has happened.  It has taught me to know
3719  myself better."
3720  
3721  "Ah, Dorian, I am so glad you take it in that way!  I was afraid I
3722  would find you plunged in remorse and tearing that nice curly hair of
3723  yours."
3724  
3725  "I have got through all that," said Dorian, shaking his head and
3726  smiling.  "I am perfectly happy now.  I know what conscience is, to
3727  begin with.  It is not what you told me it was.  It is the divinest
3728  thing in us.  Don't sneer at it, Harry, any more--at least not before
3729  me.  I want to be good.  I can't bear the idea of my soul being
3730  hideous."
3731  
3732  "A very charming artistic basis for ethics, Dorian!  I congratulate you
3733  on it.  But how are you going to begin?"
3734  
3735  "By marrying Sibyl Vane."
3736  
3737  "Marrying Sibyl Vane!" cried Lord Henry, standing up and looking at him
3738  in perplexed amazement.  "But, my dear Dorian--"
3739  
3740  "Yes, Harry, I know what you are going to say.  Something dreadful
3741  about marriage.  Don't say it.  Don't ever say things of that kind to
3742  me again.  Two days ago I asked Sibyl to marry me.  I am not going to
3743  break my word to her.  She is to be my wife."
3744  
3745  "Your wife!  Dorian! ... Didn't you get my letter?  I wrote to you this
3746  morning, and sent the note down by my own man."
3747  
3748  "Your letter?  Oh, yes, I remember.  I have not read it yet, Harry.  I
3749  was afraid there might be something in it that I wouldn't like.  You
3750  cut life to pieces with your epigrams."
3751  
3752  "You know nothing then?"
3753  
3754  "What do you mean?"
3755  
3756  Lord Henry walked across the room, and sitting down by Dorian Gray,
3757  took both his hands in his own and held them tightly.  "Dorian," he
3758  said, "my letter--don't be frightened--was to tell you that Sibyl Vane
3759  is dead."
3760  
3761  A cry of pain broke from the lad's lips, and he leaped to his feet,
3762  tearing his hands away from Lord Henry's grasp.  "Dead!  Sibyl dead!
3763  It is not true!  It is a horrible lie!  How dare you say it?"
3764  
3765  "It is quite true, Dorian," said Lord Henry, gravely.  "It is in all
3766  the morning papers.  I wrote down to you to ask you not to see any one
3767  till I came.  There will have to be an inquest, of course, and you must
3768  not be mixed up in it.  Things like that make a man fashionable in
3769  Paris.  But in London people are so prejudiced.  Here, one should never
3770  make one's _debut_ with a scandal.  One should reserve that to give an
3771  interest to one's old age.  I suppose they don't know your name at the
3772  theatre?  If they don't, it is all right.  Did any one see you going
3773  round to her room?  That is an important point."
3774  
3775  Dorian did not answer for a few moments.  He was dazed with horror.
3776  Finally he stammered, in a stifled voice, "Harry, did you say an
3777  inquest?  What did you mean by that?  Did Sibyl--? Oh, Harry, I can't
3778  bear it!  But be quick.  Tell me everything at once."
3779  
3780  "I have no doubt it was not an accident, Dorian, though it must be put
3781  in that way to the public.  It seems that as she was leaving the
3782  theatre with her mother, about half-past twelve or so, she said she had
3783  forgotten something upstairs.  They waited some time for her, but she
3784  did not come down again.  They ultimately found her lying dead on the
3785  floor of her dressing-room. She had swallowed something by mistake,
3786  some dreadful thing they use at theatres.  I don't know what it was,
3787  but it had either prussic acid or white lead in it.  I should fancy it
3788  was prussic acid, as she seems to have died instantaneously."
3789  
3790  "Harry, Harry, it is terrible!" cried the lad.
3791  
3792  "Yes; it is very tragic, of course, but you must not get yourself mixed
3793  up in it.  I see by _The Standard_ that she was seventeen.  I should have
3794  thought she was almost younger than that.  She looked such a child, and
3795  seemed to know so little about acting.  Dorian, you mustn't let this
3796  thing get on your nerves.  You must come and dine with me, and
3797  afterwards we will look in at the opera.  It is a Patti night, and
3798  everybody will be there.  You can come to my sister's box.  She has got
3799  some smart women with her."
3800  
3801  "So I have murdered Sibyl Vane," said Dorian Gray, half to himself,
3802  "murdered her as surely as if I had cut her little throat with a knife.
3803  Yet the roses are not less lovely for all that.  The birds sing just as
3804  happily in my garden.  And to-night I am to dine with you, and then go
3805  on to the opera, and sup somewhere, I suppose, afterwards.  How
3806  extraordinarily dramatic life is!  If I had read all this in a book,
3807  Harry, I think I would have wept over it.  Somehow, now that it has
3808  happened actually, and to me, it seems far too wonderful for tears.
3809  Here is the first passionate love-letter I have ever written in my
3810  life.  Strange, that my first passionate love-letter should have been
3811  addressed to a dead girl.  Can they feel, I wonder, those white silent
3812  people we call the dead?  Sibyl!  Can she feel, or know, or listen?
3813  Oh, Harry, how I loved her once!  It seems years ago to me now.  She
3814  was everything to me.  Then came that dreadful night--was it really
3815  only last night?--when she played so badly, and my heart almost broke.
3816  She explained it all to me.  It was terribly pathetic.  But I was not
3817  moved a bit.  I thought her shallow.  Suddenly something happened that
3818  made me afraid.  I can't tell you what it was, but it was terrible.  I
3819  said I would go back to her.  I felt I had done wrong.  And now she is
3820  dead.  My God!  My God!  Harry, what shall I do?  You don't know the
3821  danger I am in, and there is nothing to keep me straight.  She would
3822  have done that for me.  She had no right to kill herself.  It was
3823  selfish of her."
3824  
3825  "My dear Dorian," answered Lord Henry, taking a cigarette from his case
3826  and producing a gold-latten matchbox, "the only way a woman can ever
3827  reform a man is by boring him so completely that he loses all possible
3828  interest in life.  If you had married this girl, you would have been
3829  wretched.  Of course, you would have treated her kindly.  One can
3830  always be kind to people about whom one cares nothing.  But she would
3831  have soon found out that you were absolutely indifferent to her.  And
3832  when a woman finds that out about her husband, she either becomes
3833  dreadfully dowdy, or wears very smart bonnets that some other woman's
3834  husband has to pay for.  I say nothing about the social mistake, which
3835  would have been abject--which, of course, I would not have allowed--but
3836  I assure you that in any case the whole thing would have been an
3837  absolute failure."
3838  
3839  "I suppose it would," muttered the lad, walking up and down the room
3840  and looking horribly pale.  "But I thought it was my duty.  It is not
3841  my fault that this terrible tragedy has prevented my doing what was
3842  right.  I remember your saying once that there is a fatality about good
3843  resolutions--that they are always made too late.  Mine certainly were."
3844  
3845  "Good resolutions are useless attempts to interfere with scientific
3846  laws.  Their origin is pure vanity.  Their result is absolutely _nil_.
3847  They give us, now and then, some of those luxurious sterile emotions
3848  that have a certain charm for the weak.  That is all that can be said
3849  for them.  They are simply cheques that men draw on a bank where they
3850  have no account."
3851  
3852  "Harry," cried Dorian Gray, coming over and sitting down beside him,
3853  "why is it that I cannot feel this tragedy as much as I want to?  I
3854  don't think I am heartless.  Do you?"
3855  
3856  "You have done too many foolish things during the last fortnight to be
3857  entitled to give yourself that name, Dorian," answered Lord Henry with
3858  his sweet melancholy smile.
3859  
3860  The lad frowned.  "I don't like that explanation, Harry," he rejoined,
3861  "but I am glad you don't think I am heartless.  I am nothing of the
3862  kind.  I know I am not.  And yet I must admit that this thing that has
3863  happened does not affect me as it should.  It seems to me to be simply
3864  like a wonderful ending to a wonderful play.  It has all the terrible
3865  beauty of a Greek tragedy, a tragedy in which I took a great part, but
3866  by which I have not been wounded."
3867  
3868  "It is an interesting question," said Lord Henry, who found an
3869  exquisite pleasure in playing on the lad's unconscious egotism, "an
3870  extremely interesting question.  I fancy that the true explanation is
3871  this:  It often happens that the real tragedies of life occur in such
3872  an inartistic manner that they hurt us by their crude violence, their
3873  absolute incoherence, their absurd want of meaning, their entire lack
3874  of style.  They affect us just as vulgarity affects us.  They give us
3875  an impression of sheer brute force, and we revolt against that.
3876  Sometimes, however, a tragedy that possesses artistic elements of
3877  beauty crosses our lives.  If these elements of beauty are real, the
3878  whole thing simply appeals to our sense of dramatic effect.  Suddenly
3879  we find that we are no longer the actors, but the spectators of the
3880  play.  Or rather we are both.  We watch ourselves, and the mere wonder
3881  of the spectacle enthralls us.  In the present case, what is it that
3882  has really happened?  Some one has killed herself for love of you.  I
3883  wish that I had ever had such an experience.  It would have made me in
3884  love with love for the rest of my life.  The people who have adored
3885  me--there have not been very many, but there have been some--have
3886  always insisted on living on, long after I had ceased to care for them,
3887  or they to care for me.  They have become stout and tedious, and when I
3888  meet them, they go in at once for reminiscences.  That awful memory of
3889  woman!  What a fearful thing it is!  And what an utter intellectual
3890  stagnation it reveals!  One should absorb the colour of life, but one
3891  should never remember its details.  Details are always vulgar."
3892  
3893  "I must sow poppies in my garden," sighed Dorian.
3894  
3895  "There is no necessity," rejoined his companion.  "Life has always
3896  poppies in her hands.  Of course, now and then things linger.  I once
3897  wore nothing but violets all through one season, as a form of artistic
3898  mourning for a romance that would not die.  Ultimately, however, it did
3899  die.  I forget what killed it.  I think it was her proposing to
3900  sacrifice the whole world for me.  That is always a dreadful moment.
3901  It fills one with the terror of eternity.  Well--would you believe
3902  it?--a week ago, at Lady Hampshire's, I found myself seated at dinner
3903  next the lady in question, and she insisted on going over the whole
3904  thing again, and digging up the past, and raking up the future.  I had
3905  buried my romance in a bed of asphodel.  She dragged it out again and
3906  assured me that I had spoiled her life.  I am bound to state that she
3907  ate an enormous dinner, so I did not feel any anxiety.  But what a lack
3908  of taste she showed!  The one charm of the past is that it is the past.
3909  But women never know when the curtain has fallen.  They always want a
3910  sixth act, and as soon as the interest of the play is entirely over,
3911  they propose to continue it.  If they were allowed their own way, every
3912  comedy would have a tragic ending, and every tragedy would culminate in
3913  a farce.  They are charmingly artificial, but they have no sense of
3914  art.  You are more fortunate than I am.  I assure you, Dorian, that not
3915  one of the women I have known would have done for me what Sibyl Vane
3916  did for you.  Ordinary women always console themselves.  Some of them
3917  do it by going in for sentimental colours.  Never trust a woman who
3918  wears mauve, whatever her age may be, or a woman over thirty-five who
3919  is fond of pink ribbons.  It always means that they have a history.
3920  Others find a great consolation in suddenly discovering the good
3921  qualities of their husbands.  They flaunt their conjugal felicity in
3922  one's face, as if it were the most fascinating of sins.  Religion
3923  consoles some.  Its mysteries have all the charm of a flirtation, a
3924  woman once told me, and I can quite understand it.  Besides, nothing
3925  makes one so vain as being told that one is a sinner.  Conscience makes
3926  egotists of us all.  Yes; there is really no end to the consolations
3927  that women find in modern life.  Indeed, I have not mentioned the most
3928  important one."
3929  
3930  "What is that, Harry?" said the lad listlessly.
3931  
3932  "Oh, the obvious consolation.  Taking some one else's admirer when one
3933  loses one's own.  In good society that always whitewashes a woman.  But
3934  really, Dorian, how different Sibyl Vane must have been from all the
3935  women one meets!  There is something to me quite beautiful about her
3936  death.  I am glad I am living in a century when such wonders happen.
3937  They make one believe in the reality of the things we all play with,
3938  such as romance, passion, and love."
3939  
3940  "I was terribly cruel to her.  You forget that."
3941  
3942  "I am afraid that women appreciate cruelty, downright cruelty, more
3943  than anything else.  They have wonderfully primitive instincts.  We
3944  have emancipated them, but they remain slaves looking for their
3945  masters, all the same.  They love being dominated.  I am sure you were
3946  splendid.  I have never seen you really and absolutely angry, but I can
3947  fancy how delightful you looked.  And, after all, you said something to
3948  me the day before yesterday that seemed to me at the time to be merely
3949  fanciful, but that I see now was absolutely true, and it holds the key
3950  to everything."
3951  
3952  "What was that, Harry?"
3953  
3954  "You said to me that Sibyl Vane represented to you all the heroines of
3955  romance--that she was Desdemona one night, and Ophelia the other; that
3956  if she died as Juliet, she came to life as Imogen."
3957  
3958  "She will never come to life again now," muttered the lad, burying his
3959  face in his hands.
3960  
3961  "No, she will never come to life.  She has played her last part.  But
3962  you must think of that lonely death in the tawdry dressing-room simply
3963  as a strange lurid fragment from some Jacobean tragedy, as a wonderful
3964  scene from Webster, or Ford, or Cyril Tourneur.  The girl never really
3965  lived, and so she has never really died.  To you at least she was
3966  always a dream, a phantom that flitted through Shakespeare's plays and
3967  left them lovelier for its presence, a reed through which Shakespeare's
3968  music sounded richer and more full of joy.  The moment she touched
3969  actual life, she marred it, and it marred her, and so she passed away.
3970  Mourn for Ophelia, if you like.  Put ashes on your head because
3971  Cordelia was strangled.  Cry out against Heaven because the daughter of
3972  Brabantio died.  But don't waste your tears over Sibyl Vane.  She was
3973  less real than they are."
3974  
3975  There was a silence.  The evening darkened in the room.  Noiselessly,
3976  and with silver feet, the shadows crept in from the garden.  The
3977  colours faded wearily out of things.
3978  
3979  After some time Dorian Gray looked up.  "You have explained me to
3980  myself, Harry," he murmured with something of a sigh of relief.  "I
3981  felt all that you have said, but somehow I was afraid of it, and I
3982  could not express it to myself.  How well you know me!  But we will not
3983  talk again of what has happened.  It has been a marvellous experience.
3984  That is all.  I wonder if life has still in store for me anything as
3985  marvellous."
3986  
3987  "Life has everything in store for you, Dorian.  There is nothing that
3988  you, with your extraordinary good looks, will not be able to do."
3989  
3990  "But suppose, Harry, I became haggard, and old, and wrinkled?  What
3991  then?"
3992  
3993  "Ah, then," said Lord Henry, rising to go, "then, my dear Dorian, you
3994  would have to fight for your victories.  As it is, they are brought to
3995  you.  No, you must keep your good looks.  We live in an age that reads
3996  too much to be wise, and that thinks too much to be beautiful.  We
3997  cannot spare you.  And now you had better dress and drive down to the
3998  club.  We are rather late, as it is."
3999  
4000  "I think I shall join you at the opera, Harry.  I feel too tired to eat
4001  anything.  What is the number of your sister's box?"
4002  
4003  "Twenty-seven, I believe.  It is on the grand tier.  You will see her
4004  name on the door.  But I am sorry you won't come and dine."
4005  
4006  "I don't feel up to it," said Dorian listlessly.  "But I am awfully
4007  obliged to you for all that you have said to me.  You are certainly my
4008  best friend.  No one has ever understood me as you have."
4009  
4010  "We are only at the beginning of our friendship, Dorian," answered Lord
4011  Henry, shaking him by the hand.  "Good-bye. I shall see you before
4012  nine-thirty, I hope.  Remember, Patti is singing."
4013  
4014  As he closed the door behind him, Dorian Gray touched the bell, and in
4015  a few minutes Victor appeared with the lamps and drew the blinds down.
4016  He waited impatiently for him to go.  The man seemed to take an
4017  interminable time over everything.
4018  
4019  As soon as he had left, he rushed to the screen and drew it back.  No;
4020  there was no further change in the picture.  It had received the news
4021  of Sibyl Vane's death before he had known of it himself.  It was
4022  conscious of the events of life as they occurred.  The vicious cruelty
4023  that marred the fine lines of the mouth had, no doubt, appeared at the
4024  very moment that the girl had drunk the poison, whatever it was.  Or
4025  was it indifferent to results?  Did it merely take cognizance of what
4026  passed within the soul?  He wondered, and hoped that some day he would
4027  see the change taking place before his very eyes, shuddering as he
4028  hoped it.
4029  
4030  Poor Sibyl!  What a romance it had all been!  She had often mimicked
4031  death on the stage.  Then Death himself had touched her and taken her
4032  with him.  How had she played that dreadful last scene?  Had she cursed
4033  him, as she died?  No; she had died for love of him, and love would
4034  always be a sacrament to him now.  She had atoned for everything by the
4035  sacrifice she had made of her life.  He would not think any more of
4036  what she had made him go through, on that horrible night at the
4037  theatre.  When he thought of her, it would be as a wonderful tragic
4038  figure sent on to the world's stage to show the supreme reality of
4039  love.  A wonderful tragic figure?  Tears came to his eyes as he
4040  remembered her childlike look, and winsome fanciful ways, and shy
4041  tremulous grace.  He brushed them away hastily and looked again at the
4042  picture.
4043  
4044  He felt that the time had really come for making his choice.  Or had
4045  his choice already been made?  Yes, life had decided that for
4046  him--life, and his own infinite curiosity about life.  Eternal youth,
4047  infinite passion, pleasures subtle and secret, wild joys and wilder
4048  sins--he was to have all these things.  The portrait was to bear the
4049  burden of his shame: that was all.
4050  
4051  A feeling of pain crept over him as he thought of the desecration that
4052  was in store for the fair face on the canvas.  Once, in boyish mockery
4053  of Narcissus, he had kissed, or feigned to kiss, those painted lips
4054  that now smiled so cruelly at him.  Morning after morning he had sat
4055  before the portrait wondering at its beauty, almost enamoured of it, as
4056  it seemed to him at times.  Was it to alter now with every mood to
4057  which he yielded?  Was it to become a monstrous and loathsome thing, to
4058  be hidden away in a locked room, to be shut out from the sunlight that
4059  had so often touched to brighter gold the waving wonder of its hair?
4060  The pity of it! the pity of it!
4061  
4062  For a moment, he thought of praying that the horrible sympathy that
4063  existed between him and the picture might cease.  It had changed in
4064  answer to a prayer; perhaps in answer to a prayer it might remain
4065  unchanged.  And yet, who, that knew anything about life, would
4066  surrender the chance of remaining always young, however fantastic that
4067  chance might be, or with what fateful consequences it might be fraught?
4068  Besides, was it really under his control?  Had it indeed been prayer
4069  that had produced the substitution?  Might there not be some curious
4070  scientific reason for it all?  If thought could exercise its influence
4071  upon a living organism, might not thought exercise an influence upon
4072  dead and inorganic things?  Nay, without thought or conscious desire,
4073  might not things external to ourselves vibrate in unison with our moods
4074  and passions, atom calling to atom in secret love or strange affinity?
4075  But the reason was of no importance.  He would never again tempt by a
4076  prayer any terrible power.  If the picture was to alter, it was to
4077  alter.  That was all.  Why inquire too closely into it?
4078  
4079  For there would be a real pleasure in watching it.  He would be able to
4080  follow his mind into its secret places.  This portrait would be to him
4081  the most magical of mirrors.  As it had revealed to him his own body,
4082  so it would reveal to him his own soul.  And when winter came upon it,
4083  he would still be standing where spring trembles on the verge of
4084  summer.  When the blood crept from its face, and left behind a pallid
4085  mask of chalk with leaden eyes, he would keep the glamour of boyhood.
4086  Not one blossom of his loveliness would ever fade.  Not one pulse of
4087  his life would ever weaken.  Like the gods of the Greeks, he would be
4088  strong, and fleet, and joyous.  What did it matter what happened to the
4089  coloured image on the canvas?  He would be safe.  That was everything.
4090  
4091  He drew the screen back into its former place in front of the picture,
4092  smiling as he did so, and passed into his bedroom, where his valet was
4093  already waiting for him.  An hour later he was at the opera, and Lord
4094  Henry was leaning over his chair.
4095  
4096  
4097  
4098  CHAPTER 9
4099  
4100  As he was sitting at breakfast next morning, Basil Hallward was shown
4101  into the room.
4102  
4103  "I am so glad I have found you, Dorian," he said gravely.  "I called
4104  last night, and they told me you were at the opera.  Of course, I knew
4105  that was impossible.  But I wish you had left word where you had really
4106  gone to.  I passed a dreadful evening, half afraid that one tragedy
4107  might be followed by another.  I think you might have telegraphed for
4108  me when you heard of it first.  I read of it quite by chance in a late
4109  edition of _The Globe_ that I picked up at the club.  I came here at once
4110  and was miserable at not finding you.  I can't tell you how
4111  heart-broken I am about the whole thing.  I know what you must suffer.
4112  But where were you?  Did you go down and see the girl's mother?  For a
4113  moment I thought of following you there.  They gave the address in the
4114  paper.  Somewhere in the Euston Road, isn't it?  But I was afraid of
4115  intruding upon a sorrow that I could not lighten.  Poor woman!  What a
4116  state she must be in!  And her only child, too!  What did she say about
4117  it all?"
4118  
4119  "My dear Basil, how do I know?" murmured Dorian Gray, sipping some
4120  pale-yellow wine from a delicate, gold-beaded bubble of Venetian glass
4121  and looking dreadfully bored.  "I was at the opera.  You should have
4122  come on there.  I met Lady Gwendolen, Harry's sister, for the first
4123  time.  We were in her box.  She is perfectly charming; and Patti sang
4124  divinely.  Don't talk about horrid subjects.  If one doesn't talk about
4125  a thing, it has never happened.  It is simply expression, as Harry
4126  says, that gives reality to things.  I may mention that she was not the
4127  woman's only child.  There is a son, a charming fellow, I believe.  But
4128  he is not on the stage.  He is a sailor, or something.  And now, tell
4129  me about yourself and what you are painting."
4130  
4131  "You went to the opera?" said Hallward, speaking very slowly and with a
4132  strained touch of pain in his voice.  "You went to the opera while
4133  Sibyl Vane was lying dead in some sordid lodging?  You can talk to me
4134  of other women being charming, and of Patti singing divinely, before
4135  the girl you loved has even the quiet of a grave to sleep in?  Why,
4136  man, there are horrors in store for that little white body of hers!"
4137  
4138  "Stop, Basil!  I won't hear it!" cried Dorian, leaping to his feet.
4139  "You must not tell me about things.  What is done is done.  What is
4140  past is past."
4141  
4142  "You call yesterday the past?"
4143  
4144  "What has the actual lapse of time got to do with it?  It is only
4145  shallow people who require years to get rid of an emotion.  A man who
4146  is master of himself can end a sorrow as easily as he can invent a
4147  pleasure.  I don't want to be at the mercy of my emotions.  I want to
4148  use them, to enjoy them, and to dominate them."
4149  
4150  "Dorian, this is horrible!  Something has changed you completely.  You
4151  look exactly the same wonderful boy who, day after day, used to come
4152  down to my studio to sit for his picture.  But you were simple,
4153  natural, and affectionate then.  You were the most unspoiled creature
4154  in the whole world.  Now, I don't know what has come over you.  You
4155  talk as if you had no heart, no pity in you.  It is all Harry's
4156  influence.  I see that."
4157  
4158  The lad flushed up and, going to the window, looked out for a few
4159  moments on the green, flickering, sun-lashed garden.  "I owe a great
4160  deal to Harry, Basil," he said at last, "more than I owe to you.  You
4161  only taught me to be vain."
4162  
4163  "Well, I am punished for that, Dorian--or shall be some day."
4164  
4165  "I don't know what you mean, Basil," he exclaimed, turning round.  "I
4166  don't know what you want.  What do you want?"
4167  
4168  "I want the Dorian Gray I used to paint," said the artist sadly.
4169  
4170  "Basil," said the lad, going over to him and putting his hand on his
4171  shoulder, "you have come too late.  Yesterday, when I heard that Sibyl
4172  Vane had killed herself--"
4173  
4174  "Killed herself!  Good heavens! is there no doubt about that?" cried
4175  Hallward, looking up at him with an expression of horror.
4176  
4177  "My dear Basil!  Surely you don't think it was a vulgar accident?  Of
4178  course she killed herself."
4179  
4180  The elder man buried his face in his hands.  "How fearful," he
4181  muttered, and a shudder ran through him.
4182  
4183  "No," said Dorian Gray, "there is nothing fearful about it.  It is one
4184  of the great romantic tragedies of the age.  As a rule, people who act
4185  lead the most commonplace lives.  They are good husbands, or faithful
4186  wives, or something tedious.  You know what I mean--middle-class virtue
4187  and all that kind of thing.  How different Sibyl was!  She lived her
4188  finest tragedy.  She was always a heroine.  The last night she
4189  played--the night you saw her--she acted badly because she had known
4190  the reality of love.  When she knew its unreality, she died, as Juliet
4191  might have died.  She passed again into the sphere of art.  There is
4192  something of the martyr about her.  Her death has all the pathetic
4193  uselessness of martyrdom, all its wasted beauty.  But, as I was saying,
4194  you must not think I have not suffered.  If you had come in yesterday
4195  at a particular moment--about half-past five, perhaps, or a quarter to
4196  six--you would have found me in tears.  Even Harry, who was here, who
4197  brought me the news, in fact, had no idea what I was going through.  I
4198  suffered immensely.  Then it passed away.  I cannot repeat an emotion.
4199  No one can, except sentimentalists.  And you are awfully unjust, Basil.
4200  You come down here to console me.  That is charming of you.  You find
4201  me consoled, and you are furious.  How like a sympathetic person!  You
4202  remind me of a story Harry told me about a certain philanthropist who
4203  spent twenty years of his life in trying to get some grievance
4204  redressed, or some unjust law altered--I forget exactly what it was.
4205  Finally he succeeded, and nothing could exceed his disappointment.  He
4206  had absolutely nothing to do, almost died of _ennui_, and became a
4207  confirmed misanthrope.  And besides, my dear old Basil, if you really
4208  want to console me, teach me rather to forget what has happened, or to
4209  see it from a proper artistic point of view.  Was it not Gautier who
4210  used to write about _la consolation des arts_?  I remember picking up a
4211  little vellum-covered book in your studio one day and chancing on that
4212  delightful phrase.  Well, I am not like that young man you told me of
4213  when we were down at Marlow together, the young man who used to say
4214  that yellow satin could console one for all the miseries of life.  I
4215  love beautiful things that one can touch and handle.  Old brocades,
4216  green bronzes, lacquer-work, carved ivories, exquisite surroundings,
4217  luxury, pomp--there is much to be got from all these.  But the artistic
4218  temperament that they create, or at any rate reveal, is still more to
4219  me.  To become the spectator of one's own life, as Harry says, is to
4220  escape the suffering of life.  I know you are surprised at my talking
4221  to you like this.  You have not realized how I have developed.  I was a
4222  schoolboy when you knew me.  I am a man now.  I have new passions, new
4223  thoughts, new ideas.  I am different, but you must not like me less.  I
4224  am changed, but you must always be my friend.  Of course, I am very
4225  fond of Harry.  But I know that you are better than he is.  You are not
4226  stronger--you are too much afraid of life--but you are better.  And how
4227  happy we used to be together!  Don't leave me, Basil, and don't quarrel
4228  with me.  I am what I am.  There is nothing more to be said."
4229  
4230  The painter felt strangely moved.  The lad was infinitely dear to him,
4231  and his personality had been the great turning point in his art.  He
4232  could not bear the idea of reproaching him any more.  After all, his
4233  indifference was probably merely a mood that would pass away.  There
4234  was so much in him that was good, so much in him that was noble.
4235  
4236  "Well, Dorian," he said at length, with a sad smile, "I won't speak to
4237  you again about this horrible thing, after to-day.  I only trust your
4238  name won't be mentioned in connection with it.  The inquest is to take
