4239  place this afternoon.  Have they summoned you?"
4240  
4241  Dorian shook his head, and a look of annoyance passed over his face at
4242  the mention of the word "inquest."  There was something so crude and
4243  vulgar about everything of the kind.  "They don't know my name," he
4244  answered.
4245  
4246  "But surely she did?"
4247  
4248  "Only my Christian name, and that I am quite sure she never mentioned
4249  to any one.  She told me once that they were all rather curious to
4250  learn who I was, and that she invariably told them my name was Prince
4251  Charming.  It was pretty of her.  You must do me a drawing of Sibyl,
4252  Basil.  I should like to have something more of her than the memory of
4253  a few kisses and some broken pathetic words."
4254  
4255  "I will try and do something, Dorian, if it would please you.  But you
4256  must come and sit to me yourself again.  I can't get on without you."
4257  
4258  "I can never sit to you again, Basil.  It is impossible!" he exclaimed,
4259  starting back.
4260  
4261  The painter stared at him.  "My dear boy, what nonsense!" he cried.
4262  "Do you mean to say you don't like what I did of you?  Where is it?
4263  Why have you pulled the screen in front of it?  Let me look at it.  It
4264  is the best thing I have ever done.  Do take the screen away, Dorian.
4265  It is simply disgraceful of your servant hiding my work like that.  I
4266  felt the room looked different as I came in."
4267  
4268  "My servant has nothing to do with it, Basil.  You don't imagine I let
4269  him arrange my room for me?  He settles my flowers for me
4270  sometimes--that is all.  No; I did it myself.  The light was too strong
4271  on the portrait."
4272  
4273  "Too strong!  Surely not, my dear fellow?  It is an admirable place for
4274  it.  Let me see it."  And Hallward walked towards the corner of the
4275  room.
4276  
4277  A cry of terror broke from Dorian Gray's lips, and he rushed between
4278  the painter and the screen.  "Basil," he said, looking very pale, "you
4279  must not look at it.  I don't wish you to."
4280  
4281  "Not look at my own work!  You are not serious.  Why shouldn't I look
4282  at it?" exclaimed Hallward, laughing.
4283  
4284  "If you try to look at it, Basil, on my word of honour I will never
4285  speak to you again as long as I live.  I am quite serious.  I don't
4286  offer any explanation, and you are not to ask for any.  But, remember,
4287  if you touch this screen, everything is over between us."
4288  
4289  Hallward was thunderstruck.  He looked at Dorian Gray in absolute
4290  amazement.  He had never seen him like this before.  The lad was
4291  actually pallid with rage.  His hands were clenched, and the pupils of
4292  his eyes were like disks of blue fire.  He was trembling all over.
4293  
4294  "Dorian!"
4295  
4296  "Don't speak!"
4297  
4298  "But what is the matter?  Of course I won't look at it if you don't
4299  want me to," he said, rather coldly, turning on his heel and going over
4300  towards the window.  "But, really, it seems rather absurd that I
4301  shouldn't see my own work, especially as I am going to exhibit it in
4302  Paris in the autumn.  I shall probably have to give it another coat of
4303  varnish before that, so I must see it some day, and why not to-day?"
4304  
4305  "To exhibit it!  You want to exhibit it?" exclaimed Dorian Gray, a
4306  strange sense of terror creeping over him.  Was the world going to be
4307  shown his secret?  Were people to gape at the mystery of his life?
4308  That was impossible.  Something--he did not know what--had to be done
4309  at once.
4310  
4311  "Yes; I don't suppose you will object to that.  Georges Petit is going
4312  to collect all my best pictures for a special exhibition in the Rue de
4313  Seze, which will open the first week in October.  The portrait will
4314  only be away a month.  I should think you could easily spare it for
4315  that time.  In fact, you are sure to be out of town.  And if you keep
4316  it always behind a screen, you can't care much about it."
4317  
4318  Dorian Gray passed his hand over his forehead.  There were beads of
4319  perspiration there.  He felt that he was on the brink of a horrible
4320  danger.  "You told me a month ago that you would never exhibit it," he
4321  cried.  "Why have you changed your mind?  You people who go in for
4322  being consistent have just as many moods as others have.  The only
4323  difference is that your moods are rather meaningless.  You can't have
4324  forgotten that you assured me most solemnly that nothing in the world
4325  would induce you to send it to any exhibition.  You told Harry exactly
4326  the same thing." He stopped suddenly, and a gleam of light came into
4327  his eyes.  He remembered that Lord Henry had said to him once, half
4328  seriously and half in jest, "If you want to have a strange quarter of
4329  an hour, get Basil to tell you why he won't exhibit your picture.  He
4330  told me why he wouldn't, and it was a revelation to me."  Yes, perhaps
4331  Basil, too, had his secret.  He would ask him and try.
4332  
4333  "Basil," he said, coming over quite close and looking him straight in
4334  the face, "we have each of us a secret.  Let me know yours, and I shall
4335  tell you mine.  What was your reason for refusing to exhibit my
4336  picture?"
4337  
4338  The painter shuddered in spite of himself.  "Dorian, if I told you, you
4339  might like me less than you do, and you would certainly laugh at me.  I
4340  could not bear your doing either of those two things.  If you wish me
4341  never to look at your picture again, I am content.  I have always you
4342  to look at.  If you wish the best work I have ever done to be hidden
4343  from the world, I am satisfied.  Your friendship is dearer to me than
4344  any fame or reputation."
4345  
4346  "No, Basil, you must tell me," insisted Dorian Gray.  "I think I have a
4347  right to know."  His feeling of terror had passed away, and curiosity
4348  had taken its place.  He was determined to find out Basil Hallward's
4349  mystery.
4350  
4351  "Let us sit down, Dorian," said the painter, looking troubled.  "Let us
4352  sit down.  And just answer me one question.  Have you noticed in the
4353  picture something curious?--something that probably at first did not
4354  strike you, but that revealed itself to you suddenly?"
4355  
4356  "Basil!" cried the lad, clutching the arms of his chair with trembling
4357  hands and gazing at him with wild startled eyes.
4358  
4359  "I see you did.  Don't speak.  Wait till you hear what I have to say.
4360  Dorian, from the moment I met you, your personality had the most
4361  extraordinary influence over me.  I was dominated, soul, brain, and
4362  power, by you.  You became to me the visible incarnation of that unseen
4363  ideal whose memory haunts us artists like an exquisite dream.  I
4364  worshipped you.  I grew jealous of every one to whom you spoke.  I
4365  wanted to have you all to myself.  I was only happy when I was with
4366  you.  When you were away from me, you were still present in my art....
4367  Of course, I never let you know anything about this.  It would have
4368  been impossible.  You would not have understood it.  I hardly
4369  understood it myself.  I only knew that I had seen perfection face to
4370  face, and that the world had become wonderful to my eyes--too
4371  wonderful, perhaps, for in such mad worships there is peril, the peril
4372  of losing them, no less than the peril of keeping them....  Weeks and
4373  weeks went on, and I grew more and more absorbed in you.  Then came a
4374  new development.  I had drawn you as Paris in dainty armour, and as
4375  Adonis with huntsman's cloak and polished boar-spear. Crowned with
4376  heavy lotus-blossoms you had sat on the prow of Adrian's barge, gazing
4377  across the green turbid Nile.  You had leaned over the still pool of
4378  some Greek woodland and seen in the water's silent silver the marvel of
4379  your own face.  And it had all been what art should be--unconscious,
4380  ideal, and remote.  One day, a fatal day I sometimes think, I
4381  determined to paint a wonderful portrait of you as you actually are,
4382  not in the costume of dead ages, but in your own dress and in your own
4383  time.  Whether it was the realism of the method, or the mere wonder of
4384  your own personality, thus directly presented to me without mist or
4385  veil, I cannot tell.  But I know that as I worked at it, every flake
4386  and film of colour seemed to me to reveal my secret.  I grew afraid
4387  that others would know of my idolatry.  I felt, Dorian, that I had told
4388  too much, that I had put too much of myself into it.  Then it was that
4389  I resolved never to allow the picture to be exhibited.  You were a
4390  little annoyed; but then you did not realize all that it meant to me.
4391  Harry, to whom I talked about it, laughed at me.  But I did not mind
4392  that.  When the picture was finished, and I sat alone with it, I felt
4393  that I was right.... Well, after a few days the thing left my studio,
4394  and as soon as I had got rid of the intolerable fascination of its
4395  presence, it seemed to me that I had been foolish in imagining that I
4396  had seen anything in it, more than that you were extremely good-looking
4397  and that I could paint.  Even now I cannot help feeling that it is a
4398  mistake to think that the passion one feels in creation is ever really
4399  shown in the work one creates.  Art is always more abstract than we
4400  fancy.  Form and colour tell us of form and colour--that is all.  It
4401  often seems to me that art conceals the artist far more completely than
4402  it ever reveals him.  And so when I got this offer from Paris, I
4403  determined to make your portrait the principal thing in my exhibition.
4404  It never occurred to me that you would refuse.  I see now that you were
4405  right.  The picture cannot be shown.  You must not be angry with me,
4406  Dorian, for what I have told you.  As I said to Harry, once, you are
4407  made to be worshipped."
4408  
4409  Dorian Gray drew a long breath.  The colour came back to his cheeks,
4410  and a smile played about his lips.  The peril was over.  He was safe
4411  for the time.  Yet he could not help feeling infinite pity for the
4412  painter who had just made this strange confession to him, and wondered
4413  if he himself would ever be so dominated by the personality of a
4414  friend.  Lord Henry had the charm of being very dangerous.  But that
4415  was all.  He was too clever and too cynical to be really fond of.
4416  Would there ever be some one who would fill him with a strange
4417  idolatry?  Was that one of the things that life had in store?
4418  
4419  "It is extraordinary to me, Dorian," said Hallward, "that you should
4420  have seen this in the portrait.  Did you really see it?"
4421  
4422  "I saw something in it," he answered, "something that seemed to me very
4423  curious."
4424  
4425  "Well, you don't mind my looking at the thing now?"
4426  
4427  Dorian shook his head.  "You must not ask me that, Basil.  I could not
4428  possibly let you stand in front of that picture."
4429  
4430  "You will some day, surely?"
4431  
4432  "Never."
4433  
4434  "Well, perhaps you are right.  And now good-bye, Dorian.  You have been
4435  the one person in my life who has really influenced my art.  Whatever I
4436  have done that is good, I owe to you.  Ah! you don't know what it cost
4437  me to tell you all that I have told you."
4438  
4439  "My dear Basil," said Dorian, "what have you told me?  Simply that you
4440  felt that you admired me too much.  That is not even a compliment."
4441  
4442  "It was not intended as a compliment.  It was a confession.  Now that I
4443  have made it, something seems to have gone out of me.  Perhaps one
4444  should never put one's worship into words."
4445  
4446  "It was a very disappointing confession."
4447  
4448  "Why, what did you expect, Dorian?  You didn't see anything else in the
4449  picture, did you?  There was nothing else to see?"
4450  
4451  "No; there was nothing else to see.  Why do you ask?  But you mustn't
4452  talk about worship.  It is foolish.  You and I are friends, Basil, and
4453  we must always remain so."
4454  
4455  "You have got Harry," said the painter sadly.
4456  
4457  "Oh, Harry!" cried the lad, with a ripple of laughter.  "Harry spends
4458  his days in saying what is incredible and his evenings in doing what is
4459  improbable.  Just the sort of life I would like to lead.  But still I
4460  don't think I would go to Harry if I were in trouble.  I would sooner
4461  go to you, Basil."
4462  
4463  "You will sit to me again?"
4464  
4465  "Impossible!"
4466  
4467  "You spoil my life as an artist by refusing, Dorian.  No man comes
4468  across two ideal things.  Few come across one."
4469  
4470  "I can't explain it to you, Basil, but I must never sit to you again.
4471  There is something fatal about a portrait.  It has a life of its own.
4472  I will come and have tea with you.  That will be just as pleasant."
4473  
4474  "Pleasanter for you, I am afraid," murmured Hallward regretfully.  "And
4475  now good-bye. I am sorry you won't let me look at the picture once
4476  again.  But that can't be helped.  I quite understand what you feel
4477  about it."
4478  
4479  As he left the room, Dorian Gray smiled to himself.  Poor Basil!  How
4480  little he knew of the true reason!  And how strange it was that,
4481  instead of having been forced to reveal his own secret, he had
4482  succeeded, almost by chance, in wresting a secret from his friend!  How
4483  much that strange confession explained to him!  The painter's absurd
4484  fits of jealousy, his wild devotion, his extravagant panegyrics, his
4485  curious reticences--he understood them all now, and he felt sorry.
4486  There seemed to him to be something tragic in a friendship so coloured
4487  by romance.
4488  
4489  He sighed and touched the bell.  The portrait must be hidden away at
4490  all costs.  He could not run such a risk of discovery again.  It had
4491  been mad of him to have allowed the thing to remain, even for an hour,
4492  in a room to which any of his friends had access.
4493  
4494  
4495  
4496  CHAPTER 10
4497  
4498  When his servant entered, he looked at him steadfastly and wondered if
4499  he had thought of peering behind the screen.  The man was quite
4500  impassive and waited for his orders.  Dorian lit a cigarette and walked
4501  over to the glass and glanced into it.  He could see the reflection of
4502  Victor's face perfectly.  It was like a placid mask of servility.
4503  There was nothing to be afraid of, there.  Yet he thought it best to be
4504  on his guard.
4505  
4506  Speaking very slowly, he told him to tell the house-keeper that he
4507  wanted to see her, and then to go to the frame-maker and ask him to
4508  send two of his men round at once.  It seemed to him that as the man
4509  left the room his eyes wandered in the direction of the screen.  Or was
4510  that merely his own fancy?
4511  
4512  After a few moments, in her black silk dress, with old-fashioned thread
4513  mittens on her wrinkled hands, Mrs. Leaf bustled into the library.  He
4514  asked her for the key of the schoolroom.
4515  
4516  "The old schoolroom, Mr. Dorian?" she exclaimed.  "Why, it is full of
4517  dust.  I must get it arranged and put straight before you go into it.
4518  It is not fit for you to see, sir.  It is not, indeed."
4519  
4520  "I don't want it put straight, Leaf.  I only want the key."
4521  
4522  "Well, sir, you'll be covered with cobwebs if you go into it.  Why, it
4523  hasn't been opened for nearly five years--not since his lordship died."
4524  
4525  He winced at the mention of his grandfather.  He had hateful memories
4526  of him.  "That does not matter," he answered.  "I simply want to see
4527  the place--that is all.  Give me the key."
4528  
4529  "And here is the key, sir," said the old lady, going over the contents
4530  of her bunch with tremulously uncertain hands.  "Here is the key.  I'll
4531  have it off the bunch in a moment.  But you don't think of living up
4532  there, sir, and you so comfortable here?"
4533  
4534  "No, no," he cried petulantly.  "Thank you, Leaf.  That will do."
4535  
4536  She lingered for a few moments, and was garrulous over some detail of
4537  the household.  He sighed and told her to manage things as she thought
4538  best.  She left the room, wreathed in smiles.
4539  
4540  As the door closed, Dorian put the key in his pocket and looked round
4541  the room.  His eye fell on a large, purple satin coverlet heavily
4542  embroidered with gold, a splendid piece of late seventeenth-century
4543  Venetian work that his grandfather had found in a convent near Bologna.
4544  Yes, that would serve to wrap the dreadful thing in.  It had perhaps
4545  served often as a pall for the dead.  Now it was to hide something that
4546  had a corruption of its own, worse than the corruption of death
4547  itself--something that would breed horrors and yet would never die.
4548  What the worm was to the corpse, his sins would be to the painted image
4549  on the canvas.  They would mar its beauty and eat away its grace.  They
4550  would defile it and make it shameful.  And yet the thing would still
4551  live on.  It would be always alive.
4552  
4553  He shuddered, and for a moment he regretted that he had not told Basil
4554  the true reason why he had wished to hide the picture away.  Basil
4555  would have helped him to resist Lord Henry's influence, and the still
4556  more poisonous influences that came from his own temperament.  The love
4557  that he bore him--for it was really love--had nothing in it that was
4558  not noble and intellectual.  It was not that mere physical admiration
4559  of beauty that is born of the senses and that dies when the senses
4560  tire.  It was such love as Michelangelo had known, and Montaigne, and
4561  Winckelmann, and Shakespeare himself.  Yes, Basil could have saved him.
4562  But it was too late now.  The past could always be annihilated.
4563  Regret, denial, or forgetfulness could do that.  But the future was
4564  inevitable.  There were passions in him that would find their terrible
4565  outlet, dreams that would make the shadow of their evil real.
4566  
4567  He took up from the couch the great purple-and-gold texture that
4568  covered it, and, holding it in his hands, passed behind the screen.
4569  Was the face on the canvas viler than before?  It seemed to him that it
4570  was unchanged, and yet his loathing of it was intensified.  Gold hair,
4571  blue eyes, and rose-red lips--they all were there.  It was simply the
4572  expression that had altered.  That was horrible in its cruelty.
4573  Compared to what he saw in it of censure or rebuke, how shallow Basil's
4574  reproaches about Sibyl Vane had been!--how shallow, and of what little
4575  account!  His own soul was looking out at him from the canvas and
4576  calling him to judgement.  A look of pain came across him, and he flung
4577  the rich pall over the picture.  As he did so, a knock came to the
4578  door.  He passed out as his servant entered.
4579  
4580  "The persons are here, Monsieur."
4581  
4582  He felt that the man must be got rid of at once.  He must not be
4583  allowed to know where the picture was being taken to.  There was
4584  something sly about him, and he had thoughtful, treacherous eyes.
4585  Sitting down at the writing-table he scribbled a note to Lord Henry,
4586  asking him to send him round something to read and reminding him that
4587  they were to meet at eight-fifteen that evening.
4588  
4589  "Wait for an answer," he said, handing it to him, "and show the men in
4590  here."
4591  
4592  In two or three minutes there was another knock, and Mr. Hubbard
4593  himself, the celebrated frame-maker of South Audley Street, came in
4594  with a somewhat rough-looking young assistant.  Mr. Hubbard was a
4595  florid, red-whiskered little man, whose admiration for art was
4596  considerably tempered by the inveterate impecuniosity of most of the
4597  artists who dealt with him.  As a rule, he never left his shop.  He
4598  waited for people to come to him.  But he always made an exception in
4599  favour of Dorian Gray.  There was something about Dorian that charmed
4600  everybody.  It was a pleasure even to see him.
4601  
4602  "What can I do for you, Mr. Gray?" he said, rubbing his fat freckled
4603  hands.  "I thought I would do myself the honour of coming round in
4604  person.  I have just got a beauty of a frame, sir.  Picked it up at a
4605  sale.  Old Florentine.  Came from Fonthill, I believe.  Admirably
4606  suited for a religious subject, Mr. Gray."
4607  
4608  "I am so sorry you have given yourself the trouble of coming round, Mr.
4609  Hubbard.  I shall certainly drop in and look at the frame--though I
4610  don't go in much at present for religious art--but to-day I only want a
4611  picture carried to the top of the house for me.  It is rather heavy, so
4612  I thought I would ask you to lend me a couple of your men."
4613  
4614  "No trouble at all, Mr. Gray.  I am delighted to be of any service to
4615  you.  Which is the work of art, sir?"
4616  
4617  "This," replied Dorian, moving the screen back.  "Can you move it,
4618  covering and all, just as it is?  I don't want it to get scratched
4619  going upstairs."
4620  
4621  "There will be no difficulty, sir," said the genial frame-maker,
4622  beginning, with the aid of his assistant, to unhook the picture from
4623  the long brass chains by which it was suspended.  "And, now, where
4624  shall we carry it to, Mr. Gray?"
4625  
4626  "I will show you the way, Mr. Hubbard, if you will kindly follow me.
4627  Or perhaps you had better go in front.  I am afraid it is right at the
4628  top of the house.  We will go up by the front staircase, as it is
4629  wider."
4630  
4631  He held the door open for them, and they passed out into the hall and
4632  began the ascent.  The elaborate character of the frame had made the
4633  picture extremely bulky, and now and then, in spite of the obsequious
4634  protests of Mr. Hubbard, who had the true tradesman's spirited dislike
4635  of seeing a gentleman doing anything useful, Dorian put his hand to it
4636  so as to help them.
4637  
4638  "Something of a load to carry, sir," gasped the little man when they
4639  reached the top landing.  And he wiped his shiny forehead.
4640  
4641  "I am afraid it is rather heavy," murmured Dorian as he unlocked the
4642  door that opened into the room that was to keep for him the curious
4643  secret of his life and hide his soul from the eyes of men.
4644  
4645  He had not entered the place for more than four years--not, indeed,
4646  since he had used it first as a play-room when he was a child, and then
4647  as a study when he grew somewhat older.  It was a large,
4648  well-proportioned room, which had been specially built by the last Lord
4649  Kelso for the use of the little grandson whom, for his strange likeness
4650  to his mother, and also for other reasons, he had always hated and
4651  desired to keep at a distance.  It appeared to Dorian to have but
4652  little changed.  There was the huge Italian _cassone_, with its
4653  fantastically painted panels and its tarnished gilt mouldings, in which
4654  he had so often hidden himself as a boy.  There the satinwood book-case
4655  filled with his dog-eared schoolbooks.  On the wall behind it was
4656  hanging the same ragged Flemish tapestry where a faded king and queen
4657  were playing chess in a garden, while a company of hawkers rode by,
4658  carrying hooded birds on their gauntleted wrists.  How well he
4659  remembered it all!  Every moment of his lonely childhood came back to
4660  him as he looked round.  He recalled the stainless purity of his boyish
4661  life, and it seemed horrible to him that it was here the fatal portrait
4662  was to be hidden away.  How little he had thought, in those dead days,
4663  of all that was in store for him!
4664  
4665  But there was no other place in the house so secure from prying eyes as
4666  this.  He had the key, and no one else could enter it.  Beneath its
4667  purple pall, the face painted on the canvas could grow bestial, sodden,
4668  and unclean.  What did it matter?  No one could see it.  He himself
4669  would not see it.  Why should he watch the hideous corruption of his
4670  soul?  He kept his youth--that was enough.  And, besides, might not
4671  his nature grow finer, after all?  There was no reason that the future
4672  should be so full of shame.  Some love might come across his life, and
4673  purify him, and shield him from those sins that seemed to be already
4674  stirring in spirit and in flesh--those curious unpictured sins whose
4675  very mystery lent them their subtlety and their charm.  Perhaps, some
4676  day, the cruel look would have passed away from the scarlet sensitive
4677  mouth, and he might show to the world Basil Hallward's masterpiece.
4678  
4679  No; that was impossible.  Hour by hour, and week by week, the thing
4680  upon the canvas was growing old.  It might escape the hideousness of
4681  sin, but the hideousness of age was in store for it.  The cheeks would
4682  become hollow or flaccid.  Yellow crow's feet would creep round the
4683  fading eyes and make them horrible.  The hair would lose its
4684  brightness, the mouth would gape or droop, would be foolish or gross,
4685  as the mouths of old men are.  There would be the wrinkled throat, the
4686  cold, blue-veined hands, the twisted body, that he remembered in the
4687  grandfather who had been so stern to him in his boyhood.  The picture
4688  had to be concealed.  There was no help for it.
4689  
4690  "Bring it in, Mr. Hubbard, please," he said, wearily, turning round.
4691  "I am sorry I kept you so long.  I was thinking of something else."
4692  
4693  "Always glad to have a rest, Mr. Gray," answered the frame-maker, who
4694  was still gasping for breath.  "Where shall we put it, sir?"
4695  
4696  "Oh, anywhere.  Here:  this will do.  I don't want to have it hung up.
4697  Just lean it against the wall.  Thanks."
4698  
4699  "Might one look at the work of art, sir?"
4700  
4701  Dorian started.  "It would not interest you, Mr. Hubbard," he said,
4702  keeping his eye on the man.  He felt ready to leap upon him and fling
4703  him to the ground if he dared to lift the gorgeous hanging that
4704  concealed the secret of his life.  "I shan't trouble you any more now.
4705  I am much obliged for your kindness in coming round."
4706  
4707  "Not at all, not at all, Mr. Gray.  Ever ready to do anything for you,
4708  sir." And Mr. Hubbard tramped downstairs, followed by the assistant,
4709  who glanced back at Dorian with a look of shy wonder in his rough
4710  uncomely face.  He had never seen any one so marvellous.
4711  
4712  When the sound of their footsteps had died away, Dorian locked the door
4713  and put the key in his pocket.  He felt safe now.  No one would ever
4714  look upon the horrible thing.  No eye but his would ever see his shame.
4715  
4716  On reaching the library, he found that it was just after five o'clock
4717  and that the tea had been already brought up.  On a little table of
4718  dark perfumed wood thickly incrusted with nacre, a present from Lady
4719  Radley, his guardian's wife, a pretty professional invalid who had
4720  spent the preceding winter in Cairo, was lying a note from Lord Henry,
4721  and beside it was a book bound in yellow paper, the cover slightly torn
4722  and the edges soiled.  A copy of the third edition of _The St. James's
4723  Gazette_ had been placed on the tea-tray. It was evident that Victor had
4724  returned.  He wondered if he had met the men in the hall as they were
4725  leaving the house and had wormed out of them what they had been doing.
4726  He would be sure to miss the picture--had no doubt missed it already,
4727  while he had been laying the tea-things. The screen had not been set
4728  back, and a blank space was visible on the wall.  Perhaps some night he
4729  might find him creeping upstairs and trying to force the door of the
4730  room.  It was a horrible thing to have a spy in one's house.  He had
4731  heard of rich men who had been blackmailed all their lives by some
4732  servant who had read a letter, or overheard a conversation, or picked
4733  up a card with an address, or found beneath a pillow a withered flower
4734  or a shred of crumpled lace.
4735  
4736  He sighed, and having poured himself out some tea, opened Lord Henry's
4737  note.  It was simply to say that he sent him round the evening paper,
4738  and a book that might interest him, and that he would be at the club at
4739  eight-fifteen. He opened _The St. James's_ languidly, and looked through
4740  it.  A red pencil-mark on the fifth page caught his eye.  It drew
4741  attention to the following paragraph:
4742  
4743  
4744  INQUEST ON AN ACTRESS.--An inquest was held this morning at the Bell
4745  Tavern, Hoxton Road, by Mr. Danby, the District Coroner, on the body of
4746  Sibyl Vane, a young actress recently engaged at the Royal Theatre,
4747  Holborn.  A verdict of death by misadventure was returned.
4748  Considerable sympathy was expressed for the mother of the deceased, who
4749  was greatly affected during the giving of her own evidence, and that of
4750  Dr. Birrell, who had made the post-mortem examination of the deceased.
4751  
4752  
4753  He frowned, and tearing the paper in two, went across the room and
4754  flung the pieces away.  How ugly it all was!  And how horribly real
4755  ugliness made things!  He felt a little annoyed with Lord Henry for
4756  having sent him the report.  And it was certainly stupid of him to have
4757  marked it with red pencil.  Victor might have read it.  The man knew
4758  more than enough English for that.
4759  
4760  Perhaps he had read it and had begun to suspect something.  And, yet,
4761  what did it matter?  What had Dorian Gray to do with Sibyl Vane's
4762  death?  There was nothing to fear.  Dorian Gray had not killed her.
4763  
4764  His eye fell on the yellow book that Lord Henry had sent him.  What was
4765  it, he wondered.  He went towards the little, pearl-coloured octagonal
4766  stand that had always looked to him like the work of some strange
4767  Egyptian bees that wrought in silver, and taking up the volume, flung
4768  himself into an arm-chair and began to turn over the leaves.  After a
4769  few minutes he became absorbed.  It was the strangest book that he had
4770  ever read.  It seemed to him that in exquisite raiment, and to the
4771  delicate sound of flutes, the sins of the world were passing in dumb
4772  show before him.  Things that he had dimly dreamed of were suddenly
4773  made real to him.  Things of which he had never dreamed were gradually
4774  revealed.
4775  
4776  It was a novel without a plot and with only one character, being,
4777  indeed, simply a psychological study of a certain young Parisian who
4778  spent his life trying to realize in the nineteenth century all the
4779  passions and modes of thought that belonged to every century except his
4780  own, and to sum up, as it were, in himself the various moods through
4781  which the world-spirit had ever passed, loving for their mere
4782  artificiality those renunciations that men have unwisely called virtue,
4783  as much as those natural rebellions that wise men still call sin.  The
4784  style in which it was written was that curious jewelled style, vivid
4785  and obscure at once, full of _argot_ and of archaisms, of technical
4786  expressions and of elaborate paraphrases, that characterizes the work
4787  of some of the finest artists of the French school of _Symbolistes_.
4788  There were in it metaphors as monstrous as orchids and as subtle in
4789  colour.  The life of the senses was described in the terms of mystical
4790  philosophy.  One hardly knew at times whether one was reading the
4791  spiritual ecstasies of some mediaeval saint or the morbid confessions
4792  of a modern sinner.  It was a poisonous book.  The heavy odour of
4793  incense seemed to cling about its pages and to trouble the brain.  The
4794  mere cadence of the sentences, the subtle monotony of their music, so
4795  full as it was of complex refrains and movements elaborately repeated,
4796  produced in the mind of the lad, as he passed from chapter to chapter,
4797  a form of reverie, a malady of dreaming, that made him unconscious of
4798  the falling day and creeping shadows.
4799  
4800  Cloudless, and pierced by one solitary star, a copper-green sky gleamed
4801  through the windows.  He read on by its wan light till he could read no
4802  more.  Then, after his valet had reminded him several times of the
4803  lateness of the hour, he got up, and going into the next room, placed
4804  the book on the little Florentine table that always stood at his
4805  bedside and began to dress for dinner.
4806  
4807  It was almost nine o'clock before he reached the club, where he found
4808  Lord Henry sitting alone, in the morning-room, looking very much bored.
4809  
4810  "I am so sorry, Harry," he cried, "but really it is entirely your
4811  fault.  That book you sent me so fascinated me that I forgot how the
4812  time was going."
4813  
4814  "Yes, I thought you would like it," replied his host, rising from his
4815  chair.
4816  
4817  "I didn't say I liked it, Harry.  I said it fascinated me.  There is a
4818  great difference."
4819  
4820  "Ah, you have discovered that?" murmured Lord Henry.  And they passed
4821  into the dining-room.
4822  
4823  
4824  
4825  CHAPTER 11
4826  
4827  For years, Dorian Gray could not free himself from the influence of
4828  this book.  Or perhaps it would be more accurate to say that he never
4829  sought to free himself from it.  He procured from Paris no less than
4830  nine large-paper copies of the first edition, and had them bound in
4831  different colours, so that they might suit his various moods and the
4832  changing fancies of a nature over which he seemed, at times, to have
4833  almost entirely lost control.  The hero, the wonderful young Parisian
4834  in whom the romantic and the scientific temperaments were so strangely
4835  blended, became to him a kind of prefiguring type of himself.  And,
4836  indeed, the whole book seemed to him to contain the story of his own
4837  life, written before he had lived it.
4838  
4839  In one point he was more fortunate than the novel's fantastic hero.  He
4840  never knew--never, indeed, had any cause to know--that somewhat
4841  grotesque dread of mirrors, and polished metal surfaces, and still
4842  water which came upon the young Parisian so early in his life, and was
4843  occasioned by the sudden decay of a beau that had once, apparently,
4844  been so remarkable.  It was with an almost cruel joy--and perhaps in
4845  nearly every joy, as certainly in every pleasure, cruelty has its
4846  place--that he used to read the latter part of the book, with its
4847  really tragic, if somewhat overemphasized, account of the sorrow and
4848  despair of one who had himself lost what in others, and the world, he
4849  had most dearly valued.
4850  
4851  For the wonderful beauty that had so fascinated Basil Hallward, and
4852  many others besides him, seemed never to leave him.  Even those who had
4853  heard the most evil things against him--and from time to time strange
4854  rumours about his mode of life crept through London and became the
4855  chatter of the clubs--could not believe anything to his dishonour when
4856  they saw him.  He had always the look of one who had kept himself
4857  unspotted from the world.  Men who talked grossly became silent when
4858  Dorian Gray entered the room.  There was something in the purity of his
4859  face that rebuked them.  His mere presence seemed to recall to them the
4860  memory of the innocence that they had tarnished.  They wondered how one
4861  so charming and graceful as he was could have escaped the stain of an
4862  age that was at once sordid and sensual.
4863  
4864  Often, on returning home from one of those mysterious and prolonged
4865  absences that gave rise to such strange conjecture among those who were
4866  his friends, or thought that they were so, he himself would creep
4867  upstairs to the locked room, open the door with the key that never left
4868  him now, and stand, with a mirror, in front of the portrait that Basil
4869  Hallward had painted of him, looking now at the evil and aging face on
4870  the canvas, and now at the fair young face that laughed back at him
4871  from the polished glass.  The very sharpness of the contrast used to
4872  quicken his sense of pleasure.  He grew more and more enamoured of his
4873  own beauty, more and more interested in the corruption of his own soul.
4874  He would examine with minute care, and sometimes with a monstrous and
4875  terrible delight, the hideous lines that seared the wrinkling forehead
4876  or crawled around the heavy sensual mouth, wondering sometimes which
4877  were the more horrible, the signs of sin or the signs of age.  He would
4878  place his white hands beside the coarse bloated hands of the picture,
4879  and smile.  He mocked the misshapen body and the failing limbs.
4880  
4881  There were moments, indeed, at night, when, lying sleepless in his own
4882  delicately scented chamber, or in the sordid room of the little
4883  ill-famed tavern near the docks which, under an assumed name and in
4884  disguise, it was his habit to frequent, he would think of the ruin he
4885  had brought upon his soul with a pity that was all the more poignant
4886  because it was purely selfish.  But moments such as these were rare.
4887  That curiosity about life which Lord Henry had first stirred in him, as
4888  they sat together in the garden of their friend, seemed to increase
4889  with gratification.  The more he knew, the more he desired to know.  He
4890  had mad hungers that grew more ravenous as he fed them.
4891  
4892  Yet he was not really reckless, at any rate in his relations to
4893  society.  Once or twice every month during the winter, and on each
4894  Wednesday evening while the season lasted, he would throw open to the
4895  world his beautiful house and have the most celebrated musicians of the
4896  day to charm his guests with the wonders of their art.  His little
4897  dinners, in the settling of which Lord Henry always assisted him, were
4898  noted as much for the careful selection and placing of those invited,
4899  as for the exquisite taste shown in the decoration of the table, with
4900  its subtle symphonic arrangements of exotic flowers, and embroidered
4901  cloths, and antique plate of gold and silver.  Indeed, there were many,
4902  especially among the very young men, who saw, or fancied that they saw,
4903  in Dorian Gray the true realization of a type of which they had often
4904  dreamed in Eton or Oxford days, a type that was to combine something of
4905  the real culture of the scholar with all the grace and distinction and
4906  perfect manner of a citizen of the world.  To them he seemed to be of
4907  the company of those whom Dante describes as having sought to "make
4908  themselves perfect by the worship of beauty."  Like Gautier, he was one
4909  for whom "the visible world existed."
4910  
4911  And, certainly, to him life itself was the first, the greatest, of the
4912  arts, and for it all the other arts seemed to be but a preparation.
4913  Fashion, by which what is really fantastic becomes for a moment
4914  universal, and dandyism, which, in its own way, is an attempt to assert
4915  the absolute modernity of beauty, had, of course, their fascination for
4916  him.  His mode of dressing, and the particular styles that from time to
4917  time he affected, had their marked influence on the young exquisites of
4918  the Mayfair balls and Pall Mall club windows, who copied him in
4919  everything that he did, and tried to reproduce the accidental charm of
4920  his graceful, though to him only half-serious, fopperies.
4921  
4922  For, while he was but too ready to accept the position that was almost
4923  immediately offered to him on his coming of age, and found, indeed, a
4924  subtle pleasure in the thought that he might really become to the
4925  London of his own day what to imperial Neronian Rome the author of the
4926  Satyricon once had been, yet in his inmost heart he desired to be
4927  something more than a mere _arbiter elegantiarum_, to be consulted on the
4928  wearing of a jewel, or the knotting of a necktie, or the conduct of a
4929  cane.  He sought to elaborate some new scheme of life that would have
4930  its reasoned philosophy and its ordered principles, and find in the
4931  spiritualizing of the senses its highest realization.
4932  
4933  The worship of the senses has often, and with much justice, been
4934  decried, men feeling a natural instinct of terror about passions and
4935  sensations that seem stronger than themselves, and that they are
4936  conscious of sharing with the less highly organized forms of existence.
4937  But it appeared to Dorian Gray that the true nature of the senses had
4938  never been understood, and that they had remained savage and animal
4939  merely because the world had sought to starve them into submission or
4940  to kill them by pain, instead of aiming at making them elements of a
4941  new spirituality, of which a fine instinct for beauty was to be the
4942  dominant characteristic.  As he looked back upon man moving through
4943  history, he was haunted by a feeling of loss.  So much had been
4944  surrendered! and to such little purpose!  There had been mad wilful
4945  rejections, monstrous forms of self-torture and self-denial, whose
4946  origin was fear and whose result was a degradation infinitely more
4947  terrible than that fancied degradation from which, in their ignorance,
4948  they had sought to escape; Nature, in her wonderful irony, driving out
4949  the anchorite to feed with the wild animals of the desert and giving to
4950  the hermit the beasts of the field as his companions.
4951  
4952  Yes:  there was to be, as Lord Henry had prophesied, a new Hedonism
4953  that was to recreate life and to save it from that harsh uncomely
4954  puritanism that is having, in our own day, its curious revival.  It was
4955  to have its service of the intellect, certainly, yet it was never to
4956  accept any theory or system that would involve the sacrifice of any
4957  mode of passionate experience.  Its aim, indeed, was to be experience
4958  itself, and not the fruits of experience, sweet or bitter as they might
4959  be.  Of the asceticism that deadens the senses, as of the vulgar
4960  profligacy that dulls them, it was to know nothing.  But it was to
4961  teach man to concentrate himself upon the moments of a life that is
4962  itself but a moment.
4963  
4964  There are few of us who have not sometimes wakened before dawn, either
4965  after one of those dreamless nights that make us almost enamoured of
4966  death, or one of those nights of horror and misshapen joy, when through
4967  the chambers of the brain sweep phantoms more terrible than reality
4968  itself, and instinct with that vivid life that lurks in all grotesques,
4969  and that lends to Gothic art its enduring vitality, this art being, one
4970  might fancy, especially the art of those whose minds have been troubled
4971  with the malady of reverie.  Gradually white fingers creep through the
4972  curtains, and they appear to tremble.  In black fantastic shapes, dumb
4973  shadows crawl into the corners of the room and crouch there.  Outside,
4974  there is the stirring of birds among the leaves, or the sound of men
4975  going forth to their work, or the sigh and sob of the wind coming down
4976  from the hills and wandering round the silent house, as though it
4977  feared to wake the sleepers and yet must needs call forth sleep from
4978  her purple cave.  Veil after veil of thin dusky gauze is lifted, and by
4979  degrees the forms and colours of things are restored to them, and we
4980  watch the dawn remaking the world in its antique pattern.  The wan
4981  mirrors get back their mimic life.  The flameless tapers stand where we
4982  had left them, and beside them lies the half-cut book that we had been
4983  studying, or the wired flower that we had worn at the ball, or the
4984  letter that we had been afraid to read, or that we had read too often.
4985  Nothing seems to us changed.  Out of the unreal shadows of the night
4986  comes back the real life that we had known.  We have to resume it where
4987  we had left off, and there steals over us a terrible sense of the
4988  necessity for the continuance of energy in the same wearisome round of
4989  stereotyped habits, or a wild longing, it may be, that our eyelids
4990  might open some morning upon a world that had been refashioned anew in
4991  the darkness for our pleasure, a world in which things would have fresh
4992  shapes and colours, and be changed, or have other secrets, a world in
4993  which the past would have little or no place, or survive, at any rate,
4994  in no conscious form of obligation or regret, the remembrance even of
4995  joy having its bitterness and the memories of pleasure their pain.
4996  
4997  It was the creation of such worlds as these that seemed to Dorian Gray
4998  to be the true object, or amongst the true objects, of life; and in his
4999  search for sensations that would be at once new and delightful, and
5000  possess that element of strangeness that is so essential to romance, he
5001  would often adopt certain modes of thought that he knew to be really
5002  alien to his nature, abandon himself to their subtle influences, and
5003  then, having, as it were, caught their colour and satisfied his
5004  intellectual curiosity, leave them with that curious indifference that
5005  is not incompatible with a real ardour of temperament, and that,
5006  indeed, according to certain modern psychologists, is often a condition
5007  of it.
5008  
5009  It was rumoured of him once that he was about to join the Roman
5010  Catholic communion, and certainly the Roman ritual had always a great
5011  attraction for him.  The daily sacrifice, more awful really than all
5012  the sacrifices of the antique world, stirred him as much by its superb
5013  rejection of the evidence of the senses as by the primitive simplicity
5014  of its elements and the eternal pathos of the human tragedy that it
5015  sought to symbolize.  He loved to kneel down on the cold marble
5016  pavement and watch the priest, in his stiff flowered dalmatic, slowly
5017  and with white hands moving aside the veil of the tabernacle, or
5018  raising aloft the jewelled, lantern-shaped monstrance with that pallid
5019  wafer that at times, one would fain think, is indeed the "_panis
5020  caelestis_," the bread of angels, or, robed in the garments of the
5021  Passion of Christ, breaking the Host into the chalice and smiting his
5022  breast for his sins.  The fuming censers that the grave boys, in their
5023  lace and scarlet, tossed into the air like great gilt flowers had their
5024  subtle fascination for him.  As he passed out, he used to look with
5025  wonder at the black confessionals and long to sit in the dim shadow of
5026  one of them and listen to men and women whispering through the worn
5027  grating the true story of their lives.
5028  
5029  But he never fell into the error of arresting his intellectual
5030  development by any formal acceptance of creed or system, or of
5031  mistaking, for a house in which to live, an inn that is but suitable
5032  for the sojourn of a night, or for a few hours of a night in which
5033  there are no stars and the moon is in travail.  Mysticism, with its
5034  marvellous power of making common things strange to us, and the subtle
5035  antinomianism that always seems to accompany it, moved him for a
5036  season; and for a season he inclined to the materialistic doctrines of
5037  the _Darwinismus_ movement in Germany, and found a curious pleasure in
5038  tracing the thoughts and passions of men to some pearly cell in the
5039  brain, or some white nerve in the body, delighting in the conception of
5040  the absolute dependence of the spirit on certain physical conditions,
5041  morbid or healthy, normal or diseased.  Yet, as has been said of him
5042  before, no theory of life seemed to him to be of any importance
5043  compared with life itself.  He felt keenly conscious of how barren all
5044  intellectual speculation is when separated from action and experiment.
5045  He knew that the senses, no less than the soul, have their spiritual
5046  mysteries to reveal.
5047  
5048  And so he would now study perfumes and the secrets of their
5049  manufacture, distilling heavily scented oils and burning odorous gums
5050  from the East.  He saw that there was no mood of the mind that had not
5051  its counterpart in the sensuous life, and set himself to discover their
5052  true relations, wondering what there was in frankincense that made one
5053  mystical, and in ambergris that stirred one's passions, and in violets
5054  that woke the memory of dead romances, and in musk that troubled the
5055  brain, and in champak that stained the imagination; and seeking often
5056  to elaborate a real psychology of perfumes, and to estimate the several
5057  influences of sweet-smelling roots and scented, pollen-laden flowers;
5058  of aromatic balms and of dark and fragrant woods; of spikenard, that
5059  sickens; of hovenia, that makes men mad; and of aloes, that are said to
5060  be able to expel melancholy from the soul.
5061  
5062  At another time he devoted himself entirely to music, and in a long
5063  latticed room, with a vermilion-and-gold ceiling and walls of
5064  olive-green lacquer, he used to give curious concerts in which mad
5065  gipsies tore wild music from little zithers, or grave, yellow-shawled
5066  Tunisians plucked at the strained strings of monstrous lutes, while
5067  grinning Negroes beat monotonously upon copper drums and, crouching
5068  upon scarlet mats, slim turbaned Indians blew through long pipes of
5069  reed or brass and charmed--or feigned to charm--great hooded snakes and
5070  horrible horned adders.  The harsh intervals and shrill discords of
5071  barbaric music stirred him at times when Schubert's grace, and Chopin's
5072  beautiful sorrows, and the mighty harmonies of Beethoven himself, fell
5073  unheeded on his ear.  He collected together from all parts of the world
5074  the strangest instruments that could be found, either in the tombs of
5075  dead nations or among the few savage tribes that have survived contact
5076  with Western civilizations, and loved to touch and try them.  He had
5077  the mysterious _juruparis_ of the Rio Negro Indians, that women are not
5078  allowed to look at and that even youths may not see till they have been
5079  subjected to fasting and scourging, and the earthen jars of the
5080  Peruvians that have the shrill cries of birds, and flutes of human
5081  bones such as Alfonso de Ovalle heard in Chile, and the sonorous green
5082  jaspers that are found near Cuzco and give forth a note of singular
5083  sweetness.  He had painted gourds filled with pebbles that rattled when
5084  they were shaken; the long _clarin_ of the Mexicans, into which the
5085  performer does not blow, but through which he inhales the air; the
5086  harsh _ture_ of the Amazon tribes, that is sounded by the sentinels who
5087  sit all day long in high trees, and can be heard, it is said, at a
5088  distance of three leagues; the _teponaztli_, that has two vibrating
5089  tongues of wood and is beaten with sticks that are smeared with an
5090  elastic gum obtained from the milky juice of plants; the _yotl_-bells of
5091  the Aztecs, that are hung in clusters like grapes; and a huge
5092  cylindrical drum, covered with the skins of great serpents, like the
5093  one that Bernal Diaz saw when he went with Cortes into the Mexican
5094  temple, and of whose doleful sound he has left us so vivid a
5095  description.  The fantastic character of these instruments fascinated
5096  him, and he felt a curious delight in the thought that art, like
5097  Nature, has her monsters, things of bestial shape and with hideous
5098  voices.  Yet, after some time, he wearied of them, and would sit in his
5099  box at the opera, either alone or with Lord Henry, listening in rapt
5100  pleasure to "Tannhauser" and seeing in the prelude to that great work
5101  of art a presentation of the tragedy of his own soul.
5102  
5103  On one occasion he took up the study of jewels, and appeared at a
5104  costume ball as Anne de Joyeuse, Admiral of France, in a dress covered
5105  with five hundred and sixty pearls.  This taste enthralled him for
5106  years, and, indeed, may be said never to have left him.  He would often
5107  spend a whole day settling and resettling in their cases the various
5108  stones that he had collected, such as the olive-green chrysoberyl that
5109  turns red by lamplight, the cymophane with its wirelike line of silver,
5110  the pistachio-coloured peridot, rose-pink and wine-yellow topazes,
5111  carbuncles of fiery scarlet with tremulous, four-rayed stars, flame-red
5112  cinnamon-stones, orange and violet spinels, and amethysts with their
5113  alternate layers of ruby and sapphire.  He loved the red gold of the
5114  sunstone, and the moonstone's pearly whiteness, and the broken rainbow
5115  of the milky opal.  He procured from Amsterdam three emeralds of
5116  extraordinary size and richness of colour, and had a turquoise _de la
5117  vieille roche_ that was the envy of all the connoisseurs.
5118  
5119  He discovered wonderful stories, also, about jewels.  In Alphonso's
5120  Clericalis Disciplina a serpent was mentioned with eyes of real
5121  jacinth, and in the romantic history of Alexander, the Conqueror of
5122  Emathia was said to have found in the vale of Jordan snakes "with
5123  collars of real emeralds growing on their backs." There was a gem in
5124  the brain of the dragon, Philostratus told us, and "by the exhibition
5125  of golden letters and a scarlet robe" the monster could be thrown into
5126  a magical sleep and slain.  According to the great alchemist, Pierre de
5127  Boniface, the diamond rendered a man invisible, and the agate of India
5128  made him eloquent.  The cornelian appeased anger, and the hyacinth
5129  provoked sleep, and the amethyst drove away the fumes of wine.  The
5130  garnet cast out demons, and the hydropicus deprived the moon of her
5131  colour.  The selenite waxed and waned with the moon, and the meloceus,
5132  that discovers thieves, could be affected only by the blood of kids.
5133  Leonardus Camillus had seen a white stone taken from the brain of a
5134  newly killed toad, that was a certain antidote against poison.  The
5135  bezoar, that was found in the heart of the Arabian deer, was a charm
5136  that could cure the plague.  In the nests of Arabian birds was the
5137  aspilates, that, according to Democritus, kept the wearer from any
5138  danger by fire.
5139  
5140  The King of Ceilan rode through his city with a large ruby in his hand,
5141  as the ceremony of his coronation.  The gates of the palace of John the
5142  Priest were "made of sardius, with the horn of the horned snake
5143  inwrought, so that no man might bring poison within." Over the gable
5144  were "two golden apples, in which were two carbuncles," so that the
5145  gold might shine by day and the carbuncles by night.  In Lodge's
5146  strange romance 'A Margarite of America', it was stated that in the
5147  chamber of the queen one could behold "all the chaste ladies of the
5148  world, inchased out of silver, looking through fair mirrours of
5149  chrysolites, carbuncles, sapphires, and greene emeraults." Marco Polo
5150  had seen the inhabitants of Zipangu place rose-coloured pearls in the
5151  mouths of the dead.  A sea-monster had been enamoured of the pearl that
5152  the diver brought to King Perozes, and had slain the thief, and mourned
5153  for seven moons over its loss.  When the Huns lured the king into the
5154  great pit, he flung it away--Procopius tells the story--nor was it ever
5155  found again, though the Emperor Anastasius offered five hundred-weight
5156  of gold pieces for it.  The King of Malabar had shown to a certain
5157  Venetian a rosary of three hundred and four pearls, one for every god
5158  that he worshipped.
5159  
5160  When the Duke de Valentinois, son of Alexander VI, visited Louis XII of
5161  France, his horse was loaded with gold leaves, according to Brantome,
5162  and his cap had double rows of rubies that threw out a great light.
5163  Charles of England had ridden in stirrups hung with four hundred and
5164  twenty-one diamonds.  Richard II had a coat, valued at thirty thousand
5165  marks, which was covered with balas rubies.  Hall described Henry VIII,
5166  on his way to the Tower previous to his coronation, as wearing "a
5167  jacket of raised gold, the placard embroidered with diamonds and other
5168  rich stones, and a great bauderike about his neck of large balasses."
5169  The favourites of James I wore ear-rings of emeralds set in gold
5170  filigrane.  Edward II gave to Piers Gaveston a suit of red-gold armour
5171  studded with jacinths, a collar of gold roses set with
5172  turquoise-stones, and a skull-cap _parseme_ with pearls.  Henry II wore
5173  jewelled gloves reaching to the elbow, and had a hawk-glove sewn with
5174  twelve rubies and fifty-two great orients.  The ducal hat of Charles
5175  the Rash, the last Duke of Burgundy of his race, was hung with
5176  pear-shaped pearls and studded with sapphires.
5177  
5178  How exquisite life had once been!  How gorgeous in its pomp and
5179  decoration!  Even to read of the luxury of the dead was wonderful.
5180  
5181  Then he turned his attention to embroideries and to the tapestries that
5182  performed the office of frescoes in the chill rooms of the northern
5183  nations of Europe.  As he investigated the subject--and he always had
5184  an extraordinary faculty of becoming absolutely absorbed for the moment
5185  in whatever he took up--he was almost saddened by the reflection of the
5186  ruin that time brought on beautiful and wonderful things.  He, at any
5187  rate, had escaped that.  Summer followed summer, and the yellow
5188  jonquils bloomed and died many times, and nights of horror repeated the
5189  story of their shame, but he was unchanged.  No winter marred his face
5190  or stained his flowerlike bloom.  How different it was with material
5191  things!  Where had they passed to?  Where was the great crocus-coloured
5192  robe, on which the gods fought against the giants, that had been worked
5193  by brown girls for the pleasure of Athena?  Where the huge velarium
5194  that Nero had stretched across the Colosseum at Rome, that Titan sail
5195  of purple on which was represented the starry sky, and Apollo driving a
5196  chariot drawn by white, gilt-reined steeds?  He longed to see the
5197  curious table-napkins wrought for the Priest of the Sun, on which were
5198  displayed all the dainties and viands that could be wanted for a feast;
5199  the mortuary cloth of King Chilperic, with its three hundred golden
5200  bees; the fantastic robes that excited the indignation of the Bishop of
5201  Pontus and were figured with "lions, panthers, bears, dogs, forests,
5202  rocks, hunters--all, in fact, that a painter can copy from nature"; and
5203  the coat that Charles of Orleans once wore, on the sleeves of which
5204  were embroidered the verses of a song beginning "_Madame, je suis tout
5205  joyeux_," the musical accompaniment of the words being wrought in gold
5206  thread, and each note, of square shape in those days, formed with four
5207  pearls.  He read of the room that was prepared at the palace at Rheims
5208  for the use of Queen Joan of Burgundy and was decorated with "thirteen
5209  hundred and twenty-one parrots, made in broidery, and blazoned with the
5210  king's arms, and five hundred and sixty-one butterflies, whose wings
5211  were similarly ornamented with the arms of the queen, the whole worked
5212  in gold."  Catherine de Medicis had a mourning-bed made for her of
5213  black velvet powdered with crescents and suns.  Its curtains were of
5214  damask, with leafy wreaths and garlands, figured upon a gold and silver
5215  ground, and fringed along the edges with broideries of pearls, and it
5216  stood in a room hung with rows of the queen's devices in cut black
5217  velvet upon cloth of silver.  Louis XIV had gold embroidered caryatides
5218  fifteen feet high in his apartment.  The state bed of Sobieski, King of
5219  Poland, was made of Smyrna gold brocade embroidered in turquoises with
5220  verses from the Koran.  Its supports were of silver gilt, beautifully
5221  chased, and profusely set with enamelled and jewelled medallions.  It
5222  had been taken from the Turkish camp before Vienna, and the standard of
5223  Mohammed had stood beneath the tremulous gilt of its canopy.
5224  
5225  And so, for a whole year, he sought to accumulate the most exquisite
5226  specimens that he could find of textile and embroidered work, getting
5227  the dainty Delhi muslins, finely wrought with gold-thread palmates and
5228  stitched over with iridescent beetles' wings; the Dacca gauzes, that
5229  from their transparency are known in the East as "woven air," and
5230  "running water," and "evening dew"; strange figured cloths from Java;
5231  elaborate yellow Chinese hangings; books bound in tawny satins or fair
5232  blue silks and wrought with _fleurs-de-lis_, birds and images; veils of
5233  _lacis_ worked in Hungary point; Sicilian brocades and stiff Spanish
5234  velvets; Georgian work, with its gilt coins, and Japanese _Foukousas_,
5235  with their green-toned golds and their marvellously plumaged birds.
5236  
5237  He had a special passion, also, for ecclesiastical vestments, as indeed
5238  he had for everything connected with the service of the Church.  In the
5239  long cedar chests that lined the west gallery of his house, he had
5240  stored away many rare and beautiful specimens of what is really the
5241  raiment of the Bride of Christ, who must wear purple and jewels and
5242  fine linen that she may hide the pallid macerated body that is worn by
5243  the suffering that she seeks for and wounded by self-inflicted pain.
5244  He possessed a gorgeous cope of crimson silk and gold-thread damask,
5245  figured with a repeating pattern of golden pomegranates set in
5246  six-petalled formal blossoms, beyond which on either side was the
5247  pine-apple device wrought in seed-pearls. The orphreys were divided
5248  into panels representing scenes from the life of the Virgin, and the
5249  coronation of the Virgin was figured in coloured silks upon the hood.
5250  This was Italian work of the fifteenth century.  Another cope was of
5251  green velvet, embroidered with heart-shaped groups of acanthus-leaves,
5252  from which spread long-stemmed white blossoms, the details of which
5253  were picked out with silver thread and coloured crystals.  The morse
5254  bore a seraph's head in gold-thread raised work.  The orphreys were
5255  woven in a diaper of red and gold silk, and were starred with
5256  medallions of many saints and martyrs, among whom was St. Sebastian.
5257  He had chasubles, also, of amber-coloured silk, and blue silk and gold
5258  brocade, and yellow silk damask and cloth of gold, figured with
5259  representations of the Passion and Crucifixion of Christ, and
5260  embroidered with lions and peacocks and other emblems; dalmatics of
5261  white satin and pink silk damask, decorated with tulips and dolphins
5262  and _fleurs-de-lis_; altar frontals of crimson velvet and blue linen; and
5263  many corporals, chalice-veils, and sudaria.  In the mystic offices to
5264  which such things were put, there was something that quickened his
5265  imagination.
5266  
5267  For these treasures, and everything that he collected in his lovely
5268  house, were to be to him means of forgetfulness, modes by which he
5269  could escape, for a season, from the fear that seemed to him at times
5270  to be almost too great to be borne.  Upon the walls of the lonely
5271  locked room where he had spent so much of his boyhood, he had hung with
5272  his own hands the terrible portrait whose changing features showed him
5273  the real degradation of his life, and in front of it had draped the
5274  purple-and-gold pall as a curtain.  For weeks he would not go there,
5275  would forget the hideous painted thing, and get back his light heart,
5276  his wonderful joyousness, his passionate absorption in mere existence.
5277  Then, suddenly, some night he would creep out of the house, go down to
5278  dreadful places near Blue Gate Fields, and stay there, day after day,
5279  until he was driven away.  On his return he would sit in front of the
5280  picture, sometimes loathing it and himself, but filled, at other
5281  times, with that pride of individualism that is half the
5282  fascination of sin, and smiling with secret pleasure at the misshapen
5283  shadow that had to bear the burden that should have been his own.
5284  
5285  After a few years he could not endure to be long out of England, and
5286  gave up the villa that he had shared at Trouville with Lord Henry, as
5287  well as the little white walled-in house at Algiers where they had more
5288  than once spent the winter.  He hated to be separated from the picture
5289  that was such a part of his life, and was also afraid that during his
5290  absence some one might gain access to the room, in spite of the
5291  elaborate bars that he had caused to be placed upon the door.
5292  
5293  He was quite conscious that this would tell them nothing.  It was true
5294  that the portrait still preserved, under all the foulness and ugliness
5295  of the face, its marked likeness to himself; but what could they learn
5296  from that?  He would laugh at any one who tried to taunt him.  He had
5297  not painted it.  What was it to him how vile and full of shame it
5298  looked?  Even if he told them, would they believe it?
5299  
5300  Yet he was afraid.  Sometimes when he was down at his great house in
5301  Nottinghamshire, entertaining the fashionable young men of his own rank
5302  who were his chief companions, and astounding the county by the wanton
5303  luxury and gorgeous splendour of his mode of life, he would suddenly
5304  leave his guests and rush back to town to see that the door had not
5305  been tampered with and that the picture was still there.  What if it
5306  should be stolen?  The mere thought made him cold with horror.  Surely
5307  the world would know his secret then.  Perhaps the world already
5308  suspected it.
5309  
5310  For, while he fascinated many, there were not a few who distrusted him.
5311  He was very nearly blackballed at a West End club of which his birth
5312  and social position fully entitled him to become a member, and it was
5313  said that on one occasion, when he was brought by a friend into the
5314  smoking-room of the Churchill, the Duke of Berwick and another
5315  gentleman got up in a marked manner and went out.  Curious stories
5316  became current about him after he had passed his twenty-fifth year.  It
5317  was rumoured that he had been seen brawling with foreign sailors in a
5318  low den in the distant parts of Whitechapel, and that he consorted with
5319  thieves and coiners and knew the mysteries of their trade.  His
5320  extraordinary absences became notorious, and, when he used to reappear
5321  again in society, men would whisper to each other in corners, or pass
5322  him with a sneer, or look at him with cold searching eyes, as though
5323  they were determined to discover his secret.
5324  
5325  Of such insolences and attempted slights he, of course, took no notice,
5326  and in the opinion of most people his frank debonair manner, his
5327  charming boyish smile, and the infinite grace of that wonderful youth
5328  that seemed never to leave him, were in themselves a sufficient answer
5329  to the calumnies, for so they termed them, that were circulated about
5330  him.  It was remarked, however, that some of those who had been most
5331  intimate with him appeared, after a time, to shun him.  Women who had
5332  wildly adored him, and for his sake had braved all social censure and
5333  set convention at defiance, were seen to grow pallid with shame or
5334  horror if Dorian Gray entered the room.
5335  
5336  Yet these whispered scandals only increased in the eyes of many his
5337  strange and dangerous charm.  His great wealth was a certain element of
5338  security.  Society--civilized society, at least--is never very ready to
5339  believe anything to the detriment of those who are both rich and
5340  fascinating.  It feels instinctively that manners are of more
5341  importance than morals, and, in its opinion, the highest respectability
5342  is of much less value than the possession of a good _chef_.  And, after
5343  all, it is a very poor consolation to be told that the man who has
5344  given one a bad dinner, or poor wine, is irreproachable in his private
5345  life.  Even the cardinal virtues cannot atone for half-cold _entrees_, as
5346  Lord Henry remarked once, in a discussion on the subject, and there is
5347  possibly a good deal to be said for his view.  For the canons of good
5348  society are, or should be, the same as the canons of art.  Form is
5349  absolutely essential to it.  It should have the dignity of a ceremony,
5350  as well as its unreality, and should combine the insincere character of
5351  a romantic play with the wit and beauty that make such plays delightful
5352  to us.  Is insincerity such a terrible thing?  I think not.  It is
5353  merely a method by which we can multiply our personalities.
5354  
5355  Such, at any rate, was Dorian Gray's opinion.  He used to wonder at the
5356  shallow psychology of those who conceive the ego in man as a thing
5357  simple, permanent, reliable, and of one essence.  To him, man was a
5358  being with myriad lives and myriad sensations, a complex multiform
5359  creature that bore within itself strange legacies of thought and
5360  passion, and whose very flesh was tainted with the monstrous maladies
5361  of the dead.  He loved to stroll through the gaunt cold picture-gallery
5362  of his country house and look at the various portraits of those whose
5363  blood flowed in his veins.  Here was Philip Herbert, described by
5364  Francis Osborne, in his Memoires on the Reigns of Queen Elizabeth and
5365  King James, as one who was "caressed by the Court for his handsome
5366  face, which kept him not long company."  Was it young Herbert's life
5367  that he sometimes led?  Had some strange poisonous germ crept from body
5368  to body till it had reached his own?  Was it some dim sense of that
5369  ruined grace that had made him so suddenly, and almost without cause,
5370  give utterance, in Basil Hallward's studio, to the mad prayer that had
5371  so changed his life?  Here, in gold-embroidered red doublet, jewelled
5372  surcoat, and gilt-edged ruff and wristbands, stood Sir Anthony Sherard,
5373  with his silver-and-black armour piled at his feet.  What had this
5374  man's legacy been?  Had the lover of Giovanna of Naples bequeathed him
5375  some inheritance of sin and shame?  Were his own actions merely the
5376  dreams that the dead man had not dared to realize?  Here, from the
5377  fading canvas, smiled Lady Elizabeth Devereux, in her gauze hood, pearl
5378  stomacher, and pink slashed sleeves.  A flower was in her right hand,
5379  and her left clasped an enamelled collar of white and damask roses.  On
5380  a table by her side lay a mandolin and an apple.  There were large
5381  green rosettes upon her little pointed shoes.  He knew her life, and
5382  the strange stories that were told about her lovers.  Had he something
5383  of her temperament in him?  These oval, heavy-lidded eyes seemed to
5384  look curiously at him.  What of George Willoughby, with his powdered
5385  hair and fantastic patches?  How evil he looked!  The face was
5386  saturnine and swarthy, and the sensual lips seemed to be twisted with
5387  disdain.  Delicate lace ruffles fell over the lean yellow hands that
5388  were so overladen with rings.  He had been a macaroni of the eighteenth
5389  century, and the friend, in his youth, of Lord Ferrars.  What of the
5390  second Lord Beckenham, the companion of the Prince Regent in his
5391  wildest days, and one of the witnesses at the secret marriage with Mrs.
5392  Fitzherbert?  How proud and handsome he was, with his chestnut curls
5393  and insolent pose!  What passions had he bequeathed?  The world had
5394  looked upon him as infamous.  He had led the orgies at Carlton House.
5395  The star of the Garter glittered upon his breast.  Beside him hung the
5396  portrait of his wife, a pallid, thin-lipped woman in black.  Her blood,
5397  also, stirred within him.  How curious it all seemed!  And his mother
5398  with her Lady Hamilton face and her moist, wine-dashed lips--he knew
5399  what he had got from her.  He had got from her his beauty, and his
5400  passion for the beauty of others.  She laughed at him in her loose
5401  Bacchante dress.  There were vine leaves in her hair.  The purple
5402  spilled from the cup she was holding.  The carnations of the painting
5403  had withered, but the eyes were still wonderful in their depth and
5404  brilliancy of colour.  They seemed to follow him wherever he went.
5405  
5406  Yet one had ancestors in literature as well as in one's own race,
5407  nearer perhaps in type and temperament, many of them, and certainly
5408  with an influence of which one was more absolutely conscious.  There
5409  were times when it appeared to Dorian Gray that the whole of history
5410  was merely the record of his own life, not as he had lived it in act
5411  and circumstance, but as his imagination had created it for him, as it
5412  had been in his brain and in his passions.  He felt that he had known
5413  them all, those strange terrible figures that had passed across the
5414  stage of the world and made sin so marvellous and evil so full of
5415  subtlety.  It seemed to him that in some mysterious way their lives had
5416  been his own.
5417  
5418  The hero of the wonderful novel that had so influenced his life had
5419  himself known this curious fancy.  In the seventh chapter he tells how,
5420  crowned with laurel, lest lightning might strike him, he had sat, as
5421  Tiberius, in a garden at Capri, reading the shameful books of
5422  Elephantis, while dwarfs and peacocks strutted round him and the
5423  flute-player mocked the swinger of the censer; and, as Caligula, had
5424  caroused with the green-shirted jockeys in their stables and supped in
5425  an ivory manger with a jewel-frontleted horse; and, as Domitian, had
5426  wandered through a corridor lined with marble mirrors, looking round
5427  with haggard eyes for the reflection of the dagger that was to end his
5428  days, and sick with that ennui, that terrible _taedium vitae_, that comes
5429  on those to whom life denies nothing; and had peered through a clear
5430  emerald at the red shambles of the circus and then, in a litter of
5431  pearl and purple drawn by silver-shod mules, been carried through the
5432  Street of Pomegranates to a House of Gold and heard men cry on Nero
5433  Caesar as he passed by; and, as Elagabalus, had painted his face with
5434  colours, and plied the distaff among the women, and brought the Moon
5435  from Carthage and given her in mystic marriage to the Sun.
5436  
5437  Over and over again Dorian used to read this fantastic chapter, and the
5438  two chapters immediately following, in which, as in some curious
5439  tapestries or cunningly wrought enamels, were pictured the awful and
5440  beautiful forms of those whom vice and blood and weariness had made
5441  monstrous or mad:  Filippo, Duke of Milan, who slew his wife and
5442  painted her lips with a scarlet poison that her lover might suck death
5443  from the dead thing he fondled; Pietro Barbi, the Venetian, known as
5444  Paul the Second, who sought in his vanity to assume the title of
5445  Formosus, and whose tiara, valued at two hundred thousand florins, was
5446  bought at the price of a terrible sin; Gian Maria Visconti, who used
5447  hounds to chase living men and whose murdered body was covered with
5448  roses by a harlot who had loved him; the Borgia on his white horse,
5449  with Fratricide riding beside him and his mantle stained with the blood
5450  of Perotto; Pietro Riario, the young Cardinal Archbishop of Florence,
5451  child and minion of Sixtus IV, whose beauty was equalled only by his
5452  debauchery, and who received Leonora of Aragon in a pavilion of white
5453  and crimson silk, filled with nymphs and centaurs, and gilded a boy
5454  that he might serve at the feast as Ganymede or Hylas; Ezzelin, whose
5455  melancholy could be cured only by the spectacle of death, and who had a
5456  passion for red blood, as other men have for red wine--the son of the
5457  Fiend, as was reported, and one who had cheated his father at dice when
5458  gambling with him for his own soul; Giambattista Cibo, who in mockery
5459  took the name of Innocent and into whose torpid veins the blood of
5460  three lads was infused by a Jewish doctor; Sigismondo Malatesta, the
5461  lover of Isotta and the lord of Rimini, whose effigy was burned at Rome
5462  as the enemy of God and man, who strangled Polyssena with a napkin, and
5463  gave poison to Ginevra d'Este in a cup of emerald, and in honour of a
5464  shameful passion built a pagan church for Christian worship; Charles
5465  VI, who had so wildly adored his brother's wife that a leper had warned
5466  him of the insanity that was coming on him, and who, when his brain had
5467  sickened and grown strange, could only be soothed by Saracen cards
5468  painted with the images of love and death and madness; and, in his
5469  trimmed jerkin and jewelled cap and acanthuslike curls, Grifonetto
5470  Baglioni, who slew Astorre with his bride, and Simonetto with his page,
5471  and whose comeliness was such that, as he lay dying in the yellow
5472  piazza of Perugia, those who had hated him could not choose but weep,
5473  and Atalanta, who had cursed him, blessed him.
5474  
5475  There was a horrible fascination in them all.  He saw them at night,
5476  and they troubled his imagination in the day.  The Renaissance knew of
5477  strange manners of poisoning--poisoning by a helmet and a lighted
5478  torch, by an embroidered glove and a jewelled fan, by a gilded pomander
5479  and by an amber chain.  Dorian Gray had been poisoned by a book.  There
