5480  were moments when he looked on evil simply as a mode through which he
5481  could realize his conception of the beautiful.
5482  
5483  
5484  
5485  CHAPTER 12
5486  
5487  It was on the ninth of November, the eve of his own thirty-eighth
5488  birthday, as he often remembered afterwards.
5489  
5490  He was walking home about eleven o'clock from Lord Henry's, where he
5491  had been dining, and was wrapped in heavy furs, as the night was cold
5492  and foggy.  At the corner of Grosvenor Square and South Audley Street,
5493  a man passed him in the mist, walking very fast and with the collar of
5494  his grey ulster turned up.  He had a bag in his hand.  Dorian
5495  recognized him.  It was Basil Hallward.  A strange sense of fear, for
5496  which he could not account, came over him.  He made no sign of
5497  recognition and went on quickly in the direction of his own house.
5498  
5499  But Hallward had seen him.  Dorian heard him first stopping on the
5500  pavement and then hurrying after him.  In a few moments, his hand was
5501  on his arm.
5502  
5503  "Dorian!  What an extraordinary piece of luck!  I have been waiting for
5504  you in your library ever since nine o'clock. Finally I took pity on
5505  your tired servant and told him to go to bed, as he let me out.  I am
5506  off to Paris by the midnight train, and I particularly wanted to see
5507  you before I left.  I thought it was you, or rather your fur coat, as
5508  you passed me.  But I wasn't quite sure.  Didn't you recognize me?"
5509  
5510  "In this fog, my dear Basil?  Why, I can't even recognize Grosvenor
5511  Square.  I believe my house is somewhere about here, but I don't feel
5512  at all certain about it.  I am sorry you are going away, as I have not
5513  seen you for ages.  But I suppose you will be back soon?"
5514  
5515  "No:  I am going to be out of England for six months.  I intend to take
5516  a studio in Paris and shut myself up till I have finished a great
5517  picture I have in my head.  However, it wasn't about myself I wanted to
5518  talk.  Here we are at your door.  Let me come in for a moment.  I have
5519  something to say to you."
5520  
5521  "I shall be charmed.  But won't you miss your train?" said Dorian Gray
5522  languidly as he passed up the steps and opened the door with his
5523  latch-key.
5524  
5525  The lamplight struggled out through the fog, and Hallward looked at his
5526  watch.  "I have heaps of time," he answered.  "The train doesn't go
5527  till twelve-fifteen, and it is only just eleven.  In fact, I was on my
5528  way to the club to look for you, when I met you.  You see, I shan't
5529  have any delay about luggage, as I have sent on my heavy things.  All I
5530  have with me is in this bag, and I can easily get to Victoria in twenty
5531  minutes."
5532  
5533  Dorian looked at him and smiled.  "What a way for a fashionable painter
5534  to travel!  A Gladstone bag and an ulster!  Come in, or the fog will
5535  get into the house.  And mind you don't talk about anything serious.
5536  Nothing is serious nowadays.  At least nothing should be."
5537  
5538  Hallward shook his head, as he entered, and followed Dorian into the
5539  library.  There was a bright wood fire blazing in the large open
5540  hearth.  The lamps were lit, and an open Dutch silver spirit-case
5541  stood, with some siphons of soda-water and large cut-glass tumblers, on
5542  a little marqueterie table.
5543  
5544  "You see your servant made me quite at home, Dorian.  He gave me
5545  everything I wanted, including your best gold-tipped cigarettes.  He is
5546  a most hospitable creature.  I like him much better than the Frenchman
5547  you used to have.  What has become of the Frenchman, by the bye?"
5548  
5549  Dorian shrugged his shoulders.  "I believe he married Lady Radley's
5550  maid, and has established her in Paris as an English dressmaker.
5551  Anglomania is very fashionable over there now, I hear.  It seems silly
5552  of the French, doesn't it?  But--do you know?--he was not at all a bad
5553  servant.  I never liked him, but I had nothing to complain about.  One
5554  often imagines things that are quite absurd.  He was really very
5555  devoted to me and seemed quite sorry when he went away.  Have another
5556  brandy-and-soda? Or would you like hock-and-seltzer? I always take
5557  hock-and-seltzer myself.  There is sure to be some in the next room."
5558  
5559  "Thanks, I won't have anything more," said the painter, taking his cap
5560  and coat off and throwing them on the bag that he had placed in the
5561  corner.  "And now, my dear fellow, I want to speak to you seriously.
5562  Don't frown like that.  You make it so much more difficult for me."
5563  
5564  "What is it all about?" cried Dorian in his petulant way, flinging
5565  himself down on the sofa.  "I hope it is not about myself.  I am tired
5566  of myself to-night. I should like to be somebody else."
5567  
5568  "It is about yourself," answered Hallward in his grave deep voice, "and
5569  I must say it to you.  I shall only keep you half an hour."
5570  
5571  Dorian sighed and lit a cigarette.  "Half an hour!" he murmured.
5572  
5573  "It is not much to ask of you, Dorian, and it is entirely for your own
5574  sake that I am speaking.  I think it right that you should know that
5575  the most dreadful things are being said against you in London."
5576  
5577  "I don't wish to know anything about them.  I love scandals about other
5578  people, but scandals about myself don't interest me.  They have not got
5579  the charm of novelty."
5580  
5581  "They must interest you, Dorian.  Every gentleman is interested in his
5582  good name.  You don't want people to talk of you as something vile and
5583  degraded.  Of course, you have your position, and your wealth, and all
5584  that kind of thing.  But position and wealth are not everything.  Mind
5585  you, I don't believe these rumours at all.  At least, I can't believe
5586  them when I see you.  Sin is a thing that writes itself across a man's
5587  face.  It cannot be concealed.  People talk sometimes of secret vices.
5588  There are no such things.  If a wretched man has a vice, it shows
5589  itself in the lines of his mouth, the droop of his eyelids, the
5590  moulding of his hands even.  Somebody--I won't mention his name, but
5591  you know him--came to me last year to have his portrait done.  I had
5592  never seen him before, and had never heard anything about him at the
5593  time, though I have heard a good deal since.  He offered an extravagant
5594  price.  I refused him.  There was something in the shape of his fingers
5595  that I hated.  I know now that I was quite right in what I fancied
5596  about him.  His life is dreadful.  But you, Dorian, with your pure,
5597  bright, innocent face, and your marvellous untroubled youth--I can't
5598  believe anything against you.  And yet I see you very seldom, and you
5599  never come down to the studio now, and when I am away from you, and I
5600  hear all these hideous things that people are whispering about you, I
5601  don't know what to say.  Why is it, Dorian, that a man like the Duke of
5602  Berwick leaves the room of a club when you enter it?  Why is it that so
5603  many gentlemen in London will neither go to your house or invite you to
5604  theirs?  You used to be a friend of Lord Staveley.  I met him at dinner
5605  last week.  Your name happened to come up in conversation, in
5606  connection with the miniatures you have lent to the exhibition at the
5607  Dudley.  Staveley curled his lip and said that you might have the most
5608  artistic tastes, but that you were a man whom no pure-minded girl
5609  should be allowed to know, and whom no chaste woman should sit in the
5610  same room with.  I reminded him that I was a friend of yours, and asked
5611  him what he meant.  He told me.  He told me right out before everybody.
5612  It was horrible!  Why is your friendship so fatal to young men?  There
5613  was that wretched boy in the Guards who committed suicide.  You were
5614  his great friend.  There was Sir Henry Ashton, who had to leave England
5615  with a tarnished name.  You and he were inseparable.  What about Adrian
5616  Singleton and his dreadful end?  What about Lord Kent's only son and
5617  his career?  I met his father yesterday in St. James's Street.  He
5618  seemed broken with shame and sorrow.  What about the young Duke of
5619  Perth?  What sort of life has he got now?  What gentleman would
5620  associate with him?"
5621  
5622  "Stop, Basil.  You are talking about things of which you know nothing,"
5623  said Dorian Gray, biting his lip, and with a note of infinite contempt
5624  in his voice.  "You ask me why Berwick leaves a room when I enter it.
5625  It is because I know everything about his life, not because he knows
5626  anything about mine.  With such blood as he has in his veins, how could
5627  his record be clean?  You ask me about Henry Ashton and young Perth.
5628  Did I teach the one his vices, and the other his debauchery?  If Kent's
5629  silly son takes his wife from the streets, what is that to me?  If
5630  Adrian Singleton writes his friend's name across a bill, am I his
5631  keeper?  I know how people chatter in England.  The middle classes air
5632  their moral prejudices over their gross dinner-tables, and whisper
5633  about what they call the profligacies of their betters in order to try
5634  and pretend that they are in smart society and on intimate terms with
5635  the people they slander.  In this country, it is enough for a man to
5636  have distinction and brains for every common tongue to wag against him.
5637  And what sort of lives do these people, who pose as being moral, lead
5638  themselves?  My dear fellow, you forget that we are in the native land
5639  of the hypocrite."
5640  
5641  "Dorian," cried Hallward, "that is not the question.  England is bad
5642  enough I know, and English society is all wrong.  That is the reason
5643  why I want you to be fine.  You have not been fine.  One has a right to
5644  judge of a man by the effect he has over his friends.  Yours seem to
5645  lose all sense of honour, of goodness, of purity.  You have filled them
5646  with a madness for pleasure.  They have gone down into the depths.  You
5647  led them there.  Yes:  you led them there, and yet you can smile, as
5648  you are smiling now.  And there is worse behind.  I know you and Harry
5649  are inseparable.  Surely for that reason, if for none other, you should
5650  not have made his sister's name a by-word."
5651  
5652  "Take care, Basil.  You go too far."
5653  
5654  "I must speak, and you must listen.  You shall listen.  When you met
5655  Lady Gwendolen, not a breath of scandal had ever touched her.  Is there
5656  a single decent woman in London now who would drive with her in the
5657  park?  Why, even her children are not allowed to live with her.  Then
5658  there are other stories--stories that you have been seen creeping at
5659  dawn out of dreadful houses and slinking in disguise into the foulest
5660  dens in London.  Are they true?  Can they be true?  When I first heard
5661  them, I laughed.  I hear them now, and they make me shudder.  What
5662  about your country-house and the life that is led there?  Dorian, you
5663  don't know what is said about you.  I won't tell you that I don't want
5664  to preach to you.  I remember Harry saying once that every man who
5665  turned himself into an amateur curate for the moment always began by
5666  saying that, and then proceeded to break his word.  I do want to preach
5667  to you.  I want you to lead such a life as will make the world respect
5668  you.  I want you to have a clean name and a fair record.  I want you to
5669  get rid of the dreadful people you associate with.  Don't shrug your
5670  shoulders like that.  Don't be so indifferent.  You have a wonderful
5671  influence.  Let it be for good, not for evil.  They say that you
5672  corrupt every one with whom you become intimate, and that it is quite
5673  sufficient for you to enter a house for shame of some kind to follow
5674  after.  I don't know whether it is so or not.  How should I know?  But
5675  it is said of you.  I am told things that it seems impossible to doubt.
5676  Lord Gloucester was one of my greatest friends at Oxford.  He showed me
5677  a letter that his wife had written to him when she was dying alone in
5678  her villa at Mentone.  Your name was implicated in the most terrible
5679  confession I ever read.  I told him that it was absurd--that I knew you
5680  thoroughly and that you were incapable of anything of the kind.  Know
5681  you?  I wonder do I know you?  Before I could answer that, I should
5682  have to see your soul."
5683  
5684  "To see my soul!" muttered Dorian Gray, starting up from the sofa and
5685  turning almost white from fear.
5686  
5687  "Yes," answered Hallward gravely, and with deep-toned sorrow in his
5688  voice, "to see your soul.  But only God can do that."
5689  
5690  A bitter laugh of mockery broke from the lips of the younger man.  "You
5691  shall see it yourself, to-night!" he cried, seizing a lamp from the
5692  table.  "Come:  it is your own handiwork.  Why shouldn't you look at
5693  it?  You can tell the world all about it afterwards, if you choose.
5694  Nobody would believe you.  If they did believe you, they would like me
5695  all the better for it.  I know the age better than you do, though you
5696  will prate about it so tediously.  Come, I tell you.  You have
5697  chattered enough about corruption.  Now you shall look on it face to
5698  face."
5699  
5700  There was the madness of pride in every word he uttered.  He stamped
5701  his foot upon the ground in his boyish insolent manner.  He felt a
5702  terrible joy at the thought that some one else was to share his secret,
5703  and that the man who had painted the portrait that was the origin of
5704  all his shame was to be burdened for the rest of his life with the
5705  hideous memory of what he had done.
5706  
5707  "Yes," he continued, coming closer to him and looking steadfastly into
5708  his stern eyes, "I shall show you my soul.  You shall see the thing
5709  that you fancy only God can see."
5710  
5711  Hallward started back.  "This is blasphemy, Dorian!" he cried.  "You
5712  must not say things like that.  They are horrible, and they don't mean
5713  anything."
5714  
5715  "You think so?"  He laughed again.
5716  
5717  "I know so.  As for what I said to you to-night, I said it for your
5718  good.  You know I have been always a stanch friend to you."
5719  
5720  "Don't touch me.  Finish what you have to say."
5721  
5722  A twisted flash of pain shot across the painter's face.  He paused for
5723  a moment, and a wild feeling of pity came over him.  After all, what
5724  right had he to pry into the life of Dorian Gray?  If he had done a
5725  tithe of what was rumoured about him, how much he must have suffered!
5726  Then he straightened himself up, and walked over to the fire-place, and
5727  stood there, looking at the burning logs with their frostlike ashes and
5728  their throbbing cores of flame.
5729  
5730  "I am waiting, Basil," said the young man in a hard clear voice.
5731  
5732  He turned round.  "What I have to say is this," he cried.  "You must
5733  give me some answer to these horrible charges that are made against
5734  you.  If you tell me that they are absolutely untrue from beginning to
5735  end, I shall believe you.  Deny them, Dorian, deny them!  Can't you see
5736  what I am going through?  My God! don't tell me that you are bad, and
5737  corrupt, and shameful."
5738  
5739  Dorian Gray smiled.  There was a curl of contempt in his lips.  "Come
5740  upstairs, Basil," he said quietly.  "I keep a diary of my life from day
5741  to day, and it never leaves the room in which it is written.  I shall
5742  show it to you if you come with me."
5743  
5744  "I shall come with you, Dorian, if you wish it.  I see I have missed my
5745  train.  That makes no matter.  I can go to-morrow. But don't ask me to
5746  read anything to-night. All I want is a plain answer to my question."
5747  
5748  "That shall be given to you upstairs.  I could not give it here.  You
5749  will not have to read long."
5750  
5751  
5752  
5753  CHAPTER 13
5754  
5755  He passed out of the room and began the ascent, Basil Hallward
5756  following close behind.  They walked softly, as men do instinctively at
5757  night.  The lamp cast fantastic shadows on the wall and staircase.  A
5758  rising wind made some of the windows rattle.
5759  
5760  When they reached the top landing, Dorian set the lamp down on the
5761  floor, and taking out the key, turned it in the lock.  "You insist on
5762  knowing, Basil?" he asked in a low voice.
5763  
5764  "Yes."
5765  
5766  "I am delighted," he answered, smiling.  Then he added, somewhat
5767  harshly, "You are the one man in the world who is entitled to know
5768  everything about me.  You have had more to do with my life than you
5769  think"; and, taking up the lamp, he opened the door and went in.  A
5770  cold current of air passed them, and the light shot up for a moment in
5771  a flame of murky orange.  He shuddered.  "Shut the door behind you," he
5772  whispered, as he placed the lamp on the table.
5773  
5774  Hallward glanced round him with a puzzled expression.  The room looked
5775  as if it had not been lived in for years.  A faded Flemish tapestry, a
5776  curtained picture, an old Italian _cassone_, and an almost empty
5777  book-case--that was all that it seemed to contain, besides a chair and
5778  a table.  As Dorian Gray was lighting a half-burned candle that was
5779  standing on the mantelshelf, he saw that the whole place was covered
5780  with dust and that the carpet was in holes.  A mouse ran scuffling
5781  behind the wainscoting.  There was a damp odour of mildew.
5782  
5783  "So you think that it is only God who sees the soul, Basil?  Draw that
5784  curtain back, and you will see mine."
5785  
5786  The voice that spoke was cold and cruel.  "You are mad, Dorian, or
5787  playing a part," muttered Hallward, frowning.
5788  
5789  "You won't? Then I must do it myself," said the young man, and he tore
5790  the curtain from its rod and flung it on the ground.
5791  
5792  An exclamation of horror broke from the painter's lips as he saw in the
5793  dim light the hideous face on the canvas grinning at him.  There was
5794  something in its expression that filled him with disgust and loathing.
5795  Good heavens! it was Dorian Gray's own face that he was looking at!
5796  The horror, whatever it was, had not yet entirely spoiled that
5797  marvellous beauty.  There was still some gold in the thinning hair and
5798  some scarlet on the sensual mouth.  The sodden eyes had kept something
5799  of the loveliness of their blue, the noble curves had not yet
5800  completely passed away from chiselled nostrils and from plastic throat.
5801  Yes, it was Dorian himself.  But who had done it?  He seemed to
5802  recognize his own brushwork, and the frame was his own design.  The
5803  idea was monstrous, yet he felt afraid.  He seized the lighted candle,
5804  and held it to the picture.  In the left-hand corner was his own name,
5805  traced in long letters of bright vermilion.
5806  
5807  It was some foul parody, some infamous ignoble satire.  He had never
5808  done that.  Still, it was his own picture.  He knew it, and he felt as
5809  if his blood had changed in a moment from fire to sluggish ice.  His
5810  own picture!  What did it mean?  Why had it altered?  He turned and
5811  looked at Dorian Gray with the eyes of a sick man.  His mouth twitched,
5812  and his parched tongue seemed unable to articulate.  He passed his hand
5813  across his forehead.  It was dank with clammy sweat.
5814  
5815  The young man was leaning against the mantelshelf, watching him with
5816  that strange expression that one sees on the faces of those who are
5817  absorbed in a play when some great artist is acting.  There was neither
5818  real sorrow in it nor real joy.  There was simply the passion of the
5819  spectator, with perhaps a flicker of triumph in his eyes.  He had taken
5820  the flower out of his coat, and was smelling it, or pretending to do so.
5821  
5822  "What does this mean?" cried Hallward, at last.  His own voice sounded
5823  shrill and curious in his ears.
5824  
5825  "Years ago, when I was a boy," said Dorian Gray, crushing the flower in
5826  his hand, "you met me, flattered me, and taught me to be vain of my
5827  good looks.  One day you introduced me to a friend of yours, who
5828  explained to me the wonder of youth, and you finished a portrait of me
5829  that revealed to me the wonder of beauty.  In a mad moment that, even
5830  now, I don't know whether I regret or not, I made a wish, perhaps you
5831  would call it a prayer...."
5832  
5833  "I remember it!  Oh, how well I remember it!  No! the thing is
5834  impossible.  The room is damp.  Mildew has got into the canvas.  The
5835  paints I used had some wretched mineral poison in them.  I tell you the
5836  thing is impossible."
5837  
5838  "Ah, what is impossible?" murmured the young man, going over to the
5839  window and leaning his forehead against the cold, mist-stained glass.
5840  
5841  "You told me you had destroyed it."
5842  
5843  "I was wrong.  It has destroyed me."
5844  
5845  "I don't believe it is my picture."
5846  
5847  "Can't you see your ideal in it?" said Dorian bitterly.
5848  
5849  "My ideal, as you call it..."
5850  
5851  "As you called it."
5852  
5853  "There was nothing evil in it, nothing shameful.  You were to me such
5854  an ideal as I shall never meet again.  This is the face of a satyr."
5855  
5856  "It is the face of my soul."
5857  
5858  "Christ! what a thing I must have worshipped!  It has the eyes of a
5859  devil."
5860  
5861  "Each of us has heaven and hell in him, Basil," cried Dorian with a
5862  wild gesture of despair.
5863  
5864  Hallward turned again to the portrait and gazed at it.  "My God!  If it
5865  is true," he exclaimed, "and this is what you have done with your life,
5866  why, you must be worse even than those who talk against you fancy you
5867  to be!" He held the light up again to the canvas and examined it.  The
5868  surface seemed to be quite undisturbed and as he had left it.  It was
5869  from within, apparently, that the foulness and horror had come.
5870  Through some strange quickening of inner life the leprosies of sin were
5871  slowly eating the thing away.  The rotting of a corpse in a watery
5872  grave was not so fearful.
5873  
5874  His hand shook, and the candle fell from its socket on the floor and
5875  lay there sputtering.  He placed his foot on it and put it out.  Then
5876  he flung himself into the rickety chair that was standing by the table
5877  and buried his face in his hands.
5878  
5879  "Good God, Dorian, what a lesson!  What an awful lesson!" There was no
5880  answer, but he could hear the young man sobbing at the window.  "Pray,
5881  Dorian, pray," he murmured.  "What is it that one was taught to say in
5882  one's boyhood?  'Lead us not into temptation.  Forgive us our sins.
5883  Wash away our iniquities.'  Let us say that together.  The prayer of
5884  your pride has been answered.  The prayer of your repentance will be
5885  answered also.  I worshipped you too much.  I am punished for it.  You
5886  worshipped yourself too much.  We are both punished."
5887  
5888  Dorian Gray turned slowly around and looked at him with tear-dimmed
5889  eyes.  "It is too late, Basil," he faltered.
5890  
5891  "It is never too late, Dorian.  Let us kneel down and try if we cannot
5892  remember a prayer.  Isn't there a verse somewhere, 'Though your sins be
5893  as scarlet, yet I will make them as white as snow'?"
5894  
5895  "Those words mean nothing to me now."
5896  
5897  "Hush!  Don't say that.  You have done enough evil in your life.  My
5898  God!  Don't you see that accursed thing leering at us?"
5899  
5900  Dorian Gray glanced at the picture, and suddenly an uncontrollable
5901  feeling of hatred for Basil Hallward came over him, as though it had
5902  been suggested to him by the image on the canvas, whispered into his
5903  ear by those grinning lips.  The mad passions of a hunted animal
5904  stirred within him, and he loathed the man who was seated at the table,
5905  more than in his whole life he had ever loathed anything.  He glanced
5906  wildly around.  Something glimmered on the top of the painted chest
5907  that faced him.  His eye fell on it.  He knew what it was.  It was a
5908  knife that he had brought up, some days before, to cut a piece of cord,
5909  and had forgotten to take away with him.  He moved slowly towards it,
5910  passing Hallward as he did so.  As soon as he got behind him, he seized
5911  it and turned round.  Hallward stirred in his chair as if he was going
5912  to rise.  He rushed at him and dug the knife into the great vein that
5913  is behind the ear, crushing the man's head down on the table and
5914  stabbing again and again.
5915  
5916  There was a stifled groan and the horrible sound of some one choking
5917  with blood.  Three times the outstretched arms shot up convulsively,
5918  waving grotesque, stiff-fingered hands in the air.  He stabbed him
5919  twice more, but the man did not move.  Something began to trickle on
5920  the floor.  He waited for a moment, still pressing the head down.  Then
5921  he threw the knife on the table, and listened.
5922  
5923  He could hear nothing, but the drip, drip on the threadbare carpet.  He
5924  opened the door and went out on the landing.  The house was absolutely
5925  quiet.  No one was about.  For a few seconds he stood bending over the
5926  balustrade and peering down into the black seething well of darkness.
5927  Then he took out the key and returned to the room, locking himself in
5928  as he did so.
5929  
5930  The thing was still seated in the chair, straining over the table with
5931  bowed head, and humped back, and long fantastic arms.  Had it not been
5932  for the red jagged tear in the neck and the clotted black pool that was
5933  slowly widening on the table, one would have said that the man was
5934  simply asleep.
5935  
5936  How quickly it had all been done!  He felt strangely calm, and walking
5937  over to the window, opened it and stepped out on the balcony.  The wind
5938  had blown the fog away, and the sky was like a monstrous peacock's
5939  tail, starred with myriads of golden eyes.  He looked down and saw the
5940  policeman going his rounds and flashing the long beam of his lantern on
5941  the doors of the silent houses.  The crimson spot of a prowling hansom
5942  gleamed at the corner and then vanished.  A woman in a fluttering shawl
5943  was creeping slowly by the railings, staggering as she went.  Now and
5944  then she stopped and peered back.  Once, she began to sing in a hoarse
5945  voice.  The policeman strolled over and said something to her.  She
5946  stumbled away, laughing.  A bitter blast swept across the square.  The
5947  gas-lamps flickered and became blue, and the leafless trees shook their
5948  black iron branches to and fro.  He shivered and went back, closing the
5949  window behind him.
5950  
5951  Having reached the door, he turned the key and opened it.  He did not
5952  even glance at the murdered man.  He felt that the secret of the whole
5953  thing was not to realize the situation.  The friend who had painted the
5954  fatal portrait to which all his misery had been due had gone out of his
5955  life.  That was enough.
5956  
5957  Then he remembered the lamp.  It was a rather curious one of Moorish
5958  workmanship, made of dull silver inlaid with arabesques of burnished
5959  steel, and studded with coarse turquoises.  Perhaps it might be missed
5960  by his servant, and questions would be asked.  He hesitated for a
5961  moment, then he turned back and took it from the table.  He could not
5962  help seeing the dead thing.  How still it was!  How horribly white the
5963  long hands looked!  It was like a dreadful wax image.
5964  
5965  Having locked the door behind him, he crept quietly downstairs.  The
5966  woodwork creaked and seemed to cry out as if in pain.  He stopped
5967  several times and waited.  No:  everything was still.  It was merely
5968  the sound of his own footsteps.
5969  
5970  When he reached the library, he saw the bag and coat in the corner.
5971  They must be hidden away somewhere.  He unlocked a secret press that
5972  was in the wainscoting, a press in which he kept his own curious
5973  disguises, and put them into it.  He could easily burn them afterwards.
5974  Then he pulled out his watch.  It was twenty minutes to two.
5975  
5976  He sat down and began to think.  Every year--every month, almost--men
5977  were strangled in England for what he had done.  There had been a
5978  madness of murder in the air.  Some red star had come too close to the
5979  earth.... And yet, what evidence was there against him?  Basil Hallward
5980  had left the house at eleven.  No one had seen him come in again.  Most
5981  of the servants were at Selby Royal.  His valet had gone to bed....
5982  Paris!  Yes.  It was to Paris that Basil had gone, and by the midnight
5983  train, as he had intended.  With his curious reserved habits, it would
5984  be months before any suspicions would be roused.  Months!  Everything
5985  could be destroyed long before then.
5986  
5987  A sudden thought struck him.  He put on his fur coat and hat and went
5988  out into the hall.  There he paused, hearing the slow heavy tread of
5989  the policeman on the pavement outside and seeing the flash of the
5990  bull's-eye reflected in the window.  He waited and held his breath.
5991  
5992  After a few moments he drew back the latch and slipped out, shutting
5993  the door very gently behind him.  Then he began ringing the bell.  In
5994  about five minutes his valet appeared, half-dressed and looking very
5995  drowsy.
5996  
5997  "I am sorry to have had to wake you up, Francis," he said, stepping in;
5998  "but I had forgotten my latch-key. What time is it?"
5999  
6000  "Ten minutes past two, sir," answered the man, looking at the clock and
6001  blinking.
6002  
6003  "Ten minutes past two?  How horribly late!  You must wake me at nine
6004  to-morrow. I have some work to do."
6005  
6006  "All right, sir."
6007  
6008  "Did any one call this evening?"
6009  
6010  "Mr. Hallward, sir.  He stayed here till eleven, and then he went away
6011  to catch his train."
6012  
6013  "Oh!  I am sorry I didn't see him.  Did he leave any message?"
6014  
6015  "No, sir, except that he would write to you from Paris, if he did not
6016  find you at the club."
6017  
6018  "That will do, Francis.  Don't forget to call me at nine to-morrow."
6019  
6020  "No, sir."
6021  
6022  The man shambled down the passage in his slippers.
6023  
6024  Dorian Gray threw his hat and coat upon the table and passed into the
6025  library.  For a quarter of an hour he walked up and down the room,
6026  biting his lip and thinking.  Then he took down the Blue Book from one
6027  of the shelves and began to turn over the leaves.  "Alan Campbell, 152,
6028  Hertford Street, Mayfair."  Yes; that was the man he wanted.
6029  
6030  
6031  
6032  CHAPTER 14
6033  
6034  At nine o'clock the next morning his servant came in with a cup of
6035  chocolate on a tray and opened the shutters.  Dorian was sleeping quite
6036  peacefully, lying on his right side, with one hand underneath his
6037  cheek.  He looked like a boy who had been tired out with play, or study.
6038  
6039  The man had to touch him twice on the shoulder before he woke, and as
6040  he opened his eyes a faint smile passed across his lips, as though he
6041  had been lost in some delightful dream.  Yet he had not dreamed at all.
6042  His night had been untroubled by any images of pleasure or of pain.
6043  But youth smiles without any reason.  It is one of its chiefest charms.
6044  
6045  He turned round, and leaning upon his elbow, began to sip his
6046  chocolate.  The mellow November sun came streaming into the room.  The
6047  sky was bright, and there was a genial warmth in the air.  It was
6048  almost like a morning in May.
6049  
6050  Gradually the events of the preceding night crept with silent,
6051  blood-stained feet into his brain and reconstructed themselves there
6052  with terrible distinctness.  He winced at the memory of all that he had
6053  suffered, and for a moment the same curious feeling of loathing for
6054  Basil Hallward that had made him kill him as he sat in the chair came
6055  back to him, and he grew cold with passion.  The dead man was still
6056  sitting there, too, and in the sunlight now.  How horrible that was!
6057  Such hideous things were for the darkness, not for the day.
6058  
6059  He felt that if he brooded on what he had gone through he would sicken
6060  or grow mad.  There were sins whose fascination was more in the memory
6061  than in the doing of them, strange triumphs that gratified the pride
6062  more than the passions, and gave to the intellect a quickened sense of
6063  joy, greater than any joy they brought, or could ever bring, to the
6064  senses.  But this was not one of them.  It was a thing to be driven out
6065  of the mind, to be drugged with poppies, to be strangled lest it might
6066  strangle one itself.
6067  
6068  When the half-hour struck, he passed his hand across his forehead, and
6069  then got up hastily and dressed himself with even more than his usual
6070  care, giving a good deal of attention to the choice of his necktie and
6071  scarf-pin and changing his rings more than once.  He spent a long time
6072  also over breakfast, tasting the various dishes, talking to his valet
6073  about some new liveries that he was thinking of getting made for the
6074  servants at Selby, and going through his correspondence.  At some of
6075  the letters, he smiled.  Three of them bored him.  One he read several
6076  times over and then tore up with a slight look of annoyance in his
6077  face.  "That awful thing, a woman's memory!" as Lord Henry had once
6078  said.
6079  
6080  After he had drunk his cup of black coffee, he wiped his lips slowly
6081  with a napkin, motioned to his servant to wait, and going over to the
6082  table, sat down and wrote two letters.  One he put in his pocket, the
6083  other he handed to the valet.
6084  
6085  "Take this round to 152, Hertford Street, Francis, and if Mr. Campbell
6086  is out of town, get his address."
6087  
6088  As soon as he was alone, he lit a cigarette and began sketching upon a
6089  piece of paper, drawing first flowers and bits of architecture, and
6090  then human faces.  Suddenly he remarked that every face that he drew
6091  seemed to have a fantastic likeness to Basil Hallward.  He frowned, and
6092  getting up, went over to the book-case and took out a volume at hazard.
6093  He was determined that he would not think about what had happened until
6094  it became absolutely necessary that he should do so.
6095  
6096  When he had stretched himself on the sofa, he looked at the title-page
6097  of the book.  It was Gautier's Emaux et Camees, Charpentier's
6098  Japanese-paper edition, with the Jacquemart etching.  The binding was
6099  of citron-green leather, with a design of gilt trellis-work and dotted
6100  pomegranates.  It had been given to him by Adrian Singleton.  As he
6101  turned over the pages, his eye fell on the poem about the hand of
6102  Lacenaire, the cold yellow hand "_du supplice encore mal lavee_," with
6103  its downy red hairs and its "_doigts de faune_."  He glanced at his own
6104  white taper fingers, shuddering slightly in spite of himself, and
6105  passed on, till he came to those lovely stanzas upon Venice:
6106  
6107       Sur une gamme chromatique,
6108         Le sein de peries ruisselant,
6109       La Venus de l'Adriatique
6110         Sort de l'eau son corps rose et blanc.
6111  
6112       Les domes, sur l'azur des ondes
6113         Suivant la phrase au pur contour,
6114       S'enflent comme des gorges rondes
6115         Que souleve un soupir d'amour.
6116  
6117       L'esquif aborde et me depose,
6118         Jetant son amarre au pilier,
6119       Devant une facade rose,
6120         Sur le marbre d'un escalier.
6121  
6122  
6123  How exquisite they were!  As one read them, one seemed to be floating
6124  down the green water-ways of the pink and pearl city, seated in a black
6125  gondola with silver prow and trailing curtains.  The mere lines looked
6126  to him like those straight lines of turquoise-blue that follow one as
6127  one pushes out to the Lido.  The sudden flashes of colour reminded him
6128  of the gleam of the opal-and-iris-throated birds that flutter round the
6129  tall honeycombed Campanile, or stalk, with such stately grace, through
6130  the dim, dust-stained arcades.  Leaning back with half-closed eyes, he
6131  kept saying over and over to himself:
6132  
6133       "Devant une facade rose,
6134          Sur le marbre d'un escalier."
6135  
6136  The whole of Venice was in those two lines.  He remembered the autumn
6137  that he had passed there, and a wonderful love that had stirred him to
6138  mad delightful follies.  There was romance in every place.  But Venice,
6139  like Oxford, had kept the background for romance, and, to the true
6140  romantic, background was everything, or almost everything.  Basil had
6141  been with him part of the time, and had gone wild over Tintoret.  Poor
6142  Basil!  What a horrible way for a man to die!
6143  
6144  He sighed, and took up the volume again, and tried to forget.  He read
6145  of the swallows that fly in and out of the little _cafe_ at Smyrna where
6146  the Hadjis sit counting their amber beads and the turbaned merchants
6147  smoke their long tasselled pipes and talk gravely to each other; he
6148  read of the Obelisk in the Place de la Concorde that weeps tears of
6149  granite in its lonely sunless exile and longs to be back by the hot,
6150  lotus-covered Nile, where there are Sphinxes, and rose-red ibises, and
6151  white vultures with gilded claws, and crocodiles with small beryl eyes
6152  that crawl over the green steaming mud; he began to brood over those
6153  verses which, drawing music from kiss-stained marble, tell of that
6154  curious statue that Gautier compares to a contralto voice, the "_monstre
6155  charmant_" that couches in the porphyry-room of the Louvre.  But after a
6156  time the book fell from his hand.  He grew nervous, and a horrible fit
6157  of terror came over him.  What if Alan Campbell should be out of
6158  England?  Days would elapse before he could come back.  Perhaps he
6159  might refuse to come.  What could he do then?  Every moment was of
6160  vital importance.
6161  
6162  They had been great friends once, five years before--almost
6163  inseparable, indeed.  Then the intimacy had come suddenly to an end.
6164  When they met in society now, it was only Dorian Gray who smiled:  Alan
6165  Campbell never did.
6166  
6167  He was an extremely clever young man, though he had no real
6168  appreciation of the visible arts, and whatever little sense of the
6169  beauty of poetry he possessed he had gained entirely from Dorian.  His
6170  dominant intellectual passion was for science.  At Cambridge he had
6171  spent a great deal of his time working in the laboratory, and had taken
6172  a good class in the Natural Science Tripos of his year.  Indeed, he was
6173  still devoted to the study of chemistry, and had a laboratory of his
6174  own in which he used to shut himself up all day long, greatly to the
6175  annoyance of his mother, who had set her heart on his standing for
6176  Parliament and had a vague idea that a chemist was a person who made up
6177  prescriptions.  He was an excellent musician, however, as well, and
6178  played both the violin and the piano better than most amateurs.  In
6179  fact, it was music that had first brought him and Dorian Gray
6180  together--music and that indefinable attraction that Dorian seemed to
6181  be able to exercise whenever he wished--and, indeed, exercised often
6182  without being conscious of it.  They had met at Lady Berkshire's the
6183  night that Rubinstein played there, and after that used to be always
6184  seen together at the opera and wherever good music was going on.  For
6185  eighteen months their intimacy lasted.  Campbell was always either at
6186  Selby Royal or in Grosvenor Square.  To him, as to many others, Dorian
6187  Gray was the type of everything that is wonderful and fascinating in
6188  life.  Whether or not a quarrel had taken place between them no one
6189  ever knew.  But suddenly people remarked that they scarcely spoke when
6190  they met and that Campbell seemed always to go away early from any
6191  party at which Dorian Gray was present.  He had changed, too--was
6192  strangely melancholy at times, appeared almost to dislike hearing
6193  music, and would never himself play, giving as his excuse, when he was
6194  called upon, that he was so absorbed in science that he had no time
6195  left in which to practise.  And this was certainly true.  Every day he
6196  seemed to become more interested in biology, and his name appeared once
6197  or twice in some of the scientific reviews in connection with certain
6198  curious experiments.
6199  
6200  This was the man Dorian Gray was waiting for.  Every second he kept
6201  glancing at the clock.  As the minutes went by he became horribly
6202  agitated.  At last he got up and began to pace up and down the room,
6203  looking like a beautiful caged thing.  He took long stealthy strides.
6204  His hands were curiously cold.
6205  
6206  The suspense became unbearable.  Time seemed to him to be crawling with
6207  feet of lead, while he by monstrous winds was being swept towards the
6208  jagged edge of some black cleft of precipice.  He knew what was waiting
6209  for him there; saw it, indeed, and, shuddering, crushed with dank hands
6210  his burning lids as though he would have robbed the very brain of sight
6211  and driven the eyeballs back into their cave.  It was useless.  The
6212  brain had its own food on which it battened, and the imagination, made
6213  grotesque by terror, twisted and distorted as a living thing by pain,
6214  danced like some foul puppet on a stand and grinned through moving
6215  masks.  Then, suddenly, time stopped for him.  Yes:  that blind,
6216  slow-breathing thing crawled no more, and horrible thoughts, time being
6217  dead, raced nimbly on in front, and dragged a hideous future from its
6218  grave, and showed it to him.  He stared at it.  Its very horror made
6219  him stone.
6220  
6221  At last the door opened and his servant entered.  He turned glazed eyes
6222  upon him.
6223  
6224  "Mr. Campbell, sir," said the man.
6225  
6226  A sigh of relief broke from his parched lips, and the colour came back
6227  to his cheeks.
6228  
6229  "Ask him to come in at once, Francis."  He felt that he was himself
6230  again.  His mood of cowardice had passed away.
6231  
6232  The man bowed and retired.  In a few moments, Alan Campbell walked in,
6233  looking very stern and rather pale, his pallor being intensified by his
6234  coal-black hair and dark eyebrows.
6235  
6236  "Alan!  This is kind of you.  I thank you for coming."
6237  
6238  "I had intended never to enter your house again, Gray.  But you said it
6239  was a matter of life and death."  His voice was hard and cold.  He
6240  spoke with slow deliberation.  There was a look of contempt in the
6241  steady searching gaze that he turned on Dorian.  He kept his hands in
6242  the pockets of his Astrakhan coat, and seemed not to have noticed the
6243  gesture with which he had been greeted.
6244  
6245  "Yes:  it is a matter of life and death, Alan, and to more than one
6246  person.  Sit down."
6247  
6248  Campbell took a chair by the table, and Dorian sat opposite to him.
6249  The two men's eyes met.  In Dorian's there was infinite pity.  He knew
6250  that what he was going to do was dreadful.
6251  
6252  After a strained moment of silence, he leaned across and said, very
6253  quietly, but watching the effect of each word upon the face of him he
6254  had sent for, "Alan, in a locked room at the top of this house, a room
6255  to which nobody but myself has access, a dead man is seated at a table.
6256  He has been dead ten hours now.  Don't stir, and don't look at me like
6257  that.  Who the man is, why he died, how he died, are matters that do
6258  not concern you.  What you have to do is this--"
6259  
6260  "Stop, Gray.  I don't want to know anything further.  Whether what you
6261  have told me is true or not true doesn't concern me.  I entirely
6262  decline to be mixed up in your life.  Keep your horrible secrets to
6263  yourself.  They don't interest me any more."
6264  
6265  "Alan, they will have to interest you.  This one will have to interest
6266  you.  I am awfully sorry for you, Alan.  But I can't help myself.  You
6267  are the one man who is able to save me.  I am forced to bring you into
6268  the matter.  I have no option.  Alan, you are scientific.  You know
6269  about chemistry and things of that kind.  You have made experiments.
6270  What you have got to do is to destroy the thing that is upstairs--to
6271  destroy it so that not a vestige of it will be left.  Nobody saw this
6272  person come into the house.  Indeed, at the present moment he is
6273  supposed to be in Paris.  He will not be missed for months.  When he is
6274  missed, there must be no trace of him found here.  You, Alan, you must
6275  change him, and everything that belongs to him, into a handful of ashes
6276  that I may scatter in the air."
6277  
6278  "You are mad, Dorian."
6279  
6280  "Ah!  I was waiting for you to call me Dorian."
6281  
6282  "You are mad, I tell you--mad to imagine that I would raise a finger to
6283  help you, mad to make this monstrous confession.  I will have nothing
6284  to do with this matter, whatever it is.  Do you think I am going to
6285  peril my reputation for you?  What is it to me what devil's work you
6286  are up to?"
6287  
6288  "It was suicide, Alan."
6289  
6290  "I am glad of that.  But who drove him to it?  You, I should fancy."
6291  
6292  "Do you still refuse to do this for me?"
6293  
6294  "Of course I refuse.  I will have absolutely nothing to do with it.  I
6295  don't care what shame comes on you.  You deserve it all.  I should not
6296  be sorry to see you disgraced, publicly disgraced.  How dare you ask
6297  me, of all men in the world, to mix myself up in this horror?  I should
6298  have thought you knew more about people's characters.  Your friend Lord
6299  Henry Wotton can't have taught you much about psychology, whatever else
6300  he has taught you.  Nothing will induce me to stir a step to help you.
6301  You have come to the wrong man.  Go to some of your friends.  Don't
6302  come to me."
6303  
6304  "Alan, it was murder.  I killed him.  You don't know what he had made
6305  me suffer.  Whatever my life is, he had more to do with the making or
6306  the marring of it than poor Harry has had.  He may not have intended
6307  it, the result was the same."
6308  
6309  "Murder!  Good God, Dorian, is that what you have come to?  I shall not
6310  inform upon you.  It is not my business.  Besides, without my stirring
6311  in the matter, you are certain to be arrested.  Nobody ever commits a
6312  crime without doing something stupid.  But I will have nothing to do
6313  with it."
6314  
6315  "You must have something to do with it.  Wait, wait a moment; listen to
6316  me.  Only listen, Alan.  All I ask of you is to perform a certain
6317  scientific experiment.  You go to hospitals and dead-houses, and the
6318  horrors that you do there don't affect you.  If in some hideous
6319  dissecting-room or fetid laboratory you found this man lying on a
6320  leaden table with red gutters scooped out in it for the blood to flow
6321  through, you would simply look upon him as an admirable subject.  You
6322  would not turn a hair.  You would not believe that you were doing
6323  anything wrong.  On the contrary, you would probably feel that you were
6324  benefiting the human race, or increasing the sum of knowledge in the
6325  world, or gratifying intellectual curiosity, or something of that kind.
6326  What I want you to do is merely what you have often done before.
6327  Indeed, to destroy a body must be far less horrible than what you are
6328  accustomed to work at.  And, remember, it is the only piece of evidence
6329  against me.  If it is discovered, I am lost; and it is sure to be
6330  discovered unless you help me."
6331  
6332  "I have no desire to help you.  You forget that.  I am simply
6333  indifferent to the whole thing.  It has nothing to do with me."
6334  
6335  "Alan, I entreat you.  Think of the position I am in.  Just before you
6336  came I almost fainted with terror.  You may know terror yourself some
6337  day.  No! don't think of that.  Look at the matter purely from the
6338  scientific point of view.  You don't inquire where the dead things on
6339  which you experiment come from.  Don't inquire now.  I have told you
6340  too much as it is.  But I beg of you to do this.  We were friends once,
6341  Alan."
6342  
6343  "Don't speak about those days, Dorian--they are dead."
6344  
6345  "The dead linger sometimes.  The man upstairs will not go away.  He is
6346  sitting at the table with bowed head and outstretched arms.  Alan!
6347  Alan!  If you don't come to my assistance, I am ruined.  Why, they will
6348  hang me, Alan!  Don't you understand?  They will hang me for what I
6349  have done."
6350  
6351  "There is no good in prolonging this scene.  I absolutely refuse to do
6352  anything in the matter.  It is insane of you to ask me."
6353  
6354  "You refuse?"
6355  
6356  "Yes."
6357  
6358  "I entreat you, Alan."
6359  
6360  "It is useless."
6361  
6362  The same look of pity came into Dorian Gray's eyes.  Then he stretched
6363  out his hand, took a piece of paper, and wrote something on it.  He
6364  read it over twice, folded it carefully, and pushed it across the
6365  table.  Having done this, he got up and went over to the window.
6366  
6367  Campbell looked at him in surprise, and then took up the paper, and
6368  opened it.  As he read it, his face became ghastly pale and he fell
6369  back in his chair.  A horrible sense of sickness came over him.  He
6370  felt as if his heart was beating itself to death in some empty hollow.
6371  
6372  After two or three minutes of terrible silence, Dorian turned round and
6373  came and stood behind him, putting his hand upon his shoulder.
6374  
6375  "I am so sorry for you, Alan," he murmured, "but you leave me no
6376  alternative.  I have a letter written already.  Here it is.  You see
6377  the address.  If you don't help me, I must send it.  If you don't help
6378  me, I will send it.  You know what the result will be.  But you are
6379  going to help me.  It is impossible for you to refuse now.  I tried to
6380  spare you.  You will do me the justice to admit that.  You were stern,
6381  harsh, offensive.  You treated me as no man has ever dared to treat
6382  me--no living man, at any rate.  I bore it all.  Now it is for me to
6383  dictate terms."
6384  
6385  Campbell buried his face in his hands, and a shudder passed through him.
6386  
6387  "Yes, it is my turn to dictate terms, Alan.  You know what they are.
6388  The thing is quite simple.  Come, don't work yourself into this fever.
6389  The thing has to be done.  Face it, and do it."
6390  
6391  A groan broke from Campbell's lips and he shivered all over.  The
6392  ticking of the clock on the mantelpiece seemed to him to be dividing
6393  time into separate atoms of agony, each of which was too terrible to be
6394  borne.  He felt as if an iron ring was being slowly tightened round his
6395  forehead, as if the disgrace with which he was threatened had already
6396  come upon him.  The hand upon his shoulder weighed like a hand of lead.
6397  It was intolerable.  It seemed to crush him.
6398  
6399  "Come, Alan, you must decide at once."
6400  
6401  "I cannot do it," he said, mechanically, as though words could alter
6402  things.
6403  
6404  "You must.  You have no choice.  Don't delay."
6405  
6406  He hesitated a moment.  "Is there a fire in the room upstairs?"
6407  
6408  "Yes, there is a gas-fire with asbestos."
6409  
6410  "I shall have to go home and get some things from the laboratory."
6411  
6412  "No, Alan, you must not leave the house.  Write out on a sheet of
6413  notepaper what you want and my servant will take a cab and bring the
6414  things back to you."
6415  
6416  Campbell scrawled a few lines, blotted them, and addressed an envelope
6417  to his assistant.  Dorian took the note up and read it carefully.  Then
6418  he rang the bell and gave it to his valet, with orders to return as
6419  soon as possible and to bring the things with him.
6420  
6421  As the hall door shut, Campbell started nervously, and having got up
6422  from the chair, went over to the chimney-piece. He was shivering with a
6423  kind of ague.  For nearly twenty minutes, neither of the men spoke.  A
6424  fly buzzed noisily about the room, and the ticking of the clock was
6425  like the beat of a hammer.
6426  
6427  As the chime struck one, Campbell turned round, and looking at Dorian
6428  Gray, saw that his eyes were filled with tears.  There was something in
6429  the purity and refinement of that sad face that seemed to enrage him.
6430  "You are infamous, absolutely infamous!" he muttered.
6431  
6432  "Hush, Alan.  You have saved my life," said Dorian.
6433  
6434  "Your life?  Good heavens! what a life that is!  You have gone from
6435  corruption to corruption, and now you have culminated in crime.  In
6436  doing what I am going to do--what you force me to do--it is not of your
6437  life that I am thinking."
6438  
6439  "Ah, Alan," murmured Dorian with a sigh, "I wish you had a thousandth
6440  part of the pity for me that I have for you." He turned away as he
6441  spoke and stood looking out at the garden.  Campbell made no answer.
6442  
6443  After about ten minutes a knock came to the door, and the servant
6444  entered, carrying a large mahogany chest of chemicals, with a long coil
6445  of steel and platinum wire and two rather curiously shaped iron clamps.
6446  
6447  "Shall I leave the things here, sir?" he asked Campbell.
6448  
6449  "Yes," said Dorian.  "And I am afraid, Francis, that I have another
6450  errand for you.  What is the name of the man at Richmond who supplies
6451  Selby with orchids?"
6452  
6453  "Harden, sir."
6454  
6455  "Yes--Harden.  You must go down to Richmond at once, see Harden
6456  personally, and tell him to send twice as many orchids as I ordered,
6457  and to have as few white ones as possible.  In fact, I don't want any
6458  white ones.  It is a lovely day, Francis, and Richmond is a very pretty
6459  place--otherwise I wouldn't bother you about it."
6460  
6461  "No trouble, sir.  At what time shall I be back?"
6462  
6463  Dorian looked at Campbell.  "How long will your experiment take, Alan?"
6464  he said in a calm indifferent voice.  The presence of a third person in
6465  the room seemed to give him extraordinary courage.
6466  
6467  Campbell frowned and bit his lip.  "It will take about five hours," he
6468  answered.
6469  
6470  "It will be time enough, then, if you are back at half-past seven,
6471  Francis.  Or stay:  just leave my things out for dressing.  You can
6472  have the evening to yourself.  I am not dining at home, so I shall not
6473  want you."
6474  
6475  "Thank you, sir," said the man, leaving the room.
6476  
6477  "Now, Alan, there is not a moment to be lost.  How heavy this chest is!
6478  I'll take it for you.  You bring the other things."  He spoke rapidly
6479  and in an authoritative manner.  Campbell felt dominated by him.  They
6480  left the room together.
6481  
6482  When they reached the top landing, Dorian took out the key and turned
6483  it in the lock.  Then he stopped, and a troubled look came into his
6484  eyes.  He shuddered.  "I don't think I can go in, Alan," he murmured.
6485  
6486  "It is nothing to me.  I don't require you," said Campbell coldly.
6487  
6488  Dorian half opened the door.  As he did so, he saw the face of his
6489  portrait leering in the sunlight.  On the floor in front of it the torn
6490  curtain was lying.  He remembered that the night before he had
6491  forgotten, for the first time in his life, to hide the fatal canvas,
6492  and was about to rush forward, when he drew back with a shudder.
6493  
6494  What was that loathsome red dew that gleamed, wet and glistening, on
6495  one of the hands, as though the canvas had sweated blood?  How horrible
6496  it was!--more horrible, it seemed to him for the moment, than the
6497  silent thing that he knew was stretched across the table, the thing
6498  whose grotesque misshapen shadow on the spotted carpet showed him that
6499  it had not stirred, but was still there, as he had left it.
6500  
6501  He heaved a deep breath, opened the door a little wider, and with
6502  half-closed eyes and averted head, walked quickly in, determined that
6503  he would not look even once upon the dead man.  Then, stooping down and
6504  taking up the gold-and-purple hanging, he flung it right over the
6505  picture.
6506  
6507  There he stopped, feeling afraid to turn round, and his eyes fixed
6508  themselves on the intricacies of the pattern before him.  He heard
6509  Campbell bringing in the heavy chest, and the irons, and the other
6510  things that he had required for his dreadful work.  He began to wonder
6511  if he and Basil Hallward had ever met, and, if so, what they had
6512  thought of each other.
6513  
6514  "Leave me now," said a stern voice behind him.
6515  
6516  He turned and hurried out, just conscious that the dead man had been
6517  thrust back into the chair and that Campbell was gazing into a
6518  glistening yellow face.  As he was going downstairs, he heard the key
6519  being turned in the lock.
6520  
6521  It was long after seven when Campbell came back into the library.  He
6522  was pale, but absolutely calm.  "I have done what you asked me to do,"
6523  he muttered. "And now, good-bye. Let us never see each other again."
6524  
6525  "You have saved me from ruin, Alan.  I cannot forget that," said Dorian
6526  simply.
6527  
6528  As soon as Campbell had left, he went upstairs.  There was a horrible
6529  smell of nitric acid in the room.  But the thing that had been sitting
6530  at the table was gone.
6531  
6532  
6533  
6534  CHAPTER 15
6535  
6536  That evening, at eight-thirty, exquisitely dressed and wearing a large
6537  button-hole of Parma violets, Dorian Gray was ushered into Lady
6538  Narborough's drawing-room by bowing servants.  His forehead was
6539  throbbing with maddened nerves, and he felt wildly excited, but his
6540  manner as he bent over his hostess's hand was as easy and graceful as
6541  ever.  Perhaps one never seems so much at one's ease as when one has to
6542  play a part.  Certainly no one looking at Dorian Gray that night could
6543  have believed that he had passed through a tragedy as horrible as any
6544  tragedy of our age.  Those finely shaped fingers could never have
6545  clutched a knife for sin, nor those smiling lips have cried out on God
6546  and goodness.  He himself could not help wondering at the calm of his
6547  demeanour, and for a moment felt keenly the terrible pleasure of a
6548  double life.
6549  
6550  It was a small party, got up rather in a hurry by Lady Narborough, who
6551  was a very clever woman with what Lord Henry used to describe as the
6552  remains of really remarkable ugliness.  She had proved an excellent
6553  wife to one of our most tedious ambassadors, and having buried her
6554  husband properly in a marble mausoleum, which she had herself designed,
6555  and married off her daughters to some rich, rather elderly men, she
6556  devoted herself now to the pleasures of French fiction, French cookery,
6557  and French _esprit_ when she could get it.
6558  
6559  Dorian was one of her especial favourites, and she always told him that
6560  she was extremely glad she had not met him in early life.  "I know, my
6561  dear, I should have fallen madly in love with you," she used to say,
6562  "and thrown my bonnet right over the mills for your sake.  It is most
6563  fortunate that you were not thought of at the time.  As it was, our
6564  bonnets were so unbecoming, and the mills were so occupied in trying to
6565  raise the wind, that I never had even a flirtation with anybody.
6566  However, that was all Narborough's fault.  He was dreadfully
6567  short-sighted, and there is no pleasure in taking in a husband who
6568  never sees anything."
6569  
6570  Her guests this evening were rather tedious.  The fact was, as she
6571  explained to Dorian, behind a very shabby fan, one of her married
6572  daughters had come up quite suddenly to stay with her, and, to make
6573  matters worse, had actually brought her husband with her.  "I think it
6574  is most unkind of her, my dear," she whispered.  "Of course I go and
6575  stay with them every summer after I come from Homburg, but then an old
6576  woman like me must have fresh air sometimes, and besides, I really wake
6577  them up.  You don't know what an existence they lead down there.  It is
6578  pure unadulterated country life.  They get up early, because they have
6579  so much to do, and go to bed early, because they have so little to
6580  think about.  There has not been a scandal in the neighbourhood since
6581  the time of Queen Elizabeth, and consequently they all fall asleep
6582  after dinner.  You shan't sit next either of them.  You shall sit by me
6583  and amuse me."
6584  
6585  Dorian murmured a graceful compliment and looked round the room.  Yes:
6586  it was certainly a tedious party.  Two of the people he had never seen
6587  before, and the others consisted of Ernest Harrowden, one of those
6588  middle-aged mediocrities so common in London clubs who have no enemies,
6589  but are thoroughly disliked by their friends; Lady Ruxton, an
6590  overdressed woman of forty-seven, with a hooked nose, who was always
6591  trying to get herself compromised, but was so peculiarly plain that to
6592  her great disappointment no one would ever believe anything against
6593  her; Mrs. Erlynne, a pushing nobody, with a delightful lisp and
6594  Venetian-red hair; Lady Alice Chapman, his hostess's daughter, a dowdy
6595  dull girl, with one of those characteristic British faces that, once
6596  seen, are never remembered; and her husband, a red-cheeked,
6597  white-whiskered creature who, like so many of his class, was under the
6598  impression that inordinate joviality can atone for an entire lack of
6599  ideas.
6600  
6601  He was rather sorry he had come, till Lady Narborough, looking at the
6602  great ormolu gilt clock that sprawled in gaudy curves on the
6603  mauve-draped mantelshelf, exclaimed:  "How horrid of Henry Wotton to be
6604  so late!  I sent round to him this morning on chance and he promised
6605  faithfully not to disappoint me."
6606  
6607  It was some consolation that Harry was to be there, and when the door
6608  opened and he heard his slow musical voice lending charm to some
6609  insincere apology, he ceased to feel bored.
6610  
6611  But at dinner he could not eat anything.  Plate after plate went away
6612  untasted.  Lady Narborough kept scolding him for what she called "an
6613  insult to poor Adolphe, who invented the _menu_ specially for you," and
6614  now and then Lord Henry looked across at him, wondering at his silence
6615  and abstracted manner.  From time to time the butler filled his glass
6616  with champagne.  He drank eagerly, and his thirst seemed to increase.
6617  
6618  "Dorian," said Lord Henry at last, as the _chaud-froid_ was being handed
6619  round, "what is the matter with you to-night? You are quite out of
6620  sorts."
6621  
6622  "I believe he is in love," cried Lady Narborough, "and that he is
6623  afraid to tell me for fear I should be jealous.  He is quite right.  I
6624  certainly should."
6625  
6626  "Dear Lady Narborough," murmured Dorian, smiling, "I have not been in
6627  love for a whole week--not, in fact, since Madame de Ferrol left town."
6628  
6629  "How you men can fall in love with that woman!" exclaimed the old lady.
6630  "I really cannot understand it."
6631  
6632  "It is simply because she remembers you when you were a little girl,
6633  Lady Narborough," said Lord Henry.  "She is the one link between us and
6634  your short frocks."
6635  
6636  "She does not remember my short frocks at all, Lord Henry.  But I
6637  remember her very well at Vienna thirty years ago, and how _decolletee_
6638  she was then."
6639  
6640  "She is still _decolletee_," he answered, taking an olive in his long
6641  fingers; "and when she is in a very smart gown she looks like an
6642  _edition de luxe_ of a bad French novel.  She is really wonderful, and
6643  full of surprises.  Her capacity for family affection is extraordinary.
6644  When her third husband died, her hair turned quite gold from grief."
6645  
6646  "How can you, Harry!" cried Dorian.
6647  
6648  "It is a most romantic explanation," laughed the hostess.  "But her
6649  third husband, Lord Henry!  You don't mean to say Ferrol is the fourth?"
6650  
6651  "Certainly, Lady Narborough."
6652  
6653  "I don't believe a word of it."
6654  
6655  "Well, ask Mr. Gray.  He is one of her most intimate friends."
6656  
6657  "Is it true, Mr. Gray?"
6658  
6659  "She assures me so, Lady Narborough," said Dorian.  "I asked her
6660  whether, like Marguerite de Navarre, she had their hearts embalmed and
6661  hung at her girdle.  She told me she didn't, because none of them had
6662  had any hearts at all."
6663  
6664  "Four husbands!  Upon my word that is _trop de zele_."
6665  
6666  "_Trop d'audace_, I tell her," said Dorian.
6667  
6668  "Oh! she is audacious enough for anything, my dear.  And what is Ferrol
6669  like?  I don't know him."
6670  
6671  "The husbands of very beautiful women belong to the criminal classes,"
6672  said Lord Henry, sipping his wine.
6673  
6674  Lady Narborough hit him with her fan.  "Lord Henry, I am not at all
6675  surprised that the world says that you are extremely wicked."
6676  
6677  "But what world says that?" asked Lord Henry, elevating his eyebrows.
6678  "It can only be the next world.  This world and I are on excellent
6679  terms."
6680  
6681  "Everybody I know says you are very wicked," cried the old lady,
6682  shaking her head.
6683  
6684  Lord Henry looked serious for some moments.  "It is perfectly
6685  monstrous," he said, at last, "the way people go about nowadays saying
6686  things against one behind one's back that are absolutely and entirely
6687  true."
6688  
6689  "Isn't he incorrigible?" cried Dorian, leaning forward in his chair.
6690  
6691  "I hope so," said his hostess, laughing.  "But really, if you all
6692  worship Madame de Ferrol in this ridiculous way, I shall have to marry
6693  again so as to be in the fashion."
6694  
6695  "You will never marry again, Lady Narborough," broke in Lord Henry.
6696  "You were far too happy.  When a woman marries again, it is because she
6697  detested her first husband.  When a man marries again, it is because he
6698  adored his first wife.  Women try their luck; men risk theirs."
6699  
6700  "Narborough wasn't perfect," cried the old lady.
6701  
6702  "If he had been, you would not have loved him, my dear lady," was the
6703  rejoinder.  "Women love us for our defects.  If we have enough of them,
6704  they will forgive us everything, even our intellects.  You will never
6705  ask me to dinner again after saying this, I am afraid, Lady Narborough,
6706  but it is quite true."
6707  
6708  "Of course it is true, Lord Henry.  If we women did not love you for
6709  your defects, where would you all be?  Not one of you would ever be
6710  married.  You would be a set of unfortunate bachelors.  Not, however,
6711  that that would alter you much.  Nowadays all the married men live like
6712  bachelors, and all the bachelors like married men."
6713  
6714  "_Fin de siecle_," murmured Lord Henry.
6715  
6716  "_Fin du globe_," answered his hostess.
6717  
6718  "I wish it were _fin du globe_," said Dorian with a sigh.  "Life is a
6719  great disappointment."
6720  
6721  "Ah, my dear," cried Lady Narborough, putting on her gloves, "don't
6722  tell me that you have exhausted life.  When a man says that one knows
6723  that life has exhausted him.  Lord Henry is very wicked, and I
6724  sometimes wish that I had been; but you are made to be good--you look
6725  so good.  I must find you a nice wife.  Lord Henry, don't you think
6726  that Mr. Gray should get married?"
6727  
6728  "I am always telling him so, Lady Narborough," said Lord Henry with a
6729  bow.
6730  
6731  "Well, we must look out for a suitable match for him.  I shall go
6732  through Debrett carefully to-night and draw out a list of all the
6733  eligible young ladies."
6734  
6735  "With their ages, Lady Narborough?" asked Dorian.
6736  
6737  "Of course, with their ages, slightly edited.  But nothing must be done
6738  in a hurry.  I want it to be what _The Morning Post_ calls a suitable
6739  alliance, and I want you both to be happy."
6740  
6741  "What nonsense people talk about happy marriages!" exclaimed Lord
6742  Henry.  "A man can be happy with any woman, as long as he does not love
6743  her."
6744  
6745  "Ah! what a cynic you are!" cried the old lady, pushing back her chair
6746  and nodding to Lady Ruxton.  "You must come and dine with me soon
6747  again.  You are really an admirable tonic, much better than what Sir
6748  Andrew prescribes for me.  You must tell me what people you would like
6749  to meet, though.  I want it to be a delightful gathering."
6750  
6751  "I like men who have a future and women who have a past," he answered.
6752  "Or do you think that would make it a petticoat party?"
6753  
6754  "I fear so," she said, laughing, as she stood up.  "A thousand pardons,
6755  my dear Lady Ruxton," she added, "I didn't see you hadn't finished your
6756  cigarette."
6757  
6758  "Never mind, Lady Narborough.  I smoke a great deal too much.  I am
6759  going to limit myself, for the future."
6760  
6761  "Pray don't, Lady Ruxton," said Lord Henry.  "Moderation is a fatal
6762  thing.  Enough is as bad as a meal.  More than enough is as good as a
6763  feast."
6764  
6765  Lady Ruxton glanced at him curiously.  "You must come and explain that
6766  to me some afternoon, Lord Henry.  It sounds a fascinating theory," she
6767  murmured, as she swept out of the room.
6768  
6769  "Now, mind you don't stay too long over your politics and scandal,"
6770  cried Lady Narborough from the door.  "If you do, we are sure to
6771  squabble upstairs."
6772  
6773  The men laughed, and Mr. Chapman got up solemnly from the foot of the
6774  table and came up to the top.  Dorian Gray changed his seat and went
6775  and sat by Lord Henry.  Mr. Chapman began to talk in a loud voice about
6776  the situation in the House of Commons.  He guffawed at his adversaries.
6777  The word _doctrinaire_--word full of terror to the British
6778  mind--reappeared from time to time between his explosions.  An
6779  alliterative prefix served as an ornament of oratory.  He hoisted the
6780  Union Jack on the pinnacles of thought.  The inherited stupidity of the
6781  race--sound English common sense he jovially termed it--was shown to be
6782  the proper bulwark for society.
6783  
6784  A smile curved Lord Henry's lips, and he turned round and looked at
6785  Dorian.
6786  
6787  "Are you better, my dear fellow?" he asked.  "You seemed rather out of
6788  sorts at dinner."
6789  
6790  "I am quite well, Harry.  I am tired.  That is all."
6791  
6792  "You were charming last night.  The little duchess is quite devoted to
6793  you.  She tells me she is going down to Selby."
6794  
6795  "She has promised to come on the twentieth."
6796  
6797  "Is Monmouth to be there, too?"
6798  
6799  "Oh, yes, Harry."
6800  
6801  "He bores me dreadfully, almost as much as he bores her.  She is very
6802  clever, too clever for a woman.  She lacks the indefinable charm of
6803  weakness.  It is the feet of clay that make the gold of the image
6804  precious.  Her feet are very pretty, but they are not feet of clay.
6805  White porcelain feet, if you like.  They have been through the fire,
6806  and what fire does not destroy, it hardens.  She has had experiences."
6807  
6808  "How long has she been married?" asked Dorian.
6809  
6810  "An eternity, she tells me.  I believe, according to the peerage, it is
6811  ten years, but ten years with Monmouth must have been like eternity,
6812  with time thrown in.  Who else is coming?"
6813  
6814  "Oh, the Willoughbys, Lord Rugby and his wife, our hostess, Geoffrey
6815  Clouston, the usual set.  I have asked Lord Grotrian."
6816  
6817  "I like him," said Lord Henry.  "A great many people don't, but I find
6818  him charming.  He atones for being occasionally somewhat overdressed by
6819  being always absolutely over-educated. He is a very modern type."
6820  
6821  "I don't know if he will be able to come, Harry.  He may have to go to
6822  Monte Carlo with his father."
6823  
6824  "Ah! what a nuisance people's people are!  Try and make him come.  By
6825  the way, Dorian, you ran off very early last night.  You left before
6826  eleven.  What did you do afterwards?  Did you go straight home?"
6827  
6828  Dorian glanced at him hurriedly and frowned.
6829  
6830  "No, Harry," he said at last, "I did not get home till nearly three."
6831  
6832  "Did you go to the club?"
6833  
6834  "Yes," he answered.  Then he bit his lip.  "No, I don't mean that.  I
6835  didn't go to the club.  I walked about.  I forget what I did.... How
6836  inquisitive you are, Harry!  You always want to know what one has been
6837  doing.  I always want to forget what I have been doing.  I came in at
6838  half-past two, if you wish to know the exact time.  I had left my
6839  latch-key at home, and my servant had to let me in.  If you want any
6840  corroborative evidence on the subject, you can ask him."
6841  
6842  Lord Henry shrugged his shoulders.  "My dear fellow, as if I cared!
6843  Let us go up to the drawing-room. No sherry, thank you, Mr. Chapman.
6844  Something has happened to you, Dorian.  Tell me what it is.  You are
6845  not yourself to-night."
6846  
6847  "Don't mind me, Harry.  I am irritable, and out of temper.  I shall
6848  come round and see you to-morrow, or next day.  Make my excuses to Lady
6849  Narborough.  I shan't go upstairs.  I shall go home.  I must go home."
6850  
6851  "All right, Dorian.  I dare say I shall see you to-morrow at tea-time.
6852  The duchess is coming."
6853  
6854  "I will try to be there, Harry," he said, leaving the room.  As he
6855  drove back to his own house, he was conscious that the sense of terror
6856  he thought he had strangled had come back to him.  Lord Henry's casual
6857  questioning had made him lose his nerve for the moment, and he wanted
6858  his nerve still.  Things that were dangerous had to be destroyed.  He
6859  winced.  He hated the idea of even touching them.
6860  
6861  Yet it had to be done.  He realized that, and when he had locked the
6862  door of his library, he opened the secret press into which he had
6863  thrust Basil Hallward's coat and bag.  A huge fire was blazing.  He
6864  piled another log on it.  The smell of the singeing clothes and burning
6865  leather was horrible.  It took him three-quarters of an hour to consume
6866  everything.  At the end he felt faint and sick, and having lit some
6867  Algerian pastilles in a pierced copper brazier, he bathed his hands and
6868  forehead with a cool musk-scented vinegar.
6869  
6870  Suddenly he started.  His eyes grew strangely bright, and he gnawed
6871  nervously at his underlip.  Between two of the windows stood a large
6872  Florentine cabinet, made out of ebony and inlaid with ivory and blue
6873  lapis.  He watched it as though it were a thing that could fascinate
6874  and make afraid, as though it held something that he longed for and yet
6875  almost loathed.  His breath quickened.  A mad craving came over him.
6876  He lit a cigarette and then threw it away.  His eyelids drooped till
6877  the long fringed lashes almost touched his cheek.  But he still watched
6878  the cabinet.  At last he got up from the sofa on which he had been
6879  lying, went over to it, and having unlocked it, touched some hidden
6880  spring.  A triangular drawer passed slowly out.  His fingers moved
6881  instinctively towards it, dipped in, and closed on something.  It was a
6882  small Chinese box of black and gold-dust lacquer, elaborately wrought,
6883  the sides patterned with curved waves, and the silken cords hung with
6884  round crystals and tasselled in plaited metal threads.  He opened it.
6885  Inside was a green paste, waxy in lustre, the odour curiously heavy and
6886  persistent.
6887  
6888  He hesitated for some moments, with a strangely immobile smile upon his
6889  face.  Then shivering, though the atmosphere of the room was terribly
6890  hot, he drew himself up and glanced at the clock.  It was twenty
6891  minutes to twelve.  He put the box back, shutting the cabinet doors as
6892  he did so, and went into his bedroom.
6893  
6894  As midnight was striking bronze blows upon the dusky air, Dorian Gray,
6895  dressed commonly, and with a muffler wrapped round his throat, crept
6896  quietly out of his house.  In Bond Street he found a hansom with a good
6897  horse.  He hailed it and in a low voice gave the driver an address.
6898  
6899  The man shook his head.  "It is too far for me," he muttered.
6900  
6901  "Here is a sovereign for you," said Dorian.  "You shall have another if
6902  you drive fast."
6903  
6904  "All right, sir," answered the man, "you will be there in an hour," and
6905  after his fare had got in he turned his horse round and drove rapidly
6906  towards the river.
6907  
6908  
6909  
6910  CHAPTER 16
6911  
6912  A cold rain began to fall, and the blurred street-lamps looked ghastly
6913  in the dripping mist.  The public-houses were just closing, and dim men
6914  and women were clustering in broken groups round their doors.  From
6915  some of the bars came the sound of horrible laughter.  In others,
6916  drunkards brawled and screamed.
6917  
6918  Lying back in the hansom, with his hat pulled over his forehead, Dorian
6919  Gray watched with listless eyes the sordid shame of the great city, and
6920  now and then he repeated to himself the words that Lord Henry had said
