6921  to him on the first day they had met, "To cure the soul by means of the
6922  senses, and the senses by means of the soul."  Yes, that was the
6923  secret.  He had often tried it, and would try it again now.  There were
6924  opium dens where one could buy oblivion, dens of horror where the
6925  memory of old sins could be destroyed by the madness of sins that were
6926  new.
6927  
6928  The moon hung low in the sky like a yellow skull.  From time to time a
6929  huge misshapen cloud stretched a long arm across and hid it.  The
6930  gas-lamps grew fewer, and the streets more narrow and gloomy.  Once the
6931  man lost his way and had to drive back half a mile.  A steam rose from
6932  the horse as it splashed up the puddles.  The sidewindows of the hansom
6933  were clogged with a grey-flannel mist.
6934  
6935  "To cure the soul by means of the senses, and the senses by means of
6936  the soul!"  How the words rang in his ears!  His soul, certainly, was
6937  sick to death.  Was it true that the senses could cure it?  Innocent
6938  blood had been spilled.  What could atone for that?  Ah! for that there
6939  was no atonement; but though forgiveness was impossible, forgetfulness
6940  was possible still, and he was determined to forget, to stamp the thing
6941  out, to crush it as one would crush the adder that had stung one.
6942  Indeed, what right had Basil to have spoken to him as he had done?  Who
6943  had made him a judge over others?  He had said things that were
6944  dreadful, horrible, not to be endured.
6945  
6946  On and on plodded the hansom, going slower, it seemed to him, at each
6947  step.  He thrust up the trap and called to the man to drive faster.
6948  The hideous hunger for opium began to gnaw at him.  His throat burned
6949  and his delicate hands twitched nervously together.  He struck at the
6950  horse madly with his stick.  The driver laughed and whipped up.  He
6951  laughed in answer, and the man was silent.
6952  
6953  The way seemed interminable, and the streets like the black web of some
6954  sprawling spider.  The monotony became unbearable, and as the mist
6955  thickened, he felt afraid.
6956  
6957  Then they passed by lonely brickfields.  The fog was lighter here, and
6958  he could see the strange, bottle-shaped kilns with their orange,
6959  fanlike tongues of fire.  A dog barked as they went by, and far away in
6960  the darkness some wandering sea-gull screamed.  The horse stumbled in a
6961  rut, then swerved aside and broke into a gallop.
6962  
6963  After some time they left the clay road and rattled again over
6964  rough-paven streets.  Most of the windows were dark, but now and then
6965  fantastic shadows were silhouetted against some lamplit blind.  He
6966  watched them curiously.  They moved like monstrous marionettes and made
6967  gestures like live things.  He hated them.  A dull rage was in his
6968  heart.  As they turned a corner, a woman yelled something at them from
6969  an open door, and two men ran after the hansom for about a hundred
6970  yards.  The driver beat at them with his whip.
6971  
6972  It is said that passion makes one think in a circle.  Certainly with
6973  hideous iteration the bitten lips of Dorian Gray shaped and reshaped
6974  those subtle words that dealt with soul and sense, till he had found in
6975  them the full expression, as it were, of his mood, and justified, by
6976  intellectual approval, passions that without such justification would
6977  still have dominated his temper.  From cell to cell of his brain crept
6978  the one thought; and the wild desire to live, most terrible of all
6979  man's appetites, quickened into force each trembling nerve and fibre.
6980  Ugliness that had once been hateful to him because it made things real,
6981  became dear to him now for that very reason.  Ugliness was the one
6982  reality.  The coarse brawl, the loathsome den, the crude violence of
6983  disordered life, the very vileness of thief and outcast, were more
6984  vivid, in their intense actuality of impression, than all the gracious
6985  shapes of art, the dreamy shadows of song.  They were what he needed
6986  for forgetfulness.  In three days he would be free.
6987  
6988  Suddenly the man drew up with a jerk at the top of a dark lane.  Over
6989  the low roofs and jagged chimney-stacks of the houses rose the black
6990  masts of ships.  Wreaths of white mist clung like ghostly sails to the
6991  yards.
6992  
6993  "Somewhere about here, sir, ain't it?" he asked huskily through the
6994  trap.
6995  
6996  Dorian started and peered round.  "This will do," he answered, and
6997  having got out hastily and given the driver the extra fare he had
6998  promised him, he walked quickly in the direction of the quay.  Here and
6999  there a lantern gleamed at the stern of some huge merchantman.  The
7000  light shook and splintered in the puddles.  A red glare came from an
7001  outward-bound steamer that was coaling.  The slimy pavement looked like
7002  a wet mackintosh.
7003  
7004  He hurried on towards the left, glancing back now and then to see if he
7005  was being followed.  In about seven or eight minutes he reached a small
7006  shabby house that was wedged in between two gaunt factories.  In one of
7007  the top-windows stood a lamp.  He stopped and gave a peculiar knock.
7008  
7009  After a little time he heard steps in the passage and the chain being
7010  unhooked.  The door opened quietly, and he went in without saying a
7011  word to the squat misshapen figure that flattened itself into the
7012  shadow as he passed.  At the end of the hall hung a tattered green
7013  curtain that swayed and shook in the gusty wind which had followed him
7014  in from the street.  He dragged it aside and entered a long low room
7015  which looked as if it had once been a third-rate dancing-saloon. Shrill
7016  flaring gas-jets, dulled and distorted in the fly-blown mirrors that
7017  faced them, were ranged round the walls.  Greasy reflectors of ribbed
7018  tin backed them, making quivering disks of light.  The floor was
7019  covered with ochre-coloured sawdust, trampled here and there into mud,
7020  and stained with dark rings of spilled liquor.  Some Malays were
7021  crouching by a little charcoal stove, playing with bone counters and
7022  showing their white teeth as they chattered.  In one corner, with his
7023  head buried in his arms, a sailor sprawled over a table, and by the
7024  tawdrily painted bar that ran across one complete side stood two
7025  haggard women, mocking an old man who was brushing the sleeves of his
7026  coat with an expression of disgust.  "He thinks he's got red ants on
7027  him," laughed one of them, as Dorian passed by.  The man looked at her
7028  in terror and began to whimper.
7029  
7030  At the end of the room there was a little staircase, leading to a
7031  darkened chamber.  As Dorian hurried up its three rickety steps, the
7032  heavy odour of opium met him.  He heaved a deep breath, and his
7033  nostrils quivered with pleasure.  When he entered, a young man with
7034  smooth yellow hair, who was bending over a lamp lighting a long thin
7035  pipe, looked up at him and nodded in a hesitating manner.
7036  
7037  "You here, Adrian?" muttered Dorian.
7038  
7039  "Where else should I be?" he answered, listlessly.  "None of the chaps
7040  will speak to me now."
7041  
7042  "I thought you had left England."
7043  
7044  "Darlington is not going to do anything.  My brother paid the bill at
7045  last.  George doesn't speak to me either.... I don't care," he added
7046  with a sigh.  "As long as one has this stuff, one doesn't want friends.
7047  I think I have had too many friends."
7048  
7049  Dorian winced and looked round at the grotesque things that lay in such
7050  fantastic postures on the ragged mattresses.  The twisted limbs, the
7051  gaping mouths, the staring lustreless eyes, fascinated him.  He knew in
7052  what strange heavens they were suffering, and what dull hells were
7053  teaching them the secret of some new joy.  They were better off than he
7054  was.  He was prisoned in thought.  Memory, like a horrible malady, was
7055  eating his soul away.  From time to time he seemed to see the eyes of
7056  Basil Hallward looking at him.  Yet he felt he could not stay.  The
7057  presence of Adrian Singleton troubled him.  He wanted to be where no
7058  one would know who he was.  He wanted to escape from himself.
7059  
7060  "I am going on to the other place," he said after a pause.
7061  
7062  "On the wharf?"
7063  
7064  "Yes."
7065  
7066  "That mad-cat is sure to be there.  They won't have her in this place
7067  now."
7068  
7069  Dorian shrugged his shoulders.  "I am sick of women who love one.
7070  Women who hate one are much more interesting.  Besides, the stuff is
7071  better."
7072  
7073  "Much the same."
7074  
7075  "I like it better.  Come and have something to drink.  I must have
7076  something."
7077  
7078  "I don't want anything," murmured the young man.
7079  
7080  "Never mind."
7081  
7082  Adrian Singleton rose up wearily and followed Dorian to the bar.  A
7083  half-caste, in a ragged turban and a shabby ulster, grinned a hideous
7084  greeting as he thrust a bottle of brandy and two tumblers in front of
7085  them.  The women sidled up and began to chatter.  Dorian turned his
7086  back on them and said something in a low voice to Adrian Singleton.
7087  
7088  A crooked smile, like a Malay crease, writhed across the face of one of
7089  the women.  "We are very proud to-night," she sneered.
7090  
7091  "For God's sake don't talk to me," cried Dorian, stamping his foot on
7092  the ground.  "What do you want?  Money?  Here it is.  Don't ever talk
7093  to me again."
7094  
7095  Two red sparks flashed for a moment in the woman's sodden eyes, then
7096  flickered out and left them dull and glazed.  She tossed her head and
7097  raked the coins off the counter with greedy fingers.  Her companion
7098  watched her enviously.
7099  
7100  "It's no use," sighed Adrian Singleton.  "I don't care to go back.
7101  What does it matter?  I am quite happy here."
7102  
7103  "You will write to me if you want anything, won't you?" said Dorian,
7104  after a pause.
7105  
7106  "Perhaps."
7107  
7108  "Good night, then."
7109  
7110  "Good night," answered the young man, passing up the steps and wiping
7111  his parched mouth with a handkerchief.
7112  
7113  Dorian walked to the door with a look of pain in his face.  As he drew
7114  the curtain aside, a hideous laugh broke from the painted lips of the
7115  woman who had taken his money.  "There goes the devil's bargain!" she
7116  hiccoughed, in a hoarse voice.
7117  
7118  "Curse you!" he answered, "don't call me that."
7119  
7120  She snapped her fingers.  "Prince Charming is what you like to be
7121  called, ain't it?" she yelled after him.
7122  
7123  The drowsy sailor leaped to his feet as she spoke, and looked wildly
7124  round.  The sound of the shutting of the hall door fell on his ear.  He
7125  rushed out as if in pursuit.
7126  
7127  Dorian Gray hurried along the quay through the drizzling rain.  His
7128  meeting with Adrian Singleton had strangely moved him, and he wondered
7129  if the ruin of that young life was really to be laid at his door, as
7130  Basil Hallward had said to him with such infamy of insult.  He bit his
7131  lip, and for a few seconds his eyes grew sad.  Yet, after all, what did
7132  it matter to him?  One's days were too brief to take the burden of
7133  another's errors on one's shoulders.  Each man lived his own life and
7134  paid his own price for living it.  The only pity was one had to pay so
7135  often for a single fault.  One had to pay over and over again, indeed.
7136  In her dealings with man, destiny never closed her accounts.
7137  
7138  There are moments, psychologists tell us, when the passion for sin, or
7139  for what the world calls sin, so dominates a nature that every fibre of
7140  the body, as every cell of the brain, seems to be instinct with fearful
7141  impulses.  Men and women at such moments lose the freedom of their
7142  will.  They move to their terrible end as automatons move.  Choice is
7143  taken from them, and conscience is either killed, or, if it lives at
7144  all, lives but to give rebellion its fascination and disobedience its
7145  charm.  For all sins, as theologians weary not of reminding us, are
7146  sins of disobedience.  When that high spirit, that morning star of
7147  evil, fell from heaven, it was as a rebel that he fell.
7148  
7149  Callous, concentrated on evil, with stained mind, and soul hungry for
7150  rebellion, Dorian Gray hastened on, quickening his step as he went, but
7151  as he darted aside into a dim archway, that had served him often as a
7152  short cut to the ill-famed place where he was going, he felt himself
7153  suddenly seized from behind, and before he had time to defend himself,
7154  he was thrust back against the wall, with a brutal hand round his
7155  throat.
7156  
7157  He struggled madly for life, and by a terrible effort wrenched the
7158  tightening fingers away.  In a second he heard the click of a revolver,
7159  and saw the gleam of a polished barrel, pointing straight at his head,
7160  and the dusky form of a short, thick-set man facing him.
7161  
7162  "What do you want?" he gasped.
7163  
7164  "Keep quiet," said the man.  "If you stir, I shoot you."
7165  
7166  "You are mad.  What have I done to you?"
7167  
7168  "You wrecked the life of Sibyl Vane," was the answer, "and Sibyl Vane
7169  was my sister.  She killed herself.  I know it.  Her death is at your
7170  door.  I swore I would kill you in return.  For years I have sought
7171  you.  I had no clue, no trace.  The two people who could have described
7172  you were dead.  I knew nothing of you but the pet name she used to call
7173  you.  I heard it to-night by chance.  Make your peace with God, for
7174  to-night you are going to die."
7175  
7176  Dorian Gray grew sick with fear.  "I never knew her," he stammered.  "I
7177  never heard of her.  You are mad."
7178  
7179  "You had better confess your sin, for as sure as I am James Vane, you
7180  are going to die."  There was a horrible moment.  Dorian did not know
7181  what to say or do.  "Down on your knees!" growled the man.  "I give you
7182  one minute to make your peace--no more.  I go on board to-night for
7183  India, and I must do my job first.  One minute.  That's all."
7184  
7185  Dorian's arms fell to his side.  Paralysed with terror, he did not know
7186  what to do.  Suddenly a wild hope flashed across his brain.  "Stop," he
7187  cried.  "How long ago is it since your sister died?  Quick, tell me!"
7188  
7189  "Eighteen years," said the man.  "Why do you ask me?  What do years
7190  matter?"
7191  
7192  "Eighteen years," laughed Dorian Gray, with a touch of triumph in his
7193  voice.  "Eighteen years!  Set me under the lamp and look at my face!"
7194  
7195  James Vane hesitated for a moment, not understanding what was meant.
7196  Then he seized Dorian Gray and dragged him from the archway.
7197  
7198  Dim and wavering as was the wind-blown light, yet it served to show him
7199  the hideous error, as it seemed, into which he had fallen, for the face
7200  of the man he had sought to kill had all the bloom of boyhood, all the
7201  unstained purity of youth.  He seemed little more than a lad of twenty
7202  summers, hardly older, if older indeed at all, than his sister had been
7203  when they had parted so many years ago.  It was obvious that this was
7204  not the man who had destroyed her life.
7205  
7206  He loosened his hold and reeled back.  "My God! my God!" he cried, "and
7207  I would have murdered you!"
7208  
7209  Dorian Gray drew a long breath.  "You have been on the brink of
7210  committing a terrible crime, my man," he said, looking at him sternly.
7211  "Let this be a warning to you not to take vengeance into your own
7212  hands."
7213  
7214  "Forgive me, sir," muttered James Vane.  "I was deceived.  A chance
7215  word I heard in that damned den set me on the wrong track."
7216  
7217  "You had better go home and put that pistol away, or you may get into
7218  trouble," said Dorian, turning on his heel and going slowly down the
7219  street.
7220  
7221  James Vane stood on the pavement in horror.  He was trembling from head
7222  to foot.  After a little while, a black shadow that had been creeping
7223  along the dripping wall moved out into the light and came close to him
7224  with stealthy footsteps.  He felt a hand laid on his arm and looked
7225  round with a start.  It was one of the women who had been drinking at
7226  the bar.
7227  
7228  "Why didn't you kill him?" she hissed out, putting haggard face quite
7229  close to his.  "I knew you were following him when you rushed out from
7230  Daly's. You fool!  You should have killed him.  He has lots of money,
7231  and he's as bad as bad."
7232  
7233  "He is not the man I am looking for," he answered, "and I want no man's
7234  money.  I want a man's life.  The man whose life I want must be nearly
7235  forty now.  This one is little more than a boy.  Thank God, I have not
7236  got his blood upon my hands."
7237  
7238  The woman gave a bitter laugh.  "Little more than a boy!" she sneered.
7239  "Why, man, it's nigh on eighteen years since Prince Charming made me
7240  what I am."
7241  
7242  "You lie!" cried James Vane.
7243  
7244  She raised her hand up to heaven.  "Before God I am telling the truth,"
7245  she cried.
7246  
7247  "Before God?"
7248  
7249  "Strike me dumb if it ain't so.  He is the worst one that comes here.
7250  They say he has sold himself to the devil for a pretty face.  It's nigh
7251  on eighteen years since I met him.  He hasn't changed much since then.
7252  I have, though," she added, with a sickly leer.
7253  
7254  "You swear this?"
7255  
7256  "I swear it," came in hoarse echo from her flat mouth.  "But don't give
7257  me away to him," she whined; "I am afraid of him.  Let me have some
7258  money for my night's lodging."
7259  
7260  He broke from her with an oath and rushed to the corner of the street,
7261  but Dorian Gray had disappeared.  When he looked back, the woman had
7262  vanished also.
7263  
7264  
7265  
7266  CHAPTER 17
7267  
7268  A week later Dorian Gray was sitting in the conservatory at Selby
7269  Royal, talking to the pretty Duchess of Monmouth, who with her husband,
7270  a jaded-looking man of sixty, was amongst his guests.  It was tea-time,
7271  and the mellow light of the huge, lace-covered lamp that stood on the
7272  table lit up the delicate china and hammered silver of the service at
7273  which the duchess was presiding.  Her white hands were moving daintily
7274  among the cups, and her full red lips were smiling at something that
7275  Dorian had whispered to her.  Lord Henry was lying back in a
7276  silk-draped wicker chair, looking at them.  On a peach-coloured divan
7277  sat Lady Narborough, pretending to listen to the duke's description of
7278  the last Brazilian beetle that he had added to his collection.  Three
7279  young men in elaborate smoking-suits were handing tea-cakes to some of
7280  the women.  The house-party consisted of twelve people, and there were
7281  more expected to arrive on the next day.
7282  
7283  "What are you two talking about?" said Lord Henry, strolling over to
7284  the table and putting his cup down.  "I hope Dorian has told you about
7285  my plan for rechristening everything, Gladys.  It is a delightful idea."
7286  
7287  "But I don't want to be rechristened, Harry," rejoined the duchess,
7288  looking up at him with her wonderful eyes.  "I am quite satisfied with
7289  my own name, and I am sure Mr. Gray should be satisfied with his."
7290  
7291  "My dear Gladys, I would not alter either name for the world.  They are
7292  both perfect.  I was thinking chiefly of flowers.  Yesterday I cut an
7293  orchid, for my button-hole. It was a marvellous spotted thing, as
7294  effective as the seven deadly sins.  In a thoughtless moment I asked
7295  one of the gardeners what it was called.  He told me it was a fine
7296  specimen of _Robinsoniana_, or something dreadful of that kind.  It is a
7297  sad truth, but we have lost the faculty of giving lovely names to
7298  things.  Names are everything.  I never quarrel with actions.  My one
7299  quarrel is with words.  That is the reason I hate vulgar realism in
7300  literature.  The man who could call a spade a spade should be compelled
7301  to use one.  It is the only thing he is fit for."
7302  
7303  "Then what should we call you, Harry?" she asked.
7304  
7305  "His name is Prince Paradox," said Dorian.
7306  
7307  "I recognize him in a flash," exclaimed the duchess.
7308  
7309  "I won't hear of it," laughed Lord Henry, sinking into a chair.  "From
7310  a label there is no escape!  I refuse the title."
7311  
7312  "Royalties may not abdicate," fell as a warning from pretty lips.
7313  
7314  "You wish me to defend my throne, then?"
7315  
7316  "Yes."
7317  
7318  "I give the truths of to-morrow."
7319  
7320  "I prefer the mistakes of to-day," she answered.
7321  
7322  "You disarm me, Gladys," he cried, catching the wilfulness of her mood.
7323  
7324  "Of your shield, Harry, not of your spear."
7325  
7326  "I never tilt against beauty," he said, with a wave of his hand.
7327  
7328  "That is your error, Harry, believe me.  You value beauty far too much."
7329  
7330  "How can you say that?  I admit that I think that it is better to be
7331  beautiful than to be good.  But on the other hand, no one is more ready
7332  than I am to acknowledge that it is better to be good than to be ugly."
7333  
7334  "Ugliness is one of the seven deadly sins, then?" cried the duchess.
7335  "What becomes of your simile about the orchid?"
7336  
7337  "Ugliness is one of the seven deadly virtues, Gladys.  You, as a good
7338  Tory, must not underrate them.  Beer, the Bible, and the seven deadly
7339  virtues have made our England what she is."
7340  
7341  "You don't like your country, then?" she asked.
7342  
7343  "I live in it."
7344  
7345  "That you may censure it the better."
7346  
7347  "Would you have me take the verdict of Europe on it?" he inquired.
7348  
7349  "What do they say of us?"
7350  
7351  "That Tartuffe has emigrated to England and opened a shop."
7352  
7353  "Is that yours, Harry?"
7354  
7355  "I give it to you."
7356  
7357  "I could not use it.  It is too true."
7358  
7359  "You need not be afraid.  Our countrymen never recognize a description."
7360  
7361  "They are practical."
7362  
7363  "They are more cunning than practical.  When they make up their ledger,
7364  they balance stupidity by wealth, and vice by hypocrisy."
7365  
7366  "Still, we have done great things."
7367  
7368  "Great things have been thrust on us, Gladys."
7369  
7370  "We have carried their burden."
7371  
7372  "Only as far as the Stock Exchange."
7373  
7374  She shook her head.  "I believe in the race," she cried.
7375  
7376  "It represents the survival of the pushing."
7377  
7378  "It has development."
7379  
7380  "Decay fascinates me more."
7381  
7382  "What of art?" she asked.
7383  
7384  "It is a malady."
7385  
7386  "Love?"
7387  
7388  "An illusion."
7389  
7390  "Religion?"
7391  
7392  "The fashionable substitute for belief."
7393  
7394  "You are a sceptic."
7395  
7396  "Never!  Scepticism is the beginning of faith."
7397  
7398  "What are you?"
7399  
7400  "To define is to limit."
7401  
7402  "Give me a clue."
7403  
7404  "Threads snap.  You would lose your way in the labyrinth."
7405  
7406  "You bewilder me.  Let us talk of some one else."
7407  
7408  "Our host is a delightful topic.  Years ago he was christened Prince
7409  Charming."
7410  
7411  "Ah! don't remind me of that," cried Dorian Gray.
7412  
7413  "Our host is rather horrid this evening," answered the duchess,
7414  colouring.  "I believe he thinks that Monmouth married me on purely
7415  scientific principles as the best specimen he could find of a modern
7416  butterfly."
7417  
7418  "Well, I hope he won't stick pins into you, Duchess," laughed Dorian.
7419  
7420  "Oh! my maid does that already, Mr. Gray, when she is annoyed with me."
7421  
7422  "And what does she get annoyed with you about, Duchess?"
7423  
7424  "For the most trivial things, Mr. Gray, I assure you.  Usually because
7425  I come in at ten minutes to nine and tell her that I must be dressed by
7426  half-past eight."
7427  
7428  "How unreasonable of her!  You should give her warning."
7429  
7430  "I daren't, Mr. Gray.  Why, she invents hats for me.  You remember the
7431  one I wore at Lady Hilstone's garden-party?  You don't, but it is nice
7432  of you to pretend that you do.  Well, she made it out of nothing.  All
7433  good hats are made out of nothing."
7434  
7435  "Like all good reputations, Gladys," interrupted Lord Henry.  "Every
7436  effect that one produces gives one an enemy.  To be popular one must be
7437  a mediocrity."
7438  
7439  "Not with women," said the duchess, shaking her head; "and women rule
7440  the world.  I assure you we can't bear mediocrities.  We women, as some
7441  one says, love with our ears, just as you men love with your eyes, if
7442  you ever love at all."
7443  
7444  "It seems to me that we never do anything else," murmured Dorian.
7445  
7446  "Ah! then, you never really love, Mr. Gray," answered the duchess with
7447  mock sadness.
7448  
7449  "My dear Gladys!" cried Lord Henry.  "How can you say that?  Romance
7450  lives by repetition, and repetition converts an appetite into an art.
7451  Besides, each time that one loves is the only time one has ever loved.
7452  Difference of object does not alter singleness of passion.  It merely
7453  intensifies it.  We can have in life but one great experience at best,
7454  and the secret of life is to reproduce that experience as often as
7455  possible."
7456  
7457  "Even when one has been wounded by it, Harry?" asked the duchess after
7458  a pause.
7459  
7460  "Especially when one has been wounded by it," answered Lord Henry.
7461  
7462  The duchess turned and looked at Dorian Gray with a curious expression
7463  in her eyes.  "What do you say to that, Mr. Gray?" she inquired.
7464  
7465  Dorian hesitated for a moment.  Then he threw his head back and
7466  laughed.  "I always agree with Harry, Duchess."
7467  
7468  "Even when he is wrong?"
7469  
7470  "Harry is never wrong, Duchess."
7471  
7472  "And does his philosophy make you happy?"
7473  
7474  "I have never searched for happiness.  Who wants happiness?  I have
7475  searched for pleasure."
7476  
7477  "And found it, Mr. Gray?"
7478  
7479  "Often.  Too often."
7480  
7481  The duchess sighed.  "I am searching for peace," she said, "and if I
7482  don't go and dress, I shall have none this evening."
7483  
7484  "Let me get you some orchids, Duchess," cried Dorian, starting to his
7485  feet and walking down the conservatory.
7486  
7487  "You are flirting disgracefully with him," said Lord Henry to his
7488  cousin.  "You had better take care.  He is very fascinating."
7489  
7490  "If he were not, there would be no battle."
7491  
7492  "Greek meets Greek, then?"
7493  
7494  "I am on the side of the Trojans.  They fought for a woman."
7495  
7496  "They were defeated."
7497  
7498  "There are worse things than capture," she answered.
7499  
7500  "You gallop with a loose rein."
7501  
7502  "Pace gives life," was the _riposte_.
7503  
7504  "I shall write it in my diary to-night."
7505  
7506  "What?"
7507  
7508  "That a burnt child loves the fire."
7509  
7510  "I am not even singed.  My wings are untouched."
7511  
7512  "You use them for everything, except flight."
7513  
7514  "Courage has passed from men to women.  It is a new experience for us."
7515  
7516  "You have a rival."
7517  
7518  "Who?"
7519  
7520  He laughed.  "Lady Narborough," he whispered.  "She perfectly adores
7521  him."
7522  
7523  "You fill me with apprehension.  The appeal to antiquity is fatal to us
7524  who are romanticists."
7525  
7526  "Romanticists!  You have all the methods of science."
7527  
7528  "Men have educated us."
7529  
7530  "But not explained you."
7531  
7532  "Describe us as a sex," was her challenge.
7533  
7534  "Sphinxes without secrets."
7535  
7536  She looked at him, smiling.  "How long Mr. Gray is!" she said.  "Let us
7537  go and help him.  I have not yet told him the colour of my frock."
7538  
7539  "Ah! you must suit your frock to his flowers, Gladys."
7540  
7541  "That would be a premature surrender."
7542  
7543  "Romantic art begins with its climax."
7544  
7545  "I must keep an opportunity for retreat."
7546  
7547  "In the Parthian manner?"
7548  
7549  "They found safety in the desert.  I could not do that."
7550  
7551  "Women are not always allowed a choice," he answered, but hardly had he
7552  finished the sentence before from the far end of the conservatory came
7553  a stifled groan, followed by the dull sound of a heavy fall.  Everybody
7554  started up.  The duchess stood motionless in horror.  And with fear in
7555  his eyes, Lord Henry rushed through the flapping palms to find Dorian
7556  Gray lying face downwards on the tiled floor in a deathlike swoon.
7557  
7558  He was carried at once into the blue drawing-room and laid upon one of
7559  the sofas.  After a short time, he came to himself and looked round
7560  with a dazed expression.
7561  
7562  "What has happened?" he asked.  "Oh!  I remember.  Am I safe here,
7563  Harry?" He began to tremble.
7564  
7565  "My dear Dorian," answered Lord Henry, "you merely fainted.  That was
7566  all.  You must have overtired yourself.  You had better not come down
7567  to dinner.  I will take your place."
7568  
7569  "No, I will come down," he said, struggling to his feet.  "I would
7570  rather come down.  I must not be alone."
7571  
7572  He went to his room and dressed.  There was a wild recklessness of
7573  gaiety in his manner as he sat at table, but now and then a thrill of
7574  terror ran through him when he remembered that, pressed against the
7575  window of the conservatory, like a white handkerchief, he had seen the
7576  face of James Vane watching him.
7577  
7578  
7579  
7580  CHAPTER 18
7581  
7582  The next day he did not leave the house, and, indeed, spent most of the
7583  time in his own room, sick with a wild terror of dying, and yet
7584  indifferent to life itself.  The consciousness of being hunted, snared,
7585  tracked down, had begun to dominate him.  If the tapestry did but
7586  tremble in the wind, he shook.  The dead leaves that were blown against
7587  the leaded panes seemed to him like his own wasted resolutions and wild
7588  regrets.  When he closed his eyes, he saw again the sailor's face
7589  peering through the mist-stained glass, and horror seemed once more to
7590  lay its hand upon his heart.
7591  
7592  But perhaps it had been only his fancy that had called vengeance out of
7593  the night and set the hideous shapes of punishment before him.  Actual
7594  life was chaos, but there was something terribly logical in the
7595  imagination.  It was the imagination that set remorse to dog the feet
7596  of sin.  It was the imagination that made each crime bear its misshapen
7597  brood.  In the common world of fact the wicked were not punished, nor
7598  the good rewarded.  Success was given to the strong, failure thrust
7599  upon the weak.  That was all.  Besides, had any stranger been prowling
7600  round the house, he would have been seen by the servants or the
7601  keepers.  Had any foot-marks been found on the flower-beds, the
7602  gardeners would have reported it.  Yes, it had been merely fancy.
7603  Sibyl Vane's brother had not come back to kill him.  He had sailed away
7604  in his ship to founder in some winter sea.  From him, at any rate, he
7605  was safe.  Why, the man did not know who he was, could not know who he
7606  was.  The mask of youth had saved him.
7607  
7608  And yet if it had been merely an illusion, how terrible it was to think
7609  that conscience could raise such fearful phantoms, and give them
7610  visible form, and make them move before one!  What sort of life would
7611  his be if, day and night, shadows of his crime were to peer at him from
7612  silent corners, to mock him from secret places, to whisper in his ear
7613  as he sat at the feast, to wake him with icy fingers as he lay asleep!
7614  As the thought crept through his brain, he grew pale with terror, and
7615  the air seemed to him to have become suddenly colder.  Oh! in what a
7616  wild hour of madness he had killed his friend!  How ghastly the mere
7617  memory of the scene!  He saw it all again.  Each hideous detail came
7618  back to him with added horror.  Out of the black cave of time, terrible
7619  and swathed in scarlet, rose the image of his sin.  When Lord Henry
7620  came in at six o'clock, he found him crying as one whose heart will
7621  break.
7622  
7623  It was not till the third day that he ventured to go out.  There was
7624  something in the clear, pine-scented air of that winter morning that
7625  seemed to bring him back his joyousness and his ardour for life.  But
7626  it was not merely the physical conditions of environment that had
7627  caused the change.  His own nature had revolted against the excess of
7628  anguish that had sought to maim and mar the perfection of its calm.
7629  With subtle and finely wrought temperaments it is always so.  Their
7630  strong passions must either bruise or bend.  They either slay the man,
7631  or themselves die.  Shallow sorrows and shallow loves live on.  The
7632  loves and sorrows that are great are destroyed by their own plenitude.
7633  Besides, he had convinced himself that he had been the victim of a
7634  terror-stricken imagination, and looked back now on his fears with
7635  something of pity and not a little of contempt.
7636  
7637  After breakfast, he walked with the duchess for an hour in the garden
7638  and then drove across the park to join the shooting-party. The crisp
7639  frost lay like salt upon the grass.  The sky was an inverted cup of
7640  blue metal.  A thin film of ice bordered the flat, reed-grown lake.
7641  
7642  At the corner of the pine-wood he caught sight of Sir Geoffrey
7643  Clouston, the duchess's brother, jerking two spent cartridges out of
7644  his gun.  He jumped from the cart, and having told the groom to take
7645  the mare home, made his way towards his guest through the withered
7646  bracken and rough undergrowth.
7647  
7648  "Have you had good sport, Geoffrey?" he asked.
7649  
7650  "Not very good, Dorian.  I think most of the birds have gone to the
7651  open.  I dare say it will be better after lunch, when we get to new
7652  ground."
7653  
7654  Dorian strolled along by his side.  The keen aromatic air, the brown
7655  and red lights that glimmered in the wood, the hoarse cries of the
7656  beaters ringing out from time to time, and the sharp snaps of the guns
7657  that followed, fascinated him and filled him with a sense of delightful
7658  freedom.  He was dominated by the carelessness of happiness, by the
7659  high indifference of joy.
7660  
7661  Suddenly from a lumpy tussock of old grass some twenty yards in front
7662  of them, with black-tipped ears erect and long hinder limbs throwing it
7663  forward, started a hare.  It bolted for a thicket of alders.  Sir
7664  Geoffrey put his gun to his shoulder, but there was something in the
7665  animal's grace of movement that strangely charmed Dorian Gray, and he
7666  cried out at once, "Don't shoot it, Geoffrey.  Let it live."
7667  
7668  "What nonsense, Dorian!" laughed his companion, and as the hare bounded
7669  into the thicket, he fired.  There were two cries heard, the cry of a
7670  hare in pain, which is dreadful, the cry of a man in agony, which is
7671  worse.
7672  
7673  "Good heavens!  I have hit a beater!" exclaimed Sir Geoffrey.  "What an
7674  ass the man was to get in front of the guns!  Stop shooting there!" he
7675  called out at the top of his voice.  "A man is hurt."
7676  
7677  The head-keeper came running up with a stick in his hand.
7678  
7679  "Where, sir?  Where is he?" he shouted.  At the same time, the firing
7680  ceased along the line.
7681  
7682  "Here," answered Sir Geoffrey angrily, hurrying towards the thicket.
7683  "Why on earth don't you keep your men back?  Spoiled my shooting for
7684  the day."
7685  
7686  Dorian watched them as they plunged into the alder-clump, brushing the
7687  lithe swinging branches aside.  In a few moments they emerged, dragging
7688  a body after them into the sunlight.  He turned away in horror.  It
7689  seemed to him that misfortune followed wherever he went.  He heard Sir
7690  Geoffrey ask if the man was really dead, and the affirmative answer of
7691  the keeper.  The wood seemed to him to have become suddenly alive with
7692  faces.  There was the trampling of myriad feet and the low buzz of
7693  voices.  A great copper-breasted pheasant came beating through the
7694  boughs overhead.
7695  
7696  After a few moments--that were to him, in his perturbed state, like
7697  endless hours of pain--he felt a hand laid on his shoulder.  He started
7698  and looked round.
7699  
7700  "Dorian," said Lord Henry, "I had better tell them that the shooting is
7701  stopped for to-day. It would not look well to go on."
7702  
7703  "I wish it were stopped for ever, Harry," he answered bitterly.  "The
7704  whole thing is hideous and cruel.  Is the man ...?"
7705  
7706  He could not finish the sentence.
7707  
7708  "I am afraid so," rejoined Lord Henry.  "He got the whole charge of
7709  shot in his chest.  He must have died almost instantaneously.  Come;
7710  let us go home."
7711  
7712  They walked side by side in the direction of the avenue for nearly
7713  fifty yards without speaking.  Then Dorian looked at Lord Henry and
7714  said, with a heavy sigh, "It is a bad omen, Harry, a very bad omen."
7715  
7716  "What is?" asked Lord Henry.  "Oh! this accident, I suppose.  My dear
7717  fellow, it can't be helped.  It was the man's own fault.  Why did he
7718  get in front of the guns?  Besides, it is nothing to us.  It is rather
7719  awkward for Geoffrey, of course.  It does not do to pepper beaters.  It
7720  makes people think that one is a wild shot.  And Geoffrey is not; he
7721  shoots very straight.  But there is no use talking about the matter."
7722  
7723  Dorian shook his head.  "It is a bad omen, Harry.  I feel as if
7724  something horrible were going to happen to some of us.  To myself,
7725  perhaps," he added, passing his hand over his eyes, with a gesture of
7726  pain.
7727  
7728  The elder man laughed.  "The only horrible thing in the world is _ennui_,
7729  Dorian.  That is the one sin for which there is no forgiveness.  But we
7730  are not likely to suffer from it unless these fellows keep chattering
7731  about this thing at dinner.  I must tell them that the subject is to be
7732  tabooed.  As for omens, there is no such thing as an omen.  Destiny
7733  does not send us heralds.  She is too wise or too cruel for that.
7734  Besides, what on earth could happen to you, Dorian?  You have
7735  everything in the world that a man can want.  There is no one who would
7736  not be delighted to change places with you."
7737  
7738  "There is no one with whom I would not change places, Harry.  Don't
7739  laugh like that.  I am telling you the truth.  The wretched peasant who
7740  has just died is better off than I am.  I have no terror of death.  It
7741  is the coming of death that terrifies me.  Its monstrous wings seem to
7742  wheel in the leaden air around me.  Good heavens! don't you see a man
7743  moving behind the trees there, watching me, waiting for me?"
7744  
7745  Lord Henry looked in the direction in which the trembling gloved hand
7746  was pointing.  "Yes," he said, smiling, "I see the gardener waiting for
7747  you.  I suppose he wants to ask you what flowers you wish to have on
7748  the table to-night. How absurdly nervous you are, my dear fellow!  You
7749  must come and see my doctor, when we get back to town."
7750  
7751  Dorian heaved a sigh of relief as he saw the gardener approaching.  The
7752  man touched his hat, glanced for a moment at Lord Henry in a hesitating
7753  manner, and then produced a letter, which he handed to his master.
7754  "Her Grace told me to wait for an answer," he murmured.
7755  
7756  Dorian put the letter into his pocket.  "Tell her Grace that I am
7757  coming in," he said, coldly.  The man turned round and went rapidly in
7758  the direction of the house.
7759  
7760  "How fond women are of doing dangerous things!" laughed Lord Henry.
7761  "It is one of the qualities in them that I admire most.  A woman will
7762  flirt with anybody in the world as long as other people are looking on."
7763  
7764  "How fond you are of saying dangerous things, Harry!  In the present
7765  instance, you are quite astray.  I like the duchess very much, but I
7766  don't love her."
7767  
7768  "And the duchess loves you very much, but she likes you less, so you
7769  are excellently matched."
7770  
7771  "You are talking scandal, Harry, and there is never any basis for
7772  scandal."
7773  
7774  "The basis of every scandal is an immoral certainty," said Lord Henry,
7775  lighting a cigarette.
7776  
7777  "You would sacrifice anybody, Harry, for the sake of an epigram."
7778  
7779  "The world goes to the altar of its own accord," was the answer.
7780  
7781  "I wish I could love," cried Dorian Gray with a deep note of pathos in
7782  his voice.  "But I seem to have lost the passion and forgotten the
7783  desire.  I am too much concentrated on myself.  My own personality has
7784  become a burden to me.  I want to escape, to go away, to forget.  It
7785  was silly of me to come down here at all.  I think I shall send a wire
7786  to Harvey to have the yacht got ready.  On a yacht one is safe."
7787  
7788  "Safe from what, Dorian?  You are in some trouble.  Why not tell me
7789  what it is?  You know I would help you."
7790  
7791  "I can't tell you, Harry," he answered sadly.  "And I dare say it is
7792  only a fancy of mine.  This unfortunate accident has upset me.  I have
7793  a horrible presentiment that something of the kind may happen to me."
7794  
7795  "What nonsense!"
7796  
7797  "I hope it is, but I can't help feeling it.  Ah! here is the duchess,
7798  looking like Artemis in a tailor-made gown.  You see we have come back,
7799  Duchess."
7800  
7801  "I have heard all about it, Mr. Gray," she answered.  "Poor Geoffrey is
7802  terribly upset.  And it seems that you asked him not to shoot the hare.
7803  How curious!"
7804  
7805  "Yes, it was very curious.  I don't know what made me say it.  Some
7806  whim, I suppose.  It looked the loveliest of little live things.  But I
7807  am sorry they told you about the man.  It is a hideous subject."
7808  
7809  "It is an annoying subject," broke in Lord Henry.  "It has no
7810  psychological value at all.  Now if Geoffrey had done the thing on
7811  purpose, how interesting he would be!  I should like to know some one
7812  who had committed a real murder."
7813  
7814  "How horrid of you, Harry!" cried the duchess.  "Isn't it, Mr. Gray?
7815  Harry, Mr. Gray is ill again.  He is going to faint."
7816  
7817  Dorian drew himself up with an effort and smiled.  "It is nothing,
7818  Duchess," he murmured; "my nerves are dreadfully out of order.  That is
7819  all.  I am afraid I walked too far this morning.  I didn't hear what
7820  Harry said.  Was it very bad?  You must tell me some other time.  I
7821  think I must go and lie down.  You will excuse me, won't you?"
7822  
7823  They had reached the great flight of steps that led from the
7824  conservatory on to the terrace.  As the glass door closed behind
7825  Dorian, Lord Henry turned and looked at the duchess with his slumberous
7826  eyes.  "Are you very much in love with him?" he asked.
7827  
7828  She did not answer for some time, but stood gazing at the landscape.
7829  "I wish I knew," she said at last.
7830  
7831  He shook his head.  "Knowledge would be fatal.  It is the uncertainty
7832  that charms one.  A mist makes things wonderful."
7833  
7834  "One may lose one's way."
7835  
7836  "All ways end at the same point, my dear Gladys."
7837  
7838  "What is that?"
7839  
7840  "Disillusion."
7841  
7842  "It was my _debut_ in life," she sighed.
7843  
7844  "It came to you crowned."
7845  
7846  "I am tired of strawberry leaves."
7847  
7848  "They become you."
7849  
7850  "Only in public."
7851  
7852  "You would miss them," said Lord Henry.
7853  
7854  "I will not part with a petal."
7855  
7856  "Monmouth has ears."
7857  
7858  "Old age is dull of hearing."
7859  
7860  "Has he never been jealous?"
7861  
7862  "I wish he had been."
7863  
7864  He glanced about as if in search of something.  "What are you looking
7865  for?" she inquired.
7866  
7867  "The button from your foil," he answered.  "You have dropped it."
7868  
7869  She laughed.  "I have still the mask."
7870  
7871  "It makes your eyes lovelier," was his reply.
7872  
7873  She laughed again.  Her teeth showed like white seeds in a scarlet
7874  fruit.
7875  
7876  Upstairs, in his own room, Dorian Gray was lying on a sofa, with terror
7877  in every tingling fibre of his body.  Life had suddenly become too
7878  hideous a burden for him to bear.  The dreadful death of the unlucky
7879  beater, shot in the thicket like a wild animal, had seemed to him to
7880  pre-figure death for himself also.  He had nearly swooned at what Lord
7881  Henry had said in a chance mood of cynical jesting.
7882  
7883  At five o'clock he rang his bell for his servant and gave him orders to
7884  pack his things for the night-express to town, and to have the brougham
7885  at the door by eight-thirty. He was determined not to sleep another
7886  night at Selby Royal.  It was an ill-omened place.  Death walked there
7887  in the sunlight.  The grass of the forest had been spotted with blood.
7888  
7889  Then he wrote a note to Lord Henry, telling him that he was going up to
7890  town to consult his doctor and asking him to entertain his guests in
7891  his absence.  As he was putting it into the envelope, a knock came to
7892  the door, and his valet informed him that the head-keeper wished to see
7893  him.  He frowned and bit his lip.  "Send him in," he muttered, after
7894  some moments' hesitation.
7895  
7896  As soon as the man entered, Dorian pulled his chequebook out of a
7897  drawer and spread it out before him.
7898  
7899  "I suppose you have come about the unfortunate accident of this
7900  morning, Thornton?" he said, taking up a pen.
7901  
7902  "Yes, sir," answered the gamekeeper.
7903  
7904  "Was the poor fellow married?  Had he any people dependent on him?"
7905  asked Dorian, looking bored.  "If so, I should not like them to be left
7906  in want, and will send them any sum of money you may think necessary."
7907  
7908  "We don't know who he is, sir.  That is what I took the liberty of
7909  coming to you about."
7910  
7911  "Don't know who he is?" said Dorian, listlessly.  "What do you mean?
7912  Wasn't he one of your men?"
7913  
7914  "No, sir.  Never saw him before.  Seems like a sailor, sir."
7915  
7916  The pen dropped from Dorian Gray's hand, and he felt as if his heart
7917  had suddenly stopped beating.  "A sailor?" he cried out.  "Did you say
7918  a sailor?"
7919  
7920  "Yes, sir.  He looks as if he had been a sort of sailor; tattooed on
7921  both arms, and that kind of thing."
7922  
7923  "Was there anything found on him?" said Dorian, leaning forward and
7924  looking at the man with startled eyes.  "Anything that would tell his
7925  name?"
7926  
7927  "Some money, sir--not much, and a six-shooter. There was no name of any
7928  kind.  A decent-looking man, sir, but rough-like. A sort of sailor we
7929  think."
7930  
7931  Dorian started to his feet.  A terrible hope fluttered past him.  He
7932  clutched at it madly.  "Where is the body?" he exclaimed.  "Quick!  I
7933  must see it at once."
7934  
7935  "It is in an empty stable in the Home Farm, sir.  The folk don't like
7936  to have that sort of thing in their houses.  They say a corpse brings
7937  bad luck."
7938  
7939  "The Home Farm!  Go there at once and meet me.  Tell one of the grooms
7940  to bring my horse round.  No. Never mind.  I'll go to the stables
7941  myself.  It will save time."
7942  
7943  In less than a quarter of an hour, Dorian Gray was galloping down the
7944  long avenue as hard as he could go.  The trees seemed to sweep past him
7945  in spectral procession, and wild shadows to fling themselves across his
7946  path.  Once the mare swerved at a white gate-post and nearly threw him.
7947  He lashed her across the neck with his crop.  She cleft the dusky air
7948  like an arrow.  The stones flew from her hoofs.
7949  
7950  At last he reached the Home Farm.  Two men were loitering in the yard.
7951  He leaped from the saddle and threw the reins to one of them.  In the
7952  farthest stable a light was glimmering.  Something seemed to tell him
7953  that the body was there, and he hurried to the door and put his hand
7954  upon the latch.
7955  
7956  There he paused for a moment, feeling that he was on the brink of a
7957  discovery that would either make or mar his life.  Then he thrust the
7958  door open and entered.
7959  
7960  On a heap of sacking in the far corner was lying the dead body of a man
7961  dressed in a coarse shirt and a pair of blue trousers.  A spotted
7962  handkerchief had been placed over the face.  A coarse candle, stuck in
7963  a bottle, sputtered beside it.
7964  
7965  Dorian Gray shuddered.  He felt that his could not be the hand to take
7966  the handkerchief away, and called out to one of the farm-servants to
7967  come to him.
7968  
7969  "Take that thing off the face.  I wish to see it," he said, clutching
7970  at the door-post for support.
7971  
7972  When the farm-servant had done so, he stepped forward.  A cry of joy
7973  broke from his lips.  The man who had been shot in the thicket was
7974  James Vane.
7975  
7976  He stood there for some minutes looking at the dead body.  As he rode
7977  home, his eyes were full of tears, for he knew he was safe.
7978  
7979  
7980  
7981  CHAPTER 19
7982  
7983  "There is no use your telling me that you are going to be good," cried
7984  Lord Henry, dipping his white fingers into a red copper bowl filled
7985  with rose-water. "You are quite perfect.  Pray, don't change."
7986  
7987  Dorian Gray shook his head.  "No, Harry, I have done too many dreadful
7988  things in my life.  I am not going to do any more.  I began my good
7989  actions yesterday."
7990  
7991  "Where were you yesterday?"
7992  
7993  "In the country, Harry.  I was staying at a little inn by myself."
7994  
7995  "My dear boy," said Lord Henry, smiling, "anybody can be good in the
7996  country.  There are no temptations there.  That is the reason why
7997  people who live out of town are so absolutely uncivilized.
7998  Civilization is not by any means an easy thing to attain to.  There are
7999  only two ways by which man can reach it.  One is by being cultured, the
8000  other by being corrupt.  Country people have no opportunity of being
8001  either, so they stagnate."
8002  
8003  "Culture and corruption," echoed Dorian.  "I have known something of
8004  both.  It seems terrible to me now that they should ever be found
8005  together.  For I have a new ideal, Harry.  I am going to alter.  I
8006  think I have altered."
8007  
8008  "You have not yet told me what your good action was.  Or did you say
8009  you had done more than one?" asked his companion as he spilled into his
8010  plate a little crimson pyramid of seeded strawberries and, through a
8011  perforated, shell-shaped spoon, snowed white sugar upon them.
8012  
8013  "I can tell you, Harry.  It is not a story I could tell to any one
8014  else.  I spared somebody.  It sounds vain, but you understand what I
8015  mean.  She was quite beautiful and wonderfully like Sibyl Vane.  I
8016  think it was that which first attracted me to her.  You remember Sibyl,
8017  don't you?  How long ago that seems!  Well, Hetty was not one of our
8018  own class, of course.  She was simply a girl in a village.  But I
8019  really loved her.  I am quite sure that I loved her.  All during this
8020  wonderful May that we have been having, I used to run down and see her
8021  two or three times a week.  Yesterday she met me in a little orchard.
8022  The apple-blossoms kept tumbling down on her hair, and she was
8023  laughing.  We were to have gone away together this morning at dawn.
8024  Suddenly I determined to leave her as flowerlike as I had found her."
8025  
8026  "I should think the novelty of the emotion must have given you a thrill
8027  of real pleasure, Dorian," interrupted Lord Henry.  "But I can finish
8028  your idyll for you.  You gave her good advice and broke her heart.
8029  That was the beginning of your reformation."
8030  
8031  "Harry, you are horrible!  You mustn't say these dreadful things.
8032  Hetty's heart is not broken.  Of course, she cried and all that.  But
8033  there is no disgrace upon her.  She can live, like Perdita, in her
8034  garden of mint and marigold."
8035  
8036  "And weep over a faithless Florizel," said Lord Henry, laughing, as he
8037  leaned back in his chair.  "My dear Dorian, you have the most curiously
8038  boyish moods.  Do you think this girl will ever be really content now
8039  with any one of her own rank?  I suppose she will be married some day
8040  to a rough carter or a grinning ploughman.  Well, the fact of having
8041  met you, and loved you, will teach her to despise her husband, and she
8042  will be wretched.  From a moral point of view, I cannot say that I
8043  think much of your great renunciation.  Even as a beginning, it is
8044  poor.  Besides, how do you know that Hetty isn't floating at the
8045  present moment in some starlit mill-pond, with lovely water-lilies
8046  round her, like Ophelia?"
8047  
8048  "I can't bear this, Harry!  You mock at everything, and then suggest
8049  the most serious tragedies.  I am sorry I told you now.  I don't care
8050  what you say to me.  I know I was right in acting as I did.  Poor
8051  Hetty!  As I rode past the farm this morning, I saw her white face at
8052  the window, like a spray of jasmine.  Don't let us talk about it any
8053  more, and don't try to persuade me that the first good action I have
8054  done for years, the first little bit of self-sacrifice I have ever
8055  known, is really a sort of sin.  I want to be better.  I am going to be
8056  better.  Tell me something about yourself.  What is going on in town?
8057  I have not been to the club for days."
8058  
8059  "The people are still discussing poor Basil's disappearance."
8060  
8061  "I should have thought they had got tired of that by this time," said
8062  Dorian, pouring himself out some wine and frowning slightly.
8063  
8064  "My dear boy, they have only been talking about it for six weeks, and
8065  the British public are really not equal to the mental strain of having
8066  more than one topic every three months.  They have been very fortunate
8067  lately, however.  They have had my own divorce-case and Alan Campbell's
8068  suicide.  Now they have got the mysterious disappearance of an artist.
8069  Scotland Yard still insists that the man in the grey ulster who left
8070  for Paris by the midnight train on the ninth of November was poor
8071  Basil, and the French police declare that Basil never arrived in Paris
8072  at all.  I suppose in about a fortnight we shall be told that he has
8073  been seen in San Francisco.  It is an odd thing, but every one who
8074  disappears is said to be seen at San Francisco.  It must be a
8075  delightful city, and possess all the attractions of the next world."
8076  
8077  "What do you think has happened to Basil?" asked Dorian, holding up his
8078  Burgundy against the light and wondering how it was that he could
8079  discuss the matter so calmly.
8080  
8081  "I have not the slightest idea.  If Basil chooses to hide himself, it
8082  is no business of mine.  If he is dead, I don't want to think about
8083  him.  Death is the only thing that ever terrifies me.  I hate it."
8084  
8085  "Why?" said the younger man wearily.
8086  
8087  "Because," said Lord Henry, passing beneath his nostrils the gilt
8088  trellis of an open vinaigrette box, "one can survive everything
8089  nowadays except that.  Death and vulgarity are the only two facts in
8090  the nineteenth century that one cannot explain away.  Let us have our
8091  coffee in the music-room, Dorian.  You must play Chopin to me.  The man
8092  with whom my wife ran away played Chopin exquisitely.  Poor Victoria!
8093  I was very fond of her.  The house is rather lonely without her.  Of
8094  course, married life is merely a habit, a bad habit.  But then one
8095  regrets the loss even of one's worst habits.  Perhaps one regrets them
8096  the most.  They are such an essential part of one's personality."
8097  
8098  Dorian said nothing, but rose from the table, and passing into the next
8099  room, sat down to the piano and let his fingers stray across the white
8100  and black ivory of the keys.  After the coffee had been brought in, he
8101  stopped, and looking over at Lord Henry, said, "Harry, did it ever
8102  occur to you that Basil was murdered?"
8103  
8104  Lord Henry yawned.  "Basil was very popular, and always wore a
8105  Waterbury watch.  Why should he have been murdered?  He was not clever
8106  enough to have enemies.  Of course, he had a wonderful genius for
8107  painting.  But a man can paint like Velasquez and yet be as dull as
8108  possible.  Basil was really rather dull.  He only interested me once,
8109  and that was when he told me, years ago, that he had a wild adoration
8110  for you and that you were the dominant motive of his art."
8111  
8112  "I was very fond of Basil," said Dorian with a note of sadness in his
8113  voice.  "But don't people say that he was murdered?"
8114  
8115  "Oh, some of the papers do.  It does not seem to me to be at all
8116  probable.  I know there are dreadful places in Paris, but Basil was not
8117  the sort of man to have gone to them.  He had no curiosity.  It was his
8118  chief defect."
8119  
8120  "What would you say, Harry, if I told you that I had murdered Basil?"
8121  said the younger man.  He watched him intently after he had spoken.
8122  
8123  "I would say, my dear fellow, that you were posing for a character that
8124  doesn't suit you.  All crime is vulgar, just as all vulgarity is crime.
8125  It is not in you, Dorian, to commit a murder.  I am sorry if I hurt
8126  your vanity by saying so, but I assure you it is true.  Crime belongs
8127  exclusively to the lower orders.  I don't blame them in the smallest
8128  degree.  I should fancy that crime was to them what art is to us,
8129  simply a method of procuring extraordinary sensations."
8130  
8131  "A method of procuring sensations?  Do you think, then, that a man who
8132  has once committed a murder could possibly do the same crime again?
8133  Don't tell me that."
8134  
8135  "Oh! anything becomes a pleasure if one does it too often," cried Lord
8136  Henry, laughing.  "That is one of the most important secrets of life.
8137  I should fancy, however, that murder is always a mistake.  One should
8138  never do anything that one cannot talk about after dinner.  But let us
8139  pass from poor Basil.  I wish I could believe that he had come to such
8140  a really romantic end as you suggest, but I can't. I dare say he fell
8141  into the Seine off an omnibus and that the conductor hushed up the
8142  scandal.  Yes:  I should fancy that was his end.  I see him lying now
8143  on his back under those dull-green waters, with the heavy barges
8144  floating over him and long weeds catching in his hair.  Do you know, I
8145  don't think he would have done much more good work.  During the last
8146  ten years his painting had gone off very much."
8147  
8148  Dorian heaved a sigh, and Lord Henry strolled across the room and began
8149  to stroke the head of a curious Java parrot, a large, grey-plumaged
8150  bird with pink crest and tail, that was balancing itself upon a bamboo
8151  perch.  As his pointed fingers touched it, it dropped the white scurf
8152  of crinkled lids over black, glasslike eyes and began to sway backwards
8153  and forwards.
8154  
8155  "Yes," he continued, turning round and taking his handkerchief out of
8156  his pocket; "his painting had quite gone off.  It seemed to me to have
8157  lost something.  It had lost an ideal.  When you and he ceased to be
8158  great friends, he ceased to be a great artist.  What was it separated
8159  you?  I suppose he bored you.  If so, he never forgave you.  It's a
8160  habit bores have.  By the way, what has become of that wonderful
8161  portrait he did of you?  I don't think I have ever seen it since he
8162  finished it.  Oh!  I remember your telling me years ago that you had
8163  sent it down to Selby, and that it had got mislaid or stolen on the
8164  way.  You never got it back?  What a pity! it was really a
8165  masterpiece.  I remember I wanted to buy it.  I wish I had now.  It
8166  belonged to Basil's best period.  Since then, his work was that curious
8167  mixture of bad painting and good intentions that always entitles a man
8168  to be called a representative British artist.  Did you advertise for
8169  it?  You should."
8170  
8171  "I forget," said Dorian.  "I suppose I did.  But I never really liked
8172  it.  I am sorry I sat for it.  The memory of the thing is hateful to
8173  me.  Why do you talk of it?  It used to remind me of those curious
8174  lines in some play--Hamlet, I think--how do they run?--
8175  
8176      "Like the painting of a sorrow,
8177       A face without a heart."
8178  
8179  Yes:  that is what it was like."
8180  
8181  Lord Henry laughed.  "If a man treats life artistically, his brain is
8182  his heart," he answered, sinking into an arm-chair.
8183  
8184  Dorian Gray shook his head and struck some soft chords on the piano.
8185  "'Like the painting of a sorrow,'" he repeated, "'a face without a
8186  heart.'"
8187  
8188  The elder man lay back and looked at him with half-closed eyes.  "By
8189  the way, Dorian," he said after a pause, "'what does it profit a man if
8190  he gain the whole world and lose--how does the quotation run?--his own
8191  soul'?"
8192  
8193  The music jarred, and Dorian Gray started and stared at his friend.
8194  "Why do you ask me that, Harry?"
8195  
8196  "My dear fellow," said Lord Henry, elevating his eyebrows in surprise,
8197  "I asked you because I thought you might be able to give me an answer.
8198  That is all.  I was going through the park last Sunday, and close by
8199  the Marble Arch there stood a little crowd of shabby-looking people
8200  listening to some vulgar street-preacher. As I passed by, I heard the
8201  man yelling out that question to his audience.  It struck me as being
8202  rather dramatic.  London is very rich in curious effects of that kind.
8203  A wet Sunday, an uncouth Christian in a mackintosh, a ring of sickly
8204  white faces under a broken roof of dripping umbrellas, and a wonderful
8205  phrase flung into the air by shrill hysterical lips--it was really very
8206  good in its way, quite a suggestion.  I thought of telling the prophet
8207  that art had a soul, but that man had not.  I am afraid, however, he
8208  would not have understood me."
8209  
8210  "Don't, Harry.  The soul is a terrible reality.  It can be bought, and
8211  sold, and bartered away.  It can be poisoned, or made perfect.  There
8212  is a soul in each one of us.  I know it."
8213  
8214  "Do you feel quite sure of that, Dorian?"
8215  
8216  "Quite sure."
8217  
8218  "Ah! then it must be an illusion.  The things one feels absolutely
8219  certain about are never true.  That is the fatality of faith, and the
8220  lesson of romance.  How grave you are!  Don't be so serious.  What have
8221  you or I to do with the superstitions of our age?  No:  we have given
8222  up our belief in the soul.  Play me something.  Play me a nocturne,
8223  Dorian, and, as you play, tell me, in a low voice, how you have kept
8224  your youth.  You must have some secret.  I am only ten years older than
8225  you are, and I am wrinkled, and worn, and yellow.  You are really
8226  wonderful, Dorian.  You have never looked more charming than you do
8227  to-night. You remind me of the day I saw you first.  You were rather
8228  cheeky, very shy, and absolutely extraordinary.  You have changed, of
8229  course, but not in appearance.  I wish you would tell me your secret.
8230  To get back my youth I would do anything in the world, except take
8231  exercise, get up early, or be respectable.  Youth!  There is nothing
8232  like it.  It's absurd to talk of the ignorance of youth.  The only
8233  people to whose opinions I listen now with any respect are people much
8234  younger than myself.  They seem in front of me.  Life has revealed to
8235  them her latest wonder.  As for the aged, I always contradict the aged.
8236  I do it on principle.  If you ask them their opinion on something that
8237  happened yesterday, they solemnly give you the opinions current in
8238  1820, when people wore high stocks, believed in everything, and knew
8239  absolutely nothing.  How lovely that thing you are playing is!  I
8240  wonder, did Chopin write it at Majorca, with the sea weeping round the
8241  villa and the salt spray dashing against the panes?  It is marvellously
8242  romantic.  What a blessing it is that there is one art left to us that
8243  is not imitative!  Don't stop.  I want music to-night. It seems to me
8244  that you are the young Apollo and that I am Marsyas listening to you.
8245  I have sorrows, Dorian, of my own, that even you know nothing of.  The
8246  tragedy of old age is not that one is old, but that one is young.  I am
8247  amazed sometimes at my own sincerity.  Ah, Dorian, how happy you are!
8248  What an exquisite life you have had!  You have drunk deeply of
8249  everything.  You have crushed the grapes against your palate.  Nothing
8250  has been hidden from you.  And it has all been to you no more than the
8251  sound of music.  It has not marred you.  You are still the same."
8252  
8253  "I am not the same, Harry."
8254  
8255  "Yes, you are the same.  I wonder what the rest of your life will be.
8256  Don't spoil it by renunciations.  At present you are a perfect type.
8257  Don't make yourself incomplete.  You are quite flawless now.  You need
8258  not shake your head:  you know you are.  Besides, Dorian, don't deceive
8259  yourself.  Life is not governed by will or intention.  Life is a
8260  question of nerves, and fibres, and slowly built-up cells in which
8261  thought hides itself and passion has its dreams.  You may fancy
8262  yourself safe and think yourself strong.  But a chance tone of colour
8263  in a room or a morning sky, a particular perfume that you had once
8264  loved and that brings subtle memories with it, a line from a forgotten
8265  poem that you had come across again, a cadence from a piece of music
8266  that you had ceased to play--I tell you, Dorian, that it is on things
8267  like these that our lives depend.  Browning writes about that
8268  somewhere; but our own senses will imagine them for us.  There are
8269  moments when the odour of _lilas blanc_ passes suddenly across me, and I
8270  have to live the strangest month of my life over again.  I wish I could
8271  change places with you, Dorian.  The world has cried out against us
8272  both, but it has always worshipped you.  It always will worship you.
8273  You are the type of what the age is searching for, and what it is
8274  afraid it has found.  I am so glad that you have never done anything,
8275  never carved a statue, or painted a picture, or produced anything
8276  outside of yourself!  Life has been your art.  You have set yourself to
8277  music.  Your days are your sonnets."
8278  
8279  Dorian rose up from the piano and passed his hand through his hair.
8280  "Yes, life has been exquisite," he murmured, "but I am not going to
8281  have the same life, Harry.  And you must not say these extravagant
8282  things to me.  You don't know everything about me.  I think that if you
8283  did, even you would turn from me.  You laugh.  Don't laugh."
8284  
8285  "Why have you stopped playing, Dorian?  Go back and give me the
8286  nocturne over again.  Look at that great, honey-coloured moon that
8287  hangs in the dusky air.  She is waiting for you to charm her, and if
8288  you play she will come closer to the earth.  You won't?  Let us go to
8289  the club, then.  It has been a charming evening, and we must end it
8290  charmingly.  There is some one at White's who wants immensely to know
8291  you--young Lord Poole, Bournemouth's eldest son.  He has already copied
8292  your neckties, and has begged me to introduce him to you.  He is quite
8293  delightful and rather reminds me of you."
8294  
8295  "I hope not," said Dorian with a sad look in his eyes.  "But I am tired
8296  to-night, Harry.  I shan't go to the club.  It is nearly eleven, and I
8297  want to go to bed early."
8298  
8299  "Do stay.  You have never played so well as to-night. There was
8300  something in your touch that was wonderful.  It had more expression
8301  than I had ever heard from it before."
8302  
8303  "It is because I am going to be good," he answered, smiling.  "I am a
8304  little changed already."
8305  
8306  "You cannot change to me, Dorian," said Lord Henry.  "You and I will
8307  always be friends."
8308  
8309  "Yet you poisoned me with a book once.  I should not forgive that.
8310  Harry, promise me that you will never lend that book to any one.  It
8311  does harm."
8312  
8313  "My dear boy, you are really beginning to moralize.  You will soon be
8314  going about like the converted, and the revivalist, warning people
8315  against all the sins of which you have grown tired.  You are much too
8316  delightful to do that.  Besides, it is no use.  You and I are what we
8317  are, and will be what we will be.  As for being poisoned by a book,
8318  there is no such thing as that.  Art has no influence upon action.  It
8319  annihilates the desire to act.  It is superbly sterile.  The books that
8320  the world calls immoral are books that show the world its own shame.
8321  That is all.  But we won't discuss literature.  Come round to-morrow. I
8322  am going to ride at eleven.  We might go together, and I will take you
8323  to lunch afterwards with Lady Branksome.  She is a charming woman, and
8324  wants to consult you about some tapestries she is thinking of buying.
8325  Mind you come.  Or shall we lunch with our little duchess?  She says
8326  she never sees you now.  Perhaps you are tired of Gladys?  I thought
8327  you would be.  Her clever tongue gets on one's nerves.  Well, in any
8328  case, be here at eleven."
8329  
8330  "Must I really come, Harry?"
8331  
8332  "Certainly.  The park is quite lovely now.  I don't think there have
8333  been such lilacs since the year I met you."
8334  
8335  "Very well.  I shall be here at eleven," said Dorian.  "Good night,
8336  Harry."  As he reached the door, he hesitated for a moment, as if he
8337  had something more to say.  Then he sighed and went out.
8338  
8339  
8340  
8341  CHAPTER 20
8342  
8343  It was a lovely night, so warm that he threw his coat over his arm and
8344  did not even put his silk scarf round his throat.  As he strolled home,
8345  smoking his cigarette, two young men in evening dress passed him.  He
8346  heard one of them whisper to the other, "That is Dorian Gray." He
8347  remembered how pleased he used to be when he was pointed out, or stared
8348  at, or talked about.  He was tired of hearing his own name now.  Half
8349  the charm of the little village where he had been so often lately was
8350  that no one knew who he was.  He had often told the girl whom he had
8351  lured to love him that he was poor, and she had believed him.  He had
8352  told her once that he was wicked, and she had laughed at him and
8353  answered that wicked people were always very old and very ugly.  What a
8354  laugh she had!--just like a thrush singing.  And how pretty she had
8355  been in her cotton dresses and her large hats!  She knew nothing, but
8356  she had everything that he had lost.
8357  
8358  When he reached home, he found his servant waiting up for him.  He sent
8359  him to bed, and threw himself down on the sofa in the library, and
8360  began to think over some of the things that Lord Henry had said to him.
8361  
8362  Was it really true that one could never change?  He felt a wild longing
8363  for the unstained purity of his boyhood--his rose-white boyhood, as
8364  Lord Henry had once called it.  He knew that he had tarnished himself,
8365  filled his mind with corruption and given horror to his fancy; that he
8366  had been an evil influence to others, and had experienced a terrible
8367  joy in being so; and that of the lives that had crossed his own, it had
8368  been the fairest and the most full of promise that he had brought to
8369  shame.  But was it all irretrievable?  Was there no hope for him?
8370  
8371  Ah! in what a monstrous moment of pride and passion he had prayed that
8372  the portrait should bear the burden of his days, and he keep the
8373  unsullied splendour of eternal youth!  All his failure had been due to
8374  that.  Better for him that each sin of his life had brought its sure
8375  swift penalty along with it.  There was purification in punishment.
8376  Not "Forgive us our sins" but "Smite us for our iniquities" should be
8377  the prayer of man to a most just God.
8378  
8379  The curiously carved mirror that Lord Henry had given to him, so many
8380  years ago now, was standing on the table, and the white-limbed Cupids
8381  laughed round it as of old.  He took it up, as he had done on that
8382  night of horror when he had first noted the change in the fatal
8383  picture, and with wild, tear-dimmed eyes looked into its polished
8384  shield.  Once, some one who had terribly loved him had written to him a
8385  mad letter, ending with these idolatrous words: "The world is changed
8386  because you are made of ivory and gold.  The curves of your lips
8387  rewrite history."  The phrases came back to his memory, and he repeated
8388  them over and over to himself.  Then he loathed his own beauty, and
8389  flinging the mirror on the floor, crushed it into silver splinters
8390  beneath his heel.  It was his beauty that had ruined him, his beauty
8391  and the youth that he had prayed for.  But for those two things, his
8392  life might have been free from stain.  His beauty had been to him but a
8393  mask, his youth but a mockery.  What was youth at best?  A green, an
8394  unripe time, a time of shallow moods, and sickly thoughts.  Why had he
8395  worn its livery?  Youth had spoiled him.
8396  
8397  It was better not to think of the past.  Nothing could alter that.  It
8398  was of himself, and of his own future, that he had to think.  James
8399  Vane was hidden in a nameless grave in Selby churchyard.  Alan Campbell
8400  had shot himself one night in his laboratory, but had not revealed the
8401  secret that he had been forced to know.  The excitement, such as it
8402  was, over Basil Hallward's disappearance would soon pass away.  It was
8403  already waning.  He was perfectly safe there.  Nor, indeed, was it the
8404  death of Basil Hallward that weighed most upon his mind.  It was the
8405  living death of his own soul that troubled him.  Basil had painted the
8406  portrait that had marred his life.  He could not forgive him that.  It
8407  was the portrait that had done everything.  Basil had said things to
8408  him that were unbearable, and that he had yet borne with patience.  The
8409  murder had been simply the madness of a moment.  As for Alan Campbell,
8410  his suicide had been his own act.  He had chosen to do it.  It was
8411  nothing to him.
8412  
8413  A new life!  That was what he wanted.  That was what he was waiting
8414  for.  Surely he had begun it already.  He had spared one innocent
8415  thing, at any rate.  He would never again tempt innocence.  He would be
8416  good.
8417  
8418  As he thought of Hetty Merton, he began to wonder if the portrait in
8419  the locked room had changed.  Surely it was not still so horrible as it
8420  had been?  Perhaps if his life became pure, he would be able to expel
8421  every sign of evil passion from the face.  Perhaps the signs of evil
8422  had already gone away.  He would go and look.
8423  
8424  He took the lamp from the table and crept upstairs.  As he unbarred the
8425  door, a smile of joy flitted across his strangely young-looking face
8426  and lingered for a moment about his lips.  Yes, he would be good, and
8427  the hideous thing that he had hidden away would no longer be a terror
8428  to him.  He felt as if the load had been lifted from him already.
8429  
8430  He went in quietly, locking the door behind him, as was his custom, and
8431  dragged the purple hanging from the portrait.  A cry of pain and
8432  indignation broke from him.  He could see no change, save that in the
8433  eyes there was a look of cunning and in the mouth the curved wrinkle of
8434  the hypocrite.  The thing was still loathsome--more loathsome, if
8435  possible, than before--and the scarlet dew that spotted the hand seemed
8436  brighter, and more like blood newly spilled.  Then he trembled.  Had it
8437  been merely vanity that had made him do his one good deed?  Or the
8438  desire for a new sensation, as Lord Henry had hinted, with his mocking
8439  laugh?  Or that passion to act a part that sometimes makes us do things
8440  finer than we are ourselves?  Or, perhaps, all these?  And why was the
8441  red stain larger than it had been?  It seemed to have crept like a
8442  horrible disease over the wrinkled fingers.  There was blood on the
8443  painted feet, as though the thing had dripped--blood even on the hand
8444  that had not held the knife.  Confess?  Did it mean that he was to
8445  confess?  To give himself up and be put to death?  He laughed.  He felt
8446  that the idea was monstrous.  Besides, even if he did confess, who
8447  would believe him?  There was no trace of the murdered man anywhere.
8448  Everything belonging to him had been destroyed.  He himself had burned
8449  what had been below-stairs. The world would simply say that he was mad.
8450  They would shut him up if he persisted in his story.... Yet it was
8451  his duty to confess, to suffer public shame, and to make public
8452  atonement.  There was a God who called upon men to tell their sins to
8453  earth as well as to heaven.  Nothing that he could do would cleanse him
8454  till he had told his own sin.  His sin?  He shrugged his shoulders.
8455  The death of Basil Hallward seemed very little to him.  He was thinking
8456  of Hetty Merton.  For it was an unjust mirror, this mirror of his soul
8457  that he was looking at.  Vanity?  Curiosity?  Hypocrisy?  Had there
8458  been nothing more in his renunciation than that?  There had been
8459  something more.  At least he thought so.  But who could tell? ... No.
8460  There had been nothing more.  Through vanity he had spared her.  In
8461  hypocrisy he had worn the mask of goodness.  For curiosity's sake he
8462  had tried the denial of self.  He recognized that now.
8463  
8464  But this murder--was it to dog him all his life?  Was he always to be
8465  burdened by his past?  Was he really to confess?  Never.  There was
8466  only one bit of evidence left against him.  The picture itself--that
8467  was evidence.  He would destroy it.  Why had he kept it so long?  Once
8468  it had given him pleasure to watch it changing and growing old.  Of
8469  late he had felt no such pleasure.  It had kept him awake at night.
8470  When he had been away, he had been filled with terror lest other eyes
8471  should look upon it.  It had brought melancholy across his passions.
8472  Its mere memory had marred many moments of joy.  It had been like
8473  conscience to him.  Yes, it had been conscience.  He would destroy it.
8474  
8475  He looked round and saw the knife that had stabbed Basil Hallward.  He
8476  had cleaned it many times, till there was no stain left upon it.  It
8477  was bright, and glistened.  As it had killed the painter, so it would
8478  kill the painter's work, and all that that meant.  It would kill the
8479  past, and when that was dead, he would be free.  It would kill this
8480  monstrous soul-life, and without its hideous warnings, he would be at
8481  peace.  He seized the thing, and stabbed the picture with it.
8482  
8483  There was a cry heard, and a crash.  The cry was so horrible in its
8484  agony that the frightened servants woke and crept out of their rooms.
8485  Two gentlemen, who were passing in the square below, stopped and looked
8486  up at the great house.  They walked on till they met a policeman and
8487  brought him back.  The man rang the bell several times, but there was
8488  no answer.  Except for a light in one of the top windows, the house was
8489  all dark.  After a time, he went away and stood in an adjoining portico
8490  and watched.
8491  
8492  "Whose house is that, Constable?" asked the elder of the two gentlemen.
8493  
8494  "Mr. Dorian Gray's, sir," answered the policeman.
8495  
8496  They looked at each other, as they walked away, and sneered.  One of
8497  them was Sir Henry Ashton's uncle.
8498  
8499  Inside, in the servants' part of the house, the half-clad domestics
8500  were talking in low whispers to each other.  Old Mrs. Leaf was crying
8501  and wringing her hands.  Francis was as pale as death.
8502  
8503  After about a quarter of an hour, he got the coachman and one of the
8504  footmen and crept upstairs.  They knocked, but there was no reply.
8505  They called out.  Everything was still.  Finally, after vainly trying
8506  to force the door, they got on the roof and dropped down on to the
8507  balcony.  The windows yielded easily--their bolts were old.
8508  
8509  When they entered, they found hanging upon the wall a splendid portrait
8510  of their master as they had last seen him, in all the wonder of his
8511  exquisite youth and beauty.  Lying on the floor was a dead man, in
8512  evening dress, with a knife in his heart.  He was withered, wrinkled,
8513  and loathsome of visage.  It was not till they had examined the rings
8514  that they recognized who it was.
